# 道德经真义

**《The True Meaning of the Dao De Jing》 (Qing Dynasty) by Huang Yuanji**

**Preface (Excerpt)**

**Preface (Excerpt)**

**Note:**
The annotated edition of *The Daoist Classic of Laozi and Zhuangzi* was republished in Taiwan in 1970 under the title *The Refined Essence of the Daoist Classic*. In its preface, it states: “The Huang [commentary] is particularly profound, truly revealing the mysterious Dao, each word bearing essential meaning. Thus, it should rightly be called *‘Refined Essence’* rather than *‘Annotated’*—hence the name change upon reissue, to benefit all.”

The following excerpt is taken from Xiao Tian Shi’s preface to the large-character edition of *The Refined Essence of the Daoist Classic*.

**(I)** Across the ages, hundreds of scholars have annotated *The Daoist Classic*, with over fifty editions included in *The Daoist Canon*. The earliest commentaries were by Han Fei and He Shang Gong. Han Fei provided only *“Explicating Laozi”* and *“Parables of Laozi”*, while systematic annotation began with He Shang Gong. Among all the commentaries that have flourished over the millennia, the Wang Bi edition stands preeminent. During the Tang and Song dynasties, lesser scholars proliferated, and their works gradually increased in number—perhaps twenty to thirty enduring fine editions—but few achieved complete fidelity without error.

**(II)** The annotated editions within *The Daoist Canon* each possess their merits: the Tang Xuanzong edition emphasizes **exhausting principles and perfecting nature** (穷理尽性), **sitting in forgetfulness and abandoning traces** (坐忘遗照), **reducing matters and non-action** (损事无为), and **governing the body and affairs** (理身理事); the Song Huizong edition, comprising three versions, frequently cites passages from *The Zuo Tradition* and *The Book of Changes* (《左》、《易》) to support its interpretations. The Ming Taizu edition’s annotations adhere strictly to **cultivating harmony and achieving peace** (修齐治平), while Su Shi’s son, Su Zhe’s commentary, delves deeply into the **root essence** (根宗), offering many insights into **nature’s intrinsic essence** (性之言) and harmonizing the perspectives of three schools into a singular intent. In the Qing dynasty, Daoist talents emerged in abundance, with Longyuanzi, Song Changqing, and Master Huang Yuangji producing the finest commentaries. Among these, Master Huang’s work most effectively **follows the strengths of each school** (宗各家之所长) and **complements their shortcomings** (补百家之不足). The Song dynasty edition has already been published in the *Daoist Canon*’s third collection; this edition reprints Master Huang’s commentary within it.

**(III)** The distinctive features of Master Huang’s commentary are as follows: each chapter begins with **the eternal Dao** (常道), followed by **the Dao of alchemy** (丹道); it first discusses **worldly methods** (世法), then **alchemical methods** (丹法). The subtleties of Daoist learning are meticulously examined, with **profound meaning and refined logic** (精微，文理密察). It encompasses both **fundamentals and applications** (本末兼赅), harmonizing **form and function** (体用咸宜).

「Its excellence lies in **fusing Confucianism into Dao** (融儒入道) while leaving **no trace of artificiality** (凿空无痕); and in **expanding Dao to exalt Confucianism** (因道弘儒) while maintaining **a unified and complete whole** (浑全一体). It clarifies **the Dao and cultivates virtue** (明道修德), thereby setting **the standard for the customs of the world** (端天下之风尚); and it **nurtures the heart and harmonizes the qi** (养心养气), sufficient to **rectify the minds of all beings** (正成世之人心). The author **illuminates the Dao of immortality** (明仙道) through **every word as unbreakable truth** (字字金科玉律); and **embodies the sacred learning** (体圣学) to elucidate **mystical studies** (阐玄学), with **each utterance a direct transmission of the essence** (言言口诀心传). Deep yet accessible, warm and straightforward—**putting it into practice** (以之为用), one may **clarify the mind and perceive true nature** (明心见性), **attain sainthood and ascend to the Dao** (入圣登真), **manifest wisdom in worldly affairs** (明哲处世), or **govern the state and bring peace to the world** (治国平天下). Whether in **official life or seclusion**, it is equally applicable (仕隐咸宜). Judging by its merit, it is **indeed an immortal masterpiece** (不朽名着) among commentaries on the *Dao De Jing*. **Regardless of Daoist or Confucian schools**, all may **revere it as the supreme scripture** (无上圣经), viewing it as **the unparalleled path to sainthood and immortality** (修圣修仙之不二法门).

**(IV)** The **alchemical Dao’s texts grow ever more profound with age** (丹道经籍，愈古愈玄). In the **Upper Antiquity**, **alchemical scriptures were hidden in nine out of ten** (上古丹经，十隐八九); in the **Middle Antiquity**, **half were concealed** (中古丹经，十隐其半); until **recent times**, only **two or three out of ten were revealed** (近代丹经，十隐二三). This work by Master Huang was completed during the **transition between the Daoguang and Xianfeng reigns of the Qing dynasty** (清道咸之交), hence its ability to **unfurl the mystic mechanisms** (畅述玄机) and **display the essence of the Dao** (大露宗风), revealing **what the past sages kept secret** (往圣之所不泄者) and transmitting **what the past sages never imparted** (往圣之所不传者). In terms of **alchemical methods alone** (就丹法言丹法), this single text **already encompasses the vital essence of myriad alchemical canons** (括尽千经万典之要蕴), **without omission of great or small** (巨细无遗).

When Master Huang lectured at **Hall of Joyous Nurturing** (乐育堂), thousands of disciples entered his gate (入门弟子数千人). His works, *Hall of Joyous Nurturing Sayings* (《乐育堂语录》) and *Essentials of the Daoist Gate* (《道门语要》), have long circulated and are **held in high regard by the world** (风行，为世所重). Yet his *Oral Essentials of the Mystic Dao* (《玄宗口诀》) was **rarely copied and transmitted** (传抄很少). His texts such as *Seeking the Heart* (《求心》) and *Awakening the Heart* (《醒心》) have **already been lost** (已失传), and now that this work has been recovered, it is **unendurable to let it vanish again** (不忍再令其湮没).

**(V)** This book was originally published in **Qing Guangxu Year 10** (清光绪十年), with its plates stored in **Ziliu井, Sichuan** (四川自流井, 今自贡市自井区). Errors such as **misprints of fish for deer and boar for pig** (鲁鱼亥豕) were numerous. Three years ago, a printed edition was obtained from **Mr. Yin Qitang** (殷启唐先生), later sent back to South America. This year, the **unpunctuated edition from Mr. Wan Yang’s Hall of Nurturing Virtue** (乙亥华阳汪氏养性斋刊本), preserved by **Mr. Ma Bingwen and Mr. Ma Jiekang**, was acquired. Subsequently, a **refined edition from the Nanjing Red Cross Daoist Institute** (南京红十字会道院精刊本) was obtained through **Mr. Fu Anding** (傅安澄先生). For this reprinting, the original plan was to use the **Hall of Nurturing Virtue edition** (马养性斋刊本), but after careful collation and correction, the **Daoist Institute edition** (傅藏道院刊本) was chosen instead. **Mr. Yu Zheng** (俞以正) was too busy with Buddhist affairs to write about the provenance of these collected editions.

**Winter, 12th month, Year of the Rat (庚子孟冬月), Taipei** (台北),
**Shen Shi Xiao Tian Shi** (文山肖天石) wrote this.

**Master Huang’s Preface to the Original Edition: The Three Teachings’ Dao is the Dao of the Sage** (三教之道，圣道而已). Confucianism speaks of **ultimate sincerity** (至诚), Daoism of **golden elixir** (金丹), Buddhism of **true emptiness** (真空)—yet all **refer to the same primordial qi of the Great Void** (太虚一气), **pervading heaven, earth, and all beings** (贯乎天地人物). Only the **Sage uniquely probes its source** (独探其原) and **reaches its utmost** (造其极), **unifying with the boundless emptiness of heaven and earth** (与天地虚圆无二), thus becoming the Sage. It **can be both firm and yielding** (能刚能柔), **both circular and square** (可圆可方), **defying form or description** (无形状可拟), **transcending sound or scent** (无声臭可拘). Its **divine transformations and marvelous operations** (神灵变化) are **inexhaustible** (其妙无穷), with **aspects beyond comprehension** (有不可得而窥测者). If all is **natural and spontaneous** (皆自然天然), **originally so** (本来特事), **neither augmented by the Sage nor diminished by the ordinary** (处圣不增，处凡不减), then even **the lesser beings—birds, beasts, grasses, and trees** (鸟兽草木之微)—**share the same qi and the same principle** (同此一气、同此一理) as the Sage.

Consider the **vast, unfathomable ocean** (汪洋大海). Though its waters are **most difficult to measure** (水至难测), a **single ladleful** (一勺) contains no less than the **entire sea** (一海). The **qi of the Great Void** (太虚之气) is **likewise boundless**. Though **heaven, earth, sages, and all beings** (天地、圣贤、人物) may appear **diverse and chaotic** (纷纭错杂), with **ten thousand things unequal** (万有不齐), their **initial reception of qi and formation** (受气成形之初) all **originate from the same qi** (同此一气). Beyond this, **there is no other vital qi** (别无生气), nor **any other generative principle** (别无生理)—what differs is merely **the varied dispositions and endowments** (姿禀之各殊).

Mencius said, **"The Yao and Shun shared the same nature as ordinary people"** (尧舜与人同); and again, **"The distinction between humans and beasts is slight"** (人之异于禽兽者几希). This is **indeed a true discourse** (诚确论也).

The times are such that the **Dao/Way of the world declines daily** (世风日下), and **folk customs grow ever more deceitful** (民俗益偷). Though the **Great Dao/Way** (大道) belongs to the **plain and ordinary** (平常), people often regard it with **strange and bizarre eyes** (诡怪离奇目之). Thus, **Confucianism is ever more unlike true Confucianism** (儒益非儒), **Buddhism is ever more unlike true Buddhism** (释益非释), and **Daoism is ever more unlike true Daoism** (道益非道).

If one does not **point out the roots and origins** (指出根源), **pierce through and reveal the mysteries** (抉破窍妙), then **the Great Dao/Way will grow ever more obscured and unmanifest** (大道愈晦而不彰), and **human hearts will grow ever more corrupt and difficult to govern** (人心愈坏而难治)—until **all must be reduced to sharing the same fate as birds, beasts, grasses, and trees** (与鸟兽草木同群), while **sages and worthies are likened to mere barbarians and uncouth men** (圣贤直等诸弁髦), and **the Great Dao/Way becomes as perilous as a pile of eggs** (大道益危如累卵), **hanging precariously between heaven and earth** (虚悬天壤), with **no one daring to bear the weight of this sacred culture** (无人能任斯文之责).

In this fortunate age of **prosperity and peace** (恭逢盛世), when the **world is at rest** (天下平安), I **chanced upon** (适遇) **renowned mountain companions** (名山道友) and engaged in **discussions of the mysterious and wondrous** (谈玄说妙). Their words were **not mere idle chatter** (实非空谈者流), nor **empty rhetoric for the ears** (徒来口耳之用), but **truly beneficial** (不无小补于世). Thus, I **gained insight in their midst** (因得与于其际).

The **many esteemed friends** (群尊) **honored me with the teacher’s seat** (予以师席), so I **night and day expounded** (日夜讲论) the **"Scripture of Dao and Virtue"** (“道德”一经), treating it as **evidence for cultivating the self and establishing virtue/power** (修身立德之证). Before I knew it, **I had written page upon page** (不觉连篇累牍). Yet **their flaws were evident time and again** (第其瑕疵迭见), and **I found it hard to question such lofty minds** (殊难质诸高明).

Still, **there were places where their thoughts aligned with the lofty subtleties of the Supreme One** (有与太上微意偶合处), and **their words did offer some small benefit to the world** (不无小补于世). My **friends urged me to edit and publish** (众友请付剞厥), **to make known to the world and future generations** (公诸天下后世). Though **I truly feel much shame in this annotation** (予于此注实多抱愧), I **do not dare deceive myself or others** (不敢自欺欺人).

Yet **I could not refuse their earnest entreaties** (无奈众友念切), so I **reluctantly agreed** (始诺其请). Now that this **publication is about to commence** (兹刊刻肇始), I **therefore set forth these few words at the beginning** (故弁数言于篇首), **to recount the origins of this annotation** (以叙此注之由来).

Winter of the **10th year of Guangxu** (光绪十年孟冬月)
**Dànyàn** (谷旦) **Huángjiǎn** (黄裳) **self-preface** (元吉自序)

**Chapter One: The Gateway to All Marvels**

**The Dao that can be spoken is not the eternal Dao;**
**The name that can be named is not the eternal Name.**

Before the naming of Heaven and Earth, there was the Nameless—
The origin of all ten thousand things.
Thus, **to observe its marvel through desirelessness,**
And **to observe its subtleties through desire.**

These two, though differing in name, arise from the same source,
And are called the **Mysterious**—
**Mysterious beyond measure, the gateway to all marvels.**

Zhūzǐ said: *"The Dao is like a path, shared by all."*

In truth, it is the principle by which **Heaven, Earth, and all beings are born.**
Before Heaven and Earth were divided, this Dao hung in the boundless void;
After Heaven and Earth were opened, this Dao was entrusted to the realms of Heaven and Earth.

What Dao is this?
It existed before Heaven and Earth and endures eternally,
It follows after Heaven and Earth yet never decays.
Born before Heaven and Earth, it mingles within the void—
**Neither visible nor audible.**

Thus, the Supreme One says:
*"Speaking of the Dao, it is subtle and hidden—*
**Truly, there is no Dao to speak of.**
What can be spoken of are but its manifestations,
Not the eternal Dao itself."*

*"Speaking of names, they are empty and without substance—*
**Truly, there is no name to speak of.**
What can be named are but its dregs,
Not the eternal Name itself."*

If one does not know the Dao,
**How shall one observe it in the *Odes*?**
It says: *"The carriage of Heaven moves—*
**Without sound, without scent"**—
*The Dao cannot be grasped in words!*

Again it says: *"The mandate of Heaven is solemn and unceasing"**—
*The Dao cannot be without designation.*

Know that within the **uttermost void,**
There exists the **uttermost reality;**
Within the **uttermost emptiness,**
There exists the **uttermost substance.**

The Dao is truly beyond what can be spoken of.

The Supreme One, in boundless compassion, taught the world:
Before the **Hóngmèng** stirrings, all was **indistinct and formless**—
Though called *Nameless*, yet Heaven, Earth, and all beings are nurtured within it.
Thus, it is the **beginning of Heaven and Earth.**

When stillness endured long enough,
And **qi** moved with a pulse,
Then came the **Namable**—
A vast **Tàihé** breath of primordial qi,
Circulating through the cosmos,
Nourishing all life.
Thus, it is the **mother of all things.**

*Beginning* refers to the **cluster of primordial qi** before Heaven and Earth opened;
*Mother* refers to the **transformation and birth of all things** after their division.

For the student at the outset,
There is no other method—
Only **sitting upright with a focused mind,**
**Discarding all thoughts,**
**Observing with a veiled gaze.**

Beneath the heart, above the kidneys,
There seems a **void within the void.**
**Spirit and qi** reflect each other,
**Breath by breath, returning endlessly.**
Let them **flow in and out naturally,**
But **concentrate both spirit and qi** in the **central palace** as the primary focus.

In no time, **spirit and qi merge as one.**
Then, **listen to the indistinct murmur,**
**Without a single conscious thought.**
After long practice, **dimly aware,**
One enters the realm of **"Where-Nothing-Is."**

At this moment,
One does not know if **spirit enters qi or qi returns to spirit**—
**All is undifferentiated, beyond self and world, beyond place and Heaven,**
Yet not in **stupor**—
For if in stupor, one would become **a withered tree, cold ashes.**

The cultivator, having reached this point,
**Must extinguish the stirring of thought,**
**Yet preserve the luminous awareness.**
Only thus—**wise yet appearing foolish, enlightened yet not acting**—
In the instant of **no-knowing, no-awareness,**
A sudden **awakening stirs,**
And the **Tàijí** is established.

Know that within this **awakening,**
**Spontaneous and unforced,**
Lies one’s **original true awareness.**
The Daoists call this the **Mysterious Gateway, the Subtle Aperture**—
Found in **each inhalation and exhalation.**

When inhaling, it is **yin, stillness, non-being;**
When exhaling, it is **yang, motion, being.**
Even in this **single breath,**
There lies a **subtle aperture.**

If one seeks **true awakening,**
It is in this **moment of awakening**—
A **truly real, undeniably solid, thought-free, dross-free original self** that emerges.

Thus it is said:
**Heaven and Earth awaken with this moment and give birth to ten thousand things;**
**Humans awaken with this moment and forge the Golden Elixir.**

But this awakening is **like lightning and a spark—**
Present in the instant, yet gone in a blink.
The difference lies in **a hair’s breadth.**

The scholar must **discern this clearly in ordinary times**
To **grasp it firmly when the moment arises.**

Even the **Great Awakened Ones** of old
Achieved nothing beyond this **accumulation of awakening.**

If the cultivator does not begin with
**Desirelessness and desire, observing marvel and aperture,**
Then from where shall they establish their foundation?

Though **non-being and being, marvel and aperture**
Are but **yin-stillness and yang-motion**—
**One breath divided into two breaths,**
Yet the two breaths still return to **one breath.**

When stillness endures long enough and motion arises,
**Non-being generates being—**
Called **"One Yang is born, the Active Child."**

When motion reaches its peak and returns to stillness,
**Being returns to non-being—**
Called **"Returning to the Root, restoring the Mandate."**

All are but the **divided yin and yang of the Supreme Ultimate.**

Though differing in name,
They arise from **one source—**
The Supreme One calls it **Mysterious.**
**Mysterious** means **profound and distant.**

The scholar who seeks the **Mysterious Dao**
Must **be still again and again,**
**Be steadfast yet again,**
Until within, **all is undisturbed—**
This is **observing marvel through desirelessness.**
**The first Mysterious.**

When **qi stirs and moves,**
Though there is awareness, **no single thought arises;**
Though there is motion, **no single notion lingers.**
**One mind, no dual thoughts—**
This is **observing aperture through desire.**
**The second Mysterious.**

As for **Mysterious beyond measure,**
It is truly the **return to the Root—**
What else could be the **Gateway to All Marvels?**

Alas, though common folk possess this **subtle aperture,**
They do not **nurture it directly,**
Thus it **opens and closes in vain,**
Until it is **exhausted without end.**

The **Perfect Sage**, when the **Mysterious Gateway** and **Aperture** open,
Fixes with **one gaze** and **one hand**—
**Cultivates and nurtures** with **unremitting care,**
Never allowing it to stray for an instant—
Thus **directly attaining** the **Supreme Root Source**,
And **becoming** the **Great Awakened Golden Immortal.**

The **initial practice** lies within the **Mysterious Gateway** and **Aperture.**
The **First Chapter** of the *Taoist Canon* already points out
**The Nameless and the Named,**
**Observing Marvel through Desirelessness** and **Observing Aperture through Desire,**
Sufficiently revealing that **cultivation of the Dao**
Departs from nothing else but this **Mysterious Gateway** and **Aperture.**
Hence, the **opening four verses**
Speak of the **Great Dao’s Root Source**—
Truly **formless and stateless,**
Beyond **conception and exhaustive inquiry.**

Only in the **beginning of Heaven and Earth’s** unmanifested state,
**Chaotic and indistinct,**
Without **any discernible trace,**
Is it like when a person **directly nurtures** in **stillness.**

At the **moment Heaven and Earth suddenly split open,**
**Stillness reaches its peak and motion begins,**
**Awakening in an instant,**
This is when a person **discerns qi in movement**—
The **foundation** for **refining the elixir.**

Within **this fleeting glance,**
Without **the wisdom-pearl** or **the insight-sword,**
One cannot attain it.

Essentially, **the moment thoughts arise** is the **Mysterious Feminine,**
Truly the **beginning of Heaven’s splitting and Earth’s opening.**
Since ancient times, **divine immortals and sages**
Have all been **born from this moment of awakening and movement.**

It is also said:
**"Observing Marvel through Desirelessness,"**
**"Observing Aperture through Desire,"**
These two—**one still, one moving**—
Mutually **serve as their own roots,**
Thus **same in origin, yet different in name.**

All things with **form and likeness**
Can be **grasped through contemplation and divination,**
But this **subtle aperture**—
**Without yet present, present yet without,**
Cannot be **named or described by measure.**

Even if the **tongue hangs like a suspended river,**
It cannot **utter a single word** of its truth—
Hence it is called **Mysterious beyond Mysterious.**

The scholar **must not regard it as distant and obscure,**
Yet **still fails to inquire** into its **concrete reality.**

If one **truly seeks it** in this **dim and hazy** state,
While others see it as **flickering and vague,**
For oneself, it is **graspable and certain.**

With **time, practice brings forth vitality,**
And **each step aligns with the Dao.**
**Chapter Two: *Accomplishment without Dwelling***

All under Heaven know **beauty** as **beauty**—
Then **ugliness** already arises;
All know **goodness** as **goodness**—
Then **non-goodness** already appears.

Thus, **the presence and absence of forms** are **interdependent,**
**Difficulty and ease** **complement each other,**
**Length and shortness** **define one another,**
**High and low** **lean toward equilibrium,**
**Sounds and voices** **harmonize together,**
**Before and after** **follow in sequence.**

Hence, the **Sage acts in non-doing,**
**Practices speechless instruction,**
**All things arise** yet **no claim is made,**
**Life unfolds** yet **no possession is taken,**
**Actions are done** yet **no credit is retained,**
**Accomplishment is achieved** yet **no dwelling is taken;**
**Only by not dwelling does it not depart.**

The ancients said:
*"I urge you to exhaustively seek the origin of your vital body,
Returning to the Root and Source—this is the Elixir Sage."*
And again:
*"Exhaustively seek the origin of your vital body at the moment of receiving life,
Do not blame the Heavenly Mechanism for being fully revealed."*

From this perspective, it is clear that
At the **beginning of receiving life,**
**Heaven’s principles** are **wholly manifest,**
Without **any flaw or blemish.**
When they say **beauty** or **ugliness,**
**Goodness** or **deformity,**
These are all **harmful to the Dao.**

What is the **Great Dao** truly like?
In **Confucianism**, it is called **"Hidden and Subtle"**—
Within it lies the **essence of unseen and unheard.**
In **Buddhism**, it is called **"That"**—
Within it lies the **true reality of neither good nor evil.**
In **Daoism**, it is called **"Mysterious Gateway"**—
Within it lies the **depth of no thought and no deliberation.**

The **Great Dao’s Root Source**
Ultimately **originates from here.**
Once **imagined or conceptualized,**
It **falls into the trap of duality;**
Once **discussed or theorized,**
It **traps itself in the net of words.**
Though **ancient sages and immortals**
Praise the Dao’s **marvels** and say it is **beautiful** or **good,**
They all **flicker at its form**—
None truly **discern its essence.**

For **goodness** is **contrasted by evil,**
And **beauty** is **contrasted by deformity.**
Moreover, **beauty is present here, yet ugliness too;**
**Goodness is present here, yet deformity too.**
This is **indeed the post-Heavenly interplay of yin and yang**—
**With opposition, with victories and losses,**
Not the **pre-Heavenly single primordial qi.**

Thus, the *Taoist Canon* says:
*"All under Heaven know beauty as beauty—
Then ugliness already arises;
All know goodness as goodness—
Then non-goodness already appears."*

It is known that **people do not seek the Dao’s formless qi,**
But only **speak of beauty as beauty, goodness as goodness**—
This is **abandoning the root for the branches.**

The *Taoist Canon* **specifically instructs** the **initial practice**,
And **declares to the masses:**
When a person **begins seated meditation,**
**All thoughts must be released,**
**The mind utterly empty,**
No **single affair** **interfering within the chest.**

Only then **lower the curtain and seal the mouth,**
**Observe the formless elixir field**—
**Concentrate the spirit** and **regulate the breath,**
**Regulate the breath** and **concentrate the spirit,**
Thus, **with time, spirit and qi merge into one cluster,**
And **in an instant** they **enter the distant void**—
This is **non-doing.**

When **stillness reaches its utmost extreme,**
**Suddenly, one awakens and moves**—
This is **doing.**

From **this initial thought arising within the rules,**
**Chaotic and continuous, surging and boundless,**
**Spirit follows qi’s foundation, qi follows spirit’s motion,**
**Without knowing yet knowing, without awareness yet aware,**
The **Gateway to the Mysterious Feminine** **stands revealed.**

Thus, **strictly adhering to the rules within the rules,**
**Concentrating the spirit beyond the form,**
**One breath in, one breath out,**
**One forward, one backward,**
**Qi must return to the Mysterious Aperture.**

**Breathing follows naturalness**—
This is the **root of Heaven, Earth, and all beings,**
The **foundation of sages, immortals, and Buddhas,**
The **most secret heavenly mechanism** of our **Daoist School,**
**Not to be lightly divulged.**

For **practitioners of the Dao,**
**Better to seek it in yin and yang**—
**More concrete and verifiable**—
Than in the **indescribable void.**

**The presence and absence of forms** **interdependently arise**—
Nothing more than **stillness giving way to movement,**
**Movement giving way to stillness,**
**Emerging from the Mysterious, entering the Feminine,**
**Balancing the harmony of yin and yang.**

**Difficulty and ease** **complement each other**—
Nothing more than **rigidity yielding to flexibility,**
**Flexibility yielding to rigidity,**
**Refining in the cauldron, tempering with the sword,**
**One heating, one cooling.**

**Length and shortness** **define one another**—
This is **breathing in and out,**
**The governing and directing channels’ flow,**
**The forward breath being short, the backward breath being long.**

**High and low** **lean toward equilibrium**—
This is **fire above, causing it to descend,**
**Water below, causing it to ascend,**
The **wondrous application** of **inversion and separation.**

**Sound and voice harmonize**—
This is **spirit merging with qi**, **a hundred vessels flowing freely**,
As though a crane calls and the flock responds in unison—
Voices in resonance, unsummoned yet arriving.

**Front and back follow one another**—
This is **the Child racing behind**, **the Noon descending ahead**,
**Heaven and Earth in union**, **harmonized as one mass**,
Clinging yet never parting.

These are all **returning the post-heaven yin-yang to the pre-heaven Non-Extreme**.

The Sage knows the **root of the Way**—
**Vague and boundless, vast and untraceable**—
In governing affairs, **he prizes non-action**,
And follows the **reverent self-restraint, the flowing robe** of old.

In teaching, **he takes silence as his principle**,
And embodies the **subtle transformation of spirit-preservation**.

The Sage acts, and all things behold—
What difficulty could there be?

Thus, **plowing fields and digging wells**,
They are born into life yet forget their own deeds;
**Opening sources and regulating flow**,
They guide transformation yet erase their own traces.

Even if their **virtue fills Heaven and Earth**,
And their name resounds across the world,
The Sage would still **shun it as shameful**—
For empty fame has never borne real achievement.

How could it compare to the *Book of Documents* saying:
*"You alone do not boast, do not vaunt yourself—*
*None under Heaven can contend with you in ability, in merit,"*
Yet still **diminish the thought of human strife**?

This is surely the realm of **spirit-transformation**,
The **tranquil and settled heaven**,
Where one simply **finds contentment in self-sufficiency**,
Never forgetting the **true essence of self-realization**.

The ancients spoke of **viewing wealth and nobility as fleeting clouds**,
And **discarding fame like worn-out shoes**—
Is this not what they meant?

Yet, **when the Way is complete, virtue stands firm**,
**Achievement realized, name naturally follows**.

Though the Sage does not claim merit,
The world and future ages **ceaselessly praise the Way**—
Thus, **without speaking of merit, merit matches sun and moon**,
**Without speaking of name, name weighs heavier than antiquity**.

Only because **he does not dwell on it** does it not depart.

Students must **from the utmost emptiness and profound stillness**,
Nurture forth the **true essence of neither beauty nor goodness**,
Only then can cultivation have its foundation.

What is this Way?
**The Mysterious Pass**!

Abandon this, and there is **no life**.
The cultivator, **abandoning this Mysterious Pass**,
Has no other so-called Way.

If one takes **beauty and goodness as the Way**,
It is but **post-heaven dust and filth**.

The *Taoist Canon* warns of this,
Hoping people will **trace the source from the flow**.

Otherwise, the praise of **beauty and goodness**
Is also the virtue of the **gentlemen of the Three Dynasties**—
How could one blame them too severely?

The *Classic of Changes* says:
*"One yin, one yang—this is the Way."*
Thus, **yang is not the Way**, **yin is not the Way**—
The Way lies **between yin and yang**!

Moreover, the **Way is principle**, **yin and yang are qi**.
Principle without qi cannot stand,
Qi without principle cannot move.

To speak of the Way alone is **formless and indescribable**,
Only when observed through **yin and yang’s manifestations**
Does it truly have **concrete evidence**.

This chapter speaks of the **true essence of neither goodness nor beauty**,
Directly **uncovering the root of the Great Way**,
Hoping people will **establish their foundation and set their extreme**,
As the basis for **cultivating the self and governing the world**.

The phrases on **ease and difficulty, presence and absence**
Teach one to **return from opposing yin-yang to the true unity of qi**.
Within this, it also teaches one to **seek the Mysterious Pass**
As the **root of elixir-refining**.

As for **preserving the Center and nurturing the Elixir**,
**The Child’s vitality thriving**, **turning the River-Chariot**—
All are **layered revelations**.

Only the Sage points directly to the source,
Thus **reverent self-restraint and non-action**,
**Silent yet trusted**—
Though there is **action and doing**, in oneself there is **no trace of virtue**.

When achievement is complete and one retires,
To **view wealth and nobility as unworthy of weight**—
Who but the Sage could attain this learning?

Students ponder and gain insight—
Not only do they have a **foundation for practice**,
But also a **path to transcendence**.

Other commentaries say:
*"All under Heaven know why beauty and goodness are beauty and goodness,*
*Then they themselves will not do evil or wrong."*
This teaching is also correct.

But the *Taoist Canon* speaks mostly from the **source**,
Not falling into the **Two Vehicles**.

**Chapter Three:**
*"See no desirable things,*
*Hold no virtue in high regard—*
*Thus the people do not contend.*
*Do not prize hard-to-obtain goods—*
*Thus the people do not steal.*
*See no desirable things—*
*Thus the heart does not乱 (be disturbed)."*

Therefore, the Sage’s governance—
**Empty their minds, fill their bellies,**
**Weaken their wills, strengthen their bones,**
**Always keep the people in ignorance and desirelessness,**
So that even the wise **dare not act**.

**Non-action**—
Then there is **nothing left ungoverned**.

The Sage’s governance of the world—
**Better to do nothing than to act,**
And even better to **act without acting**.

In transforming the people and establishing customs—
**Better to transform than to influence,**
And even better to **let them rest in harmony without disturbance**.

Thus, **Yao and Shun**, with **reverent self-restraint and flowing robes**,
Brought **the Four Quarters into order**—
What **principle of transformation** was this?

It was simply:
**The ruler’s lack of scheming made the people still,**
**The ruler’s lack of desire made the people upright,**
**The ruler’s lack of greed made the people settled.**

Otherwise, **busy and agitated**,
They believe they are **benefiting the people and removing their hardships**,
Yet **they do not realize how greatly they disturb them**.

Thus it is said:
*"The people originally do not contend—*
*Yet the ruler provokes contention.*
*The people originally are not greedy—*
*Yet the ruler’s greed incites them.*
*The people originally have no thoughts, no desires—*
*Yet the ruler leads with strange arts, licentious cleverness,*
*Gay clothing, and fine foods."*

How could they **not contend, not be greedy, have no cravings, no desires**?

If one **cultivates the self and rectifies the heart**,
**Serene and unadorned, indifferent to all things**,
And **does not hold virtue and ability in high regard**,
Then the people **will naturally be at ease and not contend**.

If one **does not prize hard-to-obtain goods as noble**,
Then the people **will value modesty and shame**,
And **will not steal**.

Moreover, the **arenas of sound, color, wealth, and profit** are many—
For the eyes, **see nothing, desire nothing**,
And **each finds their own natural ease**—
Then the **empty, responsive, lively spirit**
**Naturally remains ever-present and undisturbed**.

This matter is not beyond human capacity—
Only the Sage shuts out ear and eye,
Severs all private inclinations,
Embraces the One to empty the heart.
When the heart is empty, the elixir’s source is refined.

Thus, he gathers the **Dao’s luminous yang-qi** from heaven and earth,
To transform the yin-essence,
Day by day, month by month,
Until the yin-essence naturally vanishes
And the yang-qi flourishes—
Filling the belly to preserve the form,
This is **congealing the body with the Way**,
**prolonging life with technique**—
The very method of transcending death and escaping birth.

Moreover, by focusing the qi to attain supreme softness,
Like an infant’s feeble strength,
Unable to grasp anything—
Though supremely soft,
Yet when it moves, it is firm.
Observe its vast and deep, towering and surging—
Truly it can encompass heaven and earth with ease.
All knowing and desiring—
Where has it gone?

The Sage cultivates himself thus.
And with this, he governs the world—
Within himself, no knowledge, no desire—
Not only do the foolish wander in muddled confusion,
In harmony with the silent and tranquil heaven,
But even the clever and wise scholars,
Who daily boast of their talents and rely on their intelligence,
Fearful only of failing to excel—
Now, serene and indifferent, they abandon their private knowledge,
Returning to primordial simplicity.
This is *able but does not act*—
Not because they cannot, but because they dare not.

Yet, though born between heaven and earth,
One cannot escape the void
And stand apart from all things—
There must be the Way of human relationships and daily practice.
How then can one *not* act?

But if one follows its natural course,
Acting without contrivance—
Though one acts, it remains non-action—
Just as heaven acts without speaking yet transforms all,
And the seasons proclaim their teachings without command;
The Emperor governs without action,
While ministers administer the affairs.
Action is its trace,
Non-action is its essence.
Thus, seated in majesty, the world rejoices in peace—
Ah, how splendid!

The Way is ever plain,
Not vaunting novelty,
Not prizing strangeness.
When a state honors the virtuous—
This is a noble thing,
Yet if one boasts of it,
Then the virtuous remain virtuous,
But the unworthy will also feign virtue.
Even the virtuous may reject what is rejected,
While the unworthy reject what is virtuous—
Thus, contention arises.
Each side proclaims its own merits,
Mutually supporting factions,
And the world becomes troubled.

When a state manages finances—
This is a common practice,
Yet if rare goods and distant treasures are prized,
The people will scale mountains and cross seas,
Risking peril and danger,
Undeterred by toil or fear for life,
To gather and present them to the court.
When they fail to obtain them,
They resort to every scheme—
Even theft and robbery are not shunned.

As for clothing and food—
These are daily necessities,
Yet if one demands exquisite delicacies
And fine silks,
Seeing what is desirable and desiring it—
When will extravagance cease?

Thus, the Sage values the inner over the outer,
Must empty the heart to nourish the spirit,
Fill the belly to nourish the qi,
So that spirit and qi merge as one,
Flowing harmoniously through the body—
Smooth, relaxed, and joyful,
And the will grows weak.
Moreover, the spirit steadies like a mountain,
The qi flows like a spring,
And the bones grow strong.
Ever holding to the One,
Ever guarding the Center—
Not only is one’s own desire and thought extinguished,
But even the clever and wise,
Moved by this, transform themselves,
Daring not to act arbitrarily.

Some say: *If one acts, there will be chaos and criticism;
If one does not act, there will be purity but scorn.*
Between action and non-action—
This is difficult indeed.

Yet to act in accordance with principle,
Not presumptuously—
Action and non-action are equal.
Thus, Confucius praised Shun:
*"He who governs by non-action—
Is this not Shun?"*

**Chapter Four: Blending with the Dust**

The Way is boundless yet applied—
Sometimes not full,
Deep like the source of all things.
It dulls the sharp,
Unravels the tangled,
Blends its light,
Merges with the dust.
Mysterious—
As if it exists.
I do not know whose child it is,
Resembling the Emperor before the beginning.
The Emperor—
The Supreme Sovereign.
The beginning—
The beginningless beginning.

What is the Way?
The Great Harmony, one breath,
Filling heaven and earth,
Its measure encompassing the cosmos,
Its spirit permeating past and future,
Its virtue extending to all lands and nations.
From embryonic to born, from wet to dry,
From flying to creeping, from moving to still—
There is nowhere it is not.

How vast is the Way?

Yet in its essence,
Empty and boundless,
Formless and void—
If unseen, it seems excessive;
When it manifests in use,
It is harmonious and embraced—
Applied here, it fits;
Placed there, it is appropriate.
The *Odes* say:
*"Left, left—nothing is unsuited;
Right, right—nothing is lacking."*
Truly like rivers flowing to the sea,
Yet the sea does not overflow.
Is this not the fundamental principle of all things?

Confucius said:
*"The virtue of ghosts and spirits—
It rests upon all things without omission."*
And again:
*"Speak of small things without exaggeration,
Speak of great things without omission."*
Its vast and deep—
Truly there is an inexhaustible mystery.

The Way is so difficult to describe—
How then shall later generations cultivate it?

The Most High, compassionate toward ordinary people,
Points to its essence:
Ordinary people cannot enter the Way
Because the clever and wise,
Relying on their talents,
Act arbitrarily,
Thus blocking the luminous spirit
And failing to see.

Now, to cultivate the Way,
One must know that cleverness and wisdom
Are demons that obstruct the path.
Thus, one must discard intelligence,
Dim the brightness,
Set aside private perceptions of ear and eye,
Returning to primordial chaos,
And extinguish all arrogance of talent,
All reliance on cleverness,
All haughtiness toward others—
Then the human heart dies,
And the Dao-heart is born.
Knowledge is extinguished,
And wisdom shines forth.

The ancient scholars said: Those with cleverness and talent are not to be feared—only those who sink deeply into the Way, purify the heart, and observe its principles are truly formidable. Is this not the truth?

Practitioners must primarily focus on calming the spirit, refining thoughts, reducing desires, and clarifying the heart. Yet how can one tame the restless mind—awareness, thought, and calculation—where foul and false impulses surge like tangled silk? Before one thought subsides, another arises; before one reflection fades, another emerges. I must exhort and encourage myself, granting myself both leniency and resolution—like unraveling chaotic threads, I must trace their origins and straighten them; like clearing overgrown vines, I must uproot them and level the ground. Only then do the entanglements dissolve, and the celestial sovereign remains ever serene.

Yet this is but the practice of solitary stillness—still far from thriving in tumultuous places. If one can only be still and not move, it remains a rootless study. One must also reflect in stillness, but when thrust into bustling crowds, one must be especially vigilant! In all things, yield precedence to others, place yourself last, exalt others above, and willingly submit below. Thus, you will neither clash with the world nor contend with people.

Moreover, men delight in the same while loathing the different—this is the common nature of the world. I must only follow the current, align with their kind, and harmonize with them. Though I possess a luminous and upright heart, I will never display its edges. I must only intuitively grasp its mechanisms, subtly uphold its essence—cherishing those who agree, tolerating those who differ. Hence, the man of Lu who hunted deer, and Confucius himself who hunted deer. In ancient times, when the Great Way was yet unclear, all sages blended with the vulgar to avoid harm.

Again, observe the Sixth Patriarch after receiving the robe and bowl—though his enlightenment was complete, its subtleties remained unexhausted. Thus, he lingered in the world, dwelling in Huìjì Mountain, living among hunters with unfeigned ease, escaping calamity. Had he not harmonized with the dust and blended with the mundane, how could he have preserved his life?

Through dynamic and static cultivation, ever-adapting with expedient means, the originally pure, still, and luminous essence naturally abides within. Yet it is not a fixed attainment—if it were fixed, it would not be the Way. If it had a place or abode, it would not be the Way. A single pulse of luminous spirit flickers beneath the golden court. What is this Way?

It arises before Heaven and Earth, mingling within the void. I know not whence it comes, nor whither it goes—whose child is it? The *Classic* says: *"There is a primal thing, indistinctly formed, born before Heaven and Earth."* Is this not the profound mystery of the Way?

What was before the Emperor? Nothing but the undifferentiated chaos, the indistinct *Hóngmáng*, a single breath of emptiness. When the One gave rise to myriad transformations and all things returned to spring, the Way dispersed through Heaven, Earth, and all beings—yet it remains inscrutable. Those who seek to illuminate the Way’s essence must perceive it in the moment when Heaven and Earth are on the verge of unfolding, when the unfurled suddenly unfurls, and measure it. Then they grasp the Way’s origin, and their practice will not lack foundation.

The Most High sketches the Way’s form for all to see, teaching those who seek the Great Way to first recognize its source. To seek the source, one must first purge deceit from the heart and preserve sincerity, beginning with humble, gradual cultivation. Over time, one attains the realm of divine transformation, realizing that the Way’s essence dwells perpetually within one’s nature, never leaving the embrace.

One must discern its traces in stillness, using cultivation, reflection, and preservation to safeguard true nature—neither overwhelming others with arrogance nor confusing the mind with excess. This is what Zhāng Zǐ called "liberating oneself from private desires."

Piercing the clouds, one beholds the blue sky; severing the vines, one reveals the true face. Yet when interacting with others, some bury their light, blending with the dust; some flaunt their talents or abilities; others assume the guise of recluse, displaying lofty detachment. History shows many who, through such displays, invited disaster and ruin—great harm to the Way. Like the Han dynasty’s imprisonment of scholars or the Jin dynasty’s persecution of the "pure currents," though rooted in petty treachery, it also stemmed from ignorance of the sage’s art of self-preservation.

If one blends with the vulgar, harmonizes with the world, and mingles with the dust like the *lingzhī* among common grasses or the phoenix flying with common birds—unnoticed yet fragrant, unseen yet exalted—then, through such concealment and nurturing, the still and eternal Way manifests indistinctly between the brows: present yet not present, with form yet without form.

As the *Zhōngyōng* says: *"The movements of Heaven are soundless and odorless."* Truly profound! What else could be before the Emperor?

---
**Chapter Five: Not Knowing to Uphold the Middle**

Heaven and Earth are not kind—they treat all things as sacrificial dogs.
The sage is not kind—they treat the people as sacrificial dogs.
Between Heaven and Earth, is it not like a bellows?
Empty yet unyielding, moving yet ever producing.

Much speech leads to exhaustion—better to uphold the Middle.

The ceaseless generation and transformation between Heaven and Earth, its unrestrained motion, is entirely governed by this primal *qi*, sustaining all within. It supports Heaven above, Earth below, and connects with all beings, leaving nothing outside its sway.

When this *qi* is undifferentiated and vast, governing all things in orderly harmony, it is called *lǐ* (principle).
When it flows boundlessly, encompassing all things in its transformative circulation, it is called *qi*.
The unity of *lǐ* and *qi* is called *rén* (virtue/power).

Thus, the ancient scholars said: *"The virtuous one, with all human desires purified, allows Heaven’s principle to flow freely—without a trace of artificial contrivance."*

It is also said: *"To generate and transform is called virtue/power."*

In essence, the virtuous one is like the *rén* (virtue/power) of wood—its generative principle and *qi* are fully present.

Heaven and Earth generate all things, the Sage nourishes all people—none of this is achieved without the *lǐ* (principle) and *qi* flowing through them. How could it be merely a matter of outward actions?

Thus, the Supreme Sage spoke to elucidate the Dao: *"If Heaven and Earth lacked this vital generative *qi*, and only had the teachings of spring, summer, autumn, winter, cold, heat, warmth, and coolness to circulate and regulate, then all things would have no foundation to draw upon—how could *qi* be received, how could form be established? They would be regarded as lightly as straw dogs, utterly unworthy of regard, only to be seen gradually worn away.*

*If the Sage lacked this true primordial essence, and relied solely on ministers and officials, decrees, and legal codes to instruct the common people, then the rural masses would have no means of enlightenment—how could people be transformed, how could families be sustained? Truly, they would be treated as contemptibly as straw dogs, utterly indifferent to their plight, only to see them gradually crushed.*

*Yet how is it that Heaven and Earth, though without intent, encompass all things within their wind, clouds, rain, and dew? How is it that the Sage, though silent, assists and completes all things, and all take delight within the world? It is evident that both Heaven and Earth and the Sage rely on this primal *qi*, permeating among the people and all things—though there may be exploitation, there is also generation; though there may be coercion, there is also moral cultivation. It is precisely the apparent lack of virtue/power in Heaven, Earth, and the Sage that reveals their ultimate virtue/power.*

*If people do not recognize the Sage, let them observe Heaven and Earth: above, it floats as Heaven; below, it condenses as Earth. Within, it is empty yet luminous, boundless yet without obstruction—nothing more than an opening and closing, like the bellows’ endless breath and the flute’s resonant flow, vast and unrestrained. When it is empty and without form, it follows the *qi*’s rise and fall, unyielding yet flexible. When it moves and becomes sound, it responds to the transformations of all things, ever producing the extraordinary.*

*To observe Heaven and Earth is to see the bellows and flute. How could the Sage be any different?*

*Scholars who uphold the Middle and embrace the One, empty yet without trace, vast yet without end—when they conceal it deeply, its manifestation is boundless. Compared to those who fill halls with speech and rooms with doctrine, the difference is as vast as heaven and earth. The former sets teachings by speech, exhausting them in instruction—how could it compare to treasuring our essence, enriching our *qi*, wandering beyond form, and letting *qi* circulate through every pore?*

*Truly, the study of the Middle is the essential path to cultivating the self. This is the primal *qi*—cultivating the self lies here, governing the world lies here. Beyond this, so-called institutions and laws are but the traps and snares for catching fish and rabbits. Fish and rabbits must rely on traps and snares to be obtained, yet to say they cannot be caught without them is wrong, and to say the traps and snares *are* the fish and rabbits is equally wrong.*

*The Great Dao is like supplementing yin with yang—short steps forward, long steps back; the small return of jade nectar, the great return of golden nectar—all are but traps and snares for catching fish and rabbits. If one mistakenly takes them for the source of the Dao, even a hair’s breadth of error leads to a thousand miles of deviation.*

*Only this primal *qi*—silent, odorless, formless, the shared generative *qi* of Heaven, Earth, and all beings—must be sought by the scholar in cultivation. Only then will they not engage in empty refining. Otherwise, refining essence, *qi*, spirit, and emptiness are all studies without foundation. Even if practiced from childhood, one will remain unaccomplished to old age.*

*The Supreme Sage teaches one to focus on the Middle: the key lies in the bellows and flute—students must explore this themselves! The phrase *"not virtue/power"* in this chapter is rhetorical.*

**Chapter Six: The Unending Spirit of the Valley**
The unending Spirit of the Valley is called the Mysterious Female.
The gate of the Mysterious Female is called the root of Heaven and Earth.
Enduring yet unexhausted, its use is effortless.

Cultivation is but this: because humans have a body, *qi* and nature are constrained ahead, desires obscure behind—like pure gold and fine jade, originally without flaw, yet when plunged into mud, the finest gold loses its purity, the finest jade its luster. Thus, to restore their original state, one must employ the force of washing and the skill of polishing.

The Supreme Sage instructs the starting point of cultivation: *"The Spirit of the Valley does not die."* What is the Spirit of the Valley? A mountain cave is called a valley, speaking of its emptiness; unrestrained transformation is called spirit, speaking of its vitality. "Not dying" means ever-awake and unobscured.

If one can nourish this empty, vital, and unobscured body as the foundation, then cultivation becomes easy. Yet it is formless and shadowless, impossible to grasp—only through the seen and heard can one glimpse its traces.

The ancients said: *"To make the Spirit of the Valley never die, one must establish the foundation in the Mysterious Female."* Why is it called Mysterious? Mysterious is Heaven. Why is it called Female? Female is Earth. Heaven and Earth unite, and the Mysterious Female emerges; the Mysterious Female emerges, and opening-closing is complete. Between them, one ascends and one descends, one goes and one returns, revolving within the void—this is the gate of the Mysterious Female.

Confucius said, *"The *Kàn* and *Kūn* are the gate of the *Yì*."* Is this not true?

This gate is the path of yin and yang, the realm of Heaven and Earth’s transformation, the place where all things originate. To follow it is the door of death; the Sage inverts it, making it the gate of life.

「To cultivate the elixir (*dàn*) and achieve the Dao/Way of eternal life and prolonged vision, one must not abandon this gate of the Mysterious Female—there is no other path.

What else could be the root of Heaven and Earth? When practitioners (*xiūshì*) hang the curtain and observe with contemplation, in the state of ignorance and chaos, they must extinguish the mundane mind (*fánxīn*). Suddenly, with one awakening, the Dao/Way-mind (*dàoxīn*) is born.

What is called "stillness" is the Original Spirit (*yuánshén*), and what is called "movement" is the True Intention (*zhēnyì*). Within this, the *tāixī* breath stirs—do not rigidly cling to the *dāntián* (elixir field); instead, observe its ascent and descent, its coming and going, its leisurely and lively flow, and one attains the true *tāixī* breath.

The ancients said: *"To emerge from the Mysterious and enter the Female"*—this is neither the original face of the self nor the true face of the self. Only in this space of emerging and entering does the marvelous *dì* (truth/essence) reside, which is the *qí* and *líng* (emptiness and responsiveness) of the Dao/Way.

The True Yin and True Yang, manifesting as the *qi* of True Unity (*zhēnyī*), are the essence here. The root of Heaven and Earth—could it lie beyond this?

It must be known that the Spirit of the Valley (*gǔshén*) is the principle (*lǐ*) of the Supreme Ultimate (*tàijí*); the Mysterious Female is the *qi* of yin and yang. In the preheaven (*xiāntiān*) state, principle and *qi* were originally one; in the postheaven (*hòutiān*) state, principle and *qi* cannot be spoken of together.

Those who seek the Dao/Way must not indulge in a single moment of agitation or neglect a single thought. Only by borrowing the void of the Mysterious Female to nurture the emptiness and responsiveness of the Valley Spirit—neither clinging nor rejecting, neither forgetting nor aiding—can one attain it.

Thus it is said: *"Endless as if it exists, use it without strain."* The Great Dao/Way is formless yet gives birth to Heaven and Earth; the Great Dao/Way is nameless yet brings forth all things.

The Sage, embodying the Dao/Way while appearing formless and manifesting the Dao/Way while appearing empty, reveals this most hidden and subtle essence as the Valley Spirit. The Valley Spirit—this God of the Void Valley—when questioned, seems to answer; when responded to, echoes like a sound—this is immortality (*bùsǐ*).

In the human body, it is the unobscured essence of this emptiness and responsiveness. Since humans lost their innate Heavenly conscience (*tiānhuì*), the Valley Spirit has long been submerged and obscured!

Later practitioners, wishing to preserve the Valley Spirit eternally and maintain its emptiness and responsiveness, consider it the foundation of the Golden Elixir (*jīndān*) and the root of the Dao/Way of transcendence (*xiāndào*), yet they revolve in vain without a handle.

Only by seeking the Dao/Way through the interplay of *wu* (non-being) and *yǒu* (being), observing the marvelous and the subtle, can one begin. When being and non-being are balanced, the marvelous and subtle open together, and the Mysterious Female stands revealed.

Thus it is said:
*"This orifice is not an ordinary orifice,
Heaven and Earth together form it.
It is called the God-Spirit Hole (*shénqìxué*),
Within it lies the *Kàn* and *Lǐ* essence (*kānlí jīng*).
"* Only when essence (*jīng*), *qi*, and spirit (*shén*) are fused into one does it become the marvelous orifice of being and non-being, the gate of life and death.

Otherwise, it remains an ordinary orifice, lacking the true One Primordial *qi* (*yīyuán zhēnzǎi*). When empty, it falls into coarse void; when solid, it is bound by form—both are not the body of emptiness and responsiveness.

Only this gate of the Mysterious Female—neither empty nor solid, yet both empty and solid—holds the true being that cannot be spoken of. Stillness is formless; movement has manifestations. Stillness is not the root of Heaven and Earth; movement is not the origin of all things.

Only where stillness and movement intersect is the place of the *Kàn* and *Lǐ* reversal, the realm of the Moon and Sun’s interplay—this is the true root of Heaven and the womb of Earth.

When the student reaches the true Mysterious and true Female, with each ascent and descent—this *qi* condenses into nature (*xìng*), disperses into emotions (*qíng*)—from the state of utmost emptiness and profound stillness, the *dharma* (phenomena/manifestations) emerge.

Knowing this orifice, the entire Dao/Way of immortality and transcendence is exhausted here.

*"Endless as if it exists"*—this makes clear that nourishment must be prolonged, and only then will the *tāixī* breath stir and move.
*"Use it without strain"*—this means that extraction and replenishment have their proper times, and the *fǔhuǒ* (alchemical fire) must not be recklessly added or diminished.

If humans follow the natural Dao/Way of Heaven and Earth, then the Golden Elixir will be attained.

**Chapter Seven: Heaven Endures, Earth Lasts**
Heaven endures, Earth lasts.
The reason Heaven and Earth can endure and last is that they do not generate themselves—thus they can eternally generate.

Therefore, the Sage places his body after his body, yet his body comes first; he externalizes his body, yet his body remains. Is this not because he has no selfishness?

Only because he has no selfishness can he fulfill his selfishness. The *qi* of Heaven and Earth flows boundlessly and eternally, enduring countless eons without exhaustion—all because of the silent operation of the true One Primordial Sovereign (*yuánzhě*) within them. Thus Heaven and Earth endure and last without end.

In generating and nurturing all things, in prolonging the lives of the myriad peoples, they do so without ceasing—yet Heaven and Earth have never been insufficient. Thus the *qi*-mechanism (*qìjī*) endures eternally without wear.

The Supreme Sage says, *"Heaven endures, Earth lasts"*—is this not true?

Yet why can Heaven and Earth endure and last? It is because they do not generate themselves. If they had a self-generating mind, then Heaven and Earth would have emotions, and Heaven too would age.

Only by not having self-generation can they generate all things, and by generating all things, their own generation never ceases. Thus the generation of all things is the generation of Heaven and Earth—this is called eternal generation.

The common people are mostly ignorant of this principle of generation, seeking death instead of life, seeking swift death instead of eternal life. They drown in the fields of power and wealth, sink in the depths of sound, color, profit, and gain.

They constantly calculate, endlessly scheme—before a few years pass, their essence is exhausted, their *qi* weakened, their vitality scattered, their spirit lost. They toil through countless hardships yet struggle to enjoy even one lifetime.

Seeking life while actually hastening death—how can this be lamented enough!

Thus the Sage follows Heaven and models Earth, refusing to chase after power and wealth, yet also not greedily pursuing the arts of prolonging life and increasing longevity. Only the Great Dao/Way comes first.

He clears the field of his heart, purifies it, and meticulously cultivates the root of his life (*mìngdì*). Whatever means of nourishing the body or refining the form exist, he does not spare time to contrive them.

In the end, his virtue (*dé*) is established, and none of his kind surpasses him; his name is made, and later generations still look up to his form.

Thus it is said: *"Endless as if it exists, use it without strain."*

The Sage is not one who neglects his own body while it thrives, nor who forsakes his own body while it yet remains. Truly, he far surpasses those who selfishly harm their own lives a hundredfold.

Yet the Sage is not one who feigns emotions or stands apart for show. Since ancient times, simplicity and tranquility have been his virtues—clear and pure in heart, never floating with the crowd nor clinging to form. He stands firm in great impartiality, like Heaven and Earth in their boundless generosity.

Mortals are often self-seeking, burdened with cares; the Sage holds no private thoughts, his spirit bright and detached beyond all things. A single radiant essence of emptiness and boundless void, ever-shining yet never dimmed.

Were it but a mortal frame of flesh and blood, a stinking skin-bag, constantly fretted over day and night—how could it ever remain whole and not decay, enduring and not perish? It is only through his impartiality that the Sage aligns with Heaven and Earth, shines like the sun and moon, and yet fulfills his own purpose.

Later seekers of the Dao/Way, desiring their bodies to be immortal and their spirits unbroken, though they toil in austerity, cast off defilements, and refine themselves through long practice—naturally pure and clear, another realm of Heaven and Earth, beyond worldly life and death—how could such joy be surpassed?

Heaven and Earth speak not; they rely solely on the primal *qi* of unity, circulating effortlessly between them. Thus they renew endlessly, their vital force never ceasing. Because Heaven and Earth endure eternally, they remain ever fresh across the ages.

The Sage harmonizes with Heaven and Earth, nurturing the *qi* of Great Unity, returning to the primal truth of chaos. In seclusion, his virtue becomes sublime; in action, it becomes the Way of the Sage-King.

What are the words of those who speak of self-cultivation? They seek only lonely mountains and silent sitting, never touching the affairs of men. And those who claim to govern the world? They expend all their energy and effort, their entire being entangled in calculations for others!

Such men cling to one extreme or another, each to their own private ends. They are not those who are still in quietude, nor those who act with purpose and clarity.

The Sage in adversity remains pure and untainted, his true form steadfast as mountains and rivers. In prosperity, he benefits all, his mortal form as light as a fleeting title.

When his name is known throughout the cosmos and his body stands alone before all ages—when his virtue covers the world and his essence persists beyond this life—is it not because he held nothing private?

The student who casts off all selfishness, empties the illusions of form, and forever escapes the roots of dust—his accumulated virtue will dwell in the mortal world, and his ascension will endure in Heaven and Earth. To reject self-interest and yet attain true longevity is as different from the worldly as the heavens from the mud.

Who can truly abandon the allure of external desires?

**Chapter Eight: The Supreme Virtue is Like Water**

The supreme virtue is like water.
Water benefits all things yet does not contend,
dwelling where others find revulsion,
thus approaching the Dao/Way.

Its place is humble, its heart deep,
its words trustworthy, its governance wise,
its actions effective, its movements timely.

Only because it does not contend,
does it suffer no blame.

The Great Dao/Way has no other mystery—
only the union of spirit and *qi*,
returning to the boundless *Tai Ji*,
the single breath of life given by parents at birth.

If one does not attend to the Dao/Way,
then spirit and *qi* are divided,
like lead and mercury scattered—
for water and fire are thus separated.

Mencius said: *"The people are not nourished by water and fire, they cannot live."*
This applies both to daily needs
and to the essentials of cultivation.

Sometimes fire warms water, and true yang manifests;
sometimes water sustains fire, and sweet dew arises.
The marvels of water and fire
are truly beyond measure.

Yet water and fire share the same essence—
speak of water, and fire is known.

Water’s nature is to descend;
the Dao/Way values humility.
Thus the Sage of supreme virtue
is even-tempered, his will gentle and yielding,
letting all things flourish in their own time—
none escape his beneficence.

In truth, his virtue covers the world yet he knows not of it,
his actions fill the cosmos yet he claims them not.
Only by following Heaven and Earth’s natural order,
fulfilling the place of all things,
does he remain without conflict—
truly like water in its boundless nourishment.

Not only this—
all things prefer clarity to turbidity,
elevation to descent.
Water cleanses all things yet keeps itself impure,
serves others with elevation yet humbles itself below.
Ah, water! Water!
How perfectly it mirrors the Dao!

The Sage’s nature is one with water’s—
gentle, not rigid,
humble in self-giving.
What others cannot endure, the Sage accepts as ever-present;
what others most abhor, the Sage bears with ease.
Thus he harms none, nor does he contend with any.
None but the Sage of the Dao/Way can do so.
Hence it is said: *"Dwelling where others find revulsion, he approaches the Dao."*

What the Dao/Way offers to oneself
is to occupy one’s natural place and move without hindrance.
Even in matters of gain or loss,
calamity or death—
what others cannot bear—
the Sage of the Dao/Way views all with equal measure.

The gentleman speaks of principle, not *qi*;
discusses nature, not fate—
only the cultivation of virtue and self-reflection matter.

Still, virtue arises from unity,
and its refinement follows many paths.
How could it be without a guiding source,
vast and boundless,
yet grounded in an ultimate foundation?

There must be a realm of supreme goodness,
where it rests without shifting,
only then can it grow subtly yet steadily,
as Heaven’s truth fills the day,
and human desires fade.

The *Yijing* says: *"The mountain stands firm, its back unyielding, yet it does not harm its own body. It moves within its courtyard, yet none see its person."*
This is the Sage’s humble dwelling.

To dwell in peace is to draw from its depth.
To gaze inward at his heart—
empty, yet holding nothing—
deep as the abyss, yet deeper still.

「Externally observing what is granted,
selecting worthy individuals for association—
this is the depth of his benevolence.

When it comes to his words,
a thousand gold pieces are not worth a single promise,
for *"speech is trustworthy"* (言善信).
In governing affairs,
great kindness is inferior to great virtue,
for *"good governance brings order"* (政善治).
Extending this to all things—
whether matter, deed, movement, or stillness—
he finds the Dao in every detail.

Assigning tasks to others,
he only expects them not to fail in their capacity.
Acting with kindness in motion,
he merely seeks that action aligns with its proper time.

Thus the Sage cultivates himself and governs the world in this manner.
This arises from *"resting at supreme goodness"* (止于至善),
finding his place of ease,
and then all his actions conform to proper measure.

For in every moment and place,
there is no slightest flaw or deficiency—
what need is there for contention?

Only when one does not contend,
does humanity respond with gratitude and reverence,
engraved in bone and mind—
this is the Sage’s unyielding gaze,
and where could resentment or blame arise?

Even the wicked are transformed into the good.

When his virtue reaches its peak,
he acts without effort, naturally,
all resting in the unknowing, unseeing Heaven.
Nearly forgetting the water-like goodness of supreme virtue,
gentle yet firm, unerring—
no path is unlucky.

Pointing to the heart of supreme goodness,
ever plain and ordinary,
neither fond nor hateful,
vast and boundless,
without bias or tilt,
perfectly harmonious and yielding.

Thus, though elevated, he is not arrogant;
though lowly, he does not betray.
Toward himself, no blame;
toward others, no grievance.

Yet, what makes this possible?
It is not the learning of the common world that can reach its heights,
nor the hollow studies without root that can establish its foundation.

Therefore, the highest Sage dwells in the realm most reviled by the crowd,
then reveals a place of goodness.
Ultimately, what is this place?
It is the *inch-deep sincerity of the heart* (寸衷寸地).

To attain this place is to have life and nature as a support;
to lose it is to have spirit and *qi* without master.

Without a master, there is chaos—
how could all things be in harmony,
uniting inner and outer,
and acting in self and others without contention?

Still, if one says this place *exists*,
one risks falling into rigid attachment;
if one says it *does not exist*,
one stumbles into dull emptiness.

This is likely the realm where existence and nonexistence are not fixed,
and motion and stillness are not constrained.

To cultivate the supreme Dao,
one must carefully examine its essence—
accumulate abundant *yin* merit (多积阴功),
broadly promote virtuous deeds (广敦善行),
and perhaps ascend to divine Heaven (上格神天),
or gain guidance from a teacher (或得师指),
or awaken through divine insight (或因神悟),
thus attaining understanding of this place,
and only then avoiding stray paths and left-handed doctrines,
fulfilling one’s lifelong aspirations.

When this place is clearly understood,
the Dao is half attained.

The following words on *"heart"* (心) and *"speech"* (言)
illustrate that in every moment and place,
one must examine and uphold supreme goodness,
lest one dwell in goodness without first attaining its foundation.

All that follows,
no matter how slight, must align with goodness—
this is true essential learning and foundational practice.
If one neglects this,
holding oneself and interacting with others,
all things will crumble to ruin.

I have therefore briefly revealed this,
hoping those with aspiration in the world
will not rely on their talents and intellect,
recklessly guessing and presuming,
while failing to cultivate virtue and turn back to the Dao.

Only with an open heart seeking the Way can one proceed.

---
**Chapter Nine: *Achievement and Withdrawal* (功成身退)**

To hold and fill oneself,
is inferior to having already attained.
To clutch and sharpen oneself,
cannot be preserved long.

Gold and jade fill the halls,
yet none can guard them.
Rich and noble yet proud,
one invites one’s own misfortune.

When achievement is complete and name is fulfilled,
then one withdraws—
this is the Dao of Heaven.

The ancients said:
*"To cross the river, one must use a raft;
once ashore, one need not keep the boat."*
Again they said:
*"Before attaining achievement, one must study the Way;
when achievement is attained, one must forget the Way."*
These few words are indeed the most vital for cultivating the Dao.

If one practices the Dao and fulfills deeds,
having already entered the realm of refined and vast achievement,
yet still clings and guards it,
boasting of superior learning and profound cultivation,
one cannot avoid the rise of arrogance and agitation,
leading either to withdrawal and retreat
or to contradictory and perverse actions.

If this is so,
where then is the Dao?
What remains of virtue?

The Supreme Sage says:
*"To hold and fill, it is better to cease;
to clutch and sharpen, one cannot long preserve."*

When a practitioner’s refinement is yet insufficient,
one must not fail to sift and purify a thousandfold,
to wash forth my single point of utmost purity,
as the foundation for eternal life.

If one obtains true Yang,
boiling it in the morning and refining it in the evening,
the primordial essence fills one’s body inside and out,
then one’s body stands firm as a wall a thousand *zhang* high,
one’s mind clear as an autumn moon in a frigid pool.
The external *gonads* shrink like those of a child,
and thus the realm of complete impermeability is attained.

At this time, when essence is full within the body,
it is unwise to further apply fire elixirs—
one must stop the fire and instead
be blank and unaware, hazy and unknowing,
suddenly forgetting the state of essence-fullness
as the true attainment.

If one clings to fullness without ceasing,
one risks the peril of spilled elixirs and overturned crucibles—
it is better to cease early than to suffer harm.

When the *qi* is yet insufficient,
one must boil and refine it a thousandfold,
stirring the martial and civil *divine fires*,
to forge the primordial *one-yuan true qi* forth,
as the basis for prolonging life.
When the mundane *qi* is fully refined,
it transforms into a single piece of pure Yang—
most vast, most resolute—
penetrating the very marrow of one’s sinews and bones,
vigorous as a dragon, fierce as a tiger.
What greater refinement and sharpness could there be?

I must focus the *qi* to attain softness,
just as an infant is pure and ignorant.
Only then can one long preserve the *qi*,
reaching the state where both form and spirit are wondrous,
and one is truly united with the Dao.
If one clutches sharpness without rest,
one risks the calamity of flames consuming the fields—
how could one expect to preserve it long?

Thus, one sees from human affairs that the Way of Heaven is known:
In spring, warmth prevails;
by summer, the scorching sun commands,
and the gentle warmth is gone.
In autumn, coolness descends;
by winter, the cold dominates,
and the western winds vanish.

When things grow and their purpose is fulfilled,
when the season runs its course and its name is complete,
Heaven and Earth withdraw and conceal themselves,
to nurture the virtue of Yang harmony.
If winter’s cold were to mix with spring’s warmth,
and summer’s heat were to blend with autumn’s chill,
then the Way of Heaven would be inverted,
the seasons would stray from their proper order,
and the failure to withdraw when achievement is complete
would breed perverse *qi*—
its harm to mortals would be great.

Thus it is said:
*"When achievement is complete and name is fulfilled, one withdraws—
this is the Dao of Heaven."*

If Heaven is thus,
how much more so for mortals?
In history, wise ministers and accomplished men,
whose deeds were resplendent and whose fame was renowned,
yet who did not know to retreat into the mountains,
like *Wenzhong* of Yue or *Hanxin* of Han,
suffered many calamities—
their lives cut short, their families ruined.

Therefore, those who study the Dao,
when their essence is refined and their *qi* is full,
must not fail to forget the Way and forget their form,
lest they ruin their own Dao.
If one has not yet reached this state,
how could one abandon the Way and the form?

This teaching instructs those who cultivate the Great Dao:
to discard one step, to advance another,
never to be self-satisfied or complacent,
lest arrogance and agitation arise,
and disaster strike without delay.
Even without the peril of leakage,
one would still stagnate midway,
unable to cross to the other shore
and enter the realm of divine transformation.

*The Awakening to True Reality* says:
*"Before refining the elixir, one must refine swiftly;
when refined, one must know to stop and suffice.
If one still holds to the fullness of the heart,
one risks ruin and humiliation one day."*

It is clear the Dao has no end,
achievement knows no limit—
what use is this pretended sufficiency?

In history, many cultivators
suffered calamities—
their lives cut short, their families ruined—
all because they did not know
the Way of *hiding brilliance and nurturing obscurity*,
of blending with the world and mingling with the dust.

The *Elixir Canon* says:
*"Cultivate while blending with the world,
and harmonize the light—
round when round, square when square.
Hidden or revealed, obeying or defying,
none shall recognize—
how could one teach others to see
the realm of concealment and display?"*

Thus, the true Daoists,
when their virtue is profound,
must hide themselves and not reveal their brilliance,
bowing and rising with the times,
lest they differ and be recognized,
good or evil—
only then can they long preserve their lives.

Otherwise, when cultivating virtue yet slander arises,
when the Dao is high yet defamation comes,
though it may be due to others’ depravity,
it is also from one’s own excessive display.

*The Classic of Changes* says:
*"Careless hoarding invites theft,
and primping invites licentiousness"—
truly self-inflicted.

Why then should those who are self-satisfied
expect to avoid harm?

I wish those who come after me:
before making progress, to follow the Way in practice,
when deeply cultivated, to stop and not apply the fire—
only then may one avoid the calamity of burning one’s body.

**Chapter Ten: Focusing Qi to Attain Softness**
To bear the *yingshen* (vital soul) and embrace unity,
can one avoid separation?
To focus the *qi* to attain softness,
can one be like an infant?
To purge and clear the profound vision,
can one be without blemish?
To love one’s country and govern the people,
can one be without action?
To open and close the Heavenly Gate,
can one be without yielding?
To be clear and all-pervasive,
can one be without knowing?

To *nourish* and *cherish*,
to *nourish* without possession,
to act without reliance,
to grow without slaughtering—
this is called *mysterious virtue*.

This chapter begins by speaking of the Dao of refining essence into *qi*.
Once the essence and *qi* are within the body,
one must maintain a single heart and single virtue,
and not allow them to stray even slightly.
If they stray, the three treasures—
essence, *qi*, and spirit—
will scatter and not reunite,
unable to attain the utmost,
as the foundation for elixir refinement.

Thus the Supreme Sage says:
*"To bear the yingshen and embrace unity,
can one avoid separation?"*

The *yingshen* refers to blood.
Blood is generated by the heart,
the *po* (ethereal soul) is stored in the heart—
one must surely illuminate the elixir field,
keeping the heart unmoving,
so that the *shen* (spirit) may flow
from the sun to the moon,
and the moon may return to pure dryness.

This arises when the heart’s Yang enters the kidney’s Yin,
and the divine fire illuminates the blood-water.
Though water is cold and metal is chilled,
the divine fire boils and simmers it,
and it rises naturally,
unfettered and boundless.

The Perfect Sage knows this mysterious female (玄牝) as the root of heaven and earth,
and thus within each breath—
one inhalation, one exhalation—
when the subtle Yang stirs,
he seizes it with a single glance,
grasp it with one hand,
and directs a drop of his own mercury (己汞) to meet it,
spinning left, drawing right,
raising it back to the central field (中田),
congealing it without dispersion—
then he bears the *po* (魄) and returns,
embracing unity and resting,
without pause,
and thus the true Yang flourishes,
the true *qi* surges.

From this, he operates the River Vehicle (河车),
stokes the fire from the Empty Danger Hole (虚危穴),
drawing it to the Tail Lock (尾闾),
beating the nine-layered iron drum (九重铁鼓),
and activating the Three-Legged Golden Toad (三足金蟾),
ascending it to the crown—
all must be done with singular focus,
unbroken and unwavering—
until it rises to the Mud Ball (泥丸),
the Muni’s Precious Pearl (牟尼宝珠) obtained.
If he does not then nourish it for a moment,
the Mud Ball’s Yin essence will not transform,
how can he achieve the fusion of lead and mercury (铅汞)?
Refined into sweet dew divine elixir (甘露神水),
it moistens the hundred vessels (百脉) of the body.

Having nourished the Mud Ball,
he next draws it down to the Lower Red Palace (下重楼),
entering the Vermilion Court (绛宫),
thus withdrawing the Yin symbol at noon (午退阴符).

Yet when stoking the fire,
one must take its vigor (刚),
not relying on the force of dry vigor (乾健之力),
for true gold cannot ascend.
