And when withdrawing the symbol,
one must take its gentleness (柔),
not employing the virtue of yielding compliance (柔顺之德),
or the Yang lead (阳铅) will remain scattered,
unable to subdue the mercury and forge the elixir (伏汞成丹).
Thus it is said:
*"Can one focus the *qi* to achieve gentleness like a newborn?"*

Its meaning instructs:
After the Yin is generated at noon (阴生午后),
one must illuminate with a clear mind (一心朗照),
letting the *qi* mechanism descend naturally,
as it will—
unforced and unconstrained,
yet fully understood—
this is the yielding compliance of the *Kūn* hexagram (坤卦柔顺),
the path of the virtuous one’s cultivation (君子修行之道).

When descending to the Vermilion Court (降宫) for nourishment,
returning to the Earth Vessel (土釜),
sealing it firmly,
one must only reside in tranquility (恬淡),
harmonizing and settling it,
in a mere instant—
the breath ceases as if nonexistent,
the spirit’s movement seems extinguished—
this is the attainment of gentleness (致柔).

Nourished for a while,
the spirit and *qi* return to their roots,
like fire seeds in the furnace,
long congealed and concentrated,
not allowed to scatter wildly,
naturally the true *qi* circulates,
turning through the body—
the mind at peace,
the limbs supple,
as if the body of a newborn,
fluffy and delicate,
showing the state of tender weakness beyond endurance (柔弱不堪任物),
this is sufficient proof of the elixir’s congealing (丹凝之象).

From here, lead and mercury unite,
water and fire harmonize—
one must then wash the heart and purge the mind,
cultivating the single true lead (真铅).

If the heart wanders even slightly,
the elixir will scatter—
not only will the elixir have no chance to complete,
but even the lead from the Water Village (水乡之铅)
will no longer be mine.

The *Scripture of Purity* (《清净经》) states:
*"The heart without its heart, things without their things.
The void without its void, the nonexistent without its nonexistent—
serene and ever still,
thus the constant quietude."*
What flaw could there be?

Thus it is said:
*"Can one purify and peer into the mysteries without blemish?"*

Even if the external elixir is obtained,
internal illumination is not strict—
if human desires are not yet purified,
heavenly principles not yet refined,
how could a single grain of millet elixir (黍球)
emerge empty yet complete in form (虚而成象)?

When the elixir is within the body,
one must still preserve essence and enrich *qi*,
to form the sacred embryo (圣胎).

Yet this preservation must follow nature’s course,
this enrichment must accord with spontaneity (自在).
The unforced preservation surpasses forced preservation,
the unconstrained enrichment surpasses constrained enrichment.
Otherwise, clinging to preciousness,
few escape harm.

The elixir is primordial *qi* (先天元气),
formless and undefined—
why must it be made manifest?
If one seeks its traces,
one risks stirring the post-heaven fire (火动后天),
and the great Dao of the primordial will be lost.

Thus it is said:
*"Can one love the people and govern the state without action?"*
The people are like essence (民比精也),
the state like *qi* (国喻气也).
The key to governing lies in benevolence to love the people.

Establishing laws to govern,
this is but the minor gratification of a hegemon (霸者),
far from the king’s virtue of non-action (王者的无为而治).
Repeatedly bright and refined (重熙累治),
the contest lies in action and non-action.

The path to cultivating the body:
essence steadied is like the people at ease,
*qi* abundant is like the state prosperous.
Refining oneself makes essence steadied,
nourishing directly makes *qi* abundant—
extreme purity (浩然) and great vigor (刚大),
filling both realms (两间),
as if seeing it as one’s fixed possession,
a common practice,
not boasting of ability,
not flaunting wisdom—
muddled and indistinct,
as if forgetting the very act of cultivation,
non-action yet great action emerges.

When the student reaches this stage,
essence full and *qi* abundant,
nourishing it long and steadily,
naturally the crown splits and the elixir ascends,
able to ride the clouds in high flight,
soar over the ocean isles—
compared to those who formerly adhered to the strict rules,
focusing *qi* to achieve gentleness,
the difference is vast!

Thus it is said:
*"Can one open and close the Heavenly Gate without duality?"*
This speaks of the former days,
when adjusting the spirit and nourishing the embryo (调神养胎)
could not avoid clinging to duality (守雌).
Now the Yang spirit is robust and flourishing,
free from the mundane body,
bursting through the Heavenly Gate,
ascending to the celestial expanse—
truly joyous!

How is the *qi* robust?
At this point, the heart is like a clear mirror,
the nature like still water,
illuminating the heavens,
lively on the earth—
all knowledge and perception (知觉之识神)
transform into the void and boundless (空洞之元神).

Foreknowledge and foresight (前知后晓),
burning yet dissolving (烛照糜遗),
this is clarity—
thus all paths are open without contradiction.

Yet ever serene and ever illuminated,
no trace of illumination or stillness remains;
ever clear and ever aware,
no thought of clarity or perception lingers.
It is as if all things come and are reflected,
like a high-hanging mirror,
where nothing can hide—
yet all must be rooted in non-action (无为),
action as its use (有为).

When the Yang has not yet arisen,
one accumulates essence and enriches *qi* to generate it;
once the Yang has arisen,
one treasures essence and enriches *qi* to store it.
When later the form is retained to transform the world,
accumulating merit and virtue—
though born, one does not boast of nurturing (不夸辅育之功),
though acting, one does not rely on effort (不恃矜持之力),
though long-lived, one does not depend on constraint (不假制伏之劳).

Thus, the heart of one eon is the heart of ten thousand eons—
truly, it may be called the celestial sovereign,
empowered to oversee the creation of the cosmos.
Hence, it is called the **"Mysterious Virtue/Power" (玄德)**.

When this master speaks of the nine stages of refining the foundation to obtain the elixir,
of purifying oneself to return the cinnabar,
and of shedding the mortal form to gain the pearl—
the essence lies in nothingness reaching its utmost and stillness attaining its depth,
in embracing the Three and holding the One,
in the hazy and indistinct, the boundless and formless.
From the foundation to returning the cinnabar,
all depends on the interplay of indistinct yet knowing,
and of transforming the manifest from the unmanifest.

The Dao/Way originates at the beginning of **Hongmeng (鸿蒙)**,
mixed within the void of emptiness.
It is unseen, unheard—
how then can one who practices it take hold?
The Sage knows the Dao/Way’s essence is formless,
yet its function manifests in phenomena.
Thus, with the formless as substance,
and the substantial as formless,
they steal the **qi** from the realm of **Hun-Dun (浑浑沌沌)**,
and gather the spirit within the hazy and indistinct.
To perfect the Great Oneness of the Dao/Way,
and to become an immortal sage forever—

What is meant by **"substantial yet manifest"**?
The true yin and true yang,
and all sentient beings of the same kind.

What is meant by **"formless yet void"**?
The root of the primordial Dao/Way,
and the **Dragon-Tiger (龙虎)** **Eight Trigrams (二八)** initial threads of **qi**.

Where **qi** exists without matter,
the Dao/Way shines forth.
Thus it is said:
*"When yin and yang unite, the primordial qi appears;
when yin and yang divide, the later-heaven vessels form."*

The *Yijing (易)* says:
*"Above the form, it is called Dao/Way;
below the form, it is called qi."*
Without vessels, there is no way to perceive the Dao/Way;
without the Dao/Way, there is no means to bear the vessels.

The **Most High (太上)** borrows the analogy of a **chariot (车)**:
A chariot has spokes and a hub;
thirty spokes symbolize the movement of the sun and moon,
while the hub sits at the center,
pierced by all the axles.
The hub is hollow within,
the spokes gather without—
thus it turns effortlessly.
If not for this inner void,
how could one be carried?
How could things be stored?

Hence it is said:
*"When it is void, then the chariot’s use is realized."*

Again, like pottery:
Mixing water with clay,
kneading the clay into vessels,
once fired and refined,
they are solid without yet hollow within—
yet for all practical purposes,
human utility lies not in the clay’s substance,
but in the empty void.
As with **Tao Can (陶侃)** moving bricks,
if not for the space within that is void and empty,
how could they be turned with ease?

Thus it is said:
*"When it is void, then the vessel’s use is realized."*

Further, consider building a dwelling:
One must carve doors and windows within it,
and only then does light pour forth.
Once inside this dwelling,
the doors and windows seem unnecessary—
one focuses on the empty space,
where one may rest one’s knees.
Though the dwelling is formed as substance,
true residence lies in its practical emptiness.

Hence it is said:
*"When it is void, then the dwelling’s use is realized."*

From these three analogies observed,
the void is not without form as chariot, vessel, and dwelling;
the void is not without form as carrier, storehouse, and abode.
Yet those who dwell in the formless
are the ones who enrich the practical use of the manifest.

Thus it is said:
*"The manifest is for utility, the void is for function;
both manifest and void
are mutually their roots."*

In essence, the Dao/Way is originally void and formless—
yet without yin and yang, it cannot be seen.
**Qi** belongs to yin and yang—
yet without the Dao/Way, it cannot arise.
Yin and yang are born after heaven and earth,
having definite shapes and locations,
and cannot be the elixir of eternal life.
Only by seeking the Dao/Way within yin and yang,
and returning through yin and yang to the **Great Ultimate (太极)**,
can the primordial and later-heaven be united,
and the Dao/Way be attained.

Later alchemists who refine the elixir
merely ingest the manifest **qi**,
unaware of forging the formless elixir.
If they wish to become immortals,
are they not traveling south while aiming north?

The Dao/Way has no name,
yet it is named **"Dao/Way"**.
The Dao/Way has no practice,
yet it is called **"practice"**.

For the Dao/Way as a thing,
when it reaches utmost void and utmost formlessness,
only then can it reach utmost divinity and sainthood.
Observe how the **qi** of heaven and earth is clear and empty,
with nothing at all—
yet when it is suppressed for long,
and the **qi** mechanism stirs,
then yin and yang arise.

Thus, myriad forms and colors appear,
with patterns distinct and chapters brilliant,
filling the four extremes.
Who creates them?
Who gives them birth?

All things arise from the Dao/Way generating **one qi**,
and from **one qi** transforming into yin and yang,
thus giving birth to the myriad things.
Hence it is said:
*"The Dao/Way from emptiness and nothingness generates **one qi**,
then from **one qi** produces yin and yang.
Yin and yang thus form the three transformations,
and the three transformations regenerate the flourishing of myriad changes."*

Those who practice the Dao/Way to attain its true essence
and achieve the body of immortality and sainthood
must first use yin and yang as their tools,
then take emptiness and nothingness as their foundation,
and thus the Great Dao/Way is attained.

The three **"without"** characters within the chapter
refer to the void as **"without"**—
roughly speaking, the practitioner moves from **"without"** to **"with"**
and from **"with"** back to **"without"**
until reaching a clear and empty **one qi**,
and only then does the Great Dao/Way take form.
Its meaning is doubtless derived from this.

---
**Chapter Twelve: Discarding the Extraneous, Taking the Essential**

The five colors blind the eyes;
the five tones deafen the ears;
the five flavors dull the palate;
hunting and galloping in the wild drive the mind to madness;
rare treasures bring misfortune upon one’s conduct.

Thus the sage prioritizes the belly over the eyes,
and so discards the extraneous while taking the essential.

The world’s restless pursuit—
racing the heart through the fields of sound, color, wealth, and gain—
casting the gaze far and wide,
delighting the heart and pleasing the mouth—
is this not because the joys within are truly satisfying?

Yet who knows that worldly pleasures are limited,
while the joys of the heart are boundless?

Moreover, where pleasure resides, sorrow also dwells.
What benefits the body harms the heart.

Take the brilliance of five-colored patterns—
their hues radiant, their designs dazzling—
who does not gaze upon them with delight,
or behold them with awe?
Yet the more one fixes the eyes upon them,
the more the spirit grows dizzy.

How much vital essence does a life contain?
To linger over sights and indulge the senses,
deeming them beautiful—
who does not end up with obstructed **qi**, a fading spirit,
a disordered mind,
and eyes that, ironically, grow blind?
Thus it is said: *"The five colors blind the eyes"*—
a truth most profound.

As for the melodies of silk and bamboo,
the sounds of pipes and flutes—
even the ancients did not abandon them.
Yet the foolish and debauched,
lost in female consorts and singing boys,
exhaust their vital essence for fleeting pleasure!
Before the music even ends,
its lingering echoes still resound—
yet the soul’s inner reserves are drained outward,
and the ears grow deaf.
Thus it is said: *"The five tones deafen the ears."*

As for the palate’s tastes—
harmonious sweetness and richness,
balanced in thickness and thinness—
even the sage requires them.
But gluttonous folk,
greedy for the mouth’s delights,
pile exotic dishes upon the table,
fill the kitchen with strange fare—
though they spend a fortune on a single meal,
they never hesitate.
Do they not know that what pleases the mouth harms the heart?
Moreover, the heart holds boundless true flavors—
without fatty richness, yet naturally sweet;
without rich meats, yet naturally sated.
Those who merely seek to satisfy the mouth
are but chasing empty gratification.
Thus it is said: *"The five flavors dull the palate."*

As for hunting—
in ancient times, emperors used it to eliminate pests and protect the people,
to ensure agricultural peace.
Later generations, however, pursued beasts and birds,
chasing them through fields and forests,
setting dogs loose to ravage the wilds,
even slaughtering harmless serpents, insects, and creatures without cause.
Some took pleasure in cruelty, harming life for sport,
showing no remorse.
In the end, the heavens repay such deeds—
the netherworld’s punishments are inevitable.
In an instant, misfortune follows.
Moreover, when one gallops through the wilds,
one unleashes the violent nature within—
the body grows wild, the mind grows wild.
Thus the *Tao Te Ching* warns: *"Hunting and galloping drive the mind to madness."*

Furthermore, rare and strange treasures—
emperors did not hoard them in their eyes,
for this cultivated refined tastes and peaceful customs.
Later generations, however, prized the strange and unusual,
spending half their lives scheming and toiling,
exhausting their vital essence on goods.
Yet what one covets, others covet too—
and in seeking to possess it alone,
the tide of plunder grows ever stronger.
The ancients said: *"An ordinary man bears no guilt,
yet suspicion clings to him like a shadow."*
Thus it is known that the richer the hoard,
the deeper the coming ruin—
a truth not false.

Even if one guards against it with all one’s strength,
protects it with every means—
if the heavens and spirits do not favor it,
it will still perish in the end.
Life and fate are precious—
once the soul departs, where are the treasures then?
Why not value the inner and disregard the outer?

Thus the *Tao Te Ching* solemnly warns:
*"Rare treasures bring misfortune upon one’s conduct."*
Therefore, those who grasp the high Dao
empty their minds to nurture their nature,
fill their bellies to sustain their lives;
knowing that the primordial **one qi** comes with birth
and departs with death—
it is the unbreakable treasure of the body—
if one focuses wholly upon it,
all external things become like passing clouds—
thus their spirit remains pure and unobstructed,
as if receiving offerings from mortals,
or ruling as celestial sovereigns,
yet they still appear humble and unassuming.

Now ask—
are the zithers of Shun, the ivory pipes of Zhao,
the lyres of Qi, or the lodges of Lu
still preserved today?
Long since lost to oblivion!

Thus it is known:
things are finite, but the Dao/Way is infinite;
mortals are limited, but the Dao/Way is boundless.
To seek longevity, one must set aside the finite things of the world
and cultivate the formless essence of life—
only then will one know what to value and what to discard.

Alas—
unless one is a sage of profound insight,
how can one discard the extraneous and take the essential?

The fundamental teaching for cultivating the self
is fundamentally opposed to the ways of the world.
One must know:
what the world desires, the Dao/Way rejects;
what the world pursues, the Dao/Way avoids.

What the world covets, the Dao/Way abandons.
For though fame, desire, wealth, and gain
may bring profit to the body,
they bring no benefit to the heart.
Indeed, once the heart grows greedy,
the body itself falls into discord.

As for the cultivation of one’s true nature and vital essence—
though it seems formless and intangible,
it is not something the body can deem unworthy.
If one can discard external temptations,
nurture what is inherently true,
and devote oneself wholeheartedly to cultivation,
seeking nothing from without,
then ultimately, virtue and achievement will be complete,
and the Dao/Way of longevity will be attained.

What treasure in all the world
can compare to this?

Yet fear arises:
if resolve is not steadfast,
if progress in the Dao/Way lacks courage,
if reason and desire clash,
if heaven and humanity conflict,
then reaching the utmost will be difficult indeed.

May those who follow later
first cultivate stillness and sincerity,
then refine the lead and subdue the mercury,
and when returning to the origin,
embracing simplicity and returning to truth—
what difficulty is there in ascending to consort with the immortals?

Thus the sage cultivates the inner, not the outer;
nourishes the vital core, not the senses;
discards the false to preserve the true,
and with singular focus, concentrates the spirit,
fulfilling one’s nature and establishing one’s destiny—
how could this not far surpass the world’s fleeting glories by ten thousandfold?

**Chapter Thirteen: Honor and Humiliation as Sudden Alarms**

Honor and humiliation—both must be met with sudden alarm.
To hold great calamity as one’s own self—what does this mean?

What is meant by *"honor and humiliation as sudden alarm"*?
Honor is of lesser worth; to gain it is cause for alarm,
to lose it is cause for alarm—
this is called *"honor and humiliation as sudden alarm."*

What is meant by *"to hold great calamity as one’s own self"*?
The reason I have great calamity
is that I have this body.
But if I have no body,
what calamity could I have?

Thus, to value the body as the foundation for all under heaven
is to make it worthy of trust.
To cherish the body as the foundation for all under heaven
is to make it worthy of reliance.

Mencius said: *"What is most important to guard? Guarding the body is most important."*
The *Book of Poetry* says: *"Both wise and enlightened, preserving one’s self."*

How greatly did the ancients value the body!

For with this body,
not only does one’s own nature and vital essence depend on it for existence,
but within one’s family,
none can live without relying on it.

Extending this further—
to establish the heart for heaven and earth,
to establish destiny for all things,
to continue the learning of past sages,
to bring eternal peace to future generations—
all this is governed by this body alone.

Yet though this body governs the cosmos,
it is the Dao/Way alone that governs this body.
The Dao/Way cannot exist independently in empty space;
it must rely on humanity to uphold it.
Thus it is said:
*"The body exists, so the Dao/Way exists;
the body perishes, so the Dao/Way perishes."*

Great practitioners, before the Dao/Way is complete,
must distance themselves from the world,
retreat into mountains and forests,
conceal their brilliance and nurture obscurity,
delight in the Dao/Way and endure poverty,
hearing no human voices,
speaking not of worldly affairs,
their feet never treading the dust of the world—
how could this be merely to avoid calamity and preserve the body?
It is also to establish the body and fulfill one’s destiny.

As for worldly honors and humiliations,
they are regarded as ordinary occurrences,
not worth the slightest concern.
Though suddenly summoned by imperial decree,
or honored with gifts and invitations,
though princes and ministers bestow extraordinary favors,
or though humble dwellings are adorned with imperial splendor—
things that make others rejoice to madness—
the sage remains only more detached.

Should misfortune strike, with reputation exceeding measure,
and slanderous tongues bring sudden disgrace,
should one’s family suffer unjust accusations,
the sage alone does not shift blame to others
but accepts responsibility themselves.

The ancient sages, when honored, did not lose their true nature;
when humiliated, did not lose their body—
and for good reason.
All understood the Dao/Way of preserving the body:
they did not nourish it with fame and wealth,
but cultivated it with virtue, righteousness, and morality—
thus achieving an unbreakable body,
relied upon by all ages.

Yet if one clings to the body in selfishness,
prizes it above all,
competes for name and gain,
schemes for food and clothing,
struggles ceaselessly in restless pursuit,
contending without end,
seizing without restraint—
then calamity will follow swiftly on its heels.

Thus the noble one values concealing their worth to await the right moment,
and establishing the body to uphold virtue.

The supreme sage observes that people cannot
reside in honor while fearing disaster,
nor avert calamity before it manifests—
hence the exhortation:
*"Honor and humiliation as sudden alarm,
to hold great calamity as one’s own self."*

What is meant by *"honor and humiliation as sudden alarm"*?
It is because honor is a later-acquired glory,
not an inherent nobility—
thus it is said *"honor is of lesser worth."*
Yet common human nature clings to gain and loss,
so gaining it is cause for alarm,
losing it is cause for alarm—
this is *"honor and humiliation as sudden alarm."*

What is meant by *"to hold great calamity as one’s own self"*?
It is because one’s very existence as a body
is the source of calamity.
If there were no body,
from where would calamity arise?

While the Dao/Way is not yet complete,
one must retain the body as the vessel for cultivation.
But once the shell is cast off and ascent is achieved—
when one has spirit without form,
what calamity could befall?

Even while dwelling in the world,
all entanglements are severed,
the One True Essence contained within—
though traversing the four directions,
one moves freely as they will,
and what great calamity could there be?

Those who cultivate today,
desiring to become gods of ten thousand years,
the hope of ten thousand people,
achieving extraordinary deeds,
establishing eternal merit—
must ensure that every word and action
is without the slightest excess.
Only by valuing the body and preserving it
can one become worthy of bearing the destiny of all under heaven.

Only by restraint in every movement and stillness
can one become worthy of being relied upon by all under heaven.

Like Shun, who plowed in the fields for years
before being summoned three times with sincere respect,
and who took upon himself the responsibility of ruling the people as Yao and Shun had—
or like Zhuge Liang, who reclined in Nanyang,
yet when offered silk and jade,
assumed the role of a pillar supporting the Three Kingdoms—

To entrust the fate of a six-foot infant
and the destiny of a hundred-li realm—
who else but such a one could bear this responsibility?

To seek the Dao of selflessness and rise high above the world,
yet fail to use the Way to benefit all under heaven,
and let all under heaven wander freely within the Great Dao—
this is a path far removed indeed.

This speaks of the body’s inherent nobility,
requiring no external search,
and of a virtue beyond comparison to worldly honors of rank and wealth.

If one strives with unremitting effort from this point—
as the ancients said, *"Toil for two or three years,
and joy shall endure for ten thousand ages"*—
truly, this is no falsehood.
What then are the shocks of favor or disgrace,
or the sighs of noble suffering?

When scholars have not yet attained the Great Dao,
they must rely upon this body as their vessel for cultivation.
If one merely regards food and clothing,
wealth and glory, as the essentials for nourishing the body,
then the mortal frame grows heavy,
and the Primal Dao-body is sure to suffer thereby.

Once the Primal Dao-body is impaired,
the mortal frame itself will scarcely endure long.
Thus does the common man love his body
and yet ultimately lose it.

Only the Sage knows that all things are but phantoms,
born of impermanence and destruction,
and cannot be taken as true.
Only my Primal Dao-qi is the source of my birth,
enduring for one age, or a hundred,
or ten thousand years.

Without true cultivation of this,
how could the mortal frame ever exist?
This is the inner body within man—
preserve it, and life continues;
lose it, and death ensues.

Dissipate it, and it becomes matter;
congeal it, and it becomes the Dao-immortal.
Never for an instant can it be abandoned.

The Supreme Sage teaches one to be diligent and cautious,
never daring to neglect even the smallest matter,
never allowing a single thought to waver,
and especially not to contend with others for strength or victory.
Only by serene self-contentment,
by purity without dust,
can one truly adapt to one’s own nature.

Though he has not yet emerged to aid the people,
the common folk already rejoice in his safety within the four inches.
How else could the myriad generations be saved?
The people’s reverence for him is profound and sincere.

To find no joy in one’s own pleasure,
but joy in the pleasure of all under heaven,
to feel no sorrow in one’s own woes,
but sorrow in the woes of all under heaven—
what greater trust could there be?

**Chapter XIV: The Symbol Without Symbol**
What cannot be seen is called *Yī* (the tranquil);
What cannot be heard is called *Xī* (the subtle);
What cannot be grasped is called *Wēi* (the minute).
These three cannot be questioned,
so they merge into one.

Its upper reaches are not bright,
its lower depths are not dark—
endless and nameless,
returning to the formless.
This is called the state without form,
the symbol without symbol—
the Dao of *huǎnghǔ* (vague and indistinct).

Pursue it, and you cannot see its head;
follow it, and you cannot find its tail.
To wield the ancient Dao
and govern the present with it,
to know the origins of the ancient—
this is called the Dao’s thread.

All things under heaven must have their order,
only then can one grasp the main points,
hold the leading lines,
and proceed without confusion.
How much more so for cultivating the Dao?

The source of the Great Dao is the Primal Dao-qi;
The root of the Great Dao is the Primal Dao-qi.
What is the Primal Dao-qi?
*The Odes* say: *"The mandate of Heaven, serene and unceasing."*

What is the root of the Great Dao?
*The Odes* say: *"The carriage of Heaven, without sound or scent."*
When essence and qi unite in harmony, this is the Dao.

If cultivators recognize this guiding principle,
and discern its subtle beginnings,
then by regulating emotions with wisdom,
and fixing nature with righteousness,
and by taking the Dao-qi of emptiness and nothingness as their foundation—
the path to longevity is attained.

If one takes clarity and brightness,
lucidity and distinctness,
as the method of cultivation,
I fear the Dao has no true substance,
and all efforts are in vain.

Thus the Supreme Sage describes the Primal Dao:
*"What cannot be seen is called Yī;
What cannot be heard is called Xī;
What cannot be grasped is called Wēi."*

The heart communicates with the eyes—
the eyes harbor the spirit.
The kidneys communicate with the ears—
the ears harbor the essence.
The spleen communicates with the limbs—
the limbs belong to the spleen,
the spleen belongs to earth,
earth nurtures all things,
and the true qi congeals within.
Thus the spirit and essence reside there.

If the eyes perceive something,
if the ears hear something,
if the hands act—
these are all the shaped, colored, sounded, and scented essence-qi of the post-heaven.
They can only form matter,
not the Dao itself.

Only when the eyes see nothing—
then the post-heaven wood-nature is revealed;
Only when the ears hear nothing—
then the post-heaven metal-essence is revealed;
Only when the hands grasp nothing—
then the post-heaven intent-earth is revealed.

Thus it is said:
The post-heaven water, fire, and earth
give rise to form;
The pre-heaven metal, wood, and earth
lead to immortality.

What is called *Yī*, *Xī*, and *Wēi*
are all profound and mysterious,
beyond all reach—
truly, there is what cannot be exhausted.

If one can merge the five essences into one essence,
and blend the three primal elements into one,
then the true essence-qi is here,
and the natural sovereign is here as well.

Thus *Awakening to the True* says:
*"The maiden wears green robes (fire engenders water),
the young man wraps himself in white silk (water engenders metal).
See it, yet cannot use it (post-heaven water, fire, earth),
use it, yet cannot see it (pre-heaven metal, wood, earth).
In vague indistinctness we meet (blended into one),
in boundless darkness there is change.
In a flash, flames rise—
the True Person emerges."*

When cultivators know this,
they know the source of the Great Dao,
and the key to cultivating it.

If they do not know its origin in emptiness and nothingness,
but cling to the filth of their mortal frame,
to confused and impure spirit,
to a generated and enlightened mind,
and to deliberate visualizations—
I fear their stored essence is not deep,
and their manifestation will not be smooth.

This is the work of refining essence, qi, and spirit,
so that it never departs from the state of *hún-dùn* (chaotic unity).
Once in chaotic unity,
with time the embryo grows long,
and the yang-spirit is born—
but the methods of nurturing the embryo and fostering the spirit
must also be understood,
as taught in the previous chapter:
*"Love the people, govern the realm,
and practice the Dao of non-action."*

The cultivator knows this—
when the yang-spirit moves in and out,
its flow is natural and unfettered.
At times, the spirit ascends to the upper realm,
yet one cannot trace its origin from above—
thus it is not dazzling.
At times, the spirit withdraws to the lower depths,
yet one does not neglect its hidden abode—
thus it is not obscured.

From this, it extends endlessly and subtly,
continuously and firmly,
ineffable and beyond deliberate action—
returning to the primordial state before one’s parents gave birth,
where all is indistinct and nothing exists in particular.

The *Yì* says: *"Wash the heart and retreat, hiding in the profound."*
This is its very essence!
Hence it is said: *"Reverting to the state of nothingness."*

Though nothingness it may be,
yet all things under heaven—
ten thousand affairs, ten thousand beings—
arise from this void.
Thus the *Tàishàng* speaks of the *Dao*’s formless form,
its shapeless shape—
for indeed, what form or shape does it possess?
Only a fleeting glimpse within the *huǎnghuǎng hūhū* (dimly indistinct).

If truly dimly indistinct,
then the *zhēnyuán* (true essence) will emerge.
Greet its opportunity and guide it—
one sees not from where it arises,
for its beginning is unseen—
follow its *qi* and draw it forth—
one sees not where it ends,
for its conclusion is unmarked.
Such is the vastness of the *Dao*.

Is this not the great *Zhōushā* (boundless space) of the *Jìnguān* (pure land),
refined into the finest *háománg* (hair’s tip)?
This *Dao*—what *Dao* is it?
It is the *yuánshǐ* (primordial) *qì* of *yīnqì* (one breath),
the true sovereign of the human body’s organs and bones.

To obtain it is to live;
to lose it is to die.
To preserve it fully is to be human;
to neglect it is to become mere matter—
its manifestation lies only in a hair’s breadth.

When a student of the *Dao* attains this primordial *qì*,
harmonizing and regulating the five organs and hundred bones,
then their hair and nails shine with vitality,
their skin and flesh grow smooth and supple—
this is called *"grasp the ancient Dao,
to govern the present with its power."*

If a person can recognize this primordial *qì*—
the very breath that gave rise to heaven and earth,
the unfathomable essence of antiquity—
and take it as the guiding principle of their body,
the mastermind of all affairs,
then their foundation stands firm while their actions unfold naturally,
their root is upright and their branches align in order.

If a student of the *Dao* does not regard this primordial *qì* as their foundation,
but seeks to cultivate *zhèngjué* (enlightened awareness),
falling instead into tangential paths,
then woe unto them!

This describes the substance of the *Dao*.
Only those who study the *Dao* and grasp its substance
can have a place to begin their practice.

If the *qì* of true unity is the source of innate life and vitality,
it cannot be compared to the later-born *jīng-qì-shén* (essence, *qi*, and spirit).
To perceive the life-*qì*,
one must merge the innate truth with the true essence into one,
only then attaining the *qì* of true unity.

Though this *qì* is boundless and profound,
its form and shape are indistinct,
yet all things under heaven—
those with form and shape—
emerge from this very formlessness and shapelessness.
Truly, it is the framework of the great *Dao*,
the foundation of heaven, earth, and all beings.

The *Dao* speaks of *"guarding the center"* (守中),
Buddhism of *"contemplating emptiness"* (观空),
Confucianism of *"cultivating solitude with caution"* (慎独)—
all share the same fundamental function.

Thus, from the perspective of others,
it seems as though there is nothing to see or hear,
yet within oneself, it is utterly clear—
both supremely empty and supremely real,
both utterly nonexistent and utterly present.

Hence *Zǐsī* said:
*"Do not reveal the hidden,
do not display the subtle."*

The scholar’s diligence in cultivating solitude
is indeed unremitting.

In essence, the hidden and subtle realms of solitude,
though there may be what can be revealed or substantiated,
the roots of the great *Dao*
are only profound and mysterious—
ineffable yet described,
shapeless yet symbolized,
ultimately indistinct and tranquil.

Students of the *Dao* must,
before the yang arises, await it in *huǎnghuǎng hūhū* (dimly indistinct),
when the yang is already born, greet it in *huǎnghuǎng hūhū*,
when the yang returns to the furnace and enters the cauldron,
guard and nurture it in *huǎnghuǎng hūhū*—
never stirring the mind to deliberate enlightenment,
so that they may never fail to attain the *Dao*’s return at any time or place.

Earlier, it was said:
When the yang-spirit appears,
it illuminates heaven and penetrates earth,
reaching the profound and subtle,
and upon sudden enlightenment,
the radiant landscape is pure and natural—
knowing yet as though unaware,
awake yet as though unenlightened—
how much more so at the very start of practice,
when one must not be *huǎnghuǎng hūhū* (dimly indistinct),
but instead quiet the mortal mind to birth the *Dao*-heart.

**Chapter Fifteen: Subtle and Profound Penetration**
The ancients who excelled in cultivation
were subtle and profound,
deep beyond comprehension.

Only because they were beyond comprehension
did they forcefully describe their appearance:
*"Cautious, as though crossing a river in winter,*
*solemn, as though fearing neighbors on all sides,*
*dignified, as though a guest,*
*dissipated, as though ice about to melt,*
*unadorned, as though uncarved wood,*
*vast, as though an empty valley,*
*muddy, as though turbid waters.*

Who can clarify the turbid,
letting it settle slowly into clarity?
Who can remain still,
yet stir for ages into life?

Those who preserve this *Dao* do not wish to be full,
thus their vessel never wears out.
The *Tàishàng* spoke of the *Dao*’s substance in the previous chapter;
now he speaks of those who embody the *Dao*.

Man and the *Dao* are two yet one.
The *Dao* is invisible,
yet through man, it becomes visible.
How can man become transcendent?
Through the *Dao*, he attains transcendence.

What is the *Dao*?
It is the *qì* of true unity.
The *qì* of true unity is the *dé* (virtue/power) of the *Zhōngyōng* (Doctrine of the Mean).

To cultivate the great *Dao*, what else is there?
*Wénwáng* (King Wen) served the *Shàngdì* (August Heaven) with utmost reverence,
and *Kǒngzǐ* (Confucius) stood with feet drawn in,
as though bound by a thread.

The *Dao* as *Dao*
is nothing more than this single act of reverence.
If a person can hold reverence in their heart,
never carelessly neglecting a single thought or deed,
is not the great *Dao* already within them?

Though it is so,
the *Dao* has no strange wonders—
it relies entirely on those who embody it.
Thus the *Dao* is not suspended empty in heaven and earth.

Hence the *Tàishàng* says:
The ancients who excelled in cultivation
were not dualistic in their actions,
thus their birth of beings was unfathomable.
How profound and mysterious is their attainment!

*"Motionless yet responsive,
communicating without effort."*
How supremely profound and penetrating is this!

Observe their hearts—vast and boundless—
their *qì*—abundant and flowing—
not unlike the depths of rivers and streams,
making them impossible to measure or recognize.

Thus the *Tàishàng* says:
*"Only because it is beyond recognition,
do they forcefully describe its appearance."*

「To reveal the true unknowability within,
yet forcefully describe its outward form—
their hearts ever cautious in solitude,
their sincerity ever present in all things,
like the cautious traveler crossing a river,
waiting for ice to freeze before daring to proceed;
or the vigilant night walker,
waiting for the wind to still before moving forward—
this is the utmost care.

Their solemn composure,
like a distinguished guest arriving from afar,
never daring to slacken in demeanor;
their effortless release—
unburdened by ties,
smooth as flowing water,
vanished without trace—
their loyal and virtuous hearts,
their benevolent treatment of all,
pure as the uncarved *Tàipǔ* (太朴),
unpolished yet seamless in harmony.

Their dignified bearing,
their humble self-restraint—
what differs from the profound depths of mountain valleys,
empty yet boundless,
vast yet containing all?

Their form is thus,
and their nature may be known.
All is contained within the silent *huntian* (浑天),
following the *imperial* (帝) pattern yet unrecognized.

Like *Shùn* (舜) dwelling in the mountains,
no distinction from the wild mountain folk.

Can their *hun* (浑) and *è* (噩) ways be imitated by the dull and turbid?
Though *hun* (浑) and *è* (浊) may seem alike,
saints and commoners share one principle—
the turbidity of common folk is truly turbid;
the turbidity of the sage,
though appearing like turbidity,
is in truth the utmost turbidity and the utmost clarity.

Yet the sage does not claim sainthood,
for that is how they become the sage;
the commoner does not claim ordinariness,
for that is how they remain the commoner.

Who can clarify the heart’s stains,
making it serene,
waiting for its stillness to endure,
until pure light reveals itself?

Who can nurture the nature’s innate tranquility,
soaking and expanding it,
until through prolonged cultivation,
its growth is gradual,
its essence refined like medicine,
its spirit distilled like elixir?

Is not the *Dao* of preserving life
truly found here?

This is stillness to concentrate the spirit,
movement to generate the *qi*—
to guard the center,
to live in the *yang* (陽) as the child of time.

From this, ascending and descending,
returning all to the furnace within,
gradually refining, gradually distilling,
gradually distilling, gradually concentrating—
nothing but a single, undivided heart,
all distractions emptied,
to preserve this *yang* alone.

The more one has, the more they heal;
the more one empties, the more they empty.
With a heart of utter emptiness,
no thought of fullness clinging—
thus they can restore the *one true* (真一) *qi*,
and attain the *constant Dao* (真常之道).

What does *"能敝不新成"* mean?
It is that what is newly formed
will soon decay,
but here, decay is impossible—
decay marks its traces,
no decay marks its spirit.

One true essence contained within,
ten thousand spirits manifest without—
its subtlety and profundity,
its mysterious penetration,
are indeed as such.

The seven sentences beginning with *"若"* (if)
all borrow external forms to depict the Dao’s marvels,
truly revealing its profound and unfathomable depths.

The sentences beginning with *"孰能"* (who can)
speak to those seeking to enter the Dao
yet failing to cultivate virtue.

Turbidity cannot be clarified,
yet stillness preserves the heart’s purity;
tranquility is precious when enduring,
yet movement reveals the spirit’s boundless wisdom.

Fullness invites loss,
so one must not crave excess;
novelty decays swiftly,
so one must not cling to the new.

I hope the seeker may be empty yet dignified,
uncarved yet unpolished,
vast and boundless (*hunhun huānghuāng* 浑浑灏灏),
ever displaying the sincere and trustworthy winds of virtue—
then no heart will fail to transform into Dao-heart,
no *qi* will remain unaltered into true *qi*.

Do not hesitate, thinking it too profound to measure—
press forward without delay!

**Chapter Sixteen: The Ultimate Emptiness and Sincere Stillness**
*Extreme emptiness, sincere stillness.*
Guard the utmost emptiness,
preserve the deepest stillness.
All things arise together,
and I observe their return.

Things are myriad,
each returning to its root.
Returning to the root is stillness,
stillness is restoring the vital breath.
Restoring the vital breath is constancy,
knowing constancy is enlightenment.
Ignorance of constancy brings reckless action—disaster.
Knowing constancy brings tolerance,
tolerance brings impartiality,
impartiality brings sovereignty,
sovereignty aligns with the heavens,
the heavens embody the Dao,
the Dao endures eternally—
living a life undying in truth.

If one seeks to cultivate the great Dao,
to become a *golden immortal* (金仙),
enduring countless eons without decay,
from the very first step,
they must not fail to grasp its root.

What is the root?
It is the *mysterious pass* (玄关窍).

Cultivating truth and refining the Dao
is not a single path—
how can this *mysterious pass* alone contain all its essence?

For the heavens have their heavenly root,
things have their roots of origin,
humans have their human sources—
never has one achieved supreme attainment,
or immortal deeds,
without a foundation.

Observe the heavens and earth before their opening—
indeed, they were vast and silent, without sound.
After their unfolding,
they became boundless and limitless.

Is this the heavenly root?
The boundless and formless,
indeed, cannot be called the heavenly root.
Is this the heavenly root?
The flourishing and endless,
also cannot be named the heavenly root.

Then where is this root?
It lies in the moment of *impending opening* (將開未開).

Observe humanity before birth—
indeed, they were faint and indistinct, without form.
After birth,
they proliferate endlessly, without end.

Is this the root before birth?
The indistinct and unshaped,
indeed, cannot be called the root of humanity.
Is this the root after birth?
The ever-changing and boundless,
also cannot be seen as the root of humanity.

Then where is this root?
It lies in the moment of *impending birth* (將生未生).

To cultivate the great Dao,
can one ignore this *mysterious pass* and act recklessly?

The *Tàishàng* (太上) teaches the method of nurturing the Dao and seeking the mysterious:
*"Extreme emptiness, sincere stillness,
and I observe their return."*

This is the key for the aspirant to attain the *mysterious pass*—
only by withdrawing all superficiality,
returning to the sincere and solid,
concentrating the spirit in emptiness,
nurturing the *qi* in stillness,
reaching the utmost emptiness,
preserving the deepest stillness—
will all phenomena naturally dissolve,
and one true essence be held within.»

The *Yì* says:
*"Return reveals the heart of Heaven and Earth."*

Master Zhāo further states:
*"At the midpoint of the Winter Solstice,
the Heavenly Root reaches its utmost subtlety.
When the first Yang stirs,
all things begin to be born.
This is when Heavenly Principle returns—
the ancients likened it to the *active child hour* (活子时).
It is also said:
*'When the first Yang emerges,
the dim and murky awaken.'*

This is precisely the moment when all things return to their rectitude,
when Heaven and Earth renew their cycle.
Here, the primordial *ancestral qi* of the Pre-Heavenly First Beginning
can be keenly observed.

Yet its mechanism is exceedingly subtle,
its qi exceedingly swift—
it is present now, but gone in an instant.
Like lightning or a spark,
it vanishes in the blink of an eye.

Observe the grasses and trees:
When they flourish in form—
branches and leaves,
radiant and vivid—
all their splendor and order
are insufficient as the root of rebirth,
the origin of renewal.
Only when they withdraw and return,
transforming life into death,
do they bear fruit and return to their roots—
just as the adept’s *elixir head* (丹头) does.

When they return to their roots,
they also return from motion to stillness.
Once returned to stillness,
they once again descend to the initial moment of seeding—
for things, this is returning to their origin;
for humanity, this is restoring one’s mandate.
It is no different.

Spring and autumn pass—
the old gives way to the new;
life and death alternate—
flowers bloom and then wither.
Thus, the adept’s way of restoring one’s mandate
is also the interplay of Heaven and Earth’s two *qi*,
the natural flow of a single *qi*—
the most ordinary, most constant of ways.

To know the constant is to grasp the Great Dao.
From here, one’s practice will not stray.

Yet the world’s deviant paths—
ignoring the roots of the Great Dao,
unwilling to purify the mind and clear the thoughts,
to investigate origins and return to fundamentals—
some refine the nature of perception,
some cultivate the *qi* of form,
some gather seven metals and eight stones as medicine,
some take young boys and girls to forge the elixir—
with no foundation, how can the Dao be obtained?
Moreover, fixated on one extreme,
they stray into false paths,
and many meet ruin in body and life.
All this stems from not knowing the Dao as the constant,
thus seeking hidden wonders,
treading perilous paths,
and inviting misfortune.

Only when one knows the Dao as true and eternal—
present in all people, sufficient in all things—
does one not rejoice in knowing it,
nor marvel at possessing it.
Like water and fire to humanity,
it is taken and used freely.
In emptiness, it is boundless in capacity;
to all things, it is equally impartial—
a heart of boundless fairness.

Such fairness without selfishness
is akin to the Sage.
*"My people, my kin; all things, my companions."*
Standing with Heaven and Earth as foundation,
uniting all things in a single source—
how could this not be one with Heaven?

Heaven is the Dao; the Dao is Heaven.
Beyond Heaven, there is no Dao;
beyond the Dao, there is no Heaven.
Only Heaven is supreme;
only the Sage follows its law.
Only the Dao is exalted;
only Heaven is its model.

Thus, humanity has birth and death,
but the Dao endures eternally, unspent.
Even in transcendence or shedding the shell,
there comes a time of decline.
Yet though the form perishes, the spirit does not;
though the body is lost, the *qi* is not lost.

The *Shī* says:
*"The King stands above,
illuminating Heaven."*
Is this not what is meant?

All this comes from attaining *extreme emptiness* and *sincere stillness*,
observing the signs of Return—
thus achieving the unfathomable.

Scholars dare not neglect the root and act recklessly, inviting disaster.

The *Tàishàng* teaches the fundamental practice,
the essential study of the head and mind.
Where one gains strength here,
strength is gained everywhere.
If scholars grasp this meaning,
they will diligently adhere to the rule,
unceasingly, from non-being to being,
from being to non-being—
even in a single breath,
the root of the Great Dao is present;
even in a meal’s time,
the First Beginning of the Great Dao is preserved.

From this single thread of subtle mechanism,
gathering and refining it,
gradually it swells into an unstoppable vigor—
all from the accumulation of that first Yang.

Even if the mighty *qi* fills Heaven and Earth,
if the Yang spirit pierces the constellations of *Dòu* and *Niú*,
what is it not the result of this true *qi*?

The student who fails to obtain this true *qi*
and instead refines the post-Heavenly form and spirit
is but a single hair from nine oxen,
a grain from the sea—
how dare they compare with Heaven and Earth?

Only by walking this path and uniting with Heaven and Earth
can one endure for billions of years unbroken.
The adept must earnestly grasp the core principle,
seizing the moment without loss.

**Chapter Seventeen: The Work Completed, the Body Fulfilled**

The *Tàishàng* says:
*"The highest does not know its existence"* (others render this as *"The lowest knows its existence"*, but it contradicts the text’s intent—an error in transmission);
Next, it is honored and praised;
Next, it is feared;
Next, it is insulted.
When trust is insufficient,
there is distrust.
*"Ah, how precious are words!"*
When the work is complete and the matter fulfilled,
the people all say, *"This is natural."*

(*"Ah, how precious"*—this speaks of ease and sincerity,
the careful weight of one’s declarations.)

The **Dao** of the **Tàishàng** in cultivating the body is the same as the **Dao** of governing the world—nothing more than **one true essence**.

When this **true essence** is held within, the self is refined;
when it responds to all things, all things are fulfilled.
Though there may be distinctions between inner and outer, self and others,
this **true essence** of the heart remains unchanging.

If a person attains **sincerity** without ceasing,
then the response of others is also unceasing.
If **sincerity** falters even slightly,
then the response of things is also impaired.

For all share this heart,
and this heart shares this principle:
Cultivate the self, and the world naturally harmonizes;
lose the **true essence**, and the world must descend into chaos.
From the **Three Sovereigns** and **Five Emperors** down to the present,
there has never been any deviation.

The **Tàishàng** desires that people refine themselves through the **Dao** of **sincerity and trust**,
and likewise govern others through it.
Unseen yet manifest,
unstirred yet transformative,
effortless yet accomplished—
To the self, there is no conscious method of governance;
to others, it is felt as subtle influence and transformation,
unaware of its own workings.
This was the pure wind of antiquity—
I have long failed to behold it.

Thus the **Tàishàng** says:
*"The highest does not know its existence."*
When ruler and people rejoice together in the radiant light of Heaven and Earth,
how could such divine transformation exist
without the abiding **sincerity and trust**?

As the noble winds of antiquity have faded,
the **Tàishàng’s** perfect governance is unheard of,
and the world’s customs decline further,
the **Great Dao** growing ever more estranged—
to the point where even speech and sight cannot bear witness.

If one is not far removed from antiquity,
this **Dao** may still linger,
human nature not yet corrupted,
**true sincerity** yet remaining—
though not the profound sincerity of the most ancient times,
it still shines as the brilliance of the present age.
The same governance, yet one achieves transformation without effort,
while another applies benevolence with deliberate intent.

To cherish the people as one’s own children,
to love them as family—
in such times, reverence for the worthy and respect for elders
comes naturally, like echoes in response.
Even if trust is not perfectly aligned,
virtuous transformation cannot be fully uniform—
yet through affection and love,
through encouragement and praise,
without harsh words or threats,
one inspires them to cherish virtue and fear authority.
Though not attaining the **Tàishàng’s** level,
this still follows the **Dao** and the path—
what is called *"When the Great Dao is abandoned, there arises benevolence and righteousness."*

When the **Sovereign** descends to **Emperor**,
and the **Emperor** to **King**,
all still govern through the **virtue/power** of knowing **Dao** and practicing the **Way of the King**.

As ancient customs fade further,
the **Great Dao** grows ever more neglected,
**Kings** descend to **Conquerors**,
pretending to uphold the **true essence**,
each heart its own,
each perspective its own—
far removed from the **Emperor’s** and **King’s** unified **virtue/power** of trust.

Thus, though rituals, teachings, laws, and decrees remain,
the **true essence** of the heart is utterly different.
Only through talent and cunning does one govern the world,
relying on laws and measures to guide the people—
if their actions are appropriate and measures lawful,
the people fear to break the law or violate the rules,
and such a ruler is deemed clever,
his age one of great peace.

Yet lower still—
those who rely on cleverness to drive the people,
who use deceit to rule the masses,
with deceitful and cunning hearts,
employing deceitful and cunning governance—
when they boast arrogantly, satisfied with their schemes,
none dare say they are not wiser than the **Three Kings** or more talented than the **Five Conquerors**,
yet the people of this age
live in constant dread, even in the dead of night,
for their oppression is indeed severe.
Though the people dare not speak,
their hearts are alienated,
and there is never a moment of true governance—
thus disaster and chaos follow swiftly.

Confucius said:
*"When the ruler values trust, the people act with sincerity."*
If trust is lacking within oneself,
how can one expect trust from the people?
This is how ruler and subjects deceive and betray one another.

Though institutions, decrees, teachings, and commands
are not abandoned even by the Sage,
yet sincerity and deceit differ,
and their responses are distinct.
Only when the **Emperor** or **King** sets the example,
inspiring trust in the people,
do laws stand firm and governance succeed,
words carry weight, and the people believe—
ultimately radiating brilliance to the four quarters,
accomplishing the work and fulfilling the task,
as with **Yao** transforming the times through **Yong**,
or **Shun** governing through effortless **non-action**,
while the people say, *"This is natural."*

Ah!
This is the reach of **true trust**—
how vast the difference
between this and those who lack trust within!

The **Dao** of **virtue/power** is a single thread,
permeating all things—
cultivating the self is here,
governing the world is here,
and all affairs of the world and all things
fall within this scope.

Even in this chapter’s *"Tàishàng"*—
it speaks of the highest person
embracing the highest nature,
thus called *"Tàishàng."*
**Supreme virtue** is pure, untouched, and non-acting;
the six senses are steadied.
Next, through reverence and love, one transforms the people—
sensing, one responds.
Next, through awe and severity, one governs the world.
Next, through cleverness, one guides the people—
this is called *"artifice."*
Yet its most refined essence is **trust**.
**Trust** belongs to **Earth**—
throughout cultivation, its wondrous use lies in **Earth’s sincerity**.
Thus the **Tàishàng** says:
*"Its essence is most true, within it lies **trust"**—
this is the **elixir’s foundation**.
**Trust** is nothing but **sincerity**.
If a person attains **sincerity** without ceasing,
then the **elixir** as **elixir** is already within.

Yet **trust** and deceit are ever so close—
one moment of mindfulness makes a sage,
one moment of negligence makes a fool.
The divide between humanity and beasts,
life and death—
it hinges on but a breath.

I wish the later generations would seek **true trust**
as the **constant Dao**.

Where is **trust** found?
It is the **Mystic Pass**, the single aperture—
do people know it?

**Chapter Eighteen: The Great Dao Abandoned**

When the **Great Dao** is abandoned,
there arises **benevolence and righteousness**.
When **wisdom** emerges,
there arises **great deceit**.
When familial bonds fracture,
there arises **filial piety and affection**.
When the state falls into confusion,
there arise **loyal ministers**.

Observe the ancient world:
customs were sincere and unadorned,
people were simple and unrefined,
each followed their nature,
fulfilled their destiny,
settled into their ways,
took joy in their labors—
like things moving with Heaven,
acting in accordance with nature,
requiring no contrivance or arrangement,
following the natural course of their being,
unaware of why it was so.

Zhuangzi spoke of those burdened with the weight of ritual and law—
their clumsy sincerity, their weary toil,
moving in harmony with Heaven,
rich with genuine intent.

This is how they remain unknowing and unknowable,
following the patterns of the **Dao**.

What kind of transformation is this?

Nothing more than a **mystic unity**—
unthinking, carefree,
one with the **Great Dao**.

Yet as the **Imperial Way** declines,
the **Great Dao** fades further,
and those in power speak of **benevolence and righteousness**,
diligently performing their duties,
daring not to stray.

To dwell in **righteousness** and practice **benevolence**—
this too is the noble deed of sages,
not entirely to be condemned.
But compared to ancient times of flourishing **Dao**,
when the **Great Dao** shone bright,
and hearts were simple and unadorned,
**benevolence and righteousness** existing naturally within—
how vast the difference!

Thus, the **Ancient Sage** sighed:
*"When the **Great Dao** is abandoned,
there arises **benevolence and righteousness**."*

From this, both rulers and subjects
covet the name of **benevolence and righteousness**,
while hypocrites exploit them for deceit.
Though **benevolence and righteousness** remain,
their practice has decayed.
Is this the fault of **benevolence and righteousness** itself?
Rather, it is the harm wrought by those
who force interpretations upon them,
who cling to **righteousness** and are ensnared by **benevolence**.

Still, they speak of **benevolence and righteousness**—
though not the true **Dao**,
they have not yet reached **great deceit**.

After this, customs grew ever more perverse,
hearts ever more corrupt.
Even the transmission of **benevolence and righteousness**
was nearly lost.
Now, in court and countryside, among rulers and subjects,
all flaunt their **wisdom** and display their **cleverness**—
within these exchanges,
deceit and trickery prevail.
How can they hope to govern their families or states?

Thus, father and son grow estranged,
brothers quarrel,
even husbands and wives, friends and neighbors—
each with their own heart, their own perspective—
barely resembling the harmony of **Hu and Yue**,
let alone greater unity!
If a son proves filial, the court rewards him;
if a father shows kindness, the village praises him.
Alas! **Filial piety** and **parental kindness**—
these are the eternal laws of Heaven and Earth,
the natural order of the home.
Why should they be singled out for praise?

Yet when **six relationships** are fractured,
while disobedience spreads,
and family strife erupts—
then those who practice **filial piety** and **parental kindness**
are held up with solemn reverence,
their deeds displayed to inspire the world.
How far this falls from the **Ancient Sage’s** golden age,
when **filial piety** was natural,
when **parental kindness** required no name!

Still, even this **filial piety** and **parental kindness**
are but responses to discord—
yet today’s world prefers ornamentation,
empty display,
where those who claim **filial piety** and **parental kindness**
are countless.

And after the **Five Hegemons**,
kin slaughtered kin,
lords took hostages of their rulers—
no wonder tyrants ruled above,
while insolent ministers defied below,
and the state fell into chaos.
Fortunately, **loyal ministers** arose to stabilize the land,
though success or failure could not be foreseen—
their devotion exhausted, their loyalty unyielding.
Some, like **Zhuge Liang’s** six expeditions to Qishan,
or **Jiang Ziya’s** nine campaigns into the Central Plains,
could not reverse fate with force,
nor preserve the realm with their hearts.

For ministers serve their lords—
they wish to be **good ministers**, not **loyal ones**.
If fortune favors peace,
they share in governing the state;
if war erupts,
they display their talents to aid the world.
When the age is corrupt,
Heaven reveals **loyal ministers**;
when **loyal ministers** appear,
Heaven sustains the corruption.
Yet even if **loyal ministers** restore order,
transforming martial fury into a realm of **ritual and propriety**—
this still falls short of the **Ancient Sage’s** era of effortless harmony.

Alas! **Loyal ministers** would prefer obscurity—
otherwise, one man’s loyalty brings ruin to the land.
This is the **Ancient Sage’s** lament for the times,
his sorrow for the present, his longing for the past,
wishing all—rulers and subjects—
to return to **primordial simplicity**,
to **unknowing unity**:
their **virtue** unbroken, their desires none,
their **Way** ever sufficient—
content, serene,
all dwelling in effortless peace,
unaware of their path.

Even **benevolence, wisdom, filial piety, loyal ministers**—
all are taken as given,
their existence unspoken, their names unspoken—
what greater **simplicity** is there?

Though this speaks to governance,
it also applies to self-cultivation.
The opening lines describe a **primordial age**,
untouched by artifice,
like the **uncarved block**—
no effort required,
the **Dao** complete in itself.

But once the **original nature** is lost,
one must borrow from **pre-Heaven yin and yang** to restore it.
**Yin and yang** are **benevolence and righteousness**—
the saying *"When the **Great Dao** is abandoned,
there arises **benevolence and righteousness**."*

To discern the true **qi** within and without,
to nourish with **wisdom** what is pure—
this is not entirely authentic,
for it mixes with **post-Heaven** influences,
and cannot avoid **deceit**.
This is the meaning of:
*"When **wisdom** emerges,
there arises **great deceit**."*

As for **nourishing yin to balance yang**—
this reconciles the **six senses**,
restoring **great harmony**—
the metaphor of **filial piety and kindness**.

Through **vigorous tempering and refining**—
this calms the **inner chaos**,
elevating to **clarity**—
the essence of **loyal ministers**.

Understand this, and the **Dao** is near.
What greater **Way** is there than
following one’s **original, uncarved nature**?
Those who cultivate do so by this alone.

**Chapter Nineteenth: Minimal Self-Interest and Desires**

*"Few in self-interest, scant in desires,
abandon **saintly wisdom** and discard **wisdom**,
the people’s benefit grows a hundredfold;
abandon **virtue** and cast aside **righteousness**,
the people return to **filial piety and kindness**.

Discard **artifice** and forsake **profit**,
and thieves and robbers vanish.
These three **refinements** are insufficient,
so they must be paired with their **essences**:
*seeing the unadorned and embracing the uncarved*,
few in self-interest, scant in desires,
abandon learning to be free of cares.

Under the heavens, the multitude of people and things,
the **accomplished and unaccomplished**, noble and base—
all unequal,
yet none escape the **principle of Dao** and the **flow of qi**,
merely differing in how they are **penetrated**.

When their **nature** is **obstructed** and **not in harmony**,
when **emotions** clash and **intentions** are blocked,
it is because those above lack means to **inspire**,
and those below lack means to **transform**.

In ancient times, the **most sage rulers**,
following **Nature’s Dao**,
practiced **effortless governance**,
neither delighting in **actions** for the sake of **achievement**,
nor wearying of **actions** to neglect **government**.

Though they possessed **brilliant wisdom**,
they **encompassed all within the realm of effortlessness**,
in the **pure and tranquil abode**,
so that those below, observing and being **transformed**,
naturally **honored their kin**,
**extended their elders**,
**settled their customs**,
and **found joy in their livelihoods**,
with none failing to return to their **original nature**,
and none **unfulfilled** in their **growth**.

This was the **age of antiquity**,
when people were all **sincere and thick**,
and things **proliferated abundantly**—
their benefit was indeed more than a hundredfold!

As for the **most virtuous rulers**,
naturally **embracing benevolence and compassion**,
with **pity and empathy**,
they **rescued all beings** and **benefited all things**,
as their **conduct**.

**Vast and unrestrained**,
**unadorned and pure**,
they **neither spoke of right and wrong**,
nor **straight and crooked**,
yet **followed Heaven’s movement**,
**conformed to Nature’s course**,
naturally **without favor or bias**,
returning to the **domain of Great Centrality and Rectitude**.

When the people **observed, were inspired, and transformed**,
as sons they **naturally practiced filial piety toward their parents**,
as fathers they **naturally showed kindness to their children**.
Though there were **unfilial and unkind** individuals,
accustomed to such **habits**,
they too **gradually transformed**—
how **prosperous** was this!

Later generations of **rulers with peerless brilliance**,
**exceptional acuity**,
yet who **governed with artifice**,
did not **understand the Dao’s foundation of effortlessness**—
**guiding** it was easy,
**contradicting** it was difficult.
Thus some **delighted in frequent changes**, disturbing the people,
**establishing laws and regulations**,
ultimately leading to **national calamities**,
where the people **barely survived**.

Some **loved martial exploits**, neglecting governance,
**exhausting armies and waging wanton wars**,
ending with **empty treasuries**,
the people **unable to bear their fate**—
no wonder then that **poverty and national decline** arose,
that **plundering and banditry** spread,
and **thieves and robbers ran rampant** under Heaven.

Such rulers,
**masters of artifice**,
did not **know to wield their cleverness** in **effortless Heaven**,
**effortless Earth**,
but **coveted profit** to enrich the state,
leading to **waning national strength**,
**disturbed customs**—
such was their folly.

If they could **discard artifice while embodying it**,
expressing their **true intent**,
**following the natural course**,
**rectifying the dharma without scheming for profit**,
**illuminating the Dao without calculating achievements**,
then the **virtue of the noble** would be **wind**,
the **virtue of the petty** would be **grass**—
naturally, like **water flowing downward**,
or **fire blazing upward**,
there would be **what could not be restrained**.

In such times, would the people still
**ignore propriety and shame**,
**commit theft and robbery**, or **pursue what is not right**?
None would.

This is **great wisdom as if foolish**,
**great virtue as if enduring**,
**great artifice as if clumsy**—
later generations, seeing this,
would deem them **unfit to be rulers**,
**unfit to govern**.

Yet the **pervasive influence of sage virtue**,
the **reaching of benevolent grace**,
the **application of wisdom and artifice**,
though lacking in **external adornment**,
had **far greater substance within**.

**Desires and principles** originally **do not conspire**—
sufficiency in **external adornment** is **suspect within**,
**reducing ornamentation** only **enriches the essence**.
The **sage abandons wisdom**,
the **virtuous abandon righteousness**,
the **artful abandon profit**—
all to **strengthen substance**,
**discard ornamentation**.

Though the people below are **utterly foolish**,
they **constantly take the ruler’s actions** as their **guide**.
Thus, **rejecting adornment and valuing substance**,
it **naturally unifies the petty people’s hearts** in the **original source**,
**untouched and uncarved**.

For they **see only governance of unadorned substance**,
and thus **embrace** the **true uncarved simplicity**.
Such **wholeness and naturalness**—
clothing and food,
each **settled in its proper way**;
**reciprocal courtesy and exchanges**,
each **conforming to its due measure**.
Though their **qi and constitution** are limited,
they **cannot fully prevent** the **sprouting of self-interest and desires**,
yet these would be **few and scant**.

In sum:
**Sainthood, virtue, artifice**—
all are **substance**;
**wisdom, righteousness, profit**—
all are **ornamentation**.
Abandoning **sainthood and discarding wisdom**,
abandoning **virtue and discarding righteousness**,
abandoning **artifice and discarding profit**—
all to **ensure ornamentation is insufficient**,
**substance remains abundant**,
each **with its proper domain**.

When the people **nourish themselves on virtue**,
**drink in harmony** from within—
how could they **not benefit endlessly**,
with **filial piety and kindness daily flourishing**,
and **theft transformed into goodness**?

This **prosperous governance**,
I have long failed to **behold**.

This **parable of cultivation**—
first, **preserve the heart and nurture the nature**,
when **heart and nature return to their original state**,
then the **post-Heaven qi and essence**
also **return to the pre-Heaven qi and essence**.

If one **has not yet seen the nature and clarified the heart**,
but **merely uses the post-Heaven constitution and qi**,
or the **sensate heart and awareness**,
then the **refined essence is but common essence**,
the **qi is but common qi**—
how could one **obtain the true unified essence**,
the **true unified qi** to **forge the elixir**?

Cultivators must **seek from the source** a **great fundamental principle**,
a **true head of understanding**,
to **take command**.
Then **refining essence, tempering qi, and purifying spirit**—
all would be **achieved**.

When this **meaning is awakened**,
not only would one **understand the Classic of the Most High**,
**govern the world and cultivate the self**—
everywhere **a single thread**—
but also the **Four Books and Five Classics**,
none would **not be elixir texts**.

Other commentaries say:
When those above **abandon sainthood and wisdom**,
the people **only know profit**,
so those **seeking profit** increase a hundredfold;
when they **abandon virtue and righteousness**,
the people **do not know to love their kin**,
so they **greatly oppose filial piety and kindness**—
this is **what should not be abandoned** yet is abandoned,
and such are its **harmful effects**."

When it comes to **crafty profit, sainthood, wisdom, and virtue clashing with righteousness**—
if one can **sever and abandon them**,
then where would thieves and robbers arise?
This is **what should be abandoned** and is abandoned—
its **effects** are thus.

This **discourse is truly "high."**

Below these three, it means:
**Governing the people does not require decrees**—
only that **commands must originate from what is personally practiced and aligned with**.
Seeing **unadorned simplicity** brings **determination**,
embracing **rough jade** preserves **spirit**—
few desires, little private gain—
this is **"holding the world yet not partaking"**,
not the **abundant transformation of non-action**—
how could one **ever attain this**?

**Chapter Twenty: Differing from Others**
**Abandoning learning brings no sorrow.**

How far apart are **"yes"** and **"ah"**?
How far apart are **"good"** and **"evil"**?
What people **fear**, one **cannot fail to fear**.

Ah! **Its boundlessness knows no end!**

The crowd bustles and rejoices,
as if feasting on **the grandest sacrificial meat**,
as if ascending **Spring Terrace**—
I alone remain **serene and unmarked**,
like an infant **yet unborn** (before leaving the womb).
**Drifting, drifting**—
as if **having nowhere to return** (untouched by traces)!

The crowd all **have excess**,
while I alone seem **forsaken**.
My heart is that of a **fool**!

**Muddled**—while the vulgar are **bright**,
I alone am **dim**;
the vulgar are **sharp-sighted**,
I alone am **obscured**.

**Dull**—like the sea’s **indifference to external desire**,
**whirling**—like **no fixed form**,
the crowd all **have purpose**,
while I alone am **stubborn and crude**.

I alone **differ from others**,
yet **nourish at the Mother’s breast**.

The **Sage’s attainment is supreme**,
called **"Abandoning Learning."**
Pure **vital impulse** flows unrestrained—
no thought, no worry.

The *Odes* say:
*"The Most High watches over you—
do not let your heart be divided."*

Thus, **acting from one’s natural station**,
one **indifferently accepts** poverty or wealth, gain or loss—
nowhere **not at ease**—
so it is said: **"no sorrow."**

Such a realm—
compared to the **unlearned and unskilled**—
is **not merely a chasm**,
yet the gap is **not distant**.

Like **echoes**—
both respond, yet **"yes"** is direct while **"ah"** is flattery—
the response is the same,
but the **source of response**—
how far apart are they?

From ancient times, the **divide between Sage and commoner**
lies only in **good and evil**—
and the distinction between them
rests solely on **reverence or licentiousness**,
a contest of **a single thought**—
how far apart are they?

For **human-heartedness is perilous**,
**Dao-heartedness is subtle**—
a hair’s breadth of difference
leads to **thousands of miles of error**.
What people **dread and guard against**,
I too **cannot fail to fear** what people fear.

Thus, from the **first step forward**,
one must **withdraw and gather spirit-light**,
returning it to **primordial chaos**—
whether in **action or stillness**,
in **change or constancy**,
all as **the age of Great Void**,
before Heaven and Earth were cleaved—
vast and boundless,
as if **night had no end**.

Thus, **within there is a master**,
while **external things do not disturb**.

When applied to **governance and decrees**,
the people naturally **transform and thrive**.
Those who **feed on the Sage’s virtue**
feast as if on **the grandest sacrificial meat**;
those who **dwell in the Sage’s domain**
rejoice as if on **Spring Terrace**—
how could this be compared
to **lonely cultivation in quietude**?

Thus, **fulfilling the world yet unaware of merit**,
**acting in the world yet unaware of deeds**—
while the crowd **takes delight**,
I alone am **dull and tranquil**, **faint as mist**.

Like an infant **still in the womb**,
before birth—
a **cluster of primordial qi**,
**wholly embraced**,
rising and falling, circulating without end—
**harmonizing with Heaven and Earth**,
**unaware of its destination**.

Others **act**, while I **do not**—
the crowd **has excess to sustain them**,
while I **stand alone, abandoned by the world**,
far beyond what others can reach.

From their view, few would not call me **foolish**;
yet **turning inward**, I only feel **my heart cleansed and hidden in depth**,
**at ease with Heaven, accepting my fate**—
how could this be the heart of a fool?

It is merely **great wisdom as folly**,
**great skill as clumsiness**.

Otherwise, how could I **bring such joy to labor and peace to dwelling**,
moving people so swiftly toward **spirit-transformation**?

Such is **the Great Polarity**, a **whirling chaos** held in embrace—
**muddled**, like a **chicken’s egg yet unhatched**,
with **no trace of yin or yang**,
**no knowledge at all**.

The vulgar are **brightly discerning** in all things,
while I alone am **dimly unaware**;
the vulgar are **keenly perceptive** in all things,
while I alone am **obscured in ignorance**.

Am I truly **dim and ignorant**,
**obscured and unaware**?
Rather, it is **hiding one’s traces and concealing one’s light**,
**embedding clarity in depth**—
day by day, month by month,
**refining the elixir through nine transformations**,
**reaching the Sage’s virtue**,
and becoming **an immortal unchanging through the ages**.

Its **vast transformation** is like **Heaven and Earth’s dimness**,
**embracing all phenomena within thought**.

Its **subtle spirit** is like **flowing clouds and water**—
**no fixed place**,
**nurturing all life within**.

From the **outside**, the crowd all **have purpose in the world**,
while I alone am **stubborn and crude**.
Yet **within**—
the **Way is high and beautiful**,
**transcending the masses**,
**unmatched and uniquely Sage**.

This is how I **differ from others**,
**unreachable by people**.

For **commoners scatter outward**,
losing their **original Heavenly nature**,
while **Sages cultivate inwardly**,
**preserving their inherent nature**.

The **Sage differs from the commoner**—
all because **later Heaven returns to Earlier Heaven**.

Later Heaven is **emotion**—**child’s qi**;
Earlier Heaven is **nature**—**Mother’s qi**.
Returning from **emotion to nature**,
just as a child **clings to its mother**,
**reluctant to part**—
thus it is said: **"Noble is the one who seeks nourishment at the Mother’s breast."**

Mencius said: *"The Way of learning and inquiry is nothing else—
merely the recovery of the lost heart."*

The distinction between Sage and madman lies in but a single thought.
Is the Dao so distant?
Are the methods so numerous?
If one seeks the Way,
and does not seek it in the boundless, formless moment—
where else shall they apply themselves?

Thus it is said:
*"The Dark Mother, the Dark Mother—
truly the Dark Mother.
Not in the heart, nor in the kidneys.
Investigate the origin of life and the receipt of fate—
do not blame the Heavenly mechanism for being fully revealed."*

What is there at the origin of life?
Reflect upon this,
and the Way is half attained.

First, it is said that the Sage abandons learning.
Already possessing eternal joy and inner stillness,
free from worry and care,
daily actions and habits all return to the undifferentiated Heaven.
Untouched, uncarved,
unsoiled, unstained—
that which, when gazed upon, grows ever higher,
beyond all measure,
truly that which may be aspired to but never reached.

Yet though the effort is so extreme,
the true divide lies only in the distinction between reverence and licentiousness.
Can one fear its profundity and retreat in hesitation?

Yanzi said: *"What kind of person was Shun? What kind of person am I?
Those who act likewise are no different."*
This is no falsehood.

Yet it is not the empty, idle learning of the vulgar.
The Sage cultivates innate virtue to renew the people,
letting all beings nourish themselves on virtue and drink in harmony,
playing in the radiant light of Heaven and Earth.

This Way—what Way is it?
The Way of utmost sincerity,
exhausting the nature of Self, of humanity, of things.
Ah!
When nature is thus exhausted,
what more is there to learn?
It is nothing but nourishing on the Mother’s qi.

---

**Chapter 21: The Container of the Great Virtue**

The Container of the Great Virtue
follows only the Dao.

The Dao as a thing—
