aids in establishing **order**,
serving the sovereign solely through the **Way**,
never coercing the world with arms.

Why is this so?
For he who slays another’s father and brother,
others will likewise slay his father and brother.
Human hearts yearn to return to harmony,
**Heaven’s** **Dao** favors restoration.
Grudges and retribution know no end.

Moreover, where armies pass,
the people suffer harm;
where troops march,
even chickens and dogs perish.
Thus, slaughter and death multiply—
corpses fill great ravines,
blood pools at long walls.
Highborn and lowly alike
become mere bones.
The living are fewer than the dead,
the dead outnumber the living.

Thus, villages fall silent,
fields lie abandoned,
and thorns take root.

The **killing qi** severely harms **Great Harmony**,
the **perverse winds** provoke **Heaven’s** wrath.
Consequently, **yin** and **yang** fail to blend,
rain and sun come untimely—
drought and flood alternate,
disasters strike in succession,
famine follows hard upon hunger,
and the people face ruin before nightfall.

The ancients said:
*"After a great army, famine must follow—*
*inevitable fate."*

Yet when hunger and cold press hard,
banditry grows daily;
when years bring scarcity,
rebellions erupt—
sometimes war is unavoidable.
The ancients said:
*"Warfare values swiftness over hesitation,"*
so the skilled warrior acts decisively.

A **ren**-**yi** army arrives like timely rain,
rescuing those suspended in peril,
greeted with **hújiāng** [milk and wine].
As when **King Wu** in single campaign
pacified the realm—
not for personal glory,
nor to crush the enemy’s leader alone.
How dare one indulge in slaughter for a moment,
or seize strength for oneself!

If victorious, do not boast—
boasting breeds love of war;
do not self-praise—
self-praise harbors hidden martial ambition.
Even if subduing tyrants as easily as turning a palm,
elevating the people to prosperity,
this is but the common duty of generals—
how could it be cause for arrogance?

For arrogance breeds killing.
Even if boldness leads to chaos,
saving the people from fire and water,
if three treacherous intentions lurk within—
though no blood is spilled,
the seed of slaughter is already sown.
This is **not the Way**.

Know this: war is a last resort for sages,
yet even without losing a single soldier,
without breaking a single general,
without harming a single civilian,
without damaging a single thing,
it cannot claim to restore **peace’s** refined order.
What then of those who are not sages?
Act decisively, but without coercion.

The *Odes* say:
*"Exhort the lord not to seek noble titles—*
*one man’s triumph means ten thousand bones lie dry."*
Reflect on this: war is perilous,
battle is a deadly omen—
**not the Way** of nurturing life.

What of prolonged conflict,
dragging on for years,
exhausting troops and treasury,
alienating subjects and provoking resentment?
If it can end yet does not,
how can it serve the state?

Or when armies face off in stalemate,
year after year, supplies dwindling—
a single moment’s negligence,
and the enemy seizes the pass,
cutting off grain and provisions—
soldiers flee at the first rumor,
troops scatter at the first sign,
once-mighty forces reduced to ashes.
What wonder is there in this?

For it is true: when things reach their peak, they grow old—
this is the inevitable course.

War is used to punish the un-**Dao** with the **Dao**.
Yet if one delights in war and strife,
pretending to pacify the people while harming them,
claiming to suppress chaos while fostering it—
this is the ultimate **not the Way**.

To punish the un-**Dao** while acting un-**Dao** oneself—
how much better to cultivate fields, repel bandits,
rest armies, and foster neighborly peace,
acting early rather than late.

Take **Yu’s** conquest of the **Three Miao**:
the Miao people resisted stubbornly,
yet **Yu** did not rely on arms but on **virtue**—
dancing the **dry-羽** at the two stages,
and in seventy years, the Miao submitted.
This is **威** without coercion,
**武** without force—
far surpassing brute power.
Do rulers know this?

This speaks of **fire-alchemy**,
**symbolic correspondence**,
**gathering** and **refining**—
all deliberate action.
Yet compared to waging war and conquering enemies,
it is a far graver affair,
not to be taken lightly.

As **Zengzi** tempered himself in battle,
**Zisi** warned of caution in war—
only then can one transform **qi** and essence,
restoring the original **pre-Heaven** form.

Like the unbroken maiden’s purity,
untouched and unharmed,
one may act in due season,
follow the **Way** without bias,
encounter no peril or danger,
and reach the realm of **spiritual transformation**.

But for the broken and the aged,
when **post-Heaven** lead and mercury near exhaustion—
where does life depend?
One must resort to **enumerating bamboo to summon tortoises**,
**playing the zither to call phoenixes**—
only then do **jade sprouts** and **spiritual herbs** appear,
**pills and elixirs** ready for gathering and refining,
transmuting the mortal form into nothingness,
forming the **sage-embryo** at the **Linguan** [spiritual pass].

Yet the **fire-alchemy** requires utmost precision—
without a true master’s oral transmission,
one cannot penetrate its subtleties.
Even if the secrets of **Heaven’s** mechanisms are obtained,
if inner **virtue** or outer practice falls short,
**Heaven and the numinous** will not aid.

Only with a **empty heart** seeking the **Way**,
accumulating **virtue** and cultivating **power**,
without guilt in any deed,
with **ren** in every moment,
humble, yielding, patient, and gentle—
**spirit** abiding in **qi**,
**qi** clinging to **spirit**,
unbroken continuity,
rooted in the deepest foundation—
never forgetting at the right time,
never abandoning in distraction,
always mindful without ceasing.

Thus it is said: *"He who aids the sovereign with the Dao does not rely on arms to subdue the world."* Even if one attains perfect refinement, yet after the fire-alchemical tempering, when all coarse *qi* has been purged and true *qi* has yet to accumulate, one’s state will be indeterminate—neither fully present nor fully absent, subtle and hard to discern. Moreover, there are those who, without refining, see their *qi* scatter, and those whose *qi* scatters the more they refine—all due to the mind’s fluctuations, like creeping vines that resist eradication. Hence it is said: *"Where the teacher treads, brambles spring up."* How much more so when the divine fire is kindled, and the yin-*qi* cannot be retained, while long-standing ailments and childhood infirmities are all driven forth—some exiting through sweat and filth, others manifesting as sores and toxins, in varied forms.

The cultivator must not be alarmed by these as illness; only a tranquil mind can transmute *qi*. It is clear that the Way of self-refinement must transform the common body into a jade-like one, the turbid form into a golden one. Never be startled—for alarm stirs the posterior-heaven’s vulgar fire, gravely injuring the primal *qi*.

Thus it is said: *"After a great campaign, there will surely be years of hardship."* The skilled warrior values decisiveness; the adept alchemist values divine swiftness. Hence it is said: *"Decisive, and nothing more."* When the cultivator reaches this stage of pure yang transformation, he must not rely on his strength—assuming he can go without hunger or thirst, move like the wind, dispense with stove in winter or fan in summer, and wield extraordinary powers far beyond the common man, boasting of his might. Such self-conceit stirs the posterior-heaven’s vulgar fire anew; even if he avoids external censure, his inner elixir will surely falter. What need to speak of those who, after ten months of gestation and three years of nursing, complete every task with clockwork precision, then vaunt their achievements, arrogantly lording over others? Such behavior is far from the vessel of the Dao.

Even if one succeeds in deeds, one must also succeed in repaying debts. The peril of collapse is beyond reckoning!

Worse still, if one relies on his strength while failing to cultivate humility and sincerity, even if he practices virtue and concentrates on the Dao, he will be like a rigid tree—strong yet lifeless—unable to rival the supple, fragile yet vital plant. Day by day, year by year, as time passes, his vital essence will only wane. How could he then long enjoy the pleasures of mortal life?

Thus it is said: *"When things are at their peak, decay follows."* From this, it is clear that those who exalt themselves only bring themselves low, and those who boast only court danger. How un-Dao!

Better to abandon rigid strength, to be plain and unassuming, to accept fate with equanimity, letting heaven arrange all things—thus achieving strength without striving, the Dao without effort. Here lies the peril of the initial fire-alchemical practice; let the scholar beware.

---
**Chapter Thirty-One: Tranquility and Simplicity as Supreme**

Warfare is an ominous instrument; men detest it, and thus those who follow the Dao do not dwell upon it. The gentleman, in peace, values the left; in war, the right. Warfare is no instrument of the gentleman—it is used only when unavoidable, and even then, tranquility is paramount. To rejoice in victory is to delight in killing, and he who delights in killing cannot prevail in the world.

Thus, auspicious rites favor the left, while inauspicious ones favor the right. Hence, the left is reserved for the vice-general, the right for the supreme commander, both treated with funeral rites. Many lives lost demand lamentation; victory, too, is met with funeral rites. The sage governs the world with virtue and morality as supreme, law and teaching secondary. Only when absolutely necessary does he raise armies, prepare arms, and send his eldest sons to war, his younger to bear the dead. To do so is to invite calamity—*the Book of Changes* warns deeply against it.

How much worse for those who indulge in the ferocity of the tiger’s gaze, the eagle’s boldness, who flaunt arms on the battlefield and wield might in the barracks, who see conquest as gain and war as skill! Such warfare is not merely chaotic—it is the very epitome of disorder. For the finest weapon is not the one that kills, but the one that ceases killing. The ancients called halting the blade *wu* (martial virtue); these men call exhaustion of arms their prowess—yet such is no instrument for the ruler.

What, then, is the ruler’s true instrument? The Dao. Virtue. The love of life and hatred of slaughter. To speak of arms is to invite killing.

When the clamor of slaughter resounds, when rivers and mountains tremble, even chickens and dogs are unsettled—what horror is there beyond? How much more so for worms that cling to life, ants that fear death? Even the most insignificant creatures recoil from it—what then of men? Thus, those who follow the Dao avoid it entirely.

All things value yang over yin—the left, representing yang, is the breath of life; the right, representing yin, is the seed of slaughter. Therefore, the gentleman, in ordinary times, honors the left. Only in war does he abandon the left for the right, revealing how deeply he despises arms.

Even if one must remove tyrants, punish the wicked, and rescue the people—raising righteous banners and forming disciplined ranks—warfare remains an ominous instrument, one even the sage avoids. In times of peace, a nation needs no martial preparations. When borders cry out in distress and rebellion flares, and only then must arms be raised, they must be wielded with precision, rewards and punishments meted out with rigor. Where the army marches, the people offer water and grain; where the soldiers pass, the fields remain undisturbed. This is the way of subduing both the yielding and the rigid—through awe and virtue—clearly demonstrated.

Let there be no display of martial might, no boasting of strategic brilliance. Only tranquility and non-action, ease and naturalness.

「Though dwelling in lands of tumultuous warfare, he maintains an orderly and tranquil demeanor among his troops.

With this, how could any city not be taken? With this, how could any enemy not be crushed? Victory is assured.

Yet still, such victories are but commonplace in the art of war—how can one claim glory like inscribed tripods, or record exploits in banners and armor? Would one thus set this as a model for future generations?

If this were held as a model, it would inevitably mean enduring the suffering of countless commoners to gain personal fame and fortune. Without the virtue of a life-giving being, yet harboring the intent to kill, how can this be justified?

He who delights in killing, his heart is cruel, his methods harsh and unyielding. Unable to embrace broad tolerance, he is ever petty and self-righteous.

Such a person cannot aspire to dominion over the world. If he were to achieve dominion, the masses would have no survivors.

In ancient times, auspicious rites were held on the left, and inauspicious on the right. A subordinate general, being second in rank, should reside on the left, yet he is placed on the right; a supreme general, being highest in rank, should reside on the right, yet he is placed on the left.

This is but the knowledge that he who holds absolute authority over slaughter and conquest is the supreme general. Subordinate generals must obey his commands and may not flaunt their own martial might—this is akin to treating funeral rites as military rites.

How much more fitting is it to engage in a solemn exchange of state letters, and to command soldiers to sing dirges for the deceased?

This is the utmost of caution and restraint.

And what of the saying, *“He who kills many should weep with sorrow”*? It reveals that warfare is a grievous matter, one that wounds the natural compassion for all life, and arises only from dire necessity!

Thus, those who visit ancient battlefields see corpses filling the city walls and moats, blood pooling in the trenches and ravines, as if the heavens and earth themselves weep in sorrow, and the grasses and trees sigh in grief—what then of the people?

Even if victorious in battle, with enemy chieftains subdued and the masses at peace, upon reflection, one sees that half the innocent children beneath the heavens are made to wander as refugees, and half perish. The heart is filled with sorrow!

How dare one return in triumph, boast of victories, and praise one’s own prowess?

To harbor such thoughts without a shred of virtue, yet display only remorse, is to treat oneself as one already in mourning.

Mencius said:
*“I am skilled in forming ranks, I am skilled in waging war—this is a great crime!
Only when a sovereign loves virtue does the world stand without rival.”*
And again:
*“To awe the world is not done by the might of arms.”*

This clearly demonstrates that martial prowess without slaughter, and the virtuous without enemies, are the true models for an age of peace. Yet the use of arms is not the usual way of the sage, nor something the worthy ruler esteems.

This analogy is like using fire at the furnace—an act of necessity for the aged and infirm, to revive and mend what is broken, not to be done lightly. How can one claim singular merit or boast of extraordinary skill?

Those who pursue the false paths of leftist Dao, such as kindling flames to retreat from calamity, gathering herbs to forge elixirs, or all manner of contrived actions, mistakenly believe these to be the way of the immortals. They are far astray!

Yet the vigor of youth requires no such labor—one may attain the elixir effortlessly. But the aged body, long steeped in physical decay and worldly desires, without the fierce refining and urgent tempering of Daoic practice, will find that its vital essence remains untransformed, and its desires unvanquished.

With a defiled and polluted body, how can one practice the Dao of non-action and naturalness?

This is like trying to welcome honored guests in a filthy dwelling—impossible!

One must first purify the impurities within oneself, and only then will form and matter dissolve, the root of attachment be eradicated, and the original true nature manifest freely within.

Although one may forcefully cultivate, it must still be done with ease and naturalness, lest the post-heavenly fire of effort harm one’s innate vitality.

Thus, the highest ideal is to embrace tranquility and non-action, to achieve victory without glory.

Otherwise, one has only post-heavenly cultivation without pre-heavenly foundation, mere common qi without true essence. A single breath of fiery passion—how long can it endure?

Hence it is said: *“Those who take pride in this are those who delight in killing.”*
To delight in killing is to nurture a heart brimming with slaughter—how can such a one be the master of the world and receive its blessings?

It is certain that he cannot aspire to dominion over the world.

Therefore, those who cultivate the Dao, though they employ action as their practice, must also possess a heart of benevolence and compassion, one of humility and gentleness. Only then will the post-heavenly align with the pre-heavenly.

The ancients understood the fire’s tempering without reliance on divination, and the measure of medicines without weighing scales. They moved in harmony with the heavens and acted in accordance with their true nature.

Though they engaged in action, they did no harm.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Two: Knowing the Stopping Point Brings No Peril**

The Dao/Way is ever nameless.
Though crude and simple, the world dares not submit to it.
If lords and kings could but guard it,
All things would willingly come as guests.

Heaven and earth unite in embrace,
And sweet dew descends—
No one commands it, yet it is evenly distributed.

When first the Dao/Way is cultivated with form,
And form is given a name,
One must know when to stop—
For knowing the stopping point brings no peril.

The Dao/Way in the world is like streams and valleys to the great rivers and seas.
The Dao/Way itself is boundless and formless,
Yet truly all phenomena stand in orderly array,
No person lacks it, no thing is without it.

Before humans took form,
This essence was their foundation.
After humans were born,
This essence became their root.

Though it be most subtle and hidden,
One must never for a moment be parted from it—
For separation means all affairs and things collapse into ruin.

Hence it is said: *“The Dao/Way is ever nameless.”*
Yet for the sake of those who study it,
One must give it a name.

A single grain of millet, the yang spirit three inches long,
Dwelling in the mysterious palace of self-sojourning,
Permeating the dharma realm,
Is like the pristine and complete *Taipu* (太朴).

Though its form be small,
Its function is vast.

All affairs and things in the world
Rely upon this as their sovereign—
Who dares to submit to it afterward?

Even as lords and kings wield the authority of life and death,
And stand as masters of the myriad people,
Who dares not obey their commands?

The man who attains this *Taipu* (太朴),
With unwavering reverence and devotion,
Preserves it without ever losing it—
Even from distant lands and foreign shores,
They come by sea and mountain paths,
How much more so those nearby?

Thus it is seen that all nations submit as guests,
All due to this *Taipu*’s power to safeguard.

Yet after a man is born,
His **qi** and innate nature constrain him,
And desires for things blind him—
Few indeed are those who retain the Dao/Way.

If one wishes to embrace *Taipu* and remain whole and pure,
Only by imitating Heaven and Earth can this be done.

Heaven’s **qi** descends, Earth’s **qi** ascends—
Like man’s yin-yang (*kān-lí* 坎離) union,
Water and fire in harmonious balance.

When Heaven and Earth unite,
Sweet dew descends like pearls,
Naturally falling into the *Zhonggong* (中宮)—
This is the interplay of yin and yang,
The sun and moon sharing the same palace—
Who makes them do so?
Who commands their harmony?

All is due to the Dao/Way as their sovereign.

But what name does the Dao/Way truly have?
Some call it *"Zhenqian"* (真鉛),
Others *"Jindan"* (金丹).

The ancients coined these names
Only for the benefit of later practitioners.

Once practitioners know its name,
They must seek its true essence.

From the very moment yin and yang unite,
A single point descends into the Yellow Court—
One must then remain steadfast in its place, unmoving in its dwelling,
Only then does the Great Dao endure.

Once one knows where to halt,
The central sovereign abides unshaken—
Why then should one suffer birth and death,
Or fall prey to danger and peril?

For the Dao, when dispersed abroad,
Is boundless as the vast heavens,
Mysterious as the primordial chaos.
Yet when gathered within,
It is indistinct, formless—
Retreating, yet profound in its concealment.

If scholars fail to grasp its fundamental principle,
They cannot find their true resting place.
Then they scatter without order,
Unable even to establish themselves—
How could they ever hope to transcend others?

Thus it is said:
*"The Dao in the world is like rivers and valleys to the sea."*

Only by returning to its source
Does one achieve its fullness.

As Master Zisi taught:
*"The Way of the virtuous is subtle yet profound—*
*This is the single root from which myriad branches arise,*
*And all branches return to that single root."*

In this chapter, the sweet dew is born
From the union of lead and mercury,
And *"knowing where to halt"* is the gathering of divine qi in the Yellow Court.

The *Taishang* speaks of this in general terms;
Now I shall elaborate the Dao for the seeker:

Those who wish to cultivate life and nature
Must first clarify lead and mercury.

The ancients said:
*"Mercury is the original substance of my family,*
*Lead is the immortal elixir of another house."*

If one speaks only of mind and nature,
There is no way to grasp it—
Thus the ancient immortals named it *mercury*.

This mercury is not external—
It is the soul’s essence, the vital fluid within:
From tears, saliva, semen, blood, and the refined qi of the body,
The turbid residues of post-heaven transformation,
Illuminated by divine fire for long ages,
Until it becomes the most refined essence.

This essence is the vessel of original nature—
Pure, untainted, equal to the boundless void.
If one relies solely on the post-heaven physical essence,
And stirs it with martial or civil fire,
Without divine illumination,
The essence will not transform,
And the soul’s nature will have no foundation.

Thus, those who refine the elixir
Must first refine essence into qi—
As the saying goes:
*"This essence is not the common man’s—*
*It is the Jade Emperor’s saliva from his mouth."*

The Jade Emperor is but a metaphor for the heart;
The heart’s essence is that saliva.

Once essence generates mercury,
The essence descends into the *kān* (坎) palace,
While true yang ascends,
Their union in the Yellow Court’s inner cauldron—
I gather divine qi here,
And over time, true lead emerges,
Burgeoning like silk threads—
This is what is called *"obtaining the medicine."*

Yet this essence is true water,
True yang is true qi,
And true qi is lead.
Mercury is essence, lead is the common substance—
Both are post-heaven, tangible lead and mercury,
Useful only for procreation,
Not for the elixir of immortality.

Only when mercury descends
And lead ascends,
Meeting in the central palace,
With concentrated spirit and regulated breath,
Does the vapor rise—
Like mist, like smoke,
Like tides, like the sea—
Only then is it true lead,
The foundation for elixir refinement—
The union of *kān* and *lí* yielding the medicine.

Then, stirring yang fire and yin talisman,
Ascending from the tailbone to the brain’s marrow.
The brain’s marrow, long storing yin essence,
Combines with this true lead-qi,
Forming a unity—
This is *"heaven and earth unite to form the elixir."*

When yang ascends to the brain’s marrow,
What does one perceive?
A clarity of mind and senses,
No longer the usual daze—
Like wind dispersing clouds,
The sky clears, the air is pure—
A new and wondrous state—
Only then is it true mercury.
The earlier mercury was still common mercury,
Unfit to nourish the immortal embryo.

When lead and mercury merge in the brain’s marrow,
The coarse essence and qi of the body
Combine and transform—
No longer lead, nor mercury,
But a cool, serene, and tranquil taste,
Sweet as dew, fragrant as nectar,
Not the crude, turbid essence of before—
This is what the ancients called *"monk’s milk poured upon the crown."*

It descends from the upper palate,
Swallowed and nurtured in the Yellow Court—
Sealed and perfected.

When this true essence is born,
The body softens like silk,
Neither fully asleep nor awake;
The restless mind and body now still,
Unshaken, at peace—
Firm in its place, secure in its dwelling—
What peril could there be?

This state requires supreme stillness and clarity.
As for the method of gathering,
It is achieved by single-minded concentration,
Without the slightest distraction—
As the ancients said:
*"The unforced gathering surpasses forced gathering."*

The practitioner advances step by step,
Each step bearing its own verification.
If there is no genuine foundation,
The work remains incomplete.
When the celestial mechanism is fully revealed,
One must personally verify it.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Three: Knowing Others and Knowing Oneself**

To know others is wisdom,
To know oneself is enlightenment.
To overcome others is strength,
To overcome oneself is power.

Contentment brings wealth,
Diligence brings resolve.
To remain steadfast is endurance,
To die yet not perish is longevity.

The Way of self-cultivation
Is none other than nature and fate.

If one wishes to fulfill nature and establish fate,
One must first preserve the heart and nurture nature,
Safeguard life and complete the form.
Through cultivation, refinement, accumulation, and repetition,
Original nature expands,
And fate rests within oneself.

Yet to fulfill the heart,
One must first know nature—
To grasp the essence of life,
Pure as heaven’s principle,
Unmixed with human desires—
This is true wisdom.

From this, one restrains desires and preserves principle,
Ever-mindful, ever-vigilant,
Ensuring private desires are utterly purged,
While heaven’s principle flows freely—
Piercing the original face,
Awake and unobstructed,
Ever-clear and luminous—
This is the perfect enlightenment.

This is not mere imagination—
It is self-knowing, beyond others’ comprehension.

Thus it is said:
*"To know others is wisdom,*
*To know oneself is enlightenment."*

If one seeks to establish one’s fate,
First, one must refine oneself.
Refinement of the self has two aspects:
One is **material desire**—
If material desire is not purged,
Heaven’s true nature cannot manifest.
To abandon this and yet seek elixirs to forge the *dan*,
Is like fine grain mixed with weeds and millet—
Unless the weeds are first uprooted,
Both good and bad will grow together.
Unless one first overcomes human desires,
Maintaining steadfastness and vigilance,
There will be resolve, but not necessarily power.
Thus it is said:
*"To conquer others is to have strength."*

The second is **constitutional qi**—
If one’s qi and nature are not transformed,
How can the body be made firm?
Thus, one strips away the skin to preserve the fluids,
Strips away the fluids to preserve the spirit,
Strips away the spirit to return to emptiness—
Layer by layer, stripped bare,
Only then can one align with the Dao and attain truth.
If not for a strong constitution and robust qi,
How could one overcome the six evils and subdue the myriad yin forces,
Making the body within and without as pure as jade,
Transforming the mortal form into an immortal body?
Thus it is said:
*"To conquer oneself is to be strong."*

When refinement of the self is complete,
One’s fate is established—
Is the work not fulfilled here?
Is the Dao not achieved here?

Yet, kindling fire has its time,
Extinguishing fire has its moment—
If, when the fire is at its peak,
One does not practice extinguishing it,
The essence will follow the movement of the qi and dissipate.
Thus, stopping to nourish the *dan* is like a poor man accumulating wealth—
One always feels abundant.
Having mastered extinguishing the fire,
One must further advance the fire to nourish the *dan*,
Retreat the fire to warm the *dan*.
Unless one is resolute,
One cannot maintain an unbroken, seamless breath like this.

The *Yijing* says:
*"Heaven’s movement is vigorous—
The noble one strengthens themselves without ceasing."*

Is this not the resolve of the strong and determined?

After this stage of tempering and nourishment,
One need only rest the mind and spirit,
Letting nature take its course,
Never losing one’s footing for a single moment.

As Master Zisi said:
*"The utmost sincerity knows no pause—
Without pause, it endures."*

Yet when the common body is transformed and the true spirit ascends,
It is like the death of an ordinary man.
For the death of an ordinary man means the scattering of the spirit;
But the death of the Sage means the spirit remains whole—
Though the form dies, the spirit lives on.
How could it not share in the longevity of Heaven and Earth?

This speaks of knowing the human path,
Conquering human desires—
Still, it is but the pursuit of principle and the fulfillment of nature.

Only when nature is seen and the mind is illuminated,
Piercing through to the original source,
With spirit strong and qi robust,
And all impurities purged,
Can one truly fulfill nature and establish fate.

Nature and fate are not two separate paths—
They return to the undifferentiated chaos before creation,
Where the yin-spirit is utterly extinguished
And the yang-spirit is perfected.

Even in the stages of refining essence into qi,
And refining qi into spirit,
There remains the practice of extinguishing fire to nourish the *dan*.
The *Wuzhen* says:
*"If one clings to fullness without end,
They risk sudden disgrace and ruin."*
This is what is meant.

Refining essence into qi is the beginning of forming the embryo;
Refining qi into spirit is the completion of forming the embryo.
If one does not know how to extinguish the fire,
The qi will not enter the embryo.
Though essence is refined into qi,
It may still revert to essence through the movement of qi.
And if one does not know how to extinguish the fire,
The spirit will not condense in the void—
Though qi is refined into spirit,
It may still revert to qi through the movement of the spirit.

Thus it is said:
*"Knowing sufficiency is always sufficiency—
A life free from disgrace."*
The words of the Supreme Sage are no deception.

When the spirit returns to Great Stillness,
The qi follows into Great Stillness as well.
A hundred years pass like a single day.
A wandering thought means the *dan* is lost.
Such a heavy burden and distant path—
Only the strong and determined
Can maintain their resolve
And endure without weariness over time.

With the *sanmei* fire perfected,
Ascending to the heights above,
Though dead, they live on—
Their spirit shares in the longevity of Heaven and Earth.
The entire process of the Golden Elixir is fulfilled here.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Four: The Unassuming Sage**

The Dao is vast and boundless,
Yet cannot be confined to left or right.
All things rely on it to live,
Yet it does not claim ownership of its deeds.
It clothes all things without ruling them.
To always lack desire is to be called "small."

All things return to it without it claiming mastery—
This is to be called "great."
Thus, the Sage never considers themselves great,
And so they achieve true greatness.

The Dao is a boundless, unfathomable depth.
Its vastness knows no shore.
The *Shijing* says:
*"Left, left—this is the Lord’s proper way;
Right, right—this is what the noble one possesses."*
From this, one sees how the Dao adapts to each moment,
Fulfilling all without fail,
Nourishing all without measure.

What is this Dao?
It is the origin of all things’ generation.
The Dao exists in Heaven and Earth,
And all things draw from it to live,
Yet it does not reject their chaos.
Because the Dao lacks nothing,
All things flourish without obstruction.

Though the path of nurturing life is sufficient,
And things rely on it to complete their being,
It is as if they generate and fulfill themselves—
The Dao does not appear to act,
Yet how could its achievements be otherwise?

Though it is not named as having form,
Yet Heaven and Earth are vast,
And the four seas stretch far—
No person escapes its embrace,
No thing avoids its coverage.
Let all things generate and nurture themselves,
Yet the Dao seems unaware of birth or growth.
It universally protects all,
Encompassing everything—
Truly, *"it clothes all things without claiming to give life."*

When the Dao’s work is complete,
Its grandeur is boundless and nameless.
To always lack desire is to always be pure and clear—
The eternal Dao.

When viewed as small, it is named so,
Yet not even a blade of grass or tree is excluded;
It encompasses all things without flaw,
A seamless unity where all dust is transformed.
This is the meaning of *"small beyond breaking."*

Thus it is said:
*"To always lack desire is to be called 'small.'"*

When viewed as great, it spreads across Heaven and Earth,
Nurturing all life;
Spanning ancient and present,
Containing all things—
Yet nothing is excluded, no one left out.
This is the meaning of *"great beyond bearing."*

Thus it is said:
*"All things return to it without it claiming mastery—
This is to be called 'great.'"*

How profound and hidden is the Sage’s Dao!
The Sage aligns with the True Dao—
At rest, they guard the One and embrace the Center,
Merging with the tranquil Heaven.
In action, they follow things and adapt to circumstances,
Embodying the forms of timely conduct.

Only Heaven is great,
And only the Sage follows its example.
The Sage’s greatness is truly equal to Heaven’s.
Yet the Sage never considers themselves great—
Only by not considering themselves great
Can they achieve true greatness.
This is why they are called the Great Sage.

This speaks of the Dao’s vastness—
Generating all things with abundance,
Clothing all things with sufficiency.
Whether small or great, all is contained within.
The Sage achieves greatness
Because their cultivation has true roots.

Now, the specific methods are detailed:
When seated in meditation,
First, concentrate the spirit,
Then regulate the breath.
When the spirit is fully concentrated—
There is no need for浩然正气 of utmost vigor and rigidity,
Filling Heaven and Earth;
Only that the mind is free of agitation,
The will untethered,
Feeling the heart as an empty vessel,
Not possessing even a thought.
The mind, like ice melting,
Generates no trace of awareness—
The body stands firm,
As steadfast as a mountain,
Unshaken, unmoved.
Thus, the spiritual light descends to illuminate the *qixue*,
Silently observing as the yin-*zhiqi* and the *gonggong* qi converge
Within the *danting*.

With gentle warmth, I refine the spirit-fire,
Slowly simmering the elixir,
As a faint zephyr softly breathes,
Gently fanning the flames—
Naturally, the essence melts and the qi transforms.
This is the refining of essence into qi.

How do we know it is the refining of essence into qi?
Before this, the external qi was not gathered,
And my inner spirit, unpaired,
Existed separately—
The *kan* and *li* energies remained distinct,
Unconnected, their breaths uncoordinated.
When the external qi is drawn inward
To govern the inner yin-essence,
The essence transforms at the qi’s arrival.
The upper *xīn* and lower *shèn* energies merge as one,
And the *gōnggōng* chamber rests in ease,
The *shènfǔ* in freedom.
The external breath and the inner true breath
Merge into a single qi,
Like husband and wife in union—
Gathered yet never dispersing.
Day fills, month swells,
And the true yang now manifests in form.
This is the clear sign of qi transformation.

Once the qi is refined,
The work ascends further.
What is meant by ascending?
At this stage, breath and spirit merge,
Qi and essence intertwine,
Congealing in the *dāntián*,
Twisting and flowing like a living dragon swimming—
Endless transformations in a single day.
I only concentrate the spirit within,
Direct the breath without,
And let it unfold naturally—
Naturally, stillness gives way to motion,
Motion returns to stillness.
This is the refining of qi into spirit.

When stillness endures long,
My qi grows harmonized,
And what once swirled in the *dāntián*
Is now fully refined—
Seemingly present, yet absent,
Moving yet still.
The essence is imperceptible,
But upon close examination, it is known.
At this moment, all knowing and awareness must be extinguished,
All thoughts erased, all emptiness dissolved—
Even the converging spirit-qi of the *dāntián*
Is left to stir and adjust itself,
To warm and temper on its own.
I only maintain emptiness and quietude,
Purely yielding to nature—
The spirit enters the qi unknowingly,
The qi envelops the spirit unperceived.
After such a cycle of refining,
A fragrant breeze naturally arises,
Rising through the hundred channels,
Perfuming the entire body.
This is called *shēngshén*—spirit generating qi.

Again, the spirit grows ever refined,
Wisdom ever expanding.
Within the single mind,
Only a breath arises from the *guī* channel—
Pure, subtle, and luminous as jade.
This is called *qìshēngshén*—qi generating spirit.

Thus, spirit and qi nourish each other,
Mutually engendering.
Now is the time to let go completely—
Neither clinging nor detaching,
As if with intent, yet without purpose.
This is the true practice of refining spirit back to emptiness.

These three stages of practice—
Refining essence, qi, and spirit—
Can be achieved simultaneously.
The practitioner must follow the Dao,
Not stopping at mere crude merging of spirit and qi,
Before attaining great stillness,
Nor abandoning the seat when spirit and qi blend into indistinction,
Before reaching the realm of effortless, pure, and natural ease.
One must persist in this path,
From refining essence onward,
Until qi grows long and spirit thrives,
Transforming into pure, natural clarity.
Then, applying the *guīlú* method,
Only then does one align with Heaven and Earth’s filling and emptying,
With the seasons’ spring, summer, autumn, and winter.
Thus, the practice is complete in a full cycle.

When practiced thus,
My qi grows ever harmonized,
My spirit ever still,
Within me, infinite transformations,
Endless vitality.
Day and night, the work continues—
Uninterrupted, meticulous—
Yin and yang in perfect balance,
True qi naturally perfuming,
Ascending to the *níwán*,
Descending to the *dānfǔ*,
Penetrating the hundred channels,
Piercing the flesh—
A surging force that cannot be restrained.
This is the path of the *Héchē*—
Naturally open, requiring only slight guidance.
Not like the false paths of others,
Where one forces their own empty intentions,
Deadly and rigid in their movements,
Ignoring the natural rise and fall of stillness and motion.

With time, the elixir forms, the Dao is realized—
Walking through mist, flying through air,
Consorting with Heaven.
The Sage achieves greatness—
Truly not a light matter.

---
**Chapter 35: Going Without Harm**

Hold the Great Symbol,
And all under Heaven will come.
Go without harm,
And peace and ease prevail.
Music and bait may halt the passing traveler,
But the Dao, once spoken,
Is bland, without taste.
It cannot be seen,
Cannot be heard,
Cannot be exhausted in use.
What is this Great Symbol?
It is the Dao that generates Heaven, Earth, humans, and all things.
Because it encompasses all,
It is called the Great Symbol.
What symbol is this?
It is the boundless, the limitless.
Yet without a symbol, what can be held?
And without holding, what is there to grasp?
This does not contradict the Dao.

If one can always hold it, always preserve it,
Without forgetting or forcing,
Then the Great Symbol is held,
And the Great Dao is present.

Confucius once told Yan Yuan:
*"One day of self-discipline and returning to ritual,
And all under Heaven will return to virtue."*
Thus, the Dao’s fulfillment
Is the fulfillment of all under Heaven.
Whether returning to the Dao or to virtue,
All is done with sincere and willing hearts—
What harm could there be?

I know that one qi flows continuously,
And all things transform in silence.
Flooding and draining,
Above and below in perfect harmony
Within the vast movement of Heaven.
This is the natural order,
Not something achieved in an instant.
If one merely adorns the senses for a moment,
It will still flow ceaselessly.
But like refined music that lingers in the heart,
Or bait that delights the palate—
Such things may make the traveler pause,
Linger, and forget their journey,
Yet they can only be enjoyed temporarily,
Not sustained.

Only the Dao is without taste—
Not rich, not sweet, not savory—
Yet it does not leave the palate unsatisfied,
Nor the heart unpleased.
This is the supreme flavor within the tasteless,
Beyond the world’s indulgent flavors.

---

Although the Dao has no fixed place nor form,
It is difficult to imagine, difficult to grasp.
Thus it is said:
*"See it not, hear it not—*
Yet take from it without end,
Use it without depletion,
Such is its nature.

It truly encompasses Heaven and Earth without excess,
Shapes all things without omission—
This is why the Dao is called great.

Let scholars know what to aspire to!

This speaks of how mortals must emulate the interplay of Heaven and Earth,
Only then will the ebb and flow be harmonized,
As when refined music stirs the soul,
Or fragrant bait delights the senses—
Such wonders evoke sighs of admiration.

Yet its realm is not easily attained.

If one’s passions are fierce and vitality wanes,
Without first descending from the extreme of motion,
Gradually reaching the realm of stillness,
Then the mortal mind does not die,
Nor does the Dao-mind arise.
The common breath never ceases,
While the true breath is unseen.

Only at the juncture where motion reaches its peak and stillness emerges,
Must one not intervene with true intent to govern it.
This intent is yin—
It belongs to the self as *jǐ tǔ* [1].
Soon, it becomes vague and indistinct,
As yin and yang mingle in embrace,
Entering the boundless void—
Neither dream nor waking,
Neither sleep nor awareness.
Within this settled stillness,
A sudden thread of warmth arises,
Mingling and continuous,
Qi flowing freely, spirit harmonized,
The two meeting faintly in the *Huángfáng* [2]—
On the verge of separation yet already parting,
Parting yet suddenly unified.
This is the manifestation of *true lead* [3].

The heart’s blossoms burst forth,
Warmth permeates the body,
The primordial spirit leaps—
Not forced by sensation,
But naturally arising.
Thus the signs of the Mysterious Pass appear,
And the foundation of the Great Polarity is laid.

At this moment, only a single point of true heart,
Emits true intent to gather and contain it.
This intent is yang—
It belongs to the self as *wù tǔ* [4].
In essence, it is one intent,
Yet merely the division of motion and stillness
Into the two *tǔ* [5] of *wù* and *jǐ*.

For when the Mysterious Mother remains unopened,
Within the chaos of the undifferentiated,
This true intent, the master of desirelessness,
Is called *yin tǔ* [6].
But once the Mysterious Mother opens
And the true mechanism appears,
Then one observes desire
And penetrates its depths—
Called *yang tǔ* [7].

One is the nameless beginning of Heaven and Earth,
One is the named mother of all things.
To generate Heaven, Earth, man, and all beings,
It is this single point of true intent
That flows through them all.

Those who practice must wield this true intent to govern their transformations,
Or else they will be swayed by sensation and stray in thought,
Or driven by movement and lose their focus.

*"The Celestial Pass is controlled by me,
The Earthly Axis by my heart;
The cosmos resides within the body,
All transformations spring from the mind—*
All are directed by this moment’s luminous awareness,
Which governs and sustains them.

Thus it is said:
*If one breath is too early,*
Then the true mechanism has not yet emerged—
Harvesting it yields no benefit.
*If one breath is too late,*
Then common thoughts have already arisen—
Harvesting it brings only confusion,
Unfit as medicine for refining the vital essence.

This requires great wisdom and great strength,
Only then can one discern it clearly
And grasp it firmly
As the foundation for attaining transcendence and sainthood.

Though this Mysterious Pass begins with gentle, delicate qi,
Only a faint warmth felt rising from the lower *yuán* [8],
Over time it surges through the body,
As if unstoppable in its momentum.
The student must recognize it at its subtlest stage,
Wielding true intent to guide it—
Not the slightest dispersion allowed.
With time, the qi-mechanism grows mighty,
Unrestrained yet steady—
Following its course without interference,
Neither clinging to nor resisting its flow—
Thus one attains it.

When the qi-mechanism is robust and flourishing,
Stillness alone summons the Celestial Mechanism’s movement,
Swift as thunder—
Even in the midst of all clamor,
It cannot be restrained.
One must have a heart of luminous awareness
To govern it,
Or else there will be no distinction.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Six: The State’s Sharpest Tools**
If one seeks to *conceal* them,
First, they must be *fully displayed*.
If one seeks to *weaken* them,
First, they must be *fully fortified*.
If one seeks to *abolish* them,
First, they must be *fully flourished*.
If one seeks to *seize* them,
First, they must be *fully granted*.
This is called *subtle clarity*.

Softness overcomes hardness,
Weakness overcomes strength.
Fish cannot be pulled from the depths.

The state’s sharpest tools
Must not be revealed to the people.

Heaven has its fullness and emptiness, its waxing and waning,
Man has his longevity and brevity, his poverty and abundance—
This too is the constant flow of *qi-numbers*.

Yet it applies only to the common mortal,
Not to the sage.
The sage possesses the power to *reverse* Heaven and Earth,
The virtue to *turn* the Great Polarity—
No more than overturning yin and yang,
And acting contrary to the workings of *造化* [9].

Just as autumn arrives,
All things withdraw inward,
Yet what is sought is concealed and hard to gather—
The sage then *fortifies* the movement of Heaven and Earth’s qi,
*Strengthens* the vitality of blood and breath.

When winter comes,
All things decay,
Yet what is sought is withered and hard to revive—
The sage then *reignites* the qi’s manifestations,
And *grants* the years as his own.

This is *both subtle and clear*,
*Both constant and extraordinary*—
Unfathomable to those who are not sages.

To shift from peril to safety,
From chaos to order,
Only the divine and courageous can attain this transformation.

Yet in truth, their method is but *softness* and *weakness*.
Only because of their softness can they overcome hardness,
Only because of their weakness can they overcome strength.

What is their means?
*"Man without essence perishes,
Fish without water dies—*
Once pulled from the depths,
The water dries and life’s mechanism vanishes.

Similarly, man’s *yuánzhēn* [10]—
His single essence—
Is but a fragile remnant.
Man and fish share the same reliance on water,
Yet it is not the dew of Heaven,
But the *Dao-creating* depths,
The water of the *One Primordial*.

It flows ceaselessly,
Frequently nourishing all things.

Zhuangzi once said:
*"They moisten each other with dew,
They breathe upon each other with mist—*
Yet it is better to *forget each other in the rivers and lakes*." [11]
Later generations strayed into false paths,
Taking the *shaped* and *substantial* essence
As the foundation for refining longevity—
They failed to grasp the Dao as *thing*,
*Strong yet moderate, pure yet complete*,
Arising from *vague indistinctness, boundless void, and natural emptiness*."

The secrets of alchemical fire and the refined essence of culinary arts are not imparted to the unworthy—only to the saintly master, the utterly sincere, or those who have rendered merit and virtue, who seek the Way with an empty heart and earnestly pursue a teacher. Few are those who can naturally align with the Dao’s favor.

For the mysteries of Heaven are profound and secret, the weight of Heaven and Earth is supreme, and even the spirits revere them. To recklessly transmit these truths to the unworthy invites calamity upon the Nine Ancestors. It is like the state’s most precious instruments—never to be revealed lightly. Thus, the virtuous man keeps them guarded and concealed. Do the scholars understand this?

This speaks of those who cultivate the Dao, truly wielding the cosmos in their grasp and nurturing all transformations in their hearts. Yet it is enough to observe the Way of Heaven, to hold fast to its operations, to follow it and take from it, to oppose it and apply it.

It is not a matter of harboring life’s vitality within the mechanism of destruction—what is called the overwhelming darkness of ultimate Yin, the austere brilliance of ultimate Yang. The overwhelming emerges from Heaven, the austere from Earth. To draw ultimate Yang from ultimate Yin—that is the source of opportunity.

If one can take the Yang from the black mountain’s cavern, or seize the treasure from the demon’s lair, then one has stolen life’s vitality from within the mechanism of destruction. Yet all this is drawn from the void of Heaven and Earth, seized from the emptiness and stillness of the human form—this is the true essence, not the common essence of the world.

If one can steal it without losing the proper moment, applying it with a single effort, there will be a corresponding advancement. I urge the scholar to cultivate with gentleness and weakness, to establish virtue and merit, so as to earn the favor of the divine and receive the transmitted incantations of the immortals. Otherwise, even the greatest matters—those that shake Heaven and Earth and move the spirits—will rarely be transmitted, for the laws of Heaven are most strict and cannot be defied.

Zhuangzi said:
*"If the Dao could be offered to others, then all would offer it to their lords.
If the Dao could be advanced to others, then all would advance it to their kin.
If the Dao could be given to others, then all would give it to their brothers.
If the Dao could be transmitted to others, then all would transmit it to their descendants.*
Yet none of these are possible—why? Because without inner virtue, the Dao cannot stand; without inner mastery, the Dao cannot move."

When we examine the words of the sages together, we see that the state’s most precious instruments must not be revealed lightly. Later generations of cultivators must not treat the Great Dao as common property, imparting it indiscriminately to all, lest they invite Heaven’s retribution and have no recourse for regret.

This is the meaning of *public yet not public, not public yet public*—a subtlety beyond the grasp of the unenlightened. Let them take heed.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Seven: The Dao’s Constant Non-Action**

The Dao constantly does nothing yet nothing remains undone.
If lords and kings could uphold it, all things would transform of their own accord.
When transformation stirs to act, I shall restrain it with the nameless Primordial Simplicity.
The nameless Primordial Simplicity—it too shall not desire.
Through non-desire, stillness prevails, and the world will settle of its own accord.

Though the Dao acts naturally without doing, to fixate on non-action alone becomes the doctrine of stubborn emptiness.
One must root oneself in non-action while employing action’s principles—
non-action within action, action yet still non-action—
thus the essence stands firm and the Dao moves,
the Dao is complete and virtue is fulfilled.

This is the constant responsiveness and constant stillness,
constant tranquility and constant alertness—
when released, it fills the six directions;
when withdrawn, it hides in the central void.
At this moment of boundless indistinction, there is the certainty of seated illumination without omission.
Though called non-action, action is contained within;
though called action, non-action encompasses it.
Thus the Great Dao resides within me, the Great Root ever abides.

Even the most exalted lords and kings—
if they lack this Dao as their foundation,
all things become divided and difficult to transform.
Only by holding fast to this Dao
can the people and all things harmonize in nature and sentiment,
in resonance and breath,
naturally transforming and subtly influencing one another,
until the age of Great Peace takes form.

Yet in times of enduring tranquility, ancient ways grow difficult to uphold.
This is the inevitable course of human inclination and the fixed order of things—nothing strange in it.

When creation becomes frequent and splendor arises, human hearts grow more treacherous, and calamities multiply.
This is the fate of Heaven and Earth’s cycles, beyond mortal escape.
Only the sage possesses the methods to preserve tranquility and sustain fullness,
the strategies for enduring peace and lasting governance.

In the early ages of culture’s dawn,
with non-action, non-doing, non-thought, non-care,
in the undivided, nameless Primordial Simplicity,
they cultivated it within themselves and applied it to others,
guiding those before and leading those behind,
purely in accord with Heaven, untainted by human contrivance—
thus they stabilized the court within and the realm without.

The first line of *Layered Array*: *"The sun lingers, beneficial to reside in the true"*—
this is the first stabilization for those in the age of primordial darkness.
For even stones conceal jade and mountains gleam,
while waters cradle pearls and rivers enchant.
If the Primordial Simplicity itself can exert such influence,
how much more so the nameless Primordial Simplicity?
To unite all people and things under its governance?

If it is not returned to its original unity and purity,
it will never bring the extremes to their proper convergence,
nor ensure all attain the ease of the ancestral hall.

Only by being unaware and unknowing,
by following the Will of the Emperor,
by forgetting virtue and morality,
by not recognizing the harmony of Heaven and humanity—
only then does one attain this.

Thus it is said:
*"The nameless Primordial Simplicity—it too shall not desire.
Through non-desire, stillness prevails,
and the world will settle of its own accord."*
This is the tranquil simplicity of effortless governance,
where the ruler does not know what to do to transform,
and the people do not know what to do to respond—
ruler and subjects both rest in the Dao of non-action,
acting without knowing why, yet acting perfectly.

Thus Shun governed through non-action,
and alone endured through the ages.

For those who rule over the people,
can they not take non-action as their foundation?

This discourse on governing the world
finds its root in non-action.
The way of cultivating oneself
is no different.

Yet one hears of the **Dharma/正法**—is it not more sublime than this?

If one does not cultivate it here,
then one’s **spirit/精神** shall surely be depleted,
and life’s span shall be hard to prolong.

In the blink of an eye, **qi/氣息** fades away,
and one knows not whether one becomes a ghost or a **蜮** (spectral spirit),
or a beast or a bird.

Through the cycles of the **Six Paths/六道**,
one turns endlessly—when shall one escape?

Now, the **Dharma/法筳** is widely proclaimed,
the **Great Dao/大道** expansively opened—
how can one not hasten cultivation,
lest time slip by in vain?

All things, compared to the human body’s
five senses and hundred limbs,
**spirit/精神** and **vital essence/血氣**—
if one can uphold the constant **Dao/Way of non-action/無為常道**,
then all cares shall cease of their own accord,
and the hundred bones shall all be in order.
The skin shall be moist and supple,
the hair bright and lustrous—
not unlike the **Golden Form/金相** and **Jade Nature/玉質**.

If **noble lords/侯王** can uphold it,
then all things shall transform naturally—
like the **inner light/一心內照**,
their changes become attuned and responsive.

Yet the **fire/火** is not yet refined,
and the **substance/氣質** remains impure.

At this moment, **spirit/精神** is greatly harmonized,
and **wisdom/智慧** frequently arises—
some delight in discussing the past and future,
to display their talents;
others take pleasure in speaking of great deeds and worldly achievements,
to boast before others.

All such actions stem from
the **impurity of virtue/power/道德未純**.

The elixir of jade nectar now perfected,
once more the furnace and cauldron are set in order,
the heavens and earth are reborn anew—
yet still the **Nameless Primordial/無名太朴** is poured
into the **Eight Trigrams Furnace/八卦炉**,
within by **Heaven’s Natural Divine Fire/天然神火**,
without by the **ordinary furnace of adjustment/增減凡炉**,
as time and fire refine—
even the **Nameless Primordial** is utterly forgotten.

When **ignorance and desire are absent/無知無欲**,
and tranquility and simplicity reign—
then the mortal form transforms,
returning naturally to the **Primordial Qi/先天一氣**,
and the **Dao of Transcendence/仙道** is fulfilled.

As it is said:
*"To desire nothing is to remain still—
then the world shall naturally align itself."*

This is the **Supreme Way/太上** of governing the world and cultivating the self—
its single thread that binds all else.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Eight: The Virtue of the Supreme Virtue/上德不德**

The **Supreme Virtue/上德** does not cling to virtue,
and thus virtue arises.
The **Lower Virtue/下德** clings to virtue,
and thus virtue is lost.

The **Supreme Virtue** acts without action,
yet nothing is left undone.
The **Lower Virtue** acts with action,
yet nothing is fully accomplished.

The **Supreme Benevolence** acts without contrivance,
while the **Supreme Righteousness** acts with contrivance.
The **Supreme Propriety** acts with formality,
yet when none respond,
it forces itself—
arms bared, it seizes.

Thus, when the **Dao is lost**, virtue emerges;
when **virtue is lost**, benevolence arises;
when **benevolence is lost**, righteousness takes hold;
when **righteousness is lost**, propriety is enforced.

For **propriety** is the thinnest veil of **loyalty and trust**,
yet it is the **beginning of chaos**.

**Preconceived notions** are the **ornaments of the Dao**,
the **first seeds of folly**.

Thus, the **true man** dwells in **substance, not ornament**,
in **reality, not illusion**—
and so he discards the latter for the former.

In the **ancient times**, the winds were vast and untrammled—
one surrendered to **Heaven’s course**,
the waters flowed deep and boundless—
each found ease in their **true nature**.
Above and below acted without contrivance,
rulers and people shared in joy.
**Sincerity and thickness** became the custom,
and disputes did not arise.

How pure was the **Dao** in those days!
All was due to resting in **Heaven’s effortless way**,
following **nature’s spontaneous flow**.
Each aged in their **aging**, nurtured their **young**,
honored the **wise**, cherished their **kin**,
tended fields with contentment, feasted on **virtue’s nourishment**,
and drank from **harmony’s cup**—
unaware of **virtue’s name**,
let alone the teachings of **benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom**.

Yet they embraced **primordial purity**,
let **qi’s movements** unfold naturally—
and **Heaven and Earth** flowed in unison,
as if untaught yet transformed,
effortless yet accomplished,
in perfect accord with **virtue and the Dao**,
with no conflict between **benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom**.

When **virtue and the Dao** are spoken together,
the **Dao** is the **essence**, **virtue** the **function**.
When **virtue, benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom** are discussed as one,
then **virtue and the Dao** are again the **essence**,
while the rest are **functions**.

Later sages, though establishing these teachings
to **civilize the people**,
named **virtue and the Dao**,
and divided them into **benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom**—
yet in truth, **virtue and the Dao** contain them,
and they in turn contain **virtue and the Dao**—
no separation, no lack.

But in later ages, **virtue and the Dao** were divided.
And further still, **benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom**
became fixed in one-sided extremes—
all due to the **shift of cosmic cycles** and the **deceit of human hearts**.

The **Supreme Way** wished to return all to their **roots**,
to **reclaim their origin**,
and so it sighed:
*"The man of **Supreme Virtue**, acting without contrivance,
does not know virtue—
and thus his virtue endures.
The man of **Lower Virtue**, knowing virtue’s worth,
loves its name and practices it,
fearing to lose it—
and thus his virtue is ever in peril.

His thoughts scatter and his purity shatters—
not like the **Supreme Virtue’s** single, unbroken sincerity,
where no trace of contrivance remains.
For through clinging to virtue,
he becomes **without virtue**."*

Moreover, when the **Supreme Virtue** acts without action,
the world aligns in **effortless harmony**;
when the **Lower Virtue** acts with action,
the age grows **ornate and divided**,
and all delight in **artificial contrivance**.

Is it not so?
The **carefree** still the world’s **machinations**,
while the **ambitious** ignite them anew?

Yet when **times are exhausted**, they renew;
when **things are exhausted**, they transform;
when **men are exhausted**, they return.

In this **age of turmoil**,
when customs grow **decadent** and **hearts unsteady**,
the **primordial purity** is long buried.
There must arise a **ruler who loves benevolence**,
who **clarifies the source**, **rectifies the root**,
turning chaos to order,
danger to safety—
though forced to act,
he yet **adapts to the times**,
**follows the natural course**,
so that **action remains non-action**—
thus, with **clothes of simplicity**, the world is governed.

Then there are those who **love righteousness**,
who in times of **disorder** seek to restore **peace**,
and with **zealous vigor** sweep away corruption—
yet though they may **renew the world**,
their **example spreads**,
and the **struggle for dominance** among people and things
cannot be stilled.

And when the **rulers of propriety** arise,
the hearts of men grow yet more **corrupt**.
They **observe formalities**,
debate **reciprocity**,
sometimes **giving much yet receiving little**,
sometimes **granting favors only to meet grudges**—
and thus they cannot rest in **true ease**.

Morning brings **changes in governance**,
customs shift between **loss and gain**—
they cannot **harmonize near and far**,
and at the slightest **disagreement**,
arms are raised in **conflict**,
war drums sound—
they cannot **dwell together in peace**.

Thus it is said:
*"When the **Dao is lost**, virtue arises;
when **virtue is lost**, benevolence takes hold;
when **benevolence is lost**, righteousness is enforced;
when **righteousness is lost**, propriety is observed."*

Down to this day,
men grow ever more **corrupt**,
affairs ever more **entangled**,
while **loyalty and trust** have **decayed beyond repair**—
and so **propriety** is invoked to **preserve order**.

Yet alas—
it is but **superficial adornment**,
not **sincere transformation from within**.
Daily **interactions multiply**,
**disputes swell**—
those who rely on **propriety** to **bind hearts**
have not considered:
*What is observed externally must spring from within.*
Otherwise, **disaster looms**,
and **division deepens**—
seeking **peace through conflict**,
they plunge into **greater peril**.

Thus it is said:
*"**Loyalty and trust**, though thin,
are the **root of chaos**."*

*"As for wisdom, it is not some extraordinary scheme or preemptive stratagem—it is the **Dao/Way** of **sincere discernment** (*chéng ér míng*), which comes without deliberation (*bù sī ér dé*), without study (*bù xué ér néng*), and naturally clear as a mirror. How could it be compared to the **deceitful and contrived** (*nì zhuā yì xìn*)?

Yet **the splendor of Dao/Way** is not **its true substance**. Moreover, **excessive scrutiny** (*chá chá wéi míng*) must inevitably lead to **empty fabrications and falsehoods** (*xū wū jià wěi*), without one’s awareness.

Even if one **reflects upon oneself** (*zài jǐ*), one may **boast of special insight** (*jīng tè shí*), but in truth, this is the **beginning of folly** (*yú zhī shǐ*).

Thus, a **true man** (*dà zhàng fū*) with **true insight** (*zhēn shí*) and **resolute power** (*dìng lì*) knows that **sincerity and depth** (*dūn hòu*) are the **Dao/Way of propriety** (*lǐ*), so he **takes its substance** (*qǔ qí hòu*), not its **superficial veneer** (*qǔ qí báo*). Knowing that **the splendor of Dao/Way** is not **true wisdom** (*zhī qí huá fēi zhī huá*), he **takes its substance** (*qǔ qí shí*), not its **splendor** (*qǔ qí huá*).

**Discarding and embracing** (*qù qǔ yōu yí*) are as they should be, and so the **Great Dao/Way** is not difficult to restore.

This shows that when **virtue/power** (*dào*) is lost, **benevolence** (*rén*) and **righteousness** (*yì*) arise; when **benevolence** and **righteousness** are lost, **propriety** (*lǐ*) and **wisdom** (*zhì*) emerge—ever descending, a result of **human hearts and customs** (*rén xīn fēng sú*), nothing strange about it.

As for **cultivation and nourishment** (*xiū yǎng*), **swallowing saliva and refining qi** (*yàn jīn fú qì*) brings forth a **transformation of the Dao/Way** (*dào yī biàn*); **gathering herbs and alchemizing elixirs** (*cǎi yào liàn dān*) brings forth another **transformation of the Dao/Way** (*dào yī biàn*). Down to this day, **erroneous teachings** (*fēn fēn zuǒ dào*) abound—beyond reckoning!

Who truly understands that **the Mystic Pass** (*xuān guān*)—a single aperture—is the **essence of Dao/Way cultivation** (*xiū dào zhī yào wù*)?

I now reveal it to people: To **perceive this Mystic Pass** (*rèn qí xuān guān*), one must first endure **great toil and labor** (*dà chén láo*), then **great rest and respite** (*dà xiū xiē*), before one can **penetrate its depths** (*liǎo chè*).

It is also said:
*'When thoughts arise, that is the **illness** (*bìng*); when they are not prolonged, that is the **medicine** (*yào*).'*
*'Lay down the butcher’s cleaver, and instantly become a **Buddha** (*fó*).'*

All this amounts to nothing more than **dusty emotions and fleeting thoughts** (*chén qíng zá niàn*), swirling and chaotic—when one **awakens within them** (*cóng zhōng yī jué*), that is the **Mystic Pass** (*xuān guān*), the **so-called 'returning to the shore'** (*huí tóu shì àn*).

It is also said:
*'The **far shore** (*bǐ àn*) is not distant—**the radiant light of enlightenment** (*huí guāng fǎn zhào*) is itself the **shore**.'*

But fear arises when, before the **Mystic Pass** is opened (*xuān guān wèi kāi*), one **prematurely imposes contrivance** (*xiān jiā yī fān yì sī qù sūn duó*); or when, after the **Mystic Pass** is opened (*xuān guān jì kāi*), one **adds further fixation** (*yòu jiā yī fān yì sī qù shǒu hù*).

Such **proliferating thoughts** (*niàn lǜ fēn chéng*) are like **heaven’s original clouds and veils** (*yīn tiān běn wú yún yì*)—when they disperse, the **vast and wondrous landscape of clarity** (*tài kōng miào jǐng*) reveals itself. Yet if one **again imposes smoke and dust** (*zhuǎn lìng qīng míng guǎng dà zhī tiān yīn qí yān chén*), the **clear and boundless heaven** (*qīng míng guǎng dà zhī tiān*) will be **narrowed and constricted** (*yīn qí yān chén*), its **luminosity obscured** (*zhuǎn lìng qīng míng guǎng dà zhī tiān yīn qí yān chén*), and its **darkness unrecognizable** (*hūn àn mò biàn*).

The **Buddha** said:
*'One must **abide thus** (*yìng rú shì zhù*), **subduing the heart** (*xiàng fú qí xīn*) in this way.'*

Such **profound mechanisms** (*xuān jī*) cannot be **approximated by a single word** (*zǒng shī bù dé yī háo nǐ yì*), for approximation is **not true**. Nor can they be **grasped by half a thought** (*zhuó bù dé bàn diǎn sī lǜ*), for thought is **misguided**.

Only when the **Mystic Pass** is **not yet opened** (*xuān guān wèi kāi*), do I **follow its natural illumination** (*shùn qí liǎo zhào zhī yì*). When the **Mystic Pass** is **already opened** (*xuān guān jì kāi hòu*), I **rest in its effortless clarity** (*ān qí zuò zhào zhī hún*).

If **thoughts scatter wildly** (*niàn ruò fēn chéng*), I **withdraw them** (*jī shōu huí*), and **withdrawal itself is the way** (*shōu huí jì shì*). If the **spirit is dim and obscured** (*shén rú hūn wǎng*), I **reorder it** (*zhěng dùn*), and **reordering itself is the way** (*zhěng dùn jì shì*).

How **simple and effortless** (*rú hé jiǎn jiě biàn yì*) is this?

Yet some **fix their minds on the bed while seeking repose** (*rèn qí chén shàng ān chén*), or **search for movement within stillness** (*dòng zhōng xún dòng*), or **seek stillness within motion** (*jìng lǐ qiú jìng*), thereby **falling into contrived analysis** (*qiú yú chuān záo*).

While the **Mystic Pass** lies **plainly before them** (*xuān guān fēn míng zài qián*), they **fail to recognize it due to later-day knowledge and speculation** (*yīn hòu tiān zhī lǜ zhē bì ér bù zài yǐ*).

I now reveal a **key principle** (*yào jué*): Let **thoughts swirl chaotically** (*rèn tā sī niàn fēn fēn mò kě liǎo què*), yet I **awaken within them** (*néng yī jué ér dòng biàn jiù sǎo chú*), and this **awakening is the Mystic Pass** (*zhì jì shì xuān guān*).

It is clear that **one’s Dao/Way cultivation** (*rén zhī xiū liàn*) depends solely on this **awakened and luminous mind** (*jué zhào zhī xīn*), which must always **shine forth like the sun in an empty sky** (*cháng xū guāng míng dòng zhào*), **untainted by even a speck of darkness** (*yī háo hūn hēi bù dé*). If **darkness creeps in** (*hūn hēi jì luò wū àn dì yù*), one **falls into defilement** (*luò wū àn dì yù*).

If one **can disperse the clouds and mists** (*néng bō kāi yún wù*), then **blue sky and white sun** (*qīng tiān bái rì*) lie **brightly before** (*míng míng zài qián*).

Yet if **thoughts of the world arise** (*rú shēng tā xiǎng*), one **falls into the ordinary man’s rut** (*luò fán fū wō jiù*), not the **foundation of the Immortal** (*fēi shén jiān běn*).

In summary, the **Immortal’s teachings** (*xiān jiā wú tā miào jué*) have no other **secret** (*miào jué*), only **illuminating the heart and seeing true nature** (*míng xīn jiàn xìng*), which is the **essence of cultivation** (*xiū liàn yào zhǐ*).

If one asks: *What is **elixir** (*dān*)?*
It is the **original qi** (*yuán qì*) within my **elixir field** (*dān zhōng*), the **primordial essence** (*yuán xìng*).

Yet this **original qi** (*yuán qì*) is **indivisible from my innate spirit** (*yuán shén*), both **uniting as one** (*huì hé yī chù*), which is the **single point of Heavenly Fate** (*tiān mìng*) before **the return to the Ultimate Supreme** (*fǎn huán tàijí wújí*), when **parents had not yet given birth** (*fù mǔ wèi shēng qián*).

If one **can establish life through true nature** (*néng yǐ xìng lì mìng*) and **fulfill true nature through Heavenly Fate** (*yǐ mìng liǎo xìng*), then **eternal life without death** (*cháng shēng bù sǐ*) is achieved.

But if one **seeks alchemy in the Water Realm** (*shuǐ fǔ qiú xuān*), desiring to **cultivate the Golden Elixir** (*chéng jīn yè zhī dān*), yet **lacks the innate breath of the Primordial Spirit** (*bù dé xiān tiān shén xī*), and **harvests, refines, and tempers** (*cǎi qìng pēng liàn*) without **mastering the natural ascent and descent** (*jìn tuì wēn yǎng*)—then the **Primordial Nature** (*xiān tiān yuán xìng*) and the **Primordial Fate** (*xiān tiān yuán mìng*) **cannot spontaneously unite** (*bù néng zǎn wǔ cù liù wéi chéng jīn dān*).

Though this is so, once **Primordial Nature** and **Primordial Fate** are attained (*jì dé yuán xìng yuán mìng yǐ*), if one **lacks true and genuine breath** (*yòu wú zhēn zhèng pēi xī*), it is like **a man and woman in the world who cannot find a matchmaker** (*róng rén shì nán nǚ bù dé méi bǐ*), though they **come and go in intercourse** (*wǎng lái jiāo tōng*), they **cannot form a conjugal bond** (*yì néng jié wéi fū fù*).

Thus the **elixir scriptures** (*dān jīng*) say:
*'**True intent** (*zhēn yì*) is the **matchmaker** (*méi bǐ*).'*

Now it is also said:
*'**True breath** (*zhēn xī*) is the **matchmaker** (*méi bǐ*).'*
Is this not **contrary to the ancient scriptures** (*yǐ bù yú gǔ jīng xiāng bèi hū*)?

Those who **do not know True Intent** (*bù zhī zhēn yì zhě liàn dān jiāo hé zhī shén*) are **deceived by the alchemical process** (*liàn dān jiāo hé zhī shén*); **True Breath** (*zhēn xī*) is the **instrument of alchemical union** (*liàn dān jiāo hé zhī qì*), but fundamentally, it is the **united essence of spirit and qi** (*yào zhī jié yǐ shén qì èr zhě hé wéi yī ér yǐ*).

If there is **no True Breath** (*dì wú zhēn xī*), then **True Qi** (*zhēn qì*) **cannot ascend and descend on its own** (*bù néng zì shēng zì jiàng*), nor **unite and temper itself** (*huì hé wēn yǎng*), nor **form the Mystic Pearl** (*jié chéng xuān zhū*).

Once **True Breath** is attained (*jì dé zhēn xī*), if one **lacks True Intent** (*yòu wú zhēn yì*) to **command, gather, and seal it strictly** (*hào lìng shè chí yán mì*), then **True Breath** **cannot come and go, advance and retreat** (*zhēn xī yì bù néng wǎng lái jìn tuì*), nor **remain effortlessly natural** (*rú rú zì rú*).

Thus it is said: **True Intent** is the **key to elixir cultivation** (*liàn dān zhī yào*).

Yet if **True Intent** **cannot unite with the Primordial Spirit** (*bù dé zhēn zhèng yuán shén*), then **from where does True Intent begin** (*zhēn yì cóng hé ér shǐ*)?

Only when the **Mystic Pass** is first opened (*xuān guān qiǎo kāi zhī chū*), does one **recognize this True Intent** (*rèn qǔ zhè diǎn zhēn yì*), and then **return to hold it fast** (*yú shì fǎn ér chí zhī*), as **Master Yan’s earnest devotion** (*xué yán zǐ quán quán fú yīng*), thus attaining it.

Moreover, the **Primordial Spirit’s manifestations** (*yuán shén suǒ liù lù*) are **True Intent itself** (*zhēn yì*), **a single act of goodness** (*yī shàn*), and also the **Dao/Way of attaining one to fulfill all things** (*yī dé ér wàn shì bì zhī dào*).

If the **student recognizes this clearly** (*xué rén rèn qǔ fēn míng*), then the **foundation of the Great Elixir** (*dà dān zhī běn*) is established.

Ancient **Master Qiu** said:
*'If the **breath** (*xī*) has even a **single speck of instability** (*yī háo wèi dìng*), then the **Fate** (*mìng*) is not **one’s own**.'*

I reveal to the **student** (*xué rén*): To **seek eternal life** (*qiú cháng shēng*), one must first **subdue the breath** (*xiān xū fú qì*)."

「Yet, *subduing the breath* (*fú qì*) has two meanings: one is to suppress and store this *qi* within the **Central Palace** (*zhōng gōng*), remaining **still and unmoving** (*rú rú bù dòng*); the second is to **strictly regulate and govern** (*guǎn shì yán mì*) it, for within this *subduing of the breath* (*fú qì*) lies the **Dao/Way of attaining eternal life** (*cháng shēng*).

Beyond these, there is no other path. **Practitioners** (*xiū xíng rén*) must follow this teaching diligently, lest they fail to honor my **one sincere intention** (*yī piàn kǔ zhōng*).

**Chapter Thirty-Nine: The Humble as the Foundation**

The ancients who **attained the One** (*dé yī*):

- **Heaven** (*Tiān*) attains the One to be **clear** (*qīng*),
- **Earth** (*Dì*) attains the One to be **settled** (*níng*),
- **Spirit** (*shén*) attains the One to be **responsive** (*líng*),
- **Grain** (*gǔ*) attains the One to be **full** (*yíng*),
- **All things** (*wàn wù*) attain the One to **generate** (*shēng*),
- **Lords and kings** (*hóu wáng*) attain the One to **establish their sovereignty** (*wéi tiān xià zhēn*)—
  such is the **result of attaining the One** (*qí zhì yī zhī*).

Without **clarity** (*qīng*), **Heaven** (*Tiān*) would fear **rupture** (*liè*);
without **settlement** (*níng*), **Earth** (*Dì*) would fear **upheaval** (*fā*);
without **responsiveness** (*líng*), **Spirit** (*shén*) would fear **extinction** (*xiē*);
without **generation** (*shēng*), **All things** (*wàn wù*) would fear **annihilation** (*miè*);
without **sovereignty** (*zhēn*), **Lords and kings** (*hóu wáng*) would fear their **prestige** (*guì gāo*) being **overthrown** (*jié*).

Thus, the **prestigious** (*guì*) relies on the **humble** (*jiàn*) as its **foundation** (*běn*),
and the **elevated** (*gāo*) rests upon the **lowly** (*xià*) as its **base** (*jī*).

Hence, **Lords and kings** (*hóu wáng*) call themselves **lonely** (*gū*), **bereft** (*guǎ*), **unworthy** (*bù gū*—*unworthy of the title "king"*),
for this is their way of **holding the humble as foundation** (*yǐ jiàn wéi běn*), is it not?

Therefore, **the One** (*yī*) is **sovereign** (*zhēn*) without a **chariot** (*chē*),
does not desire **polished brilliance** (*tián tián rú yù*—*like jade, smooth and gleaming*) **or** **sterile rigidity** (*luò luò rú shí*—*like stone, cold and unyielding*).

The **Great Dao/Way** (*Dà Dào*) has no other secret—
**the One** (*yī*) alone is its essence.

What is this **One** (*yī*)?

The primordial **qi** (*yuanqi*) of **Hongmeng** before differentiation—
the **boundless** (*wújí*), **chaotic** (*hún*), and **formless** (*wúxíng*)—
gave birth to the **Supreme Ultimate** (*Tàijí*), the source of all things.

In truth, the **primordial qi** (*yuanqi*) is shapeless—
this is called **Boundlessness** (*Wújí*).
All things arise from **Boundlessness** and take form—
this is the **root of the world**, called the **Supreme Ultimate** (*Tàijí*).
This is the **Dao/Way**.

The **Sage** (*shèngrén*), though nameless, names it—
thus it is called **One** (*yī*).
Without the **One**, there are no things;
without things, there is no **One**.
To possess it is to live;
to lose it is to perish.

Since the **Beginning of Origins** (*Yuánshǐ*), all that has taken form and manifested—
endlessly flowing, eternally generating—
from the vast **Great Cycle** (*Dà Zhōu*) to the minute **dust-mote** (*wēichén*), none escape it.

As the *Zhōngyōng* says:
*"It cannot be seen, cannot be heard,
yet nothing can be omitted from its embrace."*
What else could this be?

Thus, when all is considered:
The **Heaven’s** clarity comes from the **One**;
the **Earth’s** stability comes from the **One**;
the **Spirit’s** responsiveness comes from the **One**;
the **valley’s** fullness comes from the **One**;
the **birth of all things** comes from the **One**;
the **Lord’s** rectification of himself and the world
is nothing but the **One’s** sovereign virtue.

Though great and small differ in form,
noble and base diverge in path,
coarse and refined vary in substance,
dark and bright in manifestation—
whether mortal or ghost—
how could anything transcend this **One** as its encompassing embrace?

For even **Heaven**, though most exalted,
without the **One** would fear collapse;
the **Earth**, though most solid,
without the **One** would fear upheaval;
the **Spirit**, though most subtle,
without the **One** would fear stagnation;
the **echo in the valley**, though most resonant,
without the **One** would fear exhaustion;
all **things**, though most manifold,
without the **One** would fear annihilation;
**Lords and kings**, though most high and noble,
without the **One** to rectify the world,
would fear that lofty rank brings peril,
and exalted name brings ruin—
how then could the **One** be neglected?

If one follows the **One** and disperses into the ten thousand things—
vast and boundless, profound and unfathomable—
then **Heaven, Earth, Spirit, and Valley**,
all **things, and Lords and kings**,
