all rely on this **One** as their sovereign master,
spanning the heavens and filling the earth,
without gap or flaw, exhaustively complete—
such is its extremity.
Thus, nothing is higher than the **Dao/Way**,
nothing more noble than the **One**.

Yet from **non-being** to **being**, what is there to exalt?
From **subtlety** to **manifestation**, what is there to prize?
Even if nobility surpasses the **Capital** (*Jīng*—*i.e., the most exalted rank*),
it is still the **qi** rising from the **humble**—
thus it is said: *"The noble relies on the humble as its foundation."*

Even if height reaches **Boundlessness**,
it is still the **qi** ascending from the **lowly**—
thus it is said: *"The elevated rests upon the lowly as its base."*

Take **Lords and kings** of the world—
though their rank is high as **Lords**,
their title noble as **Kings**—
they know the **Dao/Way** rises from the **lowly**,
nobility from the **humble**,
thus they call themselves *"Lonely"* (*gū*), *"Bereft"* (*guǎ*), *"Unworthy"* (*bù gū*).
Is this not relying on the **humble as foundation**?

If one does not dwell in the **humble**,
but places oneself too high,
then the **heart lacks a master**, the **Dao/Way** does not stand,
the **mind is scattered**, the **Spirit** does not coalesce.
To harmonize all things in **Great Centrality** (*Dà Zhōng*) and return to the **One Dao/Way**—
how few can achieve this!

Even one who drives **many chariots** cannot govern both inner and outer,
while one who drives a **single chariot** still holds its core—
thus, having **many chariots** is like having none at all.
Greed for multiplicity is not as good as embracing the **One**.

Like **jade**, if it is **excessively polished and multiplied**,
its value diminishes;
like **stone**, if it is **layered and precarious**,
danger arises—
these are what the **Supreme Sage** does not desire.

How much better to embrace the **One**,
beginning from the **humble** and **lowly**,
ultimately reaching heights beyond compare,
nobility beyond speech—
this is the path of **cultivating the Dao/Way** to **perfection**,
where there is **only the One**, no second.

Confucius said: *"My Dao/Way is one, threading through all."*
Mencius said: *"The Dao/Way is one and only."*
Yet—what is this **One**?

The ancients spoke of **Hongmeng’s** boundless haze—
without thought or defilement,
a **void yet luminous**, **quiet yet aware**—
this singular substance.

When **expansive**, it contains the **Dharma-realm** (*Fǎjiè*);
when **constricted**, not even a **speck of dust** can stand.
When **manifest**, it reaches the **Nine Yí and Eight Huáng** without limit;
when **hidden**, it scrutinizes the **finest motes** without omission.
This is the **Extreme of Boundlessness** (*Wújí zhī jí*),
the **Spirit beyond spirit** (*Bù shén zhī shén*),
truly beyond naming or conception.
The **Dao/Way of Nature and Life** is none other than this.

The **Supreme Sage** likens **Lords and kings** to the **human heart**—
if the **heart** can constantly hold and preserve the **One**,
without forgetting or aiding its excess,
moment by moment returning inward,
ever internally illuminating,
then the **One** is never lost.
The **One** is **solitary** (*dú*).

In **solitary awareness**, with **awe and vigilance**,
this is the **original highest, noblest state**—
perhaps it can be long preserved.
Yet this is the **study of cultivating Nature** (*xìng*),
so **guarding the One in solitude** suffices to attain it.

But **nurturing Life** (*mìng*) involves action—
if one does not begin from the **lowly** and refine from the **humble**,
even the **elixir** is hard to obtain—
what of the **Golden Elixir** (*jīndān*)?

The **lowly** refers to the **Lower Dantian** (*xià dāntián*);
the **humble** refers to the **filthy lower realm**.
The seeker, desiring the **One Yang’s revival** (*yī yáng lái fù*),
must have **qi** surging upward from below—
how else would one obtain **clear and firm qi** as the **treasure of longevity**?

If not by descending into the **Lower Field of Turbidity** (*xià tián zhuó xiāng*),
using **Spirit-fire** to illuminate downward,
refining the **most potent Yang qi**—
how else would one secure the **foundation of the Elixir**?

The ancients said: *"Seeking Yang within Yin, stealing treasure from the ghost’s lair"*—
truly not false!

Above all, one must have **a single heart, no dual thoughts**—
this is the **Dao/Way of guarding the One**.

When naturally attained, **self and others are forgotten**—
then the **One** is obtained.

When the cultivator reaches this state,
all things and affairs are handled with ease and precision—
*"Obtaining the One, all matters are complete."*
Is this not true?

If one clings to form and appearance, rushing about in pursuit of myriad affairs,
entangled by the seven emotions and six desires,
bound by family, wife, and children,
reluctant to let go—
then the Dao/Way is distant indeed.

Do not speak of external distractions as beyond the Dao—
even in matters of nurturing the heart and nature,
cultivating the Dao and refining the Elixir,
advancing fire and withdrawing talismans,
gathering and sealing—
all these names are but symbolic constructs,
established as methods for later students.

If the mind harbors even a thread of impurity,
it becomes an obstruction to the Dao.

The *Tàishàng* said: *"Make the many into one, so that no wheel turns without a wheel"*—
not desiring the gleam of jade or the rigidity of stone.

The Dao is but one Dao—
why should the student seek elsewhere?

**Chapter 40: The Generated from the Non-Generated**

*"The returning is the movement of the Dao;*
*the yielding is the function of the Dao."*

Under heaven, all things are generated from the Existent,
the Existent from the Non-Generated.

The Great Dao is complete in all,
perfect in each—
why seek further?

Yet since birth, humans are bound by *qi* and obscured by things,
lured by knowledge, ensnared by desires—
thus the Dao is often drowned and lost.

Unless one internally dispels all entanglements,
externally repels all disturbances,
concentrates the spirit and regulates the breath,
abandons thought and forgets artifice—
how can the first Yang arise,
how can the Dao-qi return?

Thus it is said: *"The returning is the movement of the Dao."*
This is the foundation of Elixir refinement.

When the medicine returns to the furnace,
the Elixir coarsely forms,
mercury and lead merge as one—
day and night, one observes internally,
and the Golden Elixir emerges.

From this point onward, after gathering,
it flows continuously, ever-present—
humble yet descending, yielding yet soft—
this is the marvel of the Returned Elixir.

Yet it is not only the Returned Elixir that requires this—
from the first step to completion,
one must always focus the mind inwardly,
concentrate the *qi* to achieve softness.

For the Elixir is forged from the unified *qi* of Great Harmony;
the cultivator must treat it with humility and moderation.
The slightest roughness
stirs the vulgar fire,
harming the Dao.

Thus it is said: *"The yielding is the function of the Dao."*

Though all things under heaven
begin with form, appearance, matter, and principle,
their origin does not arise from the Existent alone.
The Sage, when the Great Dao is complete,
though manifesting in myriad transformations,
possesses all things—
yet first, they gather and refine from the utmost emptiness and non-being,
then the great function flows, the vast *qi* fills the two extremes.

If not refined from non-being,
how could one encompass heaven and earth,
be so pervasive and boundless?

Thus it is said: *"The Existent is generated from the Non-Generated."*

The essentials of a student’s cultivation:
First, from non-being to being—
from stillness, refining the subtle Yang;
Next, from being to non-being—
from agitation, returning to tranquility;
Finally, again from non-being to being—
mixed and indistinct, self and others forgotten—
over time, the Yang spirit of three inches emerges,
the golden body of sixteen feet.

Thus being and non-being alternate endlessly,
cycling without end.
The student must follow this path precisely,
lest they err.

This speaks of the Great Dao of the Golden Elixir—
nothing else, only the true *qi* flowing through the body,
filling it entirely.
In stillness, like the depths of a pool;
in motion, like the surge of tides.
Only the pure and refined can inwardly illuminate,
self-examine, and verify—
this cannot be exhausted by words.

If one understands that the moving principle is the root of my generation,
then the Elixir of eternal life—
how could it be external to this?

Moreover, it is something shared by all—
no difference, no lack.
Yet because the body moves and essence does not generate,
the mind moves and *qi* does not settle—
thus the sufferings of birth, old age, sickness, and death
endlessly cycle, without end.

If one wishes to escape all this,
they must first attain nourishment in stillness,
only then can they seize the principle in motion,
restore the original *qi* of generation.

But this moving principle is subtle in force,
delicate in *qi*—
a moment’s carelessness,
and suddenly *guǐshuǐ* arises,
transforming the original essence into post-heaven form—
useless.

Thus it is said: *"Seen, it cannot be used;*
*used, it cannot be seen."*

From this single movement onward,
if gathering is timely,
then longevity has its root,
the Great Elixir has its foundation.

If one clings to what is possessed and forces it,
fixates on what is favored and guards it rigidly—
though the medicine may sometimes be obtained,
the Elixir may await completion—
yet the *qi* is not pure and harmonious;
thus later gathering and refining are flawed,
and the unified *qi* of Great Harmony
is injured by the violent vulgar fire.

The Dao’s most potent Yang must be nurtured with endurance,
humility, and softness—
only then can the Elixir’s path be fulfilled.

Thus the *Tàishàng* taught:
*"Dull its sharpness, untangle its chaos,*
*harmonize its brilliance, blend it with the dust."*

Though the Dao moves with *qi*,
it remains the generation of being from non-being;
if the Existent is not nourished with yielding,
it cannot return to the emptiness of heaven—
how then could the Dao be complete?

People know that the return of Yang is the Dao’s moving principle,
yet they do not know that returning to the origin,
the manifest must revert to the non-manifest—
for the manifest is the Dao’s trace,
the non-manifest is the Dao’s truth.

Understanding this, cultivation need not fear a lack of foundation.

**Chapter 41: The Great Vessel Takes Long to Mature**

The superior sage hears the Dao and diligently practices it;
the mediocre sage hears the Way, now retaining it, now forgetting it;
the inferior sage hears the Dao and laughs at it—
unless one laughs, it cannot be called the Dao.

Thus the *Tàishàng* proclaimed:
*"Clarify the Way as if in darkness,
advance the Way as if retreating,
level the Way as if uneven,
virtue’s height as a valley,
great brightness as disgrace,
abundant virtue as insufficiency,
established virtue as laziness,
the straightforward as mutable,
the great square as cornerless,
the great vessel as slow to mature,
the great sound as rare in tone,
the great image as formless."*

The Dao is hidden and nameless—
only the Dao can be freely given and fulfilled.

Before heaven and earth came into being,
there was only boundless void and indistinct haze,
a vast, indistinct *qi* of primordial harmony.
After long fermentation, the *qi* transformed and filled the cosmos,
and suddenly, with a stir, the *Tàijí* took root.

Motion became Yang—light and clear *qi* rising to form heaven;
stillness became Yin—heavy and turbid *qi* settling to form earth.
When heaven and earth unfolded, all beings flourished.
Yet among countless multitudes, how few emulate heaven and earth’s function!

Only the sage, awakening from chaos, refines the Great Elixir.
To govern the body is to transform the world.
Though I dare not remain silent, I do not indiscriminately teach all.
Following circumstances and adapting to things,
this aligns with heaven and earth’s boundless, selfless measure.

At times, I meet the superior sage—
hearing my Dao, he rejoices and pursues it diligently,
without hesitation. Such a person I have long sought.

At times, I meet the mediocre sage—
my words enter his mind as commonplace,
neither strange nor wondrous, yet he still cherishes them,
yearning to attain them in a single stride.
Alas, worldly tastes are thick while Dao’s flavor is thin,
holy intent shallow while mundane thoughts run deep—
he drifts or conforms, retaining or losing it, unable to escape.

As for the inferior sage, deeply entrenched in habit,
his nature twisted, upon hearing my Dao,
he doubts it as deceitful, a delusion misleading the world,
and accuses me of gathering crowds and amassing wealth.
Yet the gentleman’s cultivation begins with the union of Yin and Yang;
the sage’s Way lies within the interplay of heaven and earth—
harmony brings life, resistance fosters transcendence.
Though their methods differ, their essence is one,
yet the vulgar scoff, calling divine transformation mere illusion.
How could such cultivation ascend beyond the celestial layers?
Or how could one, nurturing this nature, merge with heaven and earth’s flow?
Yet why are sages and immortals so few throughout history?
Thus they mock my words as boastful, my conduct as deceitful and eccentric.

Alas, this Way can only be revealed to kindred spirits,
not to those of shallow vision and limited hearing!
The mayfly knows not dusk, the cicada knows not seasons,
the well-frog knows not the sea—
why should I be surprised at their laughter?

Unless one laughs, how can one perceive the Dao’s utmost simplicity and constancy,
its utmost divinity and strangeness—
for divinity and strangeness lie within the ordinary.

The Dao itself has no form, sound, or scent—
what words can describe it?
Yet when words are spoken, it is only because later cultivators,
lacking a clear path to progress or gradual ascent,
must resort to metaphor and false names to guide them.

If one truly knows the Dao as emptiness and nature as function,
one must proceed from Yin to Yang, from low to high.
Darkness is the root of clarity, retreat the foundation of advancement.
Though one is enlightened, one must seem obscured,
so that hidden depths may reach their utmost clarity.
Though one advances, one must seem to retreat,
so that withdrawal may hasten, and progress extend further.

The Dao encompasses both near and far—
though I am in perfect accord with it,
I remain as if unchanged in myself, unaltered toward others,
equal and unassuming.

The Dao encompasses both great and small—
though I am one with virtue,
I am as if neither having nor lacking,
empty yet not full—
virtue as a valley.

When the Dao shines brightly in the world,
praised endlessly, never ceasing from lips,
I feel no personal gain, only shame,
thus it is said: *"Great brightness as disgrace."*

When virtue fills within,
acting everywhere without exhaustion,
I seem deficient, increasingly inadequate,
thus it is said: *"Abundant virtue as insufficiency."*

When establishing virtue and conduct,
aligned with heaven and earth without contradiction,
I seem self-satisfied and lazy,
lacking the will to uplift,
thus it is said: *"Established virtue as laziness."*

If one’s sincerity is complete and nature exhausted,
verified by spirits and gods without doubt,
yet I seem mutable and unreliable,
lacking firmness,
thus it is said: *"The straightforward as mutable."*

Such cultivation of heart and nature,
vigilant and refined,
though slandered, leaves no opening for intrusion;
though corrupting influences abound, no gap remains to exploit.
In the world, those who cultivate the Dao and manifest virtue
yet suffer disgrace—
let them reflect inward.
If one truly hides one’s traces, humbles oneself,
treasures disgrace, and extends virtue without claiming it,
establishes virtue without boasting—
could such a one be accused of deceit or called a fabricator?

I know this is not the case.
Even if fate is predetermined and calamities unavoidable,
what heaven does, one must accept and submit to,
resting in destiny while listening to heaven’s will.
Thus the gentleman bears lifelong concern,
not momentary distress—I am ever more certain of this.

Moreover, the Dao has no fixed form, shape, sound, or scent to describe—
those who cling to rigid propriety, guarding themselves,
cannot move freely and naturally.
I, however, am the great square without corners,
a boundless unity, unconfined by edges—
what need for hesitation?

The things that are easily attained are not beautiful to behold,
and those hastened to completion are not great vessels.

I can follow the Way step by step, ascending without seeking swift results,
not reckoning shallow gains, abiding long in its practice—
thus great attainment is surely possible, why should I feel deficient within?

Indeed, the Dao is subtle beyond words, naturally elusive.
It is best described as vague and indistinct.

I can attain to the utmost emptiness and profound stillness,
then the great sound arises without sound,
and the great form manifests without form!

Its application spans the four seas and remains ever valid,
its transmission through ten thousand generations knows no end—
how could it merely be esteemed in one age,
while failing to shine through all future generations?

The *Shijing* says:
*"There is none there who is evil,
And here is none who is not pure."*

The Dao’s establishment and practice truly possess such divine and wondrous qualities.

Between these, who is the doer and who is the recipient?
It is also said:
*"To practice the Dao is to lend virtue generously and complete it."*

This is the way of those who embrace the Dao in the world—
their use is never insufficient,
they give to all things without depletion,
and even beyond the dusty boundaries of existence, they have abundance.
Moreover, they enable the transformation of all things to flourish,
truly the highest adept.

The **Tàishàng** warns against those who do not conceal their light nor cultivate obscurity,
who establish virtue and refine the self—
those who, upon gaining even a little, boast and exalt themselves.

While the **Five Aggregates** remain unemptied and the **Six Dusts** uncleansed,
they are like a house roofed with straw and thatch,
fire that still relies on fuel to burn.

If one only cultivates oneself without seeking harmony with others,
they drift aimlessly, returning to the realm of **Nonbeing**—
the boundless wilds where nothing exists.
Even if external harm comes,
it is like lighting a fire in empty space—
it will eventually extinguish itself.

Such cultivation is true cultivation of the self.
Only when done thusly
can people and people both rest in peace within the Dao’s boundless expanse,
otherwise, they gain nothing from the Dao
and instead invite blame.

Confucius said:
*"Being without yet appearing as having,
emptiness without yet seeming full,
restraint without yet becoming expansive."*

His insight into what others despise is fitting indeed.

For those who cultivate the Dao in this manner,
they can be free from the need to seek external validation,
and also avoid the distress of external blame.

**Chapter 42: Loss and Gain**
The Dao gives birth to **One**,
**One** gives birth to **Two**,
**Two** gives birth to **Three**,
**Three** gives birth to all things.

All things carry **yin** within and embrace **yang**,
and the **succession of qi** brings harmony.

What people detest are the lonely, widowed, and childless,
yet kings and nobles prize such titles.

Thus, some things are diminished yet gain,
while others are increased yet lose.

What people revere, I also teach:
*"The strong and arrogant will not meet their end."*

I shall take this as my guiding principle.

The Daoists, from beginning to end, refine and cultivate,
holding **emptiness and nonexistence** as their ultimate essence.

The **Primordial Heavenly Sovereign**,
whose Dao-name is **Emptiness-Naturalness**,
expresses this very meaning.

From emptiness comes reality—
this is called **true reality**.
From nonexistence comes existence—
this is called **true existence**.

If one does not embrace emptiness nor nonexistence,
not only are the **Seven Emotions and Six Desires** stifled,
but the true spirit and original nature are also blocked,
leaving no means to respond to all things or transform the **yang spirit**.

Even if one practices **observing emptiness and illumination**,
and clings with even a trace of stubborn resolve,
it is still a case of the mind controlling the mind,
directly obscuring the original face beyond recognition.

In summary, **emptiness and nonexistence** are the Dao’s essence,
**harmonious succession** is its function.
If humans can align with this,
then the Dao is nearly attained.

The **Tàishàng** says: *"The Dao gives birth to One"*—
what does the Dao possess?
Only **emptiness**.

Yet within the utmost emptiness,
a single **qi** stirs—
and heaven and earth are born.

Thus it is said:
*"There was a **cosmic unity** before heaven and earth were formed."*

Before the **Ultimate Void**,
it was **mixed and chaotic**—
just this one emptiness.

When it moves, it becomes **yang**;
when it stills, it becomes **yin**—
this is the *"**Great Polarity** of the *Yijing*, from which the **Two Complements** arise."*

This is the saying:
*"The Dao gives birth to One, One gives birth to Two."*

In the human body,
it is within the **subtle and indistinct**,
that **One Awareness** splits open—
the opening and closing of **Heaven and Earth**,
the ebb and flow of **qi**—
stillness and condensation form **yin** and **essence**;
movement and circulation form **yang** and **qi**.

If there is no **true intention** of the **Primordial Spirit** to govern them,
then **yin** and **yang** scatter in disorder,
and there is no way to generate life and attain the Dao.

It is clear that between the **two qi of yin and yang**,
the **Primordial Spirit** and **true intention** are greatly relied upon to maintain their balance—
this is the saying: *"Two gives birth to Three."*

From this,
**one yin and one yang**,
**one movement and one stillness**,
**qi** flows in transformation,
its sovereignty remains unchanged—
and all things are born endlessly—
this is the saying: *"Three gives birth to all things."*

Some say:
*"Heaven One generates water, yet metal also generates water;
Earth Two generates fire, yet wood also generates fire;
Heaven Three generates wood, yet water also generates wood;
Earth Four generates metal, yet soil also generates metal."*

Using the **Five Phases** to explain the **Primordial Sovereign’s** meaning of *"One, Two, Three, and all things"*—
this is all strained interpretation;
it is not as clear as the theory:
the Dao is the **Ultimate Void**,
One is the **Great Polarity**,
Two are **yin and yang**,
Heaven One and Earth Two combine to form Three—
this is the explicit reasoning.

*"All things carry yin within and embrace yang,
and the **succession of qi** brings harmony"*
clearly shows the Dao as the **Primordial Qi of Emptiness-Naturalness**,
transforming into **yin and yang**,
and all things are born from **yin and yang**.

**Succession** means **central harmony**—
if **yin and yang** lack this central **qi**,
then the center has no master, and the master is unsettled.
Even the birth of things is difficult,
let alone cultivating the Dao?

The *Yijing* says:
*"Heaven and Earth in their **primordial state**,
all things become **pure and complete**."*

It is evident that the **Three Treasures**—**essence, qi, spirit**—
must all be fully realized,
so that **yin and yang** unite with the **Great Polarity** and become inseparable.

If **yin and yang** are distinct yet the **Great Polarity** is lost,
then the **transformative power** falters,
and the generative mechanism of all things is extinguished.
Generally speaking,
even in refining the Dao and alchemical elixirs,
one cannot do without **true yin and true yang**,
but without the **Great Harmony’s Primordial Qi**,
the elixir cannot be formed,
and the Dao is difficult to attain—
for the Dao is originally the **Great Harmony’s single qi**, structured and complete.

Generating humans or immortals
is but one principle,
the only difference lies in whether one follows or opposes it.

The **Primordial Qi** is the substance,
while **yin and yang** are its functions—
thus the Dao of **Golden Elixir** is attained.

Observe the kings and nobles,
whose rank is highest,
whose fortune most exalted—
yet they call themselves *"lonely"* (*gu*), *"widowed"* (*guǎ*), *"unworthy"* (*bùgǔ*).
What is their intent?

For the lofty are prone to peril,
the full to depletion—
like lightning’s flash, followed by thunder’s strike.
Only those who do not cling to their height,
who do not boast of their nobility,
but instead cultivate a heart of humility and gentleness,
may long preserve their wealth and power,
and escape ruin.

Thus, what common folk abhor—
*"lonely," "widowed," "unworthy"*—
are the very titles of kings and nobles.

If the **Dao** is the supreme treasure of Heaven and Earth,
how can those who cultivate it ignore the virtue of humility and gentleness?

The *Classic of Documents* says:
*"Fullness invites loss; humility invites gain."*
None have ever escaped this truth.

Gain does not begin with gain—
it must first be lost, then gained anew.
Loss does not begin with loss—
it must first be gained, then lost.

Thus, riches and poverty,
prosperity and adversity,
extreme bending yields to stretching,
and extreme stretching must bend again—
this is the cyclical **Heavenly Dao**,
the natural order,
even Heaven and Earth cannot evade it,
how much less can mortals?

Ah! Human affairs are thus—
how different is the **Great Dao**?

The adept seeks the **One Yang’s Revival** (*yī yáng lái fù*),
yet first must all entanglements be extinguished,
pure and tranquil **Harmonious Qi** alone remaining,
with no trace of restless ambition.

Only through ceaseless diminishment—
until nothing remains—
does **True Yang** arise within the congealed **group yin**,
bringing no small benefit to the self.

But if one arrogantly prizes their talents,
boasts of their wisdom,
their mind will not be empty,
and their ambition will be full—
none escape being misled by their own thoughts,
or consumed by errant fire.

To seek gain and instead suffer loss—
this is the way of all things under Heaven,
not only in the cultivation of the **Dao**.

As for all worldly affairs,
they are but illusory phantoms,
unworthy of fixation—
yet still, people take them as lessons for the uninitiated.

Know that the **Great Dao of Golden Elixir**
concerns the present moment,
yet its consequences stretch across eternity—
how can it not be taken as law?

Thus the **Supreme Sage** said:
*"What others teach, I too teach."*
What is taught?
**Ultimate gentleness** alone.

If one does not employ gentleness but instead relies on force,
they will be like the tyrants of the world—
ravaging and plundering,
yet none escape the law’s net,
or meet a good end.

Thus, the proud and violent court death,
while gentleness is the path to life—
this is truly the essential method of cultivation.

My teaching to others begins with gentleness and weakness—
how can the adept afford to neglect it?

The *Awakening to Reality* says:
*"From emptiness, the **Primordial Qi** is born,
from **Qi**, **yin and yang** arise.
**Yin and yang** form the **Three Bodies**,
and the **Three Bodies** give rise to all flourishing things."*
This is the meaning of:
*"One generates Two, Two generates Three, Three generates all things."*

At the outset of seated meditation,
one must first extinguish all emotional ties,
sweep away delusions,
attain utter emptiness and stillness,
indistinguishable from stupor—
neither fully asleep nor awake.
This is the undifferentiated **Hun-Dun** state,
the very **Dao**.

Suddenly, a stir—
a dim awakening.
After this movement,
all phenomena seem void,
yet a single **Spirit** moves alone—
embracing the **Primordial**, preserving the **One**—
some call it **True Mind**,
others **Right Awareness**,
or the **Buddha’s Perfect and Equal Awakening**.

At this moment, there is but one mind,
no duality of thought.

If the **Yang** generates and the **medicine** matures,
and its vigor is robust and harmonious,
then apply the **Two-Phase Method**:
**First Phase**: Collect and return to the **Palace**—one phase.
**Second Phase**: Seal and consolidate in the **Furnace**—another phase.

This is the **One Movement** as **Yang**—**Yang** governs ascent;
the **One Stillness** as **Yin**—**Yin** governs descent.

Observe the strength of the **Qi’s** movement—
if it is now fully vigorous,
then begin the **River-Vehicle’s** circulation,
with the **Four Phases** of collection:
**Boiling, refining, ingestion, and alchemy**—
thus **True Mercury** is purified.

This is the **collection of Yang** and **union with Yin**,
all arising from the **One**.

Even in a single breath,
a single opening or closing—
**Qi** divides from **One** into **Two** without fail.

Yet the **heart’s essence** and **kidney’s qi**,
the **heart’s yin** and **kidney’s yang**—
all depend on **True Mind** for collection,
refinement, copulation, and harmonization.

Thus, **yin and yang** as **Two Qi**,
united with **True Mind** as the **Three Bodies**,
all unfolding naturally—
no contrivance, no artificial forcing.

Yet it must begin with humility and retreat.
The slightest trace of arrogance or self-strengthening—
at best, the **Elixir** is ruined;
at worst, life is forfeited.

Thus it is said:
*"The violent do not meet a natural death—
I take this as my lesson."*

Students must know:
Before attaining the **Elixir**, rely on a **quiet and still** mind;
after attaining it, nourish with **gentle and harmonious** intent—
beware of excessive thought or worry,
of pride or self-assertion.

This is the **supreme key within the key**—
let the student take heed!

Otherwise, filled with delusions,
how can the **Dao** endure?
Such cultivation is **blind alchemy**—
all rigidity, so that even attainment becomes loss.

Such practice is **blind cultivation**—
without medicine or **Elixir**, yet rushing to collect and refine,
not only burning in vain,
but surely damaging **Spirit** and **essence**.

The harm to body and mind is no small matter—
yet still they refuse to blame themselves,
instead blaming the **Dao** as false,
the **Golden Elixir** as untrustworthy:
Such a one truly does not know the **Dao** as **Way**.

Only in **utter emptiness** and **utter gentleness**,
by preserving the mind in **quiet stillness**,
and nourishing the **Qi** in **harmony**,
can the **Dao** fail to succeed?

This speaks of the **Daoist**’s cultivation—
to heal ailments, prolong life,
ascend as an immortal, become a sage—
all rests on the **Three Treasures** alone.

Yet **essence** is not the **essence of copulation**,
but the **Primordial True Suchness**—
a luminous **Spirit**—
as when it was said:
*"Vague and indistinct, yet within it, forms exist."*

This arises from **emptiness**, and **emptiness** is the **Dao**. *"The **Dao** gives birth to One"* means **emptiness** generates **essence**, and **essence** is **nature**. **Qi** is not the **Qi** of breathing, but rather *"the Primordial True Suchness, a single **Qi** diffused"*—as was said before: *"Vague and indistinct, within it, things exist."*

This arises from **One**, and **One** is **essence**. *"One gives birth to Two"* means **essence** generates **Qi**, and **Qi** is **life-force**. **Spirit** is not the **Spirit** of deliberation, but rather *"the luminous radiance alone, clear and unwavering"*—as was said before: *"Dim and dark, within it, **essence** exists, that **essence** most true, within it, **faith** is."* From Two, the myriad transformations arise; **Two** is **Qi**, and *"Two gives birth to Three"* means **Qi** generates **Spirit**—**Spirit** being the **Primordial True Suchness** and the **True Will**.

All are transformations of the **Great Harmony**, a single **Qi**. Only by **gentleness** and **harmony** can one nurture it, and thus attain it.

If one clings to **One** with **impatience**, or harbors a **violent and harsh** **Qi**, these are of a different kind, far from the **Dao**.

Even preserving the body is difficult—how much more so to aspire to immortality! Hence the **strictures against excess**.

The **Taoist Master** teaches through **endurance of suffering** and **compassionate mercy**, so His words are thus.

Confucius expounded the *Book of Changes*, and in the **Modest** hexagram, he thrice expressed his intent, while the **golden person’s tilted vessel** and such demonstrate His profound instruction—is this not the same meaning?

**Chapter Forty-Three: The Benefit of Non-Action**

*"The **Dao** of utmost **gentleness**,
ranges across the **Dao** of utmost **firmness**.
There is no entering into the **Dao** of no interstice—
thus I know the benefit of **non-action**."*

The **Dao** of **silent instruction**,
the **benefit of non-action**,
the world few can attain.

What is the **Dao**? It is the **Primordial Chaos**, a single **Qi**.

Before Heaven and Earth opened,
this **Qi** existed in the **void**.
After Heaven and Earth were formed,
this **Qi** dwelled within Heaven and Earth.

This **Qi** was eternal before Heaven and Earth,
and does not perish after them.
When Heaven and Earth obtained this **Qi**,
they became the **Dao**.
The **Dao** being Heaven and Earth,
the **Dao** is contained within them.

Only Heaven and Earth can embrace this **Qi**,
thus their eternal transformation.
The **eternal unobstructed** is this **Qi**,
the **endlessly flowing**, sustaining all beings—also this **Qi**,
for all is interconnected by one **Qi**.

The Sage models himself after Heaven and Earth.
His **sincerity** within is what manifests outwardly;
though inner and outer differ,
the **Qi** remains undivided.
His fulfillment of self is what perfects others;
though self and others vary,
the **Qi** is never split.

What is its nature?
It is **emptiness**.

When one **Qi** is clear, all **Qi** is clear;
**emptiness** is never obscure—
when one **Qi** is clear,
all **Qi** is illuminated.

Confucius said:
*"To govern is like the North Star in its place,
while all the stars naturally bow in reverence."*

Mencius said:
*"The virtuous man surpasses the transformations,
his **Spirit** flows with Heaven and Earth above and below."*

This is the **sincerity** that,
without pause or delay,
can naturally be one with the **Qi**,
permeating and sustaining all.

Thus it is said:
*"The greatness of the world, from me it is at ease.
The multitude of beings, from me they are nourished.
The vastness of past and present, from me it is connected."*

The Sage’s **Dao** is truly boundless.
Reflecting upon this,
what is the limit of the universe?
The **Dao** can encompass it—how vast is it!
Even gold and jade, the firmest of substances,
the **Dao** can penetrate them—is it not **firm**?

Yet it is heard in the *Book of Odes*:
*"The mandate of Heaven, serene and ceaseless."*
And again:
*"The carriage of Heaven, silent and odorless."*

Here is **gentleness** beyond compare.
Though Heaven and Earth lack this **Qi**,
they would be inert and useless.
Though beings lack this **Qi**,
they would be dull and lifeless.

With it, they live;
without it, they perish.
This is *"the **Dao** of utmost **gentleness**,
ranging across the **Dao** of utmost **firmness**"*—
for without **Qi**, there is no substance.

Great as the **Three Thousand Worlds**,
small as dust or hair,
all are contained within this **Qi**.
Not only is it of utmost **gentleness**,
but it is also of **utter nothingness**—
is this not what Confucius meant by:
*"See it not, hear it not,
touch it not—yet it cannot be dismissed?"*

How can there be separation?
When things reach ultimate **gentleness**, they lose their use;
yet the **Dao**’s ultimate **gentleness** alone sustains the world’s utmost **firmness**.
When things reach **utter nothingness**, what purpose do they serve?
Only the **Dao**’s **utter nothingness** governs all existence.

This is but the **Primordial Chaos**, a single **Qi**,
flowing without obstruction.

Thus the **Taoist Master** says:
*"Thus I know the benefit of **non-action**."*

Moreover, Heaven and Earth act without **action** yet transform,
the Sage governs without **action** yet rules.
No single person or thing escapes their influence—
is this not the **Qi**’s all-encompassing and boundless sway?

Within the human body,
the **vast and pure **Qi** flows naturally,
not requiring forced circulation,
but spontaneously irrigating the whole frame,
filling every hair and pore—
its benefits are indeed profound.

As for the **Dao**’s instruction,
the ancients taught by **example**, not by words.
The **Dao** of teaching is truly not twice as real as the **Dao** of non-teaching.

Heaven does not speak, yet the seasons proceed;
the Sage does not speak, yet the world transforms.
To observe the **North Star’s** upright posture,
or the **Sage’s** humble bearing—
are these not of the same **Dao**?

Thus it is said:
*"The **Dao** of silent instruction,
the **benefit of non-action**,
the world few can attain."*

Ah, how profound!
This describes the **Dao**’s natural **non-action**,
encompassing Heaven and Earth,
nourishing all beings,
rooted in the **Great Harmony**, a single **Qi**,
flowing through the cosmos,
penetrating Heaven and humanity,
with neither great nor small,
nor hidden nor revealed,
all fully present.

Here is the **Dao** of utmost **gentleness** yet able to birth utmost **firmness**,
of **utter nothingness** yet able to contain all existence.
Thus, one **Qi** connects to all **Qi**,
one movement sets all in motion—
echoes in an empty valley,
each sound answered in kind.

This **Dao**’s divine mystery—
none can surpass it!
Who but the **Sage** could grasp it thus?

If one is but a novice,
yet holds true faith in mind,
and sets a bold resolve,
following the **Way** and keeping to its path,
from shallow to deep,
from low to high,
beginning with forced effort,
yet through time becoming natural,
only then can one pierce through its essence.

One must endure, endure again,
and bear, bear once more,
through the twelve **times** of day,
without stirring **disgust** or **retreat** in heart.
When deep understanding dawns,
one rests in **tranquility**, nurturing life’s **vitality**,
encountering abundance on all sides,
then suddenly comprehends the **Great Harmony’s** intent—
truly without a false word.

As for refining the **foundation of cultivation**,
the ancients said:
*"When **essence** is born, there is a chance to refine the **medicine**,
when the **medicine** appears, there is a chance to gather it."*

**Primordial Qi**, born of **God**, generates **essence**,
**essence** then generates **Qi**—
this is the principle by which Heaven and Earth give birth to all things,
in accord with the **Dao**.

If one follows it naturally,
though able to bear sons and daughters,
**essence** will be drained,
**Qi** will scatter,
until exhaustion and death.

The **Great Harmony** pities mortal men,
lost in the **waves of birth and death**,
endlessly **revolving**—
thus it reveals the **Dao of inverse cultivation**,
returning to the **root**, restoring youth to age,
strength to weakness,
until one attains **immortality** and **sainthood**,
forever beyond **birth and death**.

At first, it teaches one to **attain emptiness** and **nurture stillness**,
from the state of **ignorance and unawareness**,
seeking the place of **awareness and knowledge**.
The *I Ching* says:
*"Motionless yet responsive—
this is the **Dao** of **primordial transformation**."*

**Postnatal essence** has form,
**Primordial essence** leaves no trace—
yet within the **vague and indistinct**,
there is substance.
This is called **"the Mysterious Pass"**—
when stirred, the **Great Polarity** is established.
From here, one **concentrates the spirit** in **emptiness**,
**unites the Qi** in **voidness**,
**illuminates the mind** within,
observing the **breath’s** ebb and flow—
opening and closing,
rising and falling,
ascending and descending,
retrieving it back to the **Central Palace**,
bathing and nourishing it warmly.

In a short while, at the **boundless depths**,
suddenly a thought arises from within the **rules**,
a **Qi** emerges from **emptiness**—
this is **essence generating Qi**.

This **Qi** is not of form—
if it were of form,
then it would have **beginning and end**,
**limits and measure**—
how could it embrace Heaven and Earth,
penetrate the finest **hair**,
be present in all things,
and shatter all **rigidity**?

This **Qi** is the **root of life** for all things—
to grasp it is to live,
to lose it is to die.
Though it is **uttermost gentleness**,
it can command the **uttermost firmness**,
though it is **uttermost nothingness**,
it can govern the **uttermost existence**.
The ancient **immortals** likened it to **medicine**,
for it can cure **aging and sickness**,
and nourish the **embryo of immortality**.

Thus they say:
*"The elixir of **prolonged life**,
the **returning-soul nectar**,"*
and also:
*"The **True Person’s** root of **long life**."*
Indeed, it is the **world’s most precious treasure**.

The ancients declared:
*"A thousand weights of gold
cannot be exchanged for even a thread of this **Qi**."*

If mortals can obtain this **Qi**,
then **long life** is assured.

Yet the method of gathering it
must also **harmonize with the center** and **align upright**,
only then can one avoid **calamity**.

If one has **medicine** but fails to blend it properly,
or **refine it** poorly,
or takes it at an **improper time**,
or nourishes it without **right method**,
and misjudges the **fire’s** strength—
whether **cultivating** or **harvesting**,
whether **old** or **tender**,
whether **heavy** or **light** in dosage—
any error will bring disaster,
just as when **yin and yang**,
**cold and heat**,
shift out of season,
leading to **heavenly calamities**,
and all things perishing.

Students who **harmonize** the **Great Harmony’s** teachings,
both **earlier and later chapters**,
and **apply them** with care,
will never go astray.

I reveal this **cultivation method**—
truly worth more than **ten thousand gold**!
If one can **penetrate** this **secret**,
then in all things there is **grasp**,
the **elixir of long life** can be obtained,
and the **lands of the immortals** will pose no difficulty.

**Chapter Forty-Four: The More You Hoard, the Greater Your Loss**
Which is more **intimate**—
**name** or **body**?
Which is more **abundant**—
**body** or **wealth**?
Which brings more **suffering**—
**gain** or **loss**?

Thus it is said:
*"The more you cherish, the greater your **expenditure**,
the more you hoard, the greater your **depletion**."*

**Knowing contentment** brings no **humiliation**,
**knowing restraint** brings no **peril**—
thus one may **endure long**.

Men who **covet name** and **wealth**,
do not all believe
that **name** can exalt their **body**,
that **wealth** can nourish their **body**—
if the **body** lacks **name**, it will be **obscured** and **unrenowned**;
if the **body** lacks **wealth**, it will be **wretched** and **hard-pressed**.

Thus, those who **pursue name**,
forsake their **body** without **regard**;
those who **covet wealth**,
lose their **body** without **hesitation**.

The merchant **Jia** said:
*"The greedy man **serves wealth**,
the **resolute hero** **serves name**."*
Human nature is thus—
the same throughout **ancient and modern times**.

Yet consider:
Which is more **intimate**—
**name** or **body**?
**Name** is **external**,
**body** is **internal**.
Men seek **name** for their **body**—
why then **endanger the body** for **name**?
Is it not that **name** is **distant**,
while the **body** is **near**?

Which is more **abundant**—
**body** or **wealth**?
The **body** is **noble**,
**wealth** is **base**.
All seek **wealth** for their **body**—
why then **lose the body** for **wealth**?
Is it not that the **body** is **scarce**,
while **wealth** is **plentiful**?

How **foolish** their thoughts are!

If one has **name** yet **nature** is lost,
or one has **body** yet **name** is not exalted—
which is **gain**, which is **loss**?

To **forsake the body** while **wealth** remains **empty**,
or to **lose wealth** yet **life** remains **firm**—
which endures, which perishes?

By this reasoning,
it is better to **gain the body** and **lose name and wealth**,
than to **gain name and wealth** while **losing the body**.

Moreover, those who **covet name and wealth**,
**diminish their spirit** and **harm their life**—
their **excessive love** brings **great expenditure**;
those who **hoard deeply**,
employ **scheming** and **injure** their **body and mind**—
their **abundant storage** brings **great depletion**.

To be **held in esteem** is to be **feared by the state**,
to **accumulate wealth** is to be **resented by the **Creator****.

From ancient times,
men of **great power** have **suffered calamity**,
those with **much wealth** have been **put to death**—
how many such cases are there!

All because they **failed to restrain** themselves,
did not **withdraw** in time,
**greedily pursued** more without **end**,
thus **incurring the people’s enmity**,
and **earning Heaven’s punishment**.

Only those who **know contentment** and **know restraint**,
remain **steady** and **unshaken**,
free from **humiliation** and **peril**,
without **danger** or **inclination to fall**,
and thus **long preserve** their **body**,
even unto their **descendants**.

「Thus **Rang Li** avoided **the calamity of Goujian**,
and **Zhang Liang** attained **the leisure of the Red Pine Immortal**.

Truly, they were men who understood the **subtle Dao**!

Let those who follow them take heed of this **cultivated wisdom**.

This is an analogy of **knowing contentment and restraint** to **stoking the furnace and nurturing the elixir**,
using **renown** to liken **the illusory landscape**,
and **wealth** to liken **the medicinal alchemy**.

Those who **covet the phantom landscape** are often **ensnared by demons**,
and those who **pursue external transport** seldom escape **fate’s retribution**.

Before the **medicine returns to the cauldron**,
it must be **stirred by flame** to refine it;
once the **medicine enters the tripod**,
the **flame must be restrained** to cultivate it.

If the **flame is unrestrained** and one fails to **halt its force**,
not only will the **elixir fail** and the **tripod overturn**,
but one will **invite sickness and misfortune**,
and even **lose life and burn the body**,
falling into **great peril and ruin**.

And yet, the **Great Dao** is **void and boundless**,
with no grand distinction among mortals.

Some **lust after fine wine and delicacies**,
**adorn themselves with beauty and splendor**,
**hoard gold, jade, pearls, and precious stones**,
**pursue palaces and towers**;

Others **face temptations from celestial and earthly demons**,
**ghostly and divine demons**,
as **various trials** come to test their **Dao**—
some **boast of becoming immortals**,
others **claim to be true humans**,
**proudly asserting they have ascended to the Celestial Hall of Precious Ascension**—
yet because of **a single stray thought**,
their **spirit is depleted**,
and **many fail to attain the Great Dao**.

Or some **mistakenly revere the spirit**,
and **the Three Corpses** become their **bane**,
**believing there is a body beyond the body**,
while the **golden elixir**, the **supreme treasure**,
**is squandered in an instant**—
such cases are also numerous.

All these misfortunes arise from
**the flame being unrestrained** and **fire not halted**,
**the elixir failing to return** and **not being nurtured**,
thus **their aspirations scatter** while **their spirit dissipates**,
**outer distractions disrupt** and **inner essence perishes**.

For those who **cultivate the Dao**,
**illusory renown, illusory forms,**
**illusory landscapes, and illusory shapes**,
must be **dismissed with a single stroke**,
**taken with no attachment**—
so, in **knowing restraint and contentment**,
and **constantly nurturing the spiritual elixir**,
they **attain ultimate goodness**,
and **never face decline or ruin**.

**Chapter Forty-Fifth: Great Attainment as Deficiency**
Great attainment appears as deficiency,
yet its **utility is unexhausted**.

Great fullness seems as emptiness,
yet its **utility is inexhaustible**.

Great rectitude appears as bending,
great skill as clumsiness,
great eloquence as stammering.

**Calmness overcomes coldness**, **tranquility overcomes heat**,
**purity and stillness** are the **right path for the world**.

The **Dao** is naturally **void and boundless**,
**moving in accordance with Heaven**,
**acting in alignment with innate nature**,
**uniting with the creation of Heaven and Earth**,
**sharing the eternal rise of Sun and Moon**—
**having nothing yet possessing all things**,
**doing nothing yet accomplishing all things**.

When the **Great Dao** is not yet fully attained or filled,
there are still **traces of action**,
**forms and images** that can be glimpsed,
and one feels **self-satisfied and content**,
**overwhelmed with joy**;

Yet when **great attainment** is finally achieved,
it seems as if **deficiencies abound**,
and **great attainment** appears as **no attainment at all**.

When **great fullness** is reached,
it seems like **void and formless emptiness**,
and **great fullness** appears as **never having been filled**.

Is this not **the more one studies, the worse one becomes**,
**the more one excels, the more one is disparaged**?

No, it is not so.

For the **Dao** is inherently the **original good** of mankind—
**pure, empty, and without trace**,
**still and settled**—
when **form and spirit** are both **perfectly refined**,
and **one’s essence merges with the True Dao**,
**self and Dao become one**—
what **attainment** or **fullness** remains?

If **attainment and fullness** still persist,
then **self and Dao remain as two**,
and **one has not yet reached the realm of divine transformation**.

Thus, for those who **cultivate the Dao**,
**the more they possess, the more they relinquish**,
**the more they accumulate, the more they diminish**—
they **never see attainment or fullness**.

Hence, **great attainment appears as deficiency**,
and **great fullness as emptiness**.

Therefore, **all phenomena are emptied**,
and **one alone embraces the True**.

**Taking things as causes**,
**responding naturally to times**,
**truly filling Heaven and Earth**,
**penetrating past and present**,
**universal and unchanging**—

How could its **utility ever be exhausted**?
How could its **application ever be spent**?

When the mind is empty as the sea,
and the spirit still as a mountain,
yet one feels utterly useless—
then suddenly, when **qi** expands boundless,
when **virtue/power** is supreme and vast,
how could it be so straightforward?
Yet one finds the burden of constraint unbearable.

When **spirit** is wondrous and boundless,
capable of constancy and change,
how could it be so ingenious?
Yet one only feels the stupidity of ignorance.

When speech is plain yet meaning profound,
when words are few yet truth subtle,
speaking only when necessary,
uttering only when the time is right—
how could eloquence be so vast?
Yet one feels only stammering,
as if unable to speak at all.

Because of this—
this bending, this clumsiness, this stammering—
the heart aligns with **emptiness**,
the will with **humility**,
**virtue/power** with **breadth**,
and deeds grow ever more sublime.

This is indeed the **Dao** returning to **emptiness**,
learning returning to **spontaneity**.
Uniting with the turning of Heaven and Earth without knowing,
passing with the sun and moon without awareness—
thus its attainment is great and enduring.

If one follows the principle of the **Great Polarity** (*Taiji*),
harmonizing with the constant of yin and yang,
and endures prolonged cultivation—
when **lead** and **fire** are fully refined,
withstanding nine winters of cold;
when the **elixir** is solidified and settled,
forgotten after three cycles—
is this not what the **Supreme Sage** meant by
*"agitation overcomes cold, stillness overcomes heat"*?

When clarity and purity reside within,
when **emptiness** and **spirit** are unobstructed,
returning to the **mysterious and tranquil Heaven**,
embodying the **harmonious and peaceful**—
what agitation, what cold, what stillness, what heat remains?

The scholar cultivates a **quiet and still** mind,
transforming the seasons of cold and heat,
and thus the **true qi** of the body solidifies—
the **true Dao** of the world is established.
What need is there to fear the proliferation of **heterodox paths**?

This is the **Dao** in its utmost **plainness** and **constancy**,
its utmost **emptiness** and **nothingness**.
Before one attains the realm of **emptiness** and **nothingness**,
within the domain of the **ordinary**—
one only feels **fullness**, not **deficiency**;
one only feels **advantage**, not **shortcoming**.

Thus the **Supreme Sage** said:
*"The less one has, the more one gains;
the more one has, the more confused one becomes."*
The proverb states:
*"A great bell makes no sound;
a full jar makes no echo."*
Truly not false.

**Great virtue** is not virtue—
thus it is virtue;
**great action** is non-action—
thus it is action.
Not mere modest words.

The **Dao** originates from **emptiness** and **qi** as one.
Because one has **attainment**, there is **no attainment**;
because one has **no attainment**, that is **true attainment**.
Thus, the higher the **Dao**,
the lower the heart;
the greater the **virtue/power**,
the more humble the will—
this alone aligns with the **Dao** perfectly.

If one has even a slight excess,
one boasts arrogantly, proud and haughty,
contemptuous of others, domineering—
then the absence of **Dao** and **virtue/power** is evident.

Thus the **Supreme Sage** said:
*"In learning, one gains daily;
in the **Dao**, one diminishes daily—
diminish again, diminish,
until one reaches **nothingness**."*
Only then does one attain.

The scholar must not view **cultivating the Dao** and **refining the elixir**
like the arts of a hundred craftsmen,
assuming progress if it seems beneficial.
If **cultivation** is rooted in **emptiness**,
and the **work** reaches **forgetting**—
this is progress.
When **forgetting** is forgotten,
one has entered the realm of **transformation**.

For the scholar of the **Dao**,
retreat is advancement,
weakness is strength.
**Emptiness** becomes **fullness**,
**non-action** becomes **action**,
**reversal** is **uprightness**,
**subtraction** is **increase**.
Thus, the progress or stagnation of learning
depends solely on whether the heart has **forgotten** or not.

---
**Chapter 46: When the World Has the Dao**

When the world has the **Dao**,
warhorses are driven out to manure fields.
When the world lacks the **Dao**,
warhorses breed in the suburbs.

No crime is greater than **desire**,
no calamity greater than **insatiability**.
No fault is greater than **greed**.

Thus, **contentment** is **contentment**—
**contentment** is always **contentment**.

When the world has the **Dao**,
rulers and people alike are at ease.
Conquest and warfare are unnecessary,
so horses are released into the forests,
fields are plowed and tilled,
merely to fertilize the land.

When the world lacks the **Dao**,
chaos has already taken root.
Bandits and thieves rise in succession,
weapons clash daily—
without armies, how could peace be maintained?
Thus, warhorses are raised in the wilds,
awaiting the state’s needs.

The rise and fall of horses
depend on whether the world has the **Dao** or not—
their fate is bound to the world’s great order.

Alas!
How could we have rulers as rulers,
ministers as ministers,
fathers as fathers,
sons as sons—
upholding **ren** and discussing **righteousness**,
cherishing **poetry** and explaining **rites**,
restoring the **Dao** of the **Great Peace**?

Yet in times of enduring peace and prosperity,
luxury and indulgence flourish,
desires multiply, and greed emerges.
Having this, yet lacking that—
exotic skills and decadent arts
are arrayed before them.

Fine clothes and delicious food are insufficient,
so they crave palatial chambers and jade terraces.
A thousand-*li* domain seems too narrow,
and they covet the vastness of ten-thousand-*li* borders.

Alas!
Within, **lust** runs rampant;
without, **debauchery** spreads.
Adding to this is the pursuit of profit and haste,
exhausting military might and reckless warfare,
endless demands and insatiable greed—
internal and external chaos ensues.
What need is there for waiting for ruin?

All this stems from a single **desire** at its origin.
When **desire** arises, **greed** is born;
when **greed** is born, one seeks what is not yet obtained,
and fears losing what is already possessed.
If this continues,
**principles** remain unbroken,
**calamities** do not arise,
the state does not collapse,
the world does not face annihilation—
this is impossible.

Thus it is said:
*"No crime is greater than **desire**."*
If there were no **desire**,
how could **greed** arise?
If **greed** does not arise,
then the winds of **deceitful harmony, deceitful completion, and deceitful beauty**
would not easily reappear.

It is also said:
*"No calamity is greater than **insatiability**."*
If a person’s **desire** does not arise,
this **will** is always satisfied,
this **heart** is always at ease,
needing nothing from the world,
harming no one.
When things go well, one accepts them;
when things go ill, one accepts them as well—
from where would **calamity** arise?

Again, it is said:
*"No fault is greater than **greed**."*
If one knows **contentment**,
one naturally guards one’s **portion** and accepts one’s **fate**,
following the **times** and listening to **Heaven**,
without flattery or arrogance,
without strife or seizure—
walking the **straight and easy path**,
long basking in the **Great Harmony**—
what **fault** could there be?

「How much more so when the true heart is inwardly clear,
and the true nature inwardly settled,
when one cultivates oneself in stillness,
always finds joy within,
and follows one’s innate station without conforming to the external world.

Those who hold the **Dao/Way** of the world naturally preserve their world;
those who hold a state naturally preserve their state;
those who hold life naturally preserve their life.
What they fear is that once the **desire** arises,
they cannot cut it off and eliminate it,
ultimately reaching **extreme extravagance** and **unbridled greed**,
yet finding no way to save themselves.

Is there a **Dao/Way** to **desire** the world and obtain it?
From ancient times, the fate of **gain** and **loss** depends on a single thought.
A single thought, once set in motion, is difficult to reverse—
thus, vast calamity is born.
This is how a **misguided thought** leads to a **catastrophe** of unimaginable scale.

Life and death hang by a thread,
a slender chance.
If one can preserve this slender chance,
one will surely open a **vast and auspicious plan**.
This is how a **resolute thought** brings endless blessings.

Thus, one must know:
A single thought of **desire**,
though its beginning may be slight,
its end is great—
can one not be cautious?

Therefore it is said:
*"**Contentment**, **contentment**, always **contentment**."*
Those who do not know **contentment**,
the more they seek, the more they lose,
and the more they lose, the more they seek.
Thus, they exhaust their strength and weary their spirit,
endlessly toiling and laboring—
what benefit is there in this?

Do they not know that **prosperity** and **adversity**, **gain** and **loss**,
are governed by **Heaven**,
not by human effort?
Why toil and weary oneself daily,
when it is better to rest and let things be as they are?

If one knows **contentment**,
they follow their natural state,
acting without contrivance—
what need for worry or concern?
Without scheming or seeking,
where would they go without attaining what is good?
Let people take heed of this!」

「This likens the world to the human body,
and compares the horse to using fire to refine elixirs.

If a person possesses the **Dao/Way**,
then their vital essence is abundant and their **qi** is full—
why bother refining elixirs?
To follow and preserve it naturally is sufficient.

If they lack the **Dao/Way**,
then their essence is depleted and their **qi** dissipates,
and they must resort to nurturing their body and mind with the **elemental spirit** (*yuánshén*) and **true breath** (*zhēnxī*).
But at the outset of practice,
they must first cultivate externally,
hence the saying:
*"**Steed of war** is born in the wilderness."*
When their vitality flourishes and the medicine takes effect,
they then proceed with the **fire-alchemy** (*huǒdān*), **retreat of the elixir** (*tuìdān*), and **warding-off formulas** (*fǎ*)—
methods of **field combat** (*yězhàn*) and **defending the city** (*shǒuchéng*).
They gather all into the furnace,
slowly warming and nourishing.

When impurities are fully purged
and pure radiance arises,
the world is once again at peace,
the state without strife,
and the horse returns to **Mount Hua**,
hence the saying:
*"**Drive the steed back** to till the fields."*
Yet the chaos of the world
and the peril of one’s own life
all stem from a single moment of **desire** (*yù*).
If not cut off,
it festers and grows unseen,
until the vital essence is exhausted,
and life and body cannot be preserved—
how tragic is this?

Whenever **desire** arises in a person,
they must seek to fulfill it before finding satisfaction.
Even if all things go as they wish,
how can the **abyss of desire** be filled?
How can **greed** and **covetousness** be sated?
Once they have **the Rampart Pass** (*Lǒng*), they gaze upon **Mount Shu** (*Shù*)—
endlessly turning, never ceasing—
those who hold the world lose the world,
and those who possess life and body,
how could they not forfeit their very lives?

The *Shijing* says:
*"Without scheming or seeking,
why should they go without attaining what is good?"*

Only those who know **contentment** can dwell in tranquility,
constantly abiding in the **Dao/Way**-aligned realm of Heaven.
They need no **meritorious deeds** to mend their flaws,
but simply follow their natural course,
unifying with Heaven itself.

The **Supreme Sage** (*Tàishàng*) warns:
*"No sin is greater than **desire**"*—
these three words teach one to **block the source** (*dùjiān*) and **guard against the subtle** (*fángwēi*), cautioning against the **deceit of seeking** (*qīqiú*) and the **labor of contrivance** (*jiāo láo*).
This is the same teaching as Confucian **"solemn vigilance"** (*shèndú*) and Buddhist **"right awakening"** (*zhèngjué*).

Have scholars ever grasped this truth?
**Chapter Forty-Seven: *The Uncontrived Attainment* (*Bùwéi ér chéng*)」

To dwell within yet know the world beyond,
to gaze not through windows yet perceive the **Dao/Way** of Heaven.
The farther one roams, the less one knows—
thus the **Supreme Sage** acts without action, names without seeing, achieves without doing.

The **virtuous** (*junzi*) possesses all things within,
yet without stepping beyond his courtyard refines his being.
The shifts of the world, the changes of hearts,
and the mechanisms of preserving life while extinguishing ruin,
of reversing chaos into order—
all are crystal-clear within his mind.
Is this achieved through mere craft or calculation?
Truly, for **qi** and self share a single source:
to exhaust the logic of one’s own nature
is to encompass the logic of all under Heaven.
Thus, one knows the world without leaving home.

The ancients shaped creation through mind alone,
without opening windows to conceal their radiance,
yet the silent **Emperor** (*Dì*) and the ceaseless **Heavenly Mandate** (*Tiānmìng*)
and the mysteries of growth, decline, and transformation—
all are fully illuminated within.
Is this gained through mere conjecture?
It is because **Heaven and humanity are one continuum** (*tiānrén yīguàn*):
to cultivate one’s own life
is to align with the **Heavenly Command** (*Tiānmìng*).
Thus, one perceives the **Dao/Way** of Heaven without peering through windows.

If one wanders distant lands, inquiring into the world’s affairs,
or consorts with noble friends, probing the subtleties of **Heavenly Mandate**—
one may gain some knowledge.
But I fear that if one seeks knowledge outside oneself,
rather than within,
one’s understanding, though gained, will be lesser than what lies near.
Hence it is said:
*"The farther one goes, the less one knows."*

The **Dao/Way** shines brightly between doors and windows—
why abandon what is near to chase what is far?
Mencius said:
*"Speaking near yet pointing far is good speech;
upholding brevity while applying broadly is good **Dao/Way**."*
Reflecting thus, the more one studies, the nearer yet farther it becomes,
the more concise yet expansive it grows—
how can the near and the concise be neglected?

Thus the **Supreme Sage** embraces **Oneness** (*yī*), containing the three,
observing emptiness while cultivating stillness,
never leaving his door or hut,
never treading the dusty markets,
dwelling among wood and stone,
roaming with deer and swine,
not moving a step, not befriending a soul—
yet it seems lonely.
But when the spirit is settled, wisdom arises;
though he does not act, his deeds surpass those who do.
Though he lacks knowledge, his insight surpasses those who know.
Common folk take what they see as their pursuit,
but the **Sage** does not seek what is seen—
thus, day and night, he remains steadfast (*qiánqián*),
resting thought and abandoning artifice
in the unseen and unheard,
guarding against deceit and contrivance in the unmanifest.
With only inner knowing and no outward perception—
it seems as if in utter darkness—
yet the **Ultimate** (*Wújí*) gives rise to life.
Though unseen, it is ever manifest;
though unnamed, it is ever present.

As for the myriad beings of the world,
the hidden realms of spirits and ghosts—
all are governed by this **Uncontrived** **Dao/Way**.
With order and essence,
beginning and ending are fulfilled.
What is feared is being ensnared by perception,
clinging to names and forms,
boasting of achievements,
hoarding insights—
thus the mind scatters, unable to unify in **virtue** (*dé*).
The heart becomes cluttered, unlike the **Great Void** (*Tàixū*).
Hence the **Dao/Way** fails to manifest, and **virtue** remains unchanged.
No wonder one exhausts oneself in vain across lifetimes, gaining nothing.

If applied to governing the world,
one cannot harmonize with **Heavenly Principle** (*lǐ*) to achieve natural unity—
without action, the world would spontaneously align.
If applied to cultivating the self,
one cannot refine the **Void** and merge with the **Dao/Way**—
without action, the body would naturally attain true transformation.
What use is mere external seeking?

Thus the **virtuous** must be ever vigilant in solitude,
for the essence of humanity and the origin of **Heavenly Mandate**
lie in what is sought yet naturally known.
This speaks of the **Dao/Way** taking **Uncontrived** as its foundation,
**solemn vigilance** as its key—
thus, **Uncontrived** yet nothing remains undone,
**Unknowing** yet nothing remains unknown.
Yet this is not the **Uncontrived** of withered wood and dead ashes.
Earlier, I spoke of *"all phenomena as emptiness, one spirit alone illuminating"*—
this is the true intent;
and *"one awakening sets motion in motion, one **Yang** gives rise to life"*—
this is the **Original Qi** (*yuánqì*).
Gathering medicine or refining elixirs
is but the cultivation of these two—**nature** (*xìng*) and **life** (*mìng*).
Without true intent, where would **nature** depend?
Without true **qi**, how could **life** be refined?
To gather true intent with true **qi**,
when the two merge into one,
it returns to the **Supreme Ultimate** (*Tàijí*)’s beginning—
this is called the **Elixir** (*dān*).

Within **Uncontrived**, there must still be action and doing;
within **Unknowing**, there must still be knowing and awareness—
only then does one not fall into emptiness or cling to form.
When cultivation deepens, emptiness becomes form,
and form becomes emptiness.
In time, both are forgotten,
and one merges seamlessly with **Dao/Way**—
thus attaining its utmost harmony.

If one does not know the **Dao/Way** of humanity,
one may still perceive the **Dao/Way** of Heaven.
Confucius said:
*"How does Heaven speak?
The four seasons proceed, and all things are born"*—
this is the essence of **Uncontrived**.

This is the **Supreme Dao/Way**.
**Uncontrived** is **Heavenly Nature** (*tiānxìng*),
**Action** is **Heavenly Mandate** (*tiānmìng*);
**Unknowing** is the **Original Spirit** (*yuánshén*),
**Awareness** is the **Original Qi** (*yuánqì*).
Between Heaven and Earth, there must be both two and one
to achieve transformation—
both divine and not divine,
not divine yet divine—
thus attaining the marvel of **Oneness** within duality,
divinity within transformation.
This cannot be exhausted by words.
It lies with you, the cultivator:
nurture the **Void** and the **Spiritual** (*xūlíng*),
constantly guard the **Void** and the **Awakened** (*xūxǐng*).
Thus **nature** and **life** endure,
and the **Great Dao/Way** may be fulfilled.

Do not confuse **Uncontrived** with **Action**—
each side taken in isolation,
though seemingly orthodox,
leads to divergent paths.
Consider this well.

**Chapter Forty-Eight: *Daily Diminishing for the Dao/Way, Daily Increasing for Learning* (*Wéidào rì sǔn, wéixué rì yì*)**

To take the world requires **Uncontrived**—
yet when affairs arise, it is insufficient to take the world.
Scholars memorize texts and master arts,
valuing teachers and friends,
seeking much and hearing much—
only then do thoughts flourish and wisdom emerge.

The knowing grows ever broader, the deeds ever more abundant—
only then can one wield the brush to compose with ease,
or swing the axe to shape with mastery.
Thus it is said: *"To cultivate learning is to daily increase."*

But for the Dao/Way, the reverse holds true.
If one takes wide reading and mastery of ancient texts as their pursuit,
yet fails to turn inward, to self-reflection and single-minded guarding,
then though their knowledge expands and insights multiply,
their mind will scatter and their spirit grow chaotic.
Though learning increases, the Dao/Way diminishes—
until, in time, they are lost in the undifferentiated *Taiji*,
drowned in the boundless, with nothing remaining.

Thus the cultivator of the Dao/Way must proceed like peeling a banana leaf or unraveling silk—
the more they strip away, the less remains;
the more they draw forth, the more it vanishes,
until they reach the realm of *wu-wu* (non-being of non-being).
Only then have they attained it.

When cultivation reaches this stage,
naturally the divine and mysterious become unfathomable,
transformations boundless:
when gathered, it is present;
when dispersed, it is absent.
Desire one, and it is one;
desire ten thousand, and it is ten thousand.
The sun, moon, and stars revolve at my command;
winds, clouds, thunder, and rain obey my orchestration.
What greater power is there?

Yet scholars, taking one principle and discarding another,
like eating sugarcane—
once consumed, it is cast aside—
still return to nothingness.
Thus it is said: *"To cultivate the Dao/Way is to daily diminish,
diminish again and again, until reaching Uncontrived—
Uncontrived, yet nothing remains undone."*

Observe those who seek to take the world:
they cannot avoid raising armies and mobilizing troops,
brandishing weapons and comparing blades—
how could they remain in *wu-wu*?
Yet even amid affairs, they must return to *wu-wu*,
lest their mind and virtue scatter,
and their strategies fail to emerge.
Thus commanders issue stern orders:
their ears hear nothing external,
their eyes see nothing external,
their heart wanders to no external schemes—
only then can they respond with spontaneity,
and take the world as effortlessly as turning the palm.

Otherwise, chaos reigns—
the more affairs multiply, the more the mind is disordered;
the more disordered, the more the spirit grows dim.
Before the enemy even arrives, they cannot remain composed;
at the first clash, their ranks dissolve into confusion.
To hope for victory in such a battle is surely difficult.
What then of pacifying the eastern barbarians while the western nomads rise anew—
one crisis unresolved, another erupting—
if they do not know to remain still to govern movement,
or rest to await exertion,
few escape defeat and ruin.
Anciently, those who fled in defeat,
whose cities fell in ruin,
all succumbed to the perils of *having affairs*.

Thus military strategy employs tactics of surprise and ambush.
When the enemy sees banners filling the horizon,
trees trembling as soldiers,
even facing overwhelming odds,
their courage shatters—
none dare stand their ground.
Only by treating affairs as though they were nonexistent,
casting aside all entanglements,
and maintaining single-minded inner illumination—
like Zhuge Liang amidst a million troops,
his headgear and fan untroubled,
effortlessly at ease—
could he preserve the western Shu,
prolonging the Han’s survival in its hour of peril.
Had his mind been entangled in affairs,
his inner clarity would have faltered,
his spirit lost its master—
like Xu Shu, distracted by his mother’s return to Cao Cao,
unable to offer further counsel,
and thus failing to aid the emperor in restoring the realm.
How could such a one take the world?

This speaks to those cultivating the Dao/Way:
if they see only increase, not diminution,
their mind grows clouded and the Dao/Way fails to coalesce.
Thus it is said: *"Virtue/power is singular—
when divided into two or three, it grows dim."*
Only by refining and forgetting in turn,
forgetting and refining anew,
can they avoid becoming obstacles to the Dao/Way.
If memories persist, clamoring without cease,
they become the true concern of our path.
Thus they must gradually dissolve and vanish into absolute nothingness—
only then is *xing* (nature) fully realized.

Then, from nothingness, true being arises,
and one can move unseen among ghosts and spirits,
transformations beyond measure.
The text speaks of *"the world"* as a metaphor for the Dao/Way,
*"taking the world"* as cultivating it,
*"having affairs"* and *"no affairs"* as *having-for* and *Uncontrived*.
If one remains pure and Uncontrived,
they are in harmony with the primordial *qi*—
what difficulty is there in attaining the Dao/Way?
This is the essence of *taking the world*.

But if they manipulate *having-for*,
entirely relying on later-heaven exertion,
they fall into tangential paths—
this is the opposite of *taking the world*.

Some may ask: *"How can gathering herbs or refining elixirs be Uncontrived?"*
Know this: when *qi* ascends, follow its ascent—
not by first intending to ascend.
When *qi* descends, follow its descent—
not by first intending to descend.
Even if gathering is exhaustive,
if refining is manifold,
it is still pure spontaneity,
with not a trace of contrivance—
though it appears as *having-for*, it remains *Uncontrived*.
Those who merely swallow saliva or inhale *qi*
are far from attaining the elixir or the Dao/Way.

---
**Chapter Forty-Nine: *The Sage Has No Fixed Mind* (*Shèng wú chángxīn*)**

The sage has no fixed mind,
but takes the minds of the people as their own.
To the virtuous, they are virtuous;
to the unvirtuous, they are also virtuous—
thus their virtue is perfect.
To the trustworthy, they are trustworthy;
to the untrustworthy, they are also trustworthy—
thus their trust is perfect.
The sage moves among the people,
blending their heart with the world’s,
as though it were their own.

The people fix their ears and eyes upon the sage,
yet the sage treats them like children.
The sage’s mind is vast and hollow,
clear and luminous—
it contains all things without exclusion,
yet illuminates all things without attachment—
like a still mirror reflecting light,
its radiance unfailing.
It adapts to each thing as it arises,
without a trace of preconception—
how profound is its clarity!
The great encompasses all without limit,
the small is contained without omission—
beauty and ugliness, good and evil,
none escape its embrace—
how boundless is its capacity!

Yet what mind is this?
Neither forced nor contrary to nature—
thus it is said: *"The sage has no fixed mind."*
Meaning the sage does not anticipate before arrival,
nor cling after departure,
nor linger in the present—
merely responding to each situation as it arises,
adapting to circumstances,
and taking the minds of the people as their own.

And what mind do the people have?
Only to love what is good and hate what is evil.

Thus the sage rewards and encourages the people’s goodness,
while guiding and nurturing even their faults.
Goodness and fault alike—
the sage embraces them all with boundless virtue and measure.
So the good are joyfully drawn toward virtue,
while the faulty spontaneously turn from vice to righteousness—
this is the **"virtue of goodness."**
When the ruler cherishes goodness, the people dare not fail to follow.
The response is inevitable—
its mechanism unfailing.

The sage reveres and honors those of faith,
while loving and longing for even the faithless.
Faith and doubt alike—
the sage encompasses them both with unshaken sincerity.
So the faithful find harmony and deepen their trust,
while the doubtful rise resolute and turn doubt into faith—
this is the **"virtue of sincerity."**
When the ruler cherishes sincerity, the people dare not fail to act in truth.
Their reciprocal bond—
how divine its reach!

When the people’s virtue grows thick,
what doubt remains?

Since all share this heart,
and this heart shares this principle,
the sage observes myriad minds with a single mind,
harmonizes myriad principles with one principle.
Though the world be vast, it is contained by sincerity;
though the people be many, they are gathered by righteousness.
Even among the wise and deceitful, the loyal and false—
however unequal they may be—
the sage is impartial, treating all with equal kindness.
With open sincerity and public fairness,
placing his heart before theirs,
he unifies the world under one principle—
and the people daily improve without knowing it.
How profound is this transformation beyond mere teaching!
How could later methods—
exhorting filial piety, urging loyalty,
demonstrating rites and righteousness—
ever compare?

Thus it is said:
*"He blends the world’s hearts in tranquil unity."*

For he views the world as one family,
unites all under heaven as one body—
his compassion all-embracing,
his unity with the people complete.
Thus the people revere his virtue, embrace his kindness,
love him as parents, honor him as a deity,
look to him as teacher and guardian.
All they hear or see is measured against the sage’s voice and bearing—
nothing else exists in their awareness.
Hence it is said:
*"The people fix their ears and eyes upon him."*

If the people’s expectation of the sage is thus,
what else could the sage be but—
governing the multitude with gentleness,
leading them with tenderness,
like parents to children:
whether wise or foolish, virtuous or base,
his love is equal;
his guidance sincere.

If such governance fails to transform the world,
it has never been.

Thus is the way of **non-action**.

Compared to those who rely on speech and law,
the difference is as vast as heaven and earth.

In the *Classic*, **"the sage"** signifies the heart,
**"the world"** signifies the body.
The sage’s cultivation of self
does not exceed nurturing the **original spirit** and **original qi**.
