Yet humans possess an original spirit and a mundane spirit;
original qi and mundane qi.
At the outset, how could one avoid stray thoughts
or fail to stir mundane breath?
Only those who understand for long
see the original spirit emerge.
If I meet them with an even mind—
even if stray thoughts linger,
I still meet them with an even mind.
If the original spirit fails to appear,
it has never been.

Once the original spirit arises,
cultivation has its master—
then one must quietly guard the **elixir field**,
nurturing the original qi.
If the original qi moves of its own accord,
I meet it with harmonious breath—
even if mundane breath does not yet cease,
I still meet it with harmonious breath.
If the original qi fails to generate,
it will not.

Know this:
The original spirit is obscured by the mundane spirit,
like a bright mirror long sealed by dust—
unless diligently polished, how could it suddenly shine?
The original qi is drowned by mundane breath,
like white silk stained by oil—
unless thoroughly cleansed, how could it return to purity?
Yet if at this point a restless mind arises,
or an evil thought stirs,
you seek the original spirit as essence,
while the mundane mind only grows stronger.
To seek enlightenment—
is it not difficult?
To seek the original qi as master,
while mundane breath only intensifies—
how could this be easy?

Yet the sage’s governance of the world
does not distinguish good from evil, true from false—
he treats all with a single heart of kindness and sincerity:
the good he affirms as good,
the evil he also affirms as good;
the faithful he affirms as faithful,
the faithless he also affirms as faithful.
A childlike purity, complete in his embrace.
This is the **void**, this is the **Way**.
From the void, spirit naturally arises;
from the Way, qi naturally flows.
Things unfold spontaneously, without contrivance.
Otherwise, if the heart is not void,
the Way does not yet exist—
how could the spirit then manifest,
or the Way’s qi endure?

Cultivating self and governing the world—
the Way is one, the principle is singular.
To know governance is to know self-cultivation;
to grasp external causes is to grasp inner truth.
Thus, using this principle to illustrate,
its instruction to learners is profound and direct.

The student must diligently guard the true and constant,
skillfully nurture the void—
then the original spirit and original qi
will naturally return.

If a stray thought arises,
or a foreign breath stirs,
fear that the more you cultivate, the farther the Way recedes.
Let the student beware!

**Chapter Fifty: The Depth of Generative Generativity**
Birth and death.
Those who cling to life—thirteen in ten;
those who cling to death—thirteen in ten;
those who, in living, tread death’s path—thirteen in ten.
Why so?
Because of their excessive attachment to life.

The art of nurturing life is such that
one who walks the land meets no rhinoceros or tiger,
and one who enters battle is unharmed by weapons or blades.
The rhinoceros finds no place to strike with its horn,
the tiger no place to extend its claws,
the blade no place to lodge its edge.
How so?
Because there is no place of death within them.

Heaven and Earth generate all things—
though myriad transformations and changes occur without end,
their Way remains within one yin and one yang alone,
swelling and waning, advancing and retreating, preserving and perishing.
Between them, too, lies nothing but the flow of *taiji* principle and qi.

Life and death are like day and night—
cycling endlessly, ceaseless in their motion,
just as life and death cycle, alternating without pause.
Yet within this flexing and returning,
the interplay is inherently dual, without deviation.

Speaking of those who enter and exit birth and death:
three in ten thrive when encountering yang qi,
three in ten perish when meeting yin qi.
Even those who, against Heaven and Earth’s constant yin-yang,
gain life from yang, are no different from others.
Once born, knowledge unfolds, desires arise,
worldly cravings stir, and actions multiply—
thus they exhaust their essence, deplete their spirit,
shorten their years, and lose their lives—
these are the three in ten who court death’s domain.

Thus, the tally of life does not match that of death;
the workings of yin exceed those of yang.
Does the generative principle of *zào huà* fear exhaustion?
Yet the primal *taiji* is soundless, scentless—
moving, it generates yang; still, it generates yin.
It manifests as the Five Phases,
scatters as myriad things,
exhausting all oddities, beyond speech,
yet never lacking.

Thus, to follow it brings endless life;
to defy it leads to return to the source.
When life is extinguished, death is renewed;
when death is stilled, life stirs anew—
this is the Way of *zào huà*’s interdependence,
the virtue of ghosts and gods in utmost sincerity,
embedded from primordial beginnings to this day,
unchanged.

Were it otherwise, all things would live without dying,
and the teeming multitude would fill the cosmos—
Heaven and Earth would lack space to contain them,
and the mechanism of generation would falter.
It is the ebbing that rests the swelling,
the voiding that preserves the full—
this is how the principle of life endures.

If one understands the depth of Heaven and Earth’s generativity,
it lies in this ebb and flow of fullness and emptiness.
Thus, observing Heaven’s Way, holding to Heaven’s course,
one seeks life’s pulse amid death’s mechanisms,
and breathes vitality from death’s grasp.
Following the seasons’ orders—
spring’s growth, summer’s fullness, autumn’s harvest, winter’s storage—
embracing the functions of generation, flourishing, contraction, and repose,
those who align with Heaven’s order and reverse its application
are like Heaven and Earth, nurturing all things
to sustain an unobstructed cosmos for ten thousand years;
while the human body, returning to its root and origin,
becomes a saint beyond compare through the ages—
all this stems from this very principle.

This is not the task of the feeble-minded.
Only those skilled in nurturing life
employ the inverted laws of yin-yang,
the counteractive methods of *zào huà*—
descending to ascend, advancing to retreat;
in stillness, observing effortlessly; in motion, awaiting yang’s birth;
urgently propelling the *dòu* handle, leisurely guarding the *yào* furnace;
returning to *taiji*, restoring to utmost sincerity;
emerging with entry, enduring with exit—
throughout the ages, unchanging;
harmonizing with sun and moon, uniting with Heaven and Earth…
Thus, even when confronting great peril,
they emerge unscathed.
Even the fierce tiger or rhinoceros
become companions;
even the sharpest blades
dissolve into nothingness.
Why fear the rhinoceros’ horn, the tiger’s claws,
or the blade’s strike,
and calculate life and death in an instant?
How is this possible?
Because they dwell in no place of death.

「And what of the Sage who refines their nature and establishes their life’s purpose over years?
When gathered, they take form; when dispersed, they become *qi*.
The sun and moon revolve at my command,
The winds and thunders obey my bidding.

Though the tiger and rhinoceros rage fiercely,
And blades though sharp and weapons though mighty,
How can they reach the *formed*?
Only the *formless* can subdue the *formed*—
How can the *formed* ever constrain the *formless*?

Alas! All things with form have birth and death,
But the Sage, formless, transcends birth and death—
Indeed, they master the primal source of life and death.
To all things, this appears as birth and death,
Yet who would dare to harm them?
This is the tenfold fullness of Heaven and Earth,
The threefold interplay of three yang and three yin.

Humans inherit the three yang of *Buqian* (乾) and meet the three yin of *Bunkun* (坤) at death.
This is the eternal principle of Heaven and Earth—
One yin and one yang, bending and stretching,
Coming and going, cyclically interdependent.
Without yin, there is no yang;
Without death, there is no life.

Thus, retreat and concealment are but ways
To nurture the profound *virtue/power* of life’s continuity.
Yet in abandoning themselves to passion and desire,
Destroying principle and losing their humanity,
Their death differs from others—
For those who follow yin-yang’s order,
Are embraced by *Taiji*’s undifferentiated wholeness,
And move with the true *qi* of the two polarities.

But those who defy *zào huà*’s methods—
Their original *yuánqì* (元气) is lost,
And they succumb to excessive *yīnxié* (阴邪).
Hence they say, *“Ten and three”* (十有三).

Ten is the fullness, the Dao’s embrace of Heaven and Earth;
Three is Heaven’s One generating Water, Earth’s Two generating Fire,
Heaven One and Earth Two merging into Water and Fire’s trio.
Moreover, Heaven’s One generates Water,
Metal is born from Water;
Earth’s Two generates Fire,
Wood is born from Fire—
Thus the four *xiàng* (象) are complete.

Soil has no fixed place,
Roaming through the four *xiàng*,
And when the purest essence of *Taiji* rules
The *qi* of yin-yang,
And governs the mechanisms of *zào huà*,
It becomes *Wújí* (无极) in Heaven and Earth.

Yet in the human body, *Taiji*’s principle reveals itself:
At rest, as the *yuánshén* (元神) of silence and scentlessness, undivided;
In motion, as the *liángzhīliángnéng* (良知良能) of timely action and true intention—
Together, they form the *Wǔxíng* (五行).

This is the shared *virtue/power* of life’s continuity,
In all things—when present, it dwells in them;
When absent, it returns to the boundless *Tàixū* (太虚).
It neither increases nor decreases with life and death of beings.

Thus the adept at preserving life,
Entering their chamber to meditate in stillness,
Captures the moment when one yang returns,
Holding it to ascend, to descend,
And to return to the furnace for nourishment—
Ninefold transformations of the elixir,
Tenfold perfection of the fire’s *candidate* (候).

“High is the Dao, and dragons and tigers submit;
Great is *virtue/power*, and spirits grow tender”—
What harm can the tiger’s horn, the rhinoceros’ claws,
Or blades and weapons inflict?

Observe the ancients:
In secluded mountain depths,
Rhinoceroses gathered in herds,
Wolves consorted in packs,
Yet willingly tamed, content to roam freely—
Not a few such tales exist.

Or lone horsemen charging forth,
Tribes surrendering their hearts,
Discarding armor, abandoning spears,
Revering them as divine,
Loving them like kin.

And further still—
Filial piety moving Heaven,
Bandits yielding sincerity;
Strict virtue and chastity,
Causing rogues to pause in guilt—
All from the *virtue/power* of utmost sincerity,
Which stirs them deeply.

Observe again:
The rhinoceros cannot strike with its horn,
The tiger cannot place its claws,
The blade cannot find its target—
This is no false claim.

For the essence of *lǐqì* (理气) is not the yin-yang of *zào huà*.
I can remain tranquil and serene,
Reaching the boundless light,
Yet what birth and death can touch me?

Those who know birth and death are but traces;
I can erase these traces,
Returning to the undifferentiated fate,
The harmonious Heaven—
Though traces of life and death persist,
The principle remains everlasting, unobstructed.
Why then rejoice in life’s fleeting pleasure,
Or dread death’s inevitable sorrow?

The ancient Sages sacrificed life for righteousness,
And died to fulfill *rén* (仁).
To them, the knife and saw were commonplace,
And the cauldron’s boiling a trivial matter—
Why was this so?
Because they had attained the inner truth,
And feared nothing externally.

Hence it is said, *“No place of death.”*

Moreover, the fullness of Water is seven,
Combined to make thirteen as well.
**Chapter 51: Revering the Dao and Honoring Virtue/Power**

The Dao brings forth life,
*Virtue/power* sustains it,
Things take form through it,
And circumstances perfect it.
Thus all things reverence the Dao
And honor *virtue/power*.

The Dao’s reverence,
The *virtue/power*’s nobility—
None command them, yet they persist naturally.
So the Dao brings forth life,
*Virtue/power* sustains it,
Nurtures and fosters it,
Perfects and matures it,
And nourishes it with care.
Life is given yet not possessed,
Action is done yet not relied upon,
Growth is guided yet not controlled—
This is called *mystic virtue/power*.

The Dao has no name,
No name means no limit.
This is the pure *qi* of emptiness,
The shared source of life’s continuity in Heaven, Earth, and all beings.
For it is neither having nor lacking,
Neither vast nor small,
Yet encompasses all things without exception—
Hence it is called *“Great Virtue/Power.”*

The Dao is the *Taiji* shared by all things;
*Virtue/power* is the *Taiji* inherent in each thing.
Thus all things draw life from it,
Rooted in the principle of the boundless void,
The *yuánqì* (元气) vast and all-pervading.
No matter great or small, hidden or revealed,
All depend on this Dao for life,
And entrust their essence to its fate.

Within it, yin-yang harmonize their principles,
And the sun and moon revolve in its depths—
Like grasses and trees:
Resting in day and night,
Nourished by rain and dew,
And thus strengthened at their roots.

This is the Dao bringing forth life,
And *virtue/power* sustaining it.
All things, receiving this nurturing,
Are transformed by its influence—
First they take on *qi*,
With time they develop form.
Thus they grow secretly, steadily,
Day by day, month by month,
Until humans become fully human,
And things become fully what they are—
Without flaw or omission.

Thus all things reverence the Dao,
And honor *virtue/power*.
For the Dao is the principle of life’s birth,
Without which there is no means to sustain life.

*Virtue/power* is the source of nurturing all things—
Without it, there is no way to store and cultivate.

How great is the reverence for the Dao,
And the honor for *virtue/power*?
Yet both are granted from Heaven,
And take things as their occasion.
No need for forced action—
Naturally aligned with the Way.
Without contrivance, effortlessly embodying *virtue/power*.
No one commands it, no one ordains it,
Yet ever thus—
Nothing compelled, all returns to the natural.

What is this Dao?
The grand central path of Heaven and Earth,
The essential key for sages to attain immortality and enlightenment.
If one seeks to cultivate the Golden Immortal,
How else but through the Dao?

Thus, one concentrates the spirit in the void,
Unites the *qi* in the boundless.
At the juncture of emptiness and stillness,
In the faint and obscure,
The primordial true *qi* emerges—
This is the birth of the Dao.

Once the Dao is born,
Then nourish it in stillness,
Draw from its motion;
Hold it straight within,
And all phenomena dissolve into emptiness.
Ever grasp, ever preserve—
Do not forget, do not abandon—
Then the foundation of *virtue/power* is established.

Yet if one follows the Dao to bring it forth,
The Dao will grow daily;
If one relies on *virtue/power* to nurture it,
Then *virtue/power* will flourish daily.
To grow and nurture—
Like things thriving and multiplying,
Until autumn arrives and ripening is inevitable.

When the Dao is thus complete and ripe,
And its cultivation and protection continue—
What difference is there from humanity?

Essentially, it is but returning to the initial state before form,
And restoring the undivided truth.

In truth, what is this "life"?
Its life is the self-moving *qi* of the void.
Why claim it as mine to possess?

Even when the signs of life appear—
Internally circulating the Heavenly Canopy,
Externally pushing the Dipper’s handle—
It seems like action,
Yet purely entrusted to nature,
Without the slightest arrogance or contrivance—
Is this not effortless yet not relying on effort?

To cultivate thus,
*Virtue/power* advances daily,
The Dao grows daily,
And natural transformation lies within reach—
Heaven and Earth governed by the mind—
Though myriad changes arise,
They cannot disrupt the steadfastness of one who holds the essence.

This is the unruling ruler,
Exceeding all rulers—
Is this not the profound and distant *virtue/power*?

This speaks of one who steals Heaven and Earth’s primordial *qi*
As the foundation for the elixir,
Then nurtures, sustains, stores, and grows it,
Returning to the original Heaven—
Thus attaining the Dao.

Yet to steal Heaven and Earth’s primordial *qi*,
One must first recognize the boundless gateway of the formless.

Where is this gateway?
Before Hongmeng’s division,
At the dawn of Heaven and Earth,
In the indistinct and chaotic—
A sudden stirring,
And the true mechanism moves of its own accord.
This is where the primordial *qi* resides,
And the cultivator must gather it as the elixir’s seed.

Like when the myriad yins congeal and close,
All things retreat and wither—
Yet when winter solstice’s yang returns,
The Dao is born.

Thus, one refines essence and preserves life,
Nurturing within the Eight Trigram Furnace.
Over time, the *qi* swells and fills,
Like all things flourishing in summer—
This is the accumulation.

Once things are full and ripe,
Flowers bloom into fruit.
Like autumn’s harvest of treasures complete.

In the human body,
Nurturing the embryo to return to its original form—
This is completion and ripening.

When things are ripe,
They return to their origin—
Like winter’s grasses and trees bearing fruit,
Leaves falling back to roots,
Returning to the three years of suckling,
Nine years of wall-gazing,
Refining the pure yang body,
Achieving the golden-colored *dharma-body*—
One must release all entanglements,
Hold no thoughts,
Empty and hollow,
Silently awaiting the yang’s birth.

Though life arises,
It was originally self-existent—
Yet one must not cling to it as one’s own.

Even with techniques of ascent and descent,
Advancement and retreat—
These are still contrivances,
Yet all must follow the natural movement of *qi*,
Without the slightest force or coercion—
Is this not effortless yet not relying on contrivance?

As *virtue/power* advances daily,
The Dao grows daily,
And the *yin* and *yang* forces refine,
Bathing and sealing firmly—
All are governed by the original spirit within.

This is having a master yet no master,
Ruling yet no ruler—
How profound and distant is this Dao!
Thus it is called *"Playful Virtue."*

**Chapter 52: The Origin of the World**
The world has a beginning,
Which becomes the mother of the world.
Having obtained its mother,
One knows its children;
Having known its children,
One returns to guard its mother—
And thus avoids peril throughout life.

Seal the *yui* (mouth),
Close the gate,
And labor no more for life.
Open and return to clarity—
No harm will come to the body—
This is called *"Following the Constant."*

The Golden Elixir—what is it?
Nothing but the primordial true *qi* of the first Heaven.
Ancients likened it to true lead,
To golden flowers,
To white snow,
To the initial bowstring *qi* of the White Tiger—
All metaphors,
Yet ultimately referring to the union of Heaven and Earth,
Where Heaven loses a *yang* and falls into the Earth’s domain,
And Earth, obtaining this Heavenly *yang*’s golden nature,
Becomes solid and forms the *kan* (water).
Thus it is called "golden"—
For it descends from the Heavenly Trigram,
And in the body is called *yang* essence.

Though this essence dwells in the water palace,
It is the primordial *qi* of the first Heaven,
Capable of becoming the mother of elixir cultivation.
The adept, refining medicine at the furnace,
Must force the *yang* lead from the water palace as the elixir’s mother.

Thus it is said:
*"All the body’s blood is pure yin,
The body’s yang essence few recognize."*
This yang essence—
Neither inside nor outside,
Not entering the six sense-gates,
Not among the six dusts—
Hidden in the form-mountain,
Seen yet unseen,
Heard yet unheard,
Yet ever generating and unceasing—
It is the true seed and great foundation of the body.

If the yin essence does not obtain this primordial yang lead as its mother,
The yin essence will scatter easily,
With no way to congeal into the elixir.

「Thus, the *qi* of this body—
Shared by all sentient beings of the same kind—
Is refined in the alchemical furnace;

Then the primordial *yang* *qi* of the first Heaven,
And the profoundest *yin* essence,
Emerge from the state of *void* extremity and tranquil stillness,
From the hazy and boundless depths—
This is the **upper elixir mother**, also the **mother *qi****.

With *yang* fire, one compels it to ascend and surge,
Until it reaches the *nīhuán* (泥丸, "mud pellet"),
Where it mingles with the long-accumulated *yin* karma,
Descending to the upper palate,
Transforming into sweet dew—
This is the **yin essence**, also called the **child *qi****.

From this point, it descends to the **heavy palace** (重楼),
Pouring into the **divine chamber** (神房),
To be ingested and swallowed,
Warming the gentle divine fire,
Nurturing this primordial *yang* *qi* and profoundest *yin* essence—
This is what the **Supreme Sage** (太上) meant by:
*"Having obtained its mother, one knows its child;
Having known its child, one returns to guard its mother."*

At first, the mother *qi* descends to embrace the child,
Then the child *qi* clings to the mother in abiding,
And finally, child and mother unite in harmony,
Nourishing each other—
As *yin* and *yang* interact yet return to their original source,
Even death cannot harm one.

From this, one must firmly uphold the **Rule of the Middle** (规中),
Where a single spirit is contained within—
Endeavor to keep inner thoughts from straying outward,
And external thoughts from entering in,
Silencing speech, sealing the six sense-gates,
Continuously and subtly,
Without wavering or ceasing,
Neither aiding nor neglecting,
Whether diligent or not—
So throughout life, one attains the **Golden Immortal** (金仙) state.

Otherwise, if one seeks external gain,
One has already lost the internal treasure.

As it is said:
*"The mouth opens, the divine *qi* scatters;
The mind is disordered, the fire’s power wanes."*

Those who value the external over the internal—
Their vital essence is already spent,
Their life-root where is it?

Hence, they are doomed throughout life.

If one can seal the **mouth of the *yīn* vessel** (塞兑) and close the gates,
Preserving essence and gathering *qi*,
The mother *qi* and child *qi* merge to form the elixir.

The ancients said:
The **Emperor of Primordial Beginning** (元始天王),
Hangs a single **millet pearl** (黍珠) in the void,
Seemingly present yet not,
Seemingly empty yet not empty—
Only those who silently perceive with a focused mind
Can truly see it.

Nothing is smaller than this elixir,
And those who can see it are the truly wise.

When the *yang* *qi* emerges,
The entire body softens like silk—
This is the **ultimate gentleness**.

If one can guard this ultimate gentle *qi*,
Without mixing a single thought,
Without adding a single perception,
Over time, it will naturally manifest as **vast and surging *qi***,
Piercing the heavens and filling the sun.

Thus, *"Guarding gentleness is called strength."*

Yet at the outset of practice,
The divine light descends to illuminate the **sea of *qi*** (气海),
Then the fire steams and the water boils,
The **golden essence** (金精) radiates,
Like tides, like fire,
Like mist, like smoke—
I must then **withdraw sight and return hearing** (收视返听),
Safeguarding its brilliance,
Guiding it back to the **earthly crucible** (土釜),
Restoring it to my primordial *yang* *qi*—
Small gains extend life and ward off illness,
Great gains achieve immortality and sainthood—
What harm can come to the body then?

Otherwise, old age, sickness, and death,
Will arrive in the blink of an eye—
How can one not grieve?

All this is self-made,
Not predestined by Heaven.

If one does not love the Dao,
Does one not love one’s body?

Do not bring ruin upon yourself,
For regret will come too late.

This is the **true and constant Dao** (真常之道),
Only the **perfected** (至人) can follow its constancy,
Without defying its Way.

Thus, day by day, month by month,
One reaches the **divine and boundless mystery** (神妙无方),
Where transformations are unfathomable.

The saying goes:
*"Steadfastness is the foundation for becoming a Sage;
A vacant heart is the vessel for bearing the Dao."*
Can one afford to neglect it?

These words speak of the **true *yang* primordial *qi***,
Which originates from the moment one receives *qi* and takes form—
For life arises from *qi*,
And *qi* is not trifling.

Consider before birth—
Did one not possess this *qi*?
After death—
This *qi* is not necessarily extinguished.

The **primordial *yang* *qi*** (先天一气),
Suspended in the **Great Void** (太空),
Exists within things when there are things,
Returns to the Great Void when there are none.

All phenomena within Heaven and Earth,
From the most mundane to the grandest,
Have birth and death to speak of—
But this *qi* is neither born nor extinguished,
Neither defiled nor pure,
Neither increased nor diminished,
Empty yet not empty,
Not empty yet empty—
The most divine and most wondrous of all,
Hence the **initial *qi* of all ten thousand things** (天下万物生生不息之始气).

If a Daoist practitioner knows how to obtain this **initial *qi***,
Then the **Way of Longevity** (长生之道) can be attained,
And the **state of Immortal Sagehood** (神仙之位) can be verified.

For the Immortal Sage is not inherently wondrous—
It is merely the *yang* *qi* lingering upon the *yin* essence,
Refined over time until the *yin* essence transforms into *yang* *qi*,
And the *yang* *qi* returns to the *yang* spirit—
As it is said:
*"This body is not that of a common mortal,
But the Immortal Sage from the Great Luo Heaven."*

If one were to cultivate only one aspect,
How could one attain **perfection of both form and spirit** (形神俱妙),
In harmony with the Dao,
Reaching the **ultimate extremes of odd and even** (极奇极变),
The **Sage’s self-illuminating essence** (至圣自灵)?

Thus, when the fire reaches its proper stage,
The golden elixir manifests—
Naturally, the mouth forgets speech,
The tongue forgets taste,
The nose forgets scent,
The eyes see nothing,
The ears hear nothing—
As it is said:
*"The elixir field holds treasure,
Naturally, one forgets all external appearances."*

This is the principle:
The one who **lightens the external** (轻外) **heavens the internal** (重内),
The one who guards the internal **forgets the external** (忘外)—
An unchanging law.

Yet before attaining the elixir,
One must still seal the *yīn* vessel and close the gates,
To accumulate essence and gather *qi*—
And know that seeing the **small elixir** (小丹) is wisdom,
While guarding the **Great Harmony** (太和) brings natural strength.

Entering *qi* with the spirit,
Storing spirit within *qi*—
Suddenly, a single **millet pearl** appears,
Its light permeating the **Dharma Realm** (法界)—
This is the moment when the **golden elixir radiates** (金丹焕发),
And the **Great Dao is about to manifest** (大道将发).

At first, one uses the spirit to descend and await the *qi*,
Then the *qi* arises,
And one again uses the spirit to compel it upward,
Driving it back to its source—
Thus attaining the **elixir of longevity** (长生之丹),
And what harm can come to the body then?

This is achieved through constant practice and unceasing effort.

**Chapter Fifty-Three: Walking the Great Dao**
*"Let me, with some awareness, walk the Great Dao,
Only fearing the Way’s own rules."*

The Great Dao is so smooth,
Yet people prefer shortcuts.
Morning is so clear,
Yet fields grow overgrown,
Granaries remain empty.

Wearing embroidered silks, girding sharp blades,
Sated with food and drink, yet wealth overflows—
This is called the **thief’s staff** (盗竿).
How can this be the Dao?

The **gentleman’s Way** begins with husband and wife;
The **Sage’s Way** does not stray beyond yin and yang.
If one follows Heaven’s movement and acts in accordance with nature,
Cultivating virtue for oneself is **ren** (仁),
And transforming the world is **wisdom** (智).
Uniting inner and outer in perfect harmony,
Thus all measures find their proper place and all things flourish.

What need is there for contrived schemes?
What is there to fear?

Yet the Dao is fundamentally plain and ordinary,
Lacking any hidden strangeness.
In the latter ages, men weary of moderation and delight in the bizarre,
Thus straying into crooked paths and byways, unaware.

Take the court above:
Its laws and institutions are truly the means to civilize the people,
Yet the ignorant fail to recognize them,
And all are discarded as obsolete.
They favor the newly promoted while spurning old ministers,
Delight in constant change and upend governance.
Ancestral precedents are all swept away—
Like a man’s vital *qi* being drained.

The court lacks good governance, the countryside lacks models.
Thus, farmers abandon their fields to seek ease,
Land lies fallow, weeds and grasses grow unchecked—
The source of wealth is exhausted.
Expenditures grow ever greater,
Granaries are depleted, grain stores empty—
The reserves of wealth are spent.

They do not seek inner substance but only boast outward splendor.
Thus, garments must gleam with brilliance,
Blades must gleam with sharpness,
Feasts must overflow with delicacies,
Wealth must abound—
Yet they do not consider the depletion of the roots,
Only the rivalry of the branches.
To desire the inexhaustible and the unending,
To act without hindrance in all things—
Is this not delusion?

All this arises from rejecting the natural Way of Heaven,
From neglecting the ordinary, daily Dao.
It is like a thief stealing goods—
Hiding his head while exposing his tail,
Standing like a staff—
Visible only in shadow, not in form—
A metaphor for those who practice the Dao with falsehood for truth.

What of the Great Dao?
This is the sudden, clear knowing—
Not contrived, not forced.

The vast and boundless *qi*, the primordial unity of Heaven and Earth at its first opening—this is the **original *Zǔqì* of Pre-Heaven**. Just as Mencius, upon suddenly seeing a child fall into a well, had a surge of alarm, compassion, and distress—this is the **sudden knowing of true *liángzhī*** in my Dao.

The stirring of such true *liángzhī* is not difficult to recognize, yet to act upon it and sustain it forever is no simple matter. When it moves, the world before one’s eyes is clear—yet in the blink of an eye, one is drawn into transformation, desire arises, emotions stir, and unaware, it slips into the private knowledge of **Post-Heaven**. This is why it must be tempered and applied with caution—**its power is truly to be feared**.

Only when the **eye holds the wisdom-pearl** and the **chest wields the insight-sword** can one pierce through the demons of illusion and sever the threads of desire. From **plucking herbs** to **returning to the elixir**, all are the unfolding of true *liángzhī* into true *liángnéng*—a path smooth and unadorned, with no trace of the strange. This is why the **Great Dao is so effortlessly vast**.

Alas, though the **Great Dao is plain and ordinary**, those who seek swift progress and immediate results often stray from the **Kāngzhuāng Path**, venturing into narrow, twisted side trails. They mistake their own deviation for attainment, yet never awaken in their lifetime—**how lamentable!**

The body is like the morning sun: it longs for adornment yet fears annihilation. Only by **quelling evil and preserving sincerity** can one first purify desire and fully manifest Heaven’s principle. How could the **spiritual radiance** ever be allowed to fade? How could the **vital essence** ever be depleted? Without embroidered beauty, yet naturally resplendent; without oil and grain, yet fully sated—**why cling to external ornamentation or indulge in feasts?**

The **insight-sword** is keen—**no need for borrowed blades**. When the **Three Treasures** are perfected, **internal wealth** is inexhaustible—**no need for external riches**. Those who grasp this truly **steal the Heavenly *Língyáng* *qi*** to forge the elixir.

Yet today’s practitioners, abandoning **the Doctrine of the Mean**, rush toward **crooked paths**. Their bodies are thick with dust and filth—**how can they ever be cleansed?** Moreover, they recklessly provoke misfortune, losing their **Original *Yuányáng*** *qi*. Thus, they flit about in vain, never attaining the **empty void’s divine spirit**. When desolation reaches its peak, **the orifices are not entirely sealed**—**how can their vitality ever be restored?** When strength is exhausted and spirit drained, **there is no sign of repletion**. **Poverty is indeed to be lamented**—**how could their essence ever be whole?**

They boast of **external brilliance**, yet their adornments are mere pretense; they wield **blades of sharpness**, yet prize only the **waistband’s display**. **This is truly the end!**

Why do they not instead **learn how practitioners cleverly use the mechanisms** to **steal back the Original *qi***? The *I Ching* says: *“He who composes the *Yì* knows well how to steal.”*—just this is meant.

To abandon this and seek elsewhere is already **fragmented and extreme**—**how dare one claim it as the Great Dao?** The commentary below states that the phrase *“suddenly knowing”* is but a fleeting glimpse of clarity—**how could it ever be called true knowing?** Yet when tested in actual practice, one often falters in execution—hence the warning.

This reveals the **difficulty of the Great Dao**. The next section speaks of scholars who do not delve into the **root source** but only pride themselves on **external refinement**; who do not seek **true attainment** but only chase **empty fame**—just as one stands a staff and sees only its shadow, mistaking semblance for reality. **This is truly the thief of the staff.**

The lesson holds true. From ancient times, all who had the Dao—**their skin smooth and luminous, their hair crisp and bright**—these effects were shared by common folk, yet still not proof enough. **What of refining essence and *qi*?** When the **Yang Light** touches, **Yīnmái** cannot endure—just as frost and snow melt in the sun. Thus, even **aged illnesses** dissolve into **ulcers and pus**, expelled through the **large and small intestines**, not only for beginners but even at the stage of **Great Elixir Return**—**there are always transformations**.

The **Three Corpses and Six Bandits**, bleeding and oozing pus, their stench unbearable—**only those whose hearts are settled and whose faith in the principles is unshaken, who have truly penetrated the scriptures, can avoid this**.

One must know that **restraining desire and preserving sincerity**, **removing turbidity and retaining purity**—each layer involves the **dissipation of Yin *qi*** and the **subtle growth of Yang**. Practitioners of the Dao cannot ignore these **external manifestations**.

Do not fixate on **bodily glory** or **swift movement**—**these cannot be relied upon as proof**. Even **soaring through clouds, vanishing into ghosts**, **a thousand transformations in a moment, ten thousand miles in an instant**—**these cannot be trusted as the Dao**. For **strange and marvelous phenomena**, **heterodox and perverse teachings** will inevitably **lead one astray**, producing **monstrous deviations**. **Pity that a lifetime’s vitality is wasted on crooked paths!** To **aspire to transcendence** yet fall into **the Three Paths and Six Realms**—**is this not deeply regrettable?**

The essence of this chapter by the **Supreme Sage** teaches that **only by recognizing true *liángzhī* can one avoid error**. Yet today’s Dao practitioners seek only **smooth skin and strong bodies**—**they do not realize that external labor exhausts the mind, while the Original Fields lie fallow and the Treasure Granaries empty**. Many have already **damaged their Pre-Heaven essence**.

Though **Post-Heaven** may be intact, **what good is it?** Truly, when the **Three Treasures** gather, **external form naturally radiates**. Yet those who **dash outward** while **boasting of food and clothing**—**why not instead seek within, enriching the inner wealth first?** When **internal riches are full**, **external wealth comes naturally**. **Is this not unlike thieves**—**who steal not the Heavenly *Língyáng* *qi* but only the name of the Sage’s refinement?**

**Chapter Fifty-Four: Cultivating the Body**
*"The well-built stands firm, the well-embraced does not slip—*
*their descendants’ sacrifices never cease."*

**Cultivating the body**, its **virtue is complete**;
**Cultivating the family**, its **virtue overflows**;
**Cultivating the village**, its **virtue grows long**;
**Cultivating the state**, its **virtue becomes abundant**.

「**Cultivating the Way in the world**, its **virtue becomes universal**. Hence, one **observes the body through the body**, the **family through the family**, the **village through the village**, the **state through the state**, and the **world through the world**. **How do I know the world is thus?** By this.

The **Heaven and Earth** in giving birth to humans, **bestow *qi* to establish life** and **impart *lǐ* (principle/pattern) to perfect nature**. *Qi* and *lǐ* are originally unified—**nature and life** are inseparable. **All are but the pure, boundless *qi*, revolving through Heaven and Earth**, waxing and waning, ebbing and flowing, **wholly in accord with the natural**, **creative transformations** passing through **countless generations**. Though human kinds may differ and **the Creator’s works** vary in measure, **this *qi* is shared**, **this *lǐ* is shared**, **never changing in its essence**.

The **Sage dwells at the center**, **establishing the poles**, **erect and unyielding**, **independent yet not leaning**, **moderate yet never in peril**. Though **prosperity and adversity, life and death, joy and sorrow** all differ, **this *lǐ* and this *qi* flow within a single body**, **filling the two great realms (Heaven and Earth)**, **never diminished in the slightest**. **Is this not the meaning of “the well-built stands firm”?**

Otherwise, **what has form and substance**—even so **the towering Tai Mountain**, **lofty and thick**—still **faces the risk of collapse**. For **what is formed**, though firm and enduring, **finds it hard to last**. Yet **the formless *lǐ* and *qi*, unchanging with things, unmoved by numbers**, **ever renewed through the ages**. This **Dao** is **rooted in oneself**, **transforming harmoniously to all**—**naturally deep in benevolence and thick in grace**, **penetrating to the marrow and flesh**, **so the people love and cherish it, their devotion unceasing**, **none for a breath’s time detached or disconnected**.

Though **daily bound like glue or invested like bamboo**, one may say **“how firm!”**; though **mixed like water and milk**, one may say **“how harmonious!”**—yet **gathering and scattering are without constancy**, **changes and shifts are swift**, **and in the blink of an eye, discord appears**. But **benevolent heart and benevolent reputation**, **those touched by its grace love it and forget not**, **those who hear its wind cherish it and abandon not**—**how unlike the child’s dependence on father and elder brother**, **or the arm’s response to the heart’s will**, **with no separation or disconnection?** **Is this not the meaning of “the well-embraced does not slip”?**

From this, **the virtuous person** cherishes **his own virtue** and **loves his own kin**; **the petty man** enjoys **his own pleasures** and **seeks his own gain**. **All is but governance by the unadorned robe**, **sharing in the rest of the effortless**. Though the **Sage may not always remain**, **his virtue and grace deeply ingrained in the hearts of men** **endure forever, never fading**.

The *Shijing* says:
*“Enduring generations of descendants,
Do not neglect their offerings.”*
**Is this not what is meant?**

In ancient times, Confucius praised **Shun’s great filial piety**, saying:
*“In the ancestral temples, he is honored;
His descendants safeguard his legacy.”*
**This shows virtue without bounds**—**descendants’ sacrifices** **everlasting, like the ceaseless steam of offerings and the ever-renewed ancestral dishes**. The saying goes:
*“He who has virtue for ten generations
Shall have descendants who preserve it for ten generations.
He who has virtue for a hundred generations
Shall have descendants who preserve it for a hundred generations.”*
**As for great virtue enduring eternally**, even **a billion or ten billion years**, **his descendants shall follow in succession**, **ever more flourishing and lasting**, **for such is the unchanging principle**.

This is all **by the Dao of effortless naturalness**—**internally cultivated in oneself yet never lost**, **externally extended to others yet never forgotten**—**thus Heaven’s body is nourished to its fullest**, **and the world enjoys its boundless blessings**. **When a man cultivates this Dao in his own person**, **both form and spirit are perfected**, **and he is in truth united with the Dao**. **The Dao is the body, the body is the Dao**—**this Dao and this body**, **two without divergence**—**how could virtue be more true?**

Moreover, **when the Dao is cultivated in the village**, **the village unites as one**; **when the Dao is cultivated in the state**, **the state regards itself as one**. **How vast and abundant is its virtue?** If **one can remain tranquil and unforced**, **serene and desireless**, **naturally the winds of the four quarters move**, **the world turns to benevolence**, **the people cherish his virtue**, **without end**—**how could virtue be more universal?** This is **not by force or by bribery**, but **the innate goodness of Heaven**, **cultivated in the true, unrestrained heart**—**like the wanderer’s longing for home** or **the aged’s joy in reunion**, **a happiness that comes when none expects it**.

**Man and self differ in body yet share the same heart**,
**share the same life and thus respond in kind**—
**so manifesting virtue renews the people**,
**calming them comes from cultivating oneself**,
**none differing in this**.

**What is more**, the **village is the accumulation of the family**, the **state is the expansion of the village**—**the world’s vastness**, **the multitude of its people**, **are but the family, the village, the state gradually extended and broadened**, **gradually gathered and multiplied**. **Know that the Dao of one is the Dao of family, state, and Heaven as one**; **cultivating oneself is cultivating family, state, and world**. **Reflecting inward, one must extend outward**—**there is the silent transformation, the subtle change**, **the ease of custom and the shift of winds**, **and thus all rejoice in their shared happiness**.

Thus it is said:
*“First virtue and transformation, then human hearts arise;
First human hearts, then customs and mores follow.”*
**Its Dao lies in the body**,
**its virtue extends to the family**,
**and its transformation spreads like grass bowing to the wind**,
**reaching no distance it does not embrace**,
**extending through village and state unto the world**.

**Ah! How do I know the world is thus?** By this.

The *Yijing* says:
*“Great is the **primordial element of *Qián*** (Heaven)!
**Strong and unyielding, moderate and correct**—
**pure and refined, its essence is perfect!”**
This is **the Dao of the preheaven *Qián* (Heavenly Stem), the ultimate strength and vigor**, **standing firm between Heaven and Earth**, **flowing through all things**, **embracing all without remainder**, **its sincerity unceasing**. **Its force is ever-extending yet never bending**, **straight yet never obstructed**, **supporting Heaven and bearing Earth**, **reaching the Han and piercing the sky**—**never has the state been even slightly shaken**.

Yet **it is the boundless extreme of the extreme**,
**the spirit without spirit**,
**and thus stands unshaken, so firm.»

Man can establish his **body upon the boundless** (*wújí*) and **center his primordial spirit** in its function—thus the ancients spoke of **gathering the great elixir from the unmoving**, **conducting the fire-alchemy within the realm of non-action**. **Rooted in the center, erecting the pole**, the **vast and boundless *qi*** flows, **with *qi* dependent upon spirit**, **spirit and *qi* mutually resonating**, **knotted as one cluster**—thus returning to the root and restoring the command, **the Dao abides**.

Now, **the birth of man**—**spirit and *qi* unite**; **his death**—**spirit and *qi* separate**. If man can **harmonize nature and life**, **fuse spirit and *qi***, then he **embraces the primordial and preserves the One**. **My fate is mine alone, not Heaven’s**—what escape could there be?

Thus, **spirit depends upon spirit**, **qi upon qi**, **one thread flowing through generations**, **one true essence permeating all**—**capable of myriad transformations**, **vanishing as ghost and emerging as spirit**, **manifesting countless forms**, **enjoying countless eons**. How could it be said that **a child begets a grandson, who begets a child again**, **generation upon generation**, **roots deep and leaves flourishing**, **source distant yet flow unending**, **illuminating the ancestral rites across ten thousand ages without cessation**?

Yet it is no more than **using primordial *qi* as medicine** and **primordial spirit as the alchemical fire**. **Primordial *qi* is no-*qi*** (*wúqì*), **primordial spirit is no-spirit** (*bùshén*). **Refining *qi* with spirit yields the Dao**, just as **fusing fire with medicine yields the elixir**. **Common elixirs may succeed or fail**, but **true elixir knows no end nor beginning**—thus it is said: *“The Great Dao of the Golden Elixir endures ten thousand years without erosion.”*

It is nothing but **cultivating one’s own virtue** and **refining one’s own body**—**not derived from later influences**, **not borrowed from without**—**thus its virtue is true**.

Though Heaven and Earth give birth to man, **pure or impure, wise or foolish**, yet **when the august and sacred descend**, from the family to the realm, **each possesses an inherent constancy**. Thus, **a single heart can permeate ten thousand households**, **a single virtue can inspire ten thousand people**.

**Cultivating the self to harmonize the family**—**virtue overflows**; **cultivating the self to transform the village**—**virtue endures**. As for **governing the state and pacifying the world**, it is nothing but **the sage’s robe swaying while the world transforms**—**there is no external cultivation of the self that can universally attain such influence**. This is the **constant Dao of the age**.

Conversely, **if one cultivates the self**, how could it differ?

Discussing the state and the world, it is **a matter of progressing from near to far**, **layer by layer**—**not a step-by-step retreat**. From the initial practice: **refining essence**, yet **qi and spirit are present**. The second step: **refining qi**, yet **spirit and essence remain**. The third step: **refining spirit**, yet **essence and qi are still within**. **Returning to the void and merging with the Dao**, **the Dao merging with nature**—**from beginning to end, it never departs**; **to depart is to lose the Dao**.

The **body is like essence**, but **essence is not the essence of mutual resonance**—it is the **initial essence received from the Great Void’s Five Phases**, the **inherited primordial essence that forms the body**. **Cultivating the body is refining essence into qi**. The practitioner, at first, **holds this essence to live and grow**. **Refining essence to cultivate the body is no different from cultivating the body itself**—how true is this!

Speaking of **qi**, essence is **closer to the body**, while **qi is slightly more distant**. *“Cultivating the family, its virtue overflows”*—**gathering external true yang-qi** to **refine internal true yin-essence** is like **using the body to harmonize the family**; **what abundance does one gain from oneself?**

The village, when viewed from the body, is **farther still**, yet **closer than the family**. It is like **spirit**, yet **spirit is fire**. **Heat belongs to qi**, **light belongs to spirit**—**two yet one**. **Cultivating the village is refining spirit back to the void**. Thus it is said: *“Its virtue endures”*, for **long life is boundless and eternal**.

As for the **state**, compared to the village it is **nearer**, yet **farther than the body**, its breadth **not visible in a single glance**. The **state is like the void**—**cultivating the state is refining the void to merge with the Dao**. **Refining until the void**, **becoming one with the clear and empty**, **illuminating the great thousandfold world**—what of the world itself? Thus it is said: *“Its virtue is universal.”*

Whether observing **the body from the body**, **the family from the family**, **the village from the village**, **the state from the state**, or **the world from the world**—it is nothing but **using one’s own self and family as the model for the world’s self and family**, **taking the highest sage as analogy**—**its intent is intimate**, **its meaning profound**. The **Great Dao has no other path**: **refining essence to its utmost**, until it reaches **the void of natural spontaneity**. **Exhausted!**

The **learner of the Great Dao** has no other way: **only diminishing again and again**, until it reaches **the spontaneity of non-action**. **Non-action yet nothing remains undone**—**exhausted!**

Yet **internal elixirs and external elixirs**, **internal alchemy and external alchemy**, **drawing from the *Kàn* to fill the *Lí***, **extracting lead to add mercury**—**all these metaphors and names** ultimately refer to **the essence, *qi*, and spirit received from Heaven and Earth within the body**—**inherently possessed since birth**, yet **entrapped within the fleshly shell**, thus called *“yin-essence, yin-qi, yin-spirit”*; **since it comes with life itself**, it is called *“internal medicine.”*

When the practitioner begins his work, he **must first close the senses and seal the orifices**, **concentrating within**, **regulating his breath**, **merging the Three Primordials into one cauldron**—**one cauldron refining the Three Primordials**—**named “refining essence”**, yet in truth **spirit and *qi* all return to one orifice**. Only when **spirit is harmonized and *qi* flows freely**, **united as one**, does the **vital mechanism activate**, **steaming and rising**—this is called **qi-transformation**, or **gold born from water’s depths**, or **the union of parents yielding the medicine**.

This is the **human way of procreation**; if one does not know the **method of inverse cultivation**, the moment **it transforms into postnatal, tangible essence**, it **leaks through the kidney passage**. Thus: *“Conserve *qi* and retain essence—this is the decisive path to longevity.”*

**If man seeks longevity**, **the transformation of essence into *qi***—**this is the wondrous elixir of life**.

When there are signs of conflict, one must urgently **internalize the Celestial Ganges** and **push outward the Dipper’s handle**, advance and retreat the **River Chariot**, and **reclaim the Central Palace** to **reforge anew**.

This is the **refinement of the inner elixir**, where **essence, *qi*, and spirit** merge as one—how much more than mere **qi-transformation**!

One **yin**, one **yang**—one **Kan**, one **Li**—first, with the body’s inherent capacities, **converging in the Yellow Chamber**, nurturing for a moment, and **vital *qi* arises**. This is **spirit entering *qi***, refining the **heavenly earthly-external celestial yang-*qi** from within the body’s essence—**this is essence-refinement into *qi***.

Next, **harvesting this *qi* to ascend**, **guiding it to descend**, returning it to the **earthly cauldron** for repeated brewing and refining—**this is lead tempering mercury**, **subduing yang-*qi* beneath yin-essence**.

For **essence** is originally what the body inherently possesses, hence called **"separated yin-essence of the self."** **Qi** arises from essence’s transformation, hence called **"Kan-Wu yang-*qi**." It is not that **essence belongs to the heart** or **qi originates within the kidneys**.

From **Yongquan to Qihai**, all belong to **yang**—**yang is Kan**; from **Nihuan to Xuanguan**, all belong to **yin**—**yin is Li**. Thus, **water-fire *qi*** are **Kan and Li**, not **heart and kidneys** being **Kan and Li**—this is now clear.

Again, **Kan’s *qi* is called earthly soul**—in **external elixir**, it is the **White Tiger**; in **internal elixir**, it is the **golden elixir**. This **elixir** arises from **extracting lead and adding mercury**, merging into one—all belonging to the **water palace’s mysterious pearl**.

The **internal-external distinction** is layer upon layer of **peeling**, not truly separate.

**Li Palace’s essence is called heavenly spirit**—in **external elixir**, it is the **Green Dragon**; in **internal elixir**, it is **one’s true essence**. **Gold born from water** is **essence’s *qi*-transformation**—in **external elixir**, it is the **White Tiger’s initial crescent *qi***; in **internal elixir**, it is the **silver within lead**.

Again, it is called the **golden elixir**, the **great elixir of longevity**. Only this **Primordial *qi* of Heaven** sinks into the human body—if not **subdued by spiritual fire**, it will **sink and not rise**.

Even if it stirs to move, it will **tilt and fail**—like oil in a lamp: **without oil, the flame extinguishes**; **without *qi*, life perishes**. Life’s continuity lies here, hence it is the **great elixir of longevity**.

Since it **originates from Heaven but falls into Kan**, now **returning from Kan to Heaven**—this is the origin of the **golden elixir’s doctrine**.

If one seeks **longevity**, apart from this **water-bound lead**, there is **no other substance**.

Though this **golden elixir** is said to belong to all, **without spiritual fire’s brewing**, it cannot arise.

Once the **true gold is born**, then **seize the White Tiger and subdue the Dragon**, causing the **Green Dragon to submit to the Tiger**. The **Dragon and Tiger *qi*** reunite in the **Yellow Chamber**, their **mutual devouring and consuming** forming the **golden elixir**.

Returning to the **earthly cauldron**, merging with **true essence**, then **nurturing with spiritual fire** to form the **sacred embryo**. Once the embryo forms, **internally using natural true fire**, **endlessly within the spirit chamber**, while **externally adding mundane fire**, circulating through the body—**daily, one’s mercury encases and secures true essence**. Over time, it **sheds its shell and emerges**.

Ascending to **Nihuan**, refining the **void**, striving to return to **its original, natural state**. It is not that **essence, *qi*, and spirit** are **three separate treasures**, nor that **internal and external elixirs** are distinct. Unless one **sits until the cushion is worn through**, **grinds the knees raw**, and **endures self-cultivation in suffering**, how can one **penetrate its profound truth**?

I now merely outline the **general principles**, merely pointing **later learners** toward the **main path**.

The **Dao is inherently ordinary**—not extraordinary. The more refined, the more ordinary it becomes.

As for its **transformations**, which seem boundless—**to worldly eyes**, it appears **unfathomable and unattainable**. Yet, with **Laozi’s *Dao De Jing* in mind**, even the **Three Pure Ones** are merely **ordinary mortals** who achieved it.

But **mortals** take **life and death** as **joy and sorrow**, while **immortals** view them **as day and night**. **Life and death** are like **rising and resting**—**carried out naturally**, without unease.

Those who say **longevity and immortality** are **the Daoist’s delight** are mistaken. **Mortals** take **longevity as honor**, but **immortals** find **fulfillment in natural necessity**. Even if it means **sacrificing life for virtue** or **abandoning life for righteousness**, they **welcome it naturally**.

Otherwise, who does not fear **the agony of the executioner’s blade**? **Ancient worthies and sages** often saw **the cauldron as a joyous place** and **death as a peaceful path**—**seeing truth clearly**, **trusting fate’s decree**. Their **living and dying** are all **guided by this heart**.

To **live uneasily** is worse than **dying swiftly**—like **staying awake in pain**, better to **sleep long**. As long as **spirit endures and principle is whole**, what is there to honor in **life** or shame in **death**?

One must **yield to Nature’s course**, **never clinging to life in this world**. To **cherish life and fear death** is the way of **common, vulgar folk**, not the **sage’s timely response to Heaven**.

Otherwise, why did **Confucius die at seventy**, and **Yan Hui at thirty**? They **followed Heaven’s movement**, **daring not to defy it**. Who among mortals could comprehend this?

I humbly hope that scholars seek only the inner truth, not the outer trappings—viewing trials, hardships, life, and death with equal indifference.

This heart is how vast, how serene! A proverb says:
*"Recognize the Way and depart in peace,
Let Heaven’s will unfold as it may."*

Thus, one attains ease in life and tranquility in death,
Forever a true transcendent—
How superior to those who cling to life and fear death,
Now joyful within, now troubled in heart,
Their minds never finding rest!

What of those with lofty Dao? Heaven may wish to retain them
To set an example and guide the common folk.
I neither reject nor pursue this—
I simply yield to it, with no attachment.
How could I let such matters disturb my heart?

For life and death are both the Dao.
To live fully in the Dao, to die fully in the Dao—
What need then for likes or dislikes?

All who harbor preferences within
Disturb their spirit and lose their nature,
Unworthy of being called *xian* [transcendent].

**Chapter Fifty-Fifth: The Depth of Virtue/Power**
The depth of virtue/power is like that of an infant:
Poisonous creatures do not sting,
Fierce beasts do not seize,
Raptors do not strike.

Though bones are frail and sinews soft, their grasp is firm.
Unknowing of male and female union, yet life stirs—
The essence is at its peak.

Crying all day yet the throat does not grow hoarse—
Harmony is at its peak.

To know harmony is to know constancy;
To know constancy is to know clarity.
Increasing life is auspicious.
Forcing *qi* by will is strength—
But strength leads to aging, which is against the Dao.
Against the Dao is premature decay.

The *Yijing* says:
*"Heaven and Earth in their primal state,
All things refined in purity.
Male and female unite in essence,
All things born in generation."*

At the beginning of birth, distant not from Heaven,
Their *qi* is soft and tender—
Guide it by its momentum, nurture it by its opportunity,
And one may yet reach the realm of pure transformation,
The primal harmony of Heaven.

Mencius said:
*"The noble sage does not lose the heart of an infant."*

When an infant first cries upon entering the world,
Though it possesses its own universe and opens new creation,
Yet it is undifferentiated, a bundle of pure innocence.
Without knowledge, without thought—
Stillness and transformation are one,
Movement and Heaven align.

The ancient transcendent sages contained boundless light and virtue,
Their depth without limit—
Compared to the infant, they are nearly equal.

In their parents’ embrace, nurtured and cared for,
Unable to walk, unable to act,
Yet poisonous creatures cannot sting them,
And fierce beasts cannot seize them.

Though fierce birds of prey hover—
They cannot strike, for movement knows no direction,
And steps know no path.
Thus, no dread of venomous creatures,
Yet venomous creatures cannot sting them;
No dread of fierce beasts,
Yet fierce beasts cannot seize them.

Even when perched in danger upon the couch,
Or reclining in slumber on the bed,
They are not spied upon by birds of prey—
Yet birds of prey would come to strike them.
But when youth has ripened and movement is unrestrained,
Though there be the joys of wandering,
They cannot match the tranquility of the chamber—
Few escape the calamities of malevolent things.

How much more so for the infant at birth,
Unknowing of the union of male and female,
Their essence pure and vital, life’s momentum ever flourishing.

If one can concentrate the *qi* and soften like a newborn,
Then the essence may be refined.
Even when wailing ceaselessly from dawn to dusk,
Their throat never grows hoarse—
Not from deliberate calling,
Nor from forced utterance.
All is the spontaneous motion of Heaven’s mechanism,
The natural resonance of Heaven’s sounds,
Without contrivance, without artifice—
The utmost harmony.

To grasp the primal harmony within,
Is to attain the eternal Dao,
Without the slightest human effort to force or bend it,
But to preserve its eternal nature,
And rest in its inherent state.

As the *Shijing* says:
*"Both wise and enlightened,
To preserve oneself thus—*
Is this not what is meant?"

If not with harmonious and yielding *qi*
Cultivated within body and mind,
How could one be born and grow ever more vital,
The Heavenly Body flourishing without decay?
No blessing surpasses this among mortals.

Yet once strength and vigor take hold,
The Heavenly Heart is disrupted by the human heart,
And the dissipation of spirit is great.

Now, taking the primal harmony as the Dao,
Great stillness alone can bring great motion,
And supreme softness alone can overcome supreme hardness.

Thus, let the heart govern the *qi*,
And strive to make this *qi* return to the state of the infant—
Do not let the *qi* disturb the heart,
Lest the heart stray from the primal harmony.
Only then may one achieve harmony without dissipation—
How mighty is this rectification!
It is not merely the infant who embodies this.

Observe all things:
In their beginning, they are soft and fragile;
In their end, they are strong and robust.
Softness and fragility are the seeds of life,
Strength and robustness are the omens of death.
Thus, when things grow strong, they grow old,
Not as when they are young, they live.
Life preserves its Dao,
Death abandons its Dao.
Hence it is said: *"Things that age are against the Dao,
And against the Dao, it is better to have never been."*

Let those who cultivate the Dao
Early abandon the *qi* of aging,
And seek the *qi* of the infant.
If one truly attains the state of the infant—
Fearless, without terror,
Without knowledge, without thought,
A boundless undifferentiated whole,
Flowing beyond all forms—
This is harmony.

For the Dao of Heaven nurtures all things through harmony.
If one knows this,
Then vitality flows without ceasing—
Thus it is called *constant*.
To know the constant is to penetrate yin and yang,
To align with creation—
Thus it is called *enlightenment*.
To cultivate the self and establish one’s fate,
To seize the power of Heaven and Earth’s life and death—
No blessing surpasses this among mortals.

Refining the spirit and returning to the void,
Attaining the Dao of unending life—
How could this not be the ultimate strength?
What is there that is not the Dao?

This is the method of cultivating the self,
Taking the infant as its symbol.

Zhuangzi said:
*"The infant moves without knowing where,
Walks without knowing where it goes,
Its body like withered wood,
Its heart like dead ashes—
Disaster does not come,
Fortune does not arrive."*

If disaster and fortune are nonexistent,
How could there be human calamities or harm from things?

The lines on *"poisonous creatures"* express this very meaning.

Later, when gathering herbs or refining elixirs,
One must take the pristine, tender water of the first hour—
This water is the source of all life.
Like a wheel of red sun at midnight,
Clear and bright, illuminating the vast sea;
Or like a crescent moon in autumn,
Emerging in the *Geng* and *Zhen* directions—
This is when the adept’s mystical pass opens,
Vague and distant, yet this realm is attained.

For when the initial *qi* is cultivated in softness,
It is like all things bending their branches and sprouting leaves.
Nurture and nourish it thus,
And it will grow daily and monthly,
Until returning to the root of life,
And bearing the fruit of fulfillment.

But in the declining years of twilight,
Things have aged and are withering,
Unfit to be gathered as medicine.
Yet aging is not measured by years—
It refers to the *qi* of the herb growing old and unfit for elixir.

If measured by years,
Even at eighty or ninety,
One may still cultivate the Dao of eternal youth.
For as long as breath remains,
This very *qi* of primal harmony suffuses the body,
Without the slightest deficiency.

The adept who has not pierced through the celestial realm within the waters of the self—
with inner and outer *qi* flowing as two distinct currents—
will often pass by the truth before their very eyes.

The student who seeks to cultivate the Great Dao of the Golden Elixir
must empty the heart to inquire of the Way,
accumulate virtue to reverse the heavens,
or the true teacher will never be drawn to manifest,
and the *White Tiger Canon* will remain unfound.
Even if youth enters the Dao,
they will surely grow old without attainment.

Many have erred by following false teachers,
wandering down false paths,
and wasted their lifetimes’ essence in the realm of beasts.
Let the student beware!

**Chapter 56: The Most Noble for All Under Heaven**

The wise do not speak; those who speak do not understand.
Close the mouth, shut the gate,
dull the sharpness, resolve the tangled,
harmonize the light, blend with the dust—
this is called *Mystic Unity*.

Thus, one cannot be too close nor too distant,
too beneficial nor too harmful,
too noble nor too lowly.
Therefore, they are the most noble for all under heaven.

Generally, those without virtue,
when they hear a good word or see a good deed,
immediately chatter grandly to stir others,
thus pleasing the moment without ever
transforming their face or purifying their heart,
or reflecting inwardly to verify the truth.

Confucius said, *"Hearsay and gossip are the abandonment of virtue,"*
and this is indeed not false.

But the true knower of the Great Dao,
when they occasionally grasp something,
is occupied morning and evening with solemn vigilance—
how could they have the leisure to speak for others’ ears?
Even if they ponder the old to know the new,
and suddenly grasp the meaning,
they possess it themselves and find it in themselves—
like one sated with food and drink,
drunk and full,
knowing the taste for themselves alone,
yet unable to convey it to others.

Those who love to speak of such things
must surely lack something within themselves.

Moreover, virtue is for oneself, cultivation is for oneself—
once truly known, it must be guarded all the more.
Fear that a single careless word
might cause even a breath to stray,
and the Way lost from the heart.
This is why the wise do not speak.

If they speak, it is because they lack virtue within themselves,
truly knowing nothing of the Dao.
If they truly knew, why would they so lightly reveal it?
Thus, those who speak do not understand—
this is even more evident.

Besides, the essence is what cannot be spoken of,
and what can be spoken of is but dross.
The wise do not fail to speak because they cannot,
but because it is difficult to speak.
Those who speak do not fail to know,
but only see the surface.

As the saying goes, *"When you’ve grasped it, close your mouth."*
Indeed, knowing the Dao is not easy—
how could one waste their *qi* with words,
or taint their spirit with idle chatter,
boasting of talents and abilities to stand out,
only to invite the disdain of the vulgar?

Thus, the high ones of the Way
close the mouth and shut the gate to nurture their *qi*;
dull the sharpness and resolve the tangled to steady their spirit;
harmonize their light and blend with the dust,
bowing and rising with the times,
floating and sinking with the world,
as if foolish, as if drunk,
as if slow of speech, as if mad—
when the multitude is confused, they too are confused,
neither flaunting their oddities nor setting themselves apart,
contradicting neither themselves nor the world.

Should even a trace of pretense or ambition arise,
they cannot help but speak grandly to satisfy a moment’s pleasure.
Such people seek either fame or gain—
have they not heard the *Taishang*’s words to Confucius?

*"Those who can be fed with wine and meat,
I will whip and beat.
Those who can be honored with rank and wealth,
I will pierce and insult."*

Only by secluding oneself to cultivate in secret,
embracing the primal and guarding the One,
remaining silent and still,
*qi* dim and mysterious,
tranquil and without desire,
acting without action, doing without doing—
then one cannot be too close nor too distant,
too beneficial nor too harmful,
too noble nor too lowly.

This is to seek within oneself, not without,
not seeking from others,
fulfilling one’s nature and returning to one’s life—
thus, they are the most noble for all under heaven.

Within the Three Realms, only the Dao is supreme.
I cultivate my Dao, and thus my Dao is noble—
nothing under heaven surpasses this.

The *Taishang* said: *"Few know me, thus I am noble."*
Do the students know this as well?

The person of the Way must not speak lightly,
for few in the world understand the Dao.
If one indulges in idle talk,
not only does it harm oneself internally,
but it also invites external scorn from others.

The *Classic of Changes* says: *"If secrets are not kept, harm will follow."*
Throughout history, many cultivators
have invited disaster by carelessly revealing secrets.

Thus, the virtuous are cautious and do not speak lightly.
Even if one speaks to the worthy,
they proceed with utmost caution and deliberation,
testing again and again,
before solemnly vowing to heaven and earth,
ascending the platform to teach—
yet they dare not speak too high or too far,
lest they prattle endlessly.

This shows the ancient practice of concealing brilliance and obscuring the light,
and their reverence for the Dao and awe of heaven.

Those who speak lightly know nothing within themselves.
If they truly knew, they would be too occupied with self-cultivation and verification
to have leisure for idle talk.

Those who speak without restraint
are already bound to fall from nobility.

With my Dao noble and my Way exalted,
I can extend from one age to ten thousand ages—
who under heaven can surpass this?

Let the student cultivate what lies within,
moment by moment, inwardly observing,
lest the wind of idle discourse arise.

**Chapter 57: Governing the Realm with Rectitude**

Govern the realm with rectitude,
employ the strange in warfare,
and take the world without conflict.
How do I know this to be true?

Because: the more taboos there are under heaven,
the poorer the people become;

When the people pursue profit and greed, the state grows corrupt;
when the people indulge in cleverness, strange things proliferate;
when laws and decrees multiply, thieves and bandits abound.

Thus the Sage said:
*"I do without action, yet the people transform themselves.
I love stillness, yet the people align themselves.
I take no action, yet the people grow wealthy.
I desire nothing, yet the people return to simplicity."*

Confucius said: *"My Dao is one, threading through all things."*
This is to say: the Dao is singular, yet all things under heaven—
ten thousand affairs, ten thousand beings—
are but this Dao manifesting and flowing.
As the saying goes: *"One root branches into myriad forms,
yet myriad forms return to one root."*

To govern oneself and the world—
this is the great principle.
The Dao of governing the world
lies in ensuring that scholars, farmers, artisans, and merchants
each find their proper place in life;
that filial piety and brotherly love, loyalty and trust
follow the innate goodness of humanity.
These are daily, ever-present matters—
the great principles of the world,
the eternal laws of ages past and to come,
the constant Dao, the true Way.
What each person ought to fulfill
is what each person inherently possesses as virtue.

For those who rule the people,
practicing frugality in person,
exhausting oneself in filial piety and benevolence,
leading by example for all under heaven,
and governing with solemn dignity—
one must guide them without ceasing,
calming with stillness, steadying with composure,
avoiding frivolous actions that would embolden disorder,
and maintaining rectitude so that all things fall into order naturally,
like wind sweeping grass flat.
Thus, as Mencius said:
*"Rectify the ruler, and the state is settled."*
And again:
*"The world has endured for ages."*
Now one age of order, then one of chaos—
a cycle that repeats without fail,
from ancient times to this day.

Though in times of order, ritual and music prevail,
and in times of chaos, arms and warfare dominate,
when two armies face each other in battle,
when great enemies clash,
the fate of the state and the lives of the people
rest upon a single general—
how could this not be of utmost weight?

Though the arts of strategy and calculation,
the cleverness of schemes and tactics,
are not the pursuits of the virtuous,
yet to uphold Heaven’s mandate in punishing rebels
and to wield great righteousness in relieving the people
is not to shun extraordinary means to victory.

Thus, military strategy includes:
ambushes and covert raids,
exploiting fatigue and exhaustion,
dividing and sowing discord,
feigning weakness to deceive,
moving like ghosts and spirits in the unseen.
Using the strange in warfare,
one is undefeated in battle,
unconquered in assault,
spilling no blood,
draining no wealth,
while the people live long in a land of order,
enjoying eternal peace—
how could this not be the Way of Non-Action?

Yet to unite mountains and rivers under one rule,
to merge heaven and earth under one sovereignty—
how could there be no affairs at all?
But if one lets all things unfold naturally
while maintaining an unshaken, composed heart,
then naturally one harmonizes with Heaven,
and Heaven’s favor descends;
one is revered by the people,
and all under heaven willingly submits.
Thus it is said:
*"The sages of Tang and Yu governed with a cup of wine,
while Tang and Wu conquered with a single move on the board."*

Only when one does not covet the world
can one take the world without effort—
thus the mind remains undisturbed,
and one’s capacity matches that of Heaven and Earth.
Therefore, ruling China and pacifying the barbarians
comes effortlessly, as if by fate.

Such is the Dao of governing the world.
How do I know this to be true?
Because the Dao of governing the world
is none other than the Dao of governing oneself—
the body is like a state.
What one sees, hears, speaks, and does
must conform to ritual;
one’s thoughts and deliberations
must align with humaneness.
Neither internal distractions nor external intrusions
should disrupt the settled nature—
once the mind is steadied, the body follows suit.
When stillness is cultivated over time,
Heaven’s natural impulses move on their own.

To use the ordinary method of nurturing life
as the alchemical method of reversal,
approaching the furnace with fire—
this is fraught with danger.
The Sage likens it to warfare:
one must act according to the times,
seize opportunities as they arise,
harvest at the right moment,
refine with precision,
choose the right terrain for battle,
and defend with strategy.
One must wait for the opportune moment,
seize the advantage,
and strike with extraordinary tactics.

As for stopping the fire when the medicine is sufficient,
warming the furnace when the elixir is ripe,
ascending the sun’s essence to the void,
nurturing the immortal embryo in its unbroken state—
one must still cultivate the divine chamber in stillness,
remaining utterly detached from all affairs,
only then can the elixir be perfected,
and immortality achieved.
This is the Dao of governing oneself—
a metaphor for the achievement of governing the world.
Thus, I know the Dao of governing the world
through the method of governing oneself.

To take the world requires Non-Action,
yet to hold the world requires avoiding excess.
Otherwise, with endless edicts and prohibitions,
suspended laws and decrees,
the people will grow weary of compliance,
and corruption will inevitably follow,
neglecting their duties.
Thus, when laws multiply, the people grow poorer.

Gold, jade, pearls, and jewels,
carriages, horses, fine clothes—
though these are the people’s tools of utility,
once greed arises, desire is insatiable,
and the mind grows clouded,
the qi grows turbid.
Simplicity and sincerity fade,
while deceit and cunning prevail.
When a clever artisan emerges,
people admire and emulate them,
and soon strange and bizarre things fill the courts.
Alas!

In the flourishing ages of the Three Dynasties,
the rulers were divine and sagely,
the people pure and virtuous.
Laws were enacted but not enforced,
institutions established but not applied—
thus they were called ages of prosperity.

Now, however, legal nets are vast and dense,
like a fine mesh;
five-tiered punishments loom heavy,
with no mercy for transgressors;
three-chapter codes stand severe,
with no pardon for offenders.
Yet why, when laws grow stricter,
do crimes multiply?
Why, when decrees proliferate,
do thieves increase?
It is because virtue is insufficient to win the people’s hearts,
and thus laws cannot instill fear in their resolve.

From ancient times, those who drove the people to rebellion
were never those who lacked such coercive measures.
This is evident since the Qin and Han dynasties.

The ancient Sage said:
*"Heaven rules through Non-Action and is thereby honored;
man rules through Non-Action and is thereby burdened."*
If I govern with reverence and simplicity,
not overwhelming the people with excess,
not confusing the state with constant change,
but instead maintaining solemnity in the highest halls,
and stillness in the deepest palaces—
then the people will naturally progress toward goodness,
unaware of who has guided them.

「Yet, with a tranquil heart as my dwelling and profound silence as my defense, I attend solely to the cultivation of sincere intent and the rectification of my mind—
but who knows that rectifying one’s own self is the means to rectify the court,
and rectifying the court is the means to rectify the people?
All this arises from that very stillness and composure.

When the people are rectified, they honor their own kin and uphold their own elders,
transgressing no bounds, and thus their households endure forever.
Then, they till the fields to eat, dig wells to drink,
rise with the sun and rest with the moon.
Granaries overflow with abundance,
and their every gesture is free from care—
for the people grow rich naturally.

Such is the way when rulers allow things to unfold naturally,
acting without contrivance,
and thus the people are transformed and moved by them.

Moreover, in serene repose and quietude,
with natural ease and effortless stillness,
one attains the tranquil essence—
the *真* (*zhen*, "true nature")—
and the people, in response, will not fail to cultivate the virtues of loyalty and sincerity,
nor will they lose their innate simplicity and unadorned customs.

The *Classic of Documents* (*Shu*) states:
*"If one person is fundamentally virtuous (*元良*, *yuánliáng*), all nations will stand firm (*贞*, *zhēn*)."*
Its principle lies hidden in subtlety,
yet its effect is manifest in the harmony of Heaven and Earth.

My wish is that those who seek to govern the world
focus first on rectifying the ruler’s heart,
and those who seek to cultivate their own person
prioritize nourishing the *天君* (*Tiānjūn*, "Heavenly Sovereign"—i.e., the mind).
This truth is already clear—
no need for further elaboration.

In my reflections on seated meditation (*打坐*, *dǎzuò*), at first,
