then **desire can be checked** (*èyù*) **[17]**
and **sincerity preserved** (*cúnchéng*), **[18]**
while **evil inclinations are repelled** (*xiányé*) **[19]**
and **rightness restored** (*guīzhèng*). **[20]

For the **sprouts** (*méngniè*) **[21]**
are still tender and weak,
and **human desire** (*rényù*) **[22]**
is easily cut off—
then **Heaven’s principle** (*tiānlǐ*) **[23]**
can return as it was.

The ancients called this the **Mystic Pass** (*Xuānguān*) **[2]**
or the **"Gate of Life, Door of Death"** (*Shēngmén Sǐhù*). **[24]
When the **human heart retreats and hides** (*rénxīn tuìcáng*) **[25]**
and **Heaven’s heart shines forth** (*tiānxīn zhàoyào*), **[26]**
it is because, in the time of **non-being and non-chaos** (*wèiyǒu wèiluàn*), **[27]**
one **acts upon and governs** (*wéiyú zhìyú*) **[28]**
them.

Yet when the **first Yang stirs** (*yīyáng chūdòng*), **[29]**
its mechanism is subtle,
its force swift.

By the **second and third Yang**, **[30]**
the **spirit condenses** (*shén níng*) **[31]**
and the **qi gathers** (*qì jù*), **[32]**
the **true essence moves** (*zhēnjīng zìdòng*) **[33]**
—vast as the tide’s rise,
flowing like ice melting into water.

All these changes
begin from the **Mystic Pass** (*Xuānguān*) **[2]**
and grow from the **subtle to the manifest** (*zìwēi érzháo*), **[34]**
from the **small to the great** (*yúxiǎo érdà*), **[35]**
from the **near to the distant** (*zìjìn éryuǎn*). **[36]

To **advance the fire and talismans** (*jìn huǒ jìn fú*), **[37]**
to **transport the River Chariot** (*héchē bānyùn*), **[38]**
to **regenerate Yang Lead** (*yángqiān zàishēng*) **[39]**
and **reunite Yin Mercury** (*yīnggǒng fùhé*), **[40]**
to **cook and refine** (*shí pēng shí liàn*) **[41]**
gradually, until the **spirit is complete** (*shén wán*) **[31]**
and the **qi is robust** (*qì zhuàng*), **[9]**
when the **elixir is ripe** (*yào shú*) **[42]**
and the **Dan is perfected** (*dān yuán*), **[43]**
and even the **six senses tremble** (*liùgēn zhèndòng*) **[44]**
while the **six powers are fully realized** (*liùtōng jùzú*), **[45]**
—all this stems from the **Mystic Pass** (*Xuānguān*) **[2]**
once it begins to move.

At this **initial stirring** (*chūdòng*), **[46]**
the **source is purest** (*shuǐyuán zhìqīng*).
The ancients said:
*"The white tiger’s first experience is the greatest treasure,
the divine pool’s water is true gold."*
**[47]

If one **awakens to this** (*yījué*) **[48]**
and **restrains it firmly** (*bǎchí dédìng*), **[49]**
then daily, one **refines one’s own Mercury** (*bāngù yǐjīng*), **[50]**
**encloses and guards the Yin essence** (*bāogù yīnjīng*), **[51]**
just as the **new crescent moon** (*xīnyuè*) **[52]**
first appears as a faint trace,
then grows to half its wheel by the **upper arc** (*shàngxián*), **[53]**
and finally fills to fullness at the **fifteenth day** (*shíwǔ yíngmǎn*). **[54]

Thus, the sage understands
that all under Heaven’s affairs and things
rise from the **low to the high** (*yúbēi zhìgāo*), **[55]**
from the **near to the distant** (*yújìn zhìyuǎn*), **[56]**
all following the **natural Way** (*zìrán zhīdào*) **[57]**
within them.

So, the sage **acts without acting** (*wéiyú wúwéi*) **[58]**
and **grasps without grasping** (*zhíyú wúzhí*) **[59]**
—just as Heaven does not speak
yet the **four seasons proceed** (*sìshí xíng*) **[60]**
and **all things are born** (*bǎiwù shēng*).
How unlike the common folk,
who **reckon ruin as accomplishment** (*huìnuì shìshì*) **[61]**
and **failure as success** (*bàinuì shìgōng*)! **[62]

Those who follow this path
do so from a **single expanse of pristine clarity** (*yīpiàn xūlíng*), **[63]**
wholly undivided.

They **do not know what to desire** (*zìwù suǒyù*) **[64]**
and **equally forget the idea of non-desire** (*yìbìng wángwúyù*).
Thus, it is said:
*"Desire is not desire."* **[65]

Even the **precious millet** (*shúzhū*) **[66]**
is not sought for its own sake—
its **being arises from non-being** (*zìwú ér yǒu*), **[67]**
so though it exists,
it remains **non-being** (*réngwú*).
This **natural virtue** (*xiū suǒ zìjù*) **[68]**
is possessed by those who cultivate the Way,
not needing to be sought from without.

Even if one **studies** (*yǒuxué*), **[69]**
it is as though one **studies nothing** (*réngshì wúxué*).
Thus, it is said:
*"Learning is not learning."* **[70]

Similarly, with **one’s own purity** (*yījǐ zhīchún*) **[71]**
transforming the **impurity of all under Heaven** (*tiānxià zhībó*), **[72]**
or **gathering all under Heaven’s impurity** (*hé tiānxià zhībó*)
into **one’s own purity** (*guī yījǐ zhīchún*), **[73]
the sage’s **guiding and nurturing** (*yòuyè zhòngrén*) **[74]**
and **assisting all things** (*fǔxiàng wànwù*) **[75]**
are rooted in the natural Way alone—
how unlike those who **flaunt their private wisdom** (*chěng qí sīzhì*)! **[76]

**Chapter Sixty-Fifth: The Sage Who Cultivates the Way**
The ancients who were **good at cultivating the Way** (*shàn wéidào*) **[77]**
did not seek to **enlighten the people** (*míng mín*) **[78]**
but rather to **make them simple** (*yú zhī*). **[79]

The people are difficult to govern
because their **wisdom is excessive** (*zhī duō*).
Thus, to **govern with wisdom** (*yǐzhī zhìguó*) **[80]**
is to **plunder the state** (*guó zhī zéi*); **[81]
to **not govern with wisdom** (*bùyǐzhī zhìguó*)
is to **bring the state blessings** (*guó zhī fú*).
Knowing these two principles is the **pattern** (*kǎishì*). **[82]

To **constantly know the pattern** (*chángzhī kǎishì*)
is called **profound virtue** (*Xuándé*).
**[1]**
This **profound virtue** (*Xuándé*) **[1]**
is deep,
far-reaching,
and **contrary to things** (*yǔwù fǎn*).
Only then does one reach **great harmony** (*Dàshùn*). **[83]

Under Heaven, all affairs **prioritize wisdom** (*shàngzhī*) **[84]**
but the **Way alone does not** (*dào bù shàngzhī*)
and instead **values simplicity** (*shàngyú*).
Simplicity is near to the Way.

The **sage’s lineage** (*shèngmén yīguàn xīnchuán*) **[85]**
has been **transmitted like kindling** (*yú yú zhī*).
Only **simple and unrefined Zēngzǐ** (*yú Rú zhī Zēngzǐ*) **[86]**
received it.

Thus, the ancients who were sages
**governed the world with the Way** (*yǐdào zhìtiānxià*)
and **met the people through the Way** (*yǔ mín xiāngjiàn yú dào*) **[87]**
—but not like this,
for it is better to **transform with the people in the Way** (*yǔ mín xiānghuà yú dào*) **[88]**
than to **simply meet them in the Way**.
In **undifferentiated unity** (*húnhún è’ěr*), **[89]**
both return to the **clear and still Heaven** (*qīngjìng zhī tiān*) **[90]—
while those who merely **till fields and dig wells** (*yīshí gēngtián záo jǐng*) **[91]**
rise with the sun and labor,
then rest as the sun sets,
**forgetting the Emperor’s power** (*wàng dìlì yú héyǒu*) **[92]**
and **following the Emperor’s Way** (*shùn dì yú bùzhī*).
**[93]

Ah! How refined is their simplicity!**[94]」

「Descending to later generations, the scholars and officials do not value *qu* (愚) but instead value *zhi* (智). **[95]**
Then, scheming and contrivance proliferate, human hearts grow ever more corrupt,
greed and vulgarity worsen daily, and customs become increasingly deceitful.
The ruin of this people’s moral standards and rites, their transgressions of law and regulations,
and the pity of those who do not fear death—**[96]**
it is difficult to enumerate them all.
All are those who prize talent and cleverness, seeking false gains,
while even the people’s *qu* (愚) now indulge in dark machinations.
The people’s natural sincerity is pierced through,
while deceit and guile abound—**[97]**
they cheat and scheme against one another,
and those who govern them indeed have more to manage than they can handle.
Thus it is said:
“**To govern the state with cleverness is the state’s ruin,
to not govern the state with cleverness is the state’s blessing.**” **[98]**
Why is this so?

It is because if the people are given knowledge,
their undifferentiated sincerity is already broken;
if they remain in ignorance and unawareness,
their nameless simplicity is preserved.
The two differ in cleverness and *qu* (愚),
and their benefits and harms are distinct.
Better to value *qu* (愚) and gain benefit
than to value cleverness and incur harm.

Knowing these two principles,
not only does one govern the world thus,
but also cultivate one’s self—**[99]**
they are both worthy of being models.

Knowing these models,
one is near the **Dao/Way**.

Great practitioners of the **Dao/Way**,
in the state of not seeing and not hearing, **[100]**
return to the spirit of no thought and no concern.
This cannot be achieved by merely closing one’s ears and eyes,
or by suppressing cleverness and awareness—**[101]**
only then can one attain **stillness and tranquility**.

If there is even a trace of calculation,
or a single scheming thought,
then the **Primordial Purity** (*Tàipǔ*) **[102]**
is incomplete, and the **undifferentiated unity** (*húnhún*) **[103]**
is lost—**[104]**
ruined indeed.

Knowing that cleverness harms oneself,
while *qu* (愚) benefits one’s body,
to not flaunt one’s cleverness
and to joyfully uphold one’s *qu* (愚)—**[105]**
this is called **Mysterious Virtue/Power** (*Xuándé*).
All that can be named is not **Mysterious Virtue/Power**;
only what cannot be named or spoken of,
deep and boundless, distant and unfathomable—**[106]**
this can be called **Mysterious Virtue/Power**.

Though it shares with flying and creeping things,
and with the dull and sluggish, **[107]**
the same lack of desire and knowledge,
they cannot attain the **ultimate source** (*jīngyuán*) **[108]**
or exhaust its threads—**[109]**
they merely live in perpetual darkness.
Humans, however, progress from coarse to refined,
from the source to its branches,
until they reach **the Three Primordials united**, **[110]**
and the **Great Ultimate returns to the Supreme Ultimate**. **[111]**
Even then, they may still achieve **transformation into the divine** (*shénhuà*), **[112]**
but it is not yet truly reversing with things. **[113]

To govern the state, one should not value cleverness,
but to cultivate the **Dao/Way**, one must especially cherish *qu* (愚).
Indeed, cleverness is the state’s thief,
while *qu* (愚) is the seed of the **Dao/Way**.

How can *qu* (愚) be the seed of the **Dao/Way**?
Consider: within **undifferentiated unity** (*húnhún*), **[114]**
there is no thought or concern,
no knowledge—**[115]**
is this not what is called *qu* (愚)?

Suddenly awakening within it,
this is the **non-arising, non-perishing** (*wúshēng wúmiè*) **[116]**
original nature.
No one can claim to hold onto this awakening,
to cultivate the **Supreme and Perfect Awakening** (*shàngshì zhèngjué zhèngjué*) **[117]**
and thereby escape the cycle of rebirth,
unsubjected to the **influence of yin and yang** (*yīnyáng gǔzhào*), **[118]**
unbound by ghosts and spirits.

Even within this **undifferentiated unity** (*húnhún*), **[119]**
if one suddenly awakens,
and I guard this true awakening without letting it disperse,
this single awakening has already reached **Prajñāpāramitā** (*bōluómí*). **[120]
If one can steadfastly uphold it,
constantly guard it,
then the seeds of rebirth
will be severed from here.

If one gives rise to another thought,
or forms another view,
this is the **postnatal awareness of desire** (*shòutiān shíyù zhī shén*) **[121]**
mixing in—**[122]**
what is said:
“**Infinite movements give rise to the root of birth and death,
the deluded call it the original person.**” **[123]

In essence, the spirit is one,
but desire makes it two.
Two thoughts, three minds,
are the **defiled roots and dust** (*záwàng gēnchén*) **[124]**
that lead to the path of birth and death.
Only with one mind,
no dual thoughts,
having right awareness,
no deluded mind—**[125]**
the **Dao/Way** lies here.

If one can further dissolve this single right awareness,
this is the **Great Ultimate returning to the Supreme Ultimate** (*Tàijí huán yú Wújí*), **[126]**
and the **Golden Immortals’** (*Jīnxian*) **[127]**
realization lies within this refining of the void.

What is **refining the void** (*liànxū*)?
It is like in the state of **undifferentiated unity** (*húnhún*), **[128]**
muddled and confused,
as *qu* (愚) as if drunk,
with no awareness and no knowledge—**[129]**
this is the void.
When seated practice reaches **no self, no person**, **[130]**
no place, no heaven,
this too is refining the void.

It is further said:
“The essentials of learning the **Dao/Way** begin with forgetting the self,
then forgetting others, **[131]**
then forgetting the **Dao/Way** itself,
until one reaches the **forgetting of forgetting** (*wàngwàng zhī jí*) **[132]**
—this is the ultimate attainment.

If one can take this single moment as master,
and use true awakening as function,
the **Dao/Way** is not distant.

However, though the method of refining the void is thus,
its practice must begin with refining the nature.
Ancient people called this **forging the mirror** (*zhùjìng*). **[133]

If the mind is not refined,
one is dim and confused,
unaware even if it is right before one’s eyes—**[134]**
how much more so for those who possess the **Six Perfections** (*liùtōng*)? **[135]
If one’s mind is still clouded by private desires,
then one has not yet reached the void.

After true awakening,
one must not allow even a thread or fragment of desire to remain in one’s chest—**[136]
then the **Numinous Platform’s** (*Língtái*) **[137]**
precious mirror will always shine brightly,
and yet it need not be fully perfected and complete—**[138]
it is the emission of a single ray of light.

Even within this **undifferentiated unity** (*húnhún*), **[139]**
if one suddenly gains a single awareness,
and no longer knows anything else,
if one suddenly awakens,
and no longer seeks another awakening—**[140]
this single moment is already piercing and bright,
reaching all four directions without obstruction.

Though students of the **Dao/Way** have a full vessel of private desires,
suddenly to expect purity like white jade or gold—**[141]
how could this be achieved by a single moment of voidness?
It is necessary first to forge the **Yin and Yang Swords** (*cíhóng èr jiàn*), **[142]**
to eliminate the **demons of form and no-form** (*yǒuxíng wúxíng zhī mó*). **[143]

If these swords are not sharp,
then the **demons of desire, form, heaven, and humans** (*yùmó, sèmó, tiānmó, rénmó*) **[144]
cannot be swept away and thoroughly purged,
and the **true face of the Great Primordial** (*Qínyuán zhēnmiànmù*) **[145]
cannot be revealed.

For human desires and heavenly principles
are mixed for many years,
though one wishes to stand alone in the central current,
there are forces difficult to resist.

Thus, though one knows clearly,
one still transgresses—**[146]
this is because those who draw one into human desires are many,
while those who draw one into heavenly principles are few.

Now I tell those who study:
To achieve a **pure and still body of dharma** (*qīngjìng fǎshēn*), **[147]
one must first have a pure and still spirit.
To obtain a pure and still spirit,
one must have **vast and boundless qi**.
What is called forging the swords has no other method—**[148]
it is by nurturing the **morning qi** (*píngdàn zhī qì*) **[149]
directly without harm,
until one attains the **vast and mighty** (*hàorán gāngdà*). **[150]」

Once the spirit-sword is forged and its edge sharp,
it can cleave demons and sever evil.
At that time,
no matter how alluring the seductive sounds or beautiful forms may be,
the mind remains undisturbed—
even if a fair maiden lies beside one in sleep,
one would not know.
No matter how fierce the malevolent spirits or ominous omens approach,
the spirit remains unshaken—
even if a mountain like Tai collapses before one,
there would be no fear.

Those who forge this spirit-sword
must possess a will as resolute as the divine—
yet they often falter in practice,
starting and stopping,
unwilling to firmly establish their footing at once.

If one can sever with a single stroke,
and extinguish a private thought the moment it arises,
never allowing it to resurface,
then the path of birth-and-death’s cycle is cut.
The Daoist student has no other secret—
only fear of failing to recognize the mirror-sword of clarity.
If one recognizes it,
then in this instant, the luminous mirror shines universally,
admitting no evil or delusion,
while the wisdom-sword hangs suspended—
desire-demons are severed at once.
From this moment, if one holds fast to a single thought,
then the divine chamber may be formed,
and the elixir refined.

This luminous mirror and wisdom-sword
are the essential tasks of the cultivator.
If the sword’s edge is dull,
how can it sever evil spirits?
Thus, the more one restrains the heart,
the more it becomes disordered.
If the precious mirror lacks light,
how can one distinguish principle from desire?
Thus, the more one strives to overcome,
the more it multiplies.

Mencius spoke of nurturing *qi* but not of nurturing the heart—
truly, when *qi* is sufficient, the heart naturally settles.
Those who merely force the heart,
without knowing that righteousness generates *qi*,
are far from the Dao.

Li Erqu said:
*"The human heart is naturally joyous,
yet it binds itself with private desires.
When private desires first arise,
the clear knowing still perceives it.
Once perceived, it is dispelled at once—
this heart remains joyous as before."*

The humble old man said:
*"Luminous and silent, it pervades the sands of the river—
the mundane and the sage originally share one home.
When not a thought arises, the whole body manifests;
the six senses stir, yet are veiled by clouds.
Severing vexations only increases suffering,
seeking True Suchness is also delusion.
Worldly affairs follow their course without obstruction,
Nirvana and death are but empty flowers."*

Let those who study heart-nature ponder these words thrice!

**Chapter 66: The King of All Valleys**
The reason the river and sea can be the king of all valleys
is that they excel in descending—
thus they become the king of all valleys.

Therefore, the Sage who wishes to surpass others
must first speak in a way that places them below;
who wishes to lead others
must first place themselves behind.
Thus, the Sage, though in high station, is not feared,
though in the forefront, is not resented.
Thus, the world willingly follows without tiring.

Because they do not contend,
the world cannot contend with them.

All people wish others to serve them,
yet some, though unwilling to serve, are served,
while others, though eager to be served, are served even less—
this is because they exalt themselves,
unwilling to lower their hearts or humble their *qi*.

Observe how the river and sea are where all valleys flow—
it is because they descend that they become the king of valleys.
If the river and sea were to place themselves above the valleys,
though the valleys might still flow toward them,
what would receive them?
Thus, the Sage perceives this early:
to surpass others, one must speak in a way that places them below—
as Yao consulted the Four Mountains,
and Shun inquired of the Four Gates—
all teachings, commands, and matters
were sought from ministers and neighbors,
never exalting their own wisdom.
This is why the more one descends,
the more others ascend.

To lead others, one must place oneself behind—
as Yu, Gao, and Yi, though enlightened first,
in all things yielded precedence to others,
placing themselves last.
This is why the more one retreats,
the more others advance.

Because they place themselves below and behind,
even seated on the imperial throne,
they lack the awe of tyranny—
thus, they are not feared.
Though they hold the mandate of the divine,
they lack the harshness of domination—
thus, they are not resented.
Therefore, the world willingly follows them as leaders,
without a trace of aversion.

The *Odes* say:
*"There is none there who is evil,
here none who is exhausted.
May it be that night and day,
to perpetuate one’s good name."*
What else is this but:
because they do not contend for superiority or precedence,
others naturally place them above and before,
revering their teachings, fearing their spirit,
and endlessly receiving their grace and virtue—
how could they then dare to contend for superiority or precedence,
and vie with the Sage for victory?

This is an analogy for elixir refinement:
first, the divine fire descends into the elixir field,
then the fire steams and the water boils,
and gold forms at the water’s base—
only then does the elixir of longevity emerge.

Humans receive the central essence of Heaven and Earth to live,
originally complete and whole.
Yet after birth,
*qi* and constitution constrain them,
and desires obscure them—
the original *qi* of Heaven,
long scattered through the body’s stale *qi*,
cannot be gathered in one place for ages.

Now, to converge the Five Phases,
harmonize the Four Symbols,
unite them in the central palace,
and return to the mysterious orifice—
one must first release all entanglements,
allow no private thoughts to arise,
lower the curtain to seal the mouth,
view with the eyes through the nose,
oppose the nose to the navel,
and descend the heart-fire into the elixir field—
in but a moment’s work,
the mysterious passage opens,
and the primal Yang returns.
The *qi* of the whole body
naturally gathers in the elixir field,
fusing and effervescing,
a joy beyond naming.

Yet at the outset of observation,
if the fire is not intense, the metal does not emerge from the ore;
if the fire is too fierce, it scorches the essence and blood,
stifling the vital spark.
Only when it clings yet does not adhere,
is present yet absent,
does the *qi* of the elixir field leap with movement.

This is like the river and sea’s ability to descend to all valleys—
the valleys thus flow toward them.
The Sage can descend to all under Heaven—
thus all under Heaven turns to them.

In a single body,
the heart is the most exalted and noble,
while all other limbs are small and lowly.

「——The Dao/Way thus uses the vastness of the river and sea and the nobility of the Sage to illustrate the heart,
while the smallness of the valleys and the lowliness of the myriad people serve to illustrate the hundred bodies,
illustrating the **elixir field**.

Practitioners of the Dao/Way must also take "descending" as their foundation,
take "lowliness" as their base,
and not place themselves above the noble and exalted,
so that they may descend beneath others—
thus what they achieve will be effortless.

As for ordinary mortals,
their essence (*shén*) is the master,
while their *qi* follows its movements of activity and rest,
giving rise to birth and death,
to the creation and nurturing of males and females.

But the **Perfect Person** (*zhìrén*) takes *qi* as the master,
and their essence (*shén*) obeys its transformations and shifts.
As the *Treasure of Awakening* says,
*"Though others are the hosts, I am the guest"*—this is so.

For those who truly cultivate the Dao/Way,
when the *qi* moves,
they reverse the workings of Nature,
upset the order of Heaven and Earth,
and merely listen as it ascends and descends,
enters and exits the furnace,
sealing and refining—
then await the true essence,
cycling and turning,
never relying on the essence (*shén*) to govern it.

But when they observe the true *qi* in its harmonious surge,
and reverse the natural order in their cultivation,
the work is complete in nine cycles,
the elixir ripens, the pearl becomes luminous—
is this not exalted, towering above all?

Resplendent, they stand first—
yet how can they be the revered ones of the ten thousand households,
the objects of admiration for all under Heaven?

Only when they dwell in lowliness and follow behind in this manner,
does their single-minded and tranquil aspiration,
their humble and harmonious heart,
prevent the overturning of the elixir field or the furnace,
the flight of mercury or the scattering of lead.
Thus, when they are above, others do not weigh them down;
when they are before, others do not harm them.
Since they do not contend,
all under Heaven cannot contend with them.

**Chapter 67: I Possess Three Treasures**
All under Heaven say I am great—
it seems I do not resemble them.
For only the great can seem not to resemble them;
if I resembled them, long ago I would have been small.

I possess Three Treasures,
which I hold and preserve:
the first is called **compassion** (*cí*),
the second is called **frugality** (*jiǎn*),
the third is **not daring to act before all under Heaven**.

For compassion (*cí*) brings forth courage;
frugality (*jiǎn*) brings forth breadth;
not daring to act before all under Heaven
brings forth completion and longevity.

Now to abandon compassion (*cí*) for courage,
to abandon frugality (*jiǎn*) for breadth,
to abandon following behind for acting first—
this is death.

For compassion (*cí*), when used in battle, brings victory;
when used in defense, brings firmness.
When Heaven intends to save them,
it shields them with compassion (*cí*).

The Dao/Way is originally boundless,
yet it is also the **Great Ultimate** (*tàijí*).
Neither great nor small,
neither great nor small—
it is both great and small.

There are indeed words beyond thought and conjecture that cannot be exhausted.
If one insists on calling it "great,"
then *"the boundless and mighty *qi*,
filling the space between Heaven and Earth"* is the description.
If one seeks to depict it as "small,"
then *"the nameless gem,
most hidden and subtle,
concealed in the vast expanse"* is the portrayal.

In its relation to humanity,
to possess it means life,
to lose it means death.
All arise from the boundless yet manifest,
from the subtle yet becoming substantial.

For indeed, the origin lies in the subtle,
while its unfolding is in the great.
Better to speak of its smallness to reveal the Dao/Way
than to speak of its greatness to explain it.

Thus the Dao/Way says,
*"All under Heaven says I am great."*

For "I" is the Dao/Way itself.
The Dao/Way originally has no form, no body—
yet now it is called "great,"
which implies the Dao/Way has form and body that can be likened—
this seems not to resemble the Dao/Way.

Only the great cannot be named as "great,"
so it does not resemble what others call "great."
If one wishes to embody the Dao/Way of Heaven,
to resemble my form,
from the opening of Heaven to the present day,
those who established the poles of Heaven,
who expounded the Dao/Way and clarified the teachings—
long ago,
they all used the **Ultimate of Boundlessness** (*wújí*) as the ultimate,
the **non-divine essence** (*bùshén*’s *shén*) as the essence,
the most subtle and minute as the Dao/Way.

Yet the Dao/Way is so silent and odorless,
so vague and distant—
how then can scholars begin to cultivate it?

The Dao/Way says,
*"I possess Three Treasures,
which I hold and preserve."*
If one earnestly does not lose them,
treasures them as treasures,
and never forgets them,
then one may return to the origin,
restore the source,
and revive the primordial descent of the Sovereign.

What are these Three Treasures?
The first is compassion (*cí*), which is also **benevolence** (*rén*).
Benevolent and compassionate,
it is the fundamental essence of Heaven.
The gentleman embodies benevolence (*rén*),
and this suffices to nurture humanity.

Moreover, it encompasses the four cardinal virtues,
and includes all virtues—
benevolence (*rén*) is within them,
and thus the Dao/Way is within them.
Pursue it to its fullest,
and it can embrace Heaven and Earth,
permeate past and present.

This is the foundation of the **Golden Elixir** (*jīndān*),
which cultivators should earnestly treasure and reflect upon.

Yet its Dao/Way extends to the utmost greatness,
while its roots arise from the utmost subtlety.
If one does not know to forget all thoughts,
to inwardly illuminate with a single awareness,
but instead only strives for breadth and becomes scattered,
for comprehensiveness and becomes superficial,
then they will gain nothing of benevolence (*rén*),
nothing of the Dao/Way.

Only when one turns inward to seek within themselves,
steadfastly guards it in the heart,
and when they wish to establish, they establish for others,
when they wish to reach, they reach for others—
this is the ancient saying:
*"To grasp one is to accomplish all ten thousand affairs."*
Is this not the essence of frugality (*jiǎn*)?

For frugality (*jiǎn*) is the method to seek benevolence (*rén*),
the key to cultivating the Dao/Way.
Scholars, having known its compassion (*cí*),
should further nourish it with frugality (*jiǎn*),
only then can they align with the Dao/Way.

Although,
if one is self-proclaimed as great and exalted,
and lacks the measure of humility and harmony,
then within they only know themselves,
and outwardly they disdain all others.
They boast of their cleverness,
and arrogantly claim wisdom—
they proudly declare themselves as those who first awaken and first harmonize,
often believing that none under Heaven can surpass them—
in the end,
their nature is not tranquil,
their *qi* is not harmonious,
and even if they wish for the elixir to complete nine cycles,
for the Dao/Way to reach the ninth heaven—
this is difficult indeed.

The ancients said:
*"The essential key to refining the elixir is
to take **awakened awareness** (*língjué*) as the Dao/Way’s form,
and **harmonious surge** (*chōnghé*) as the Dao/Way’s function.
Thus, in all things and places,
one should not dare to act before all under Heaven."*

Moreover, compassion (*cí*) is
the innate capacity of human hearts.
To exhaust one’s own heart
in establishing the fate of all things—
what a vast vow!
To nourish the nature of a single inch
in seeking the peace of all things—
what a mighty spirit!

Those who guard compassion (*cí*)
are those who cultivate courage.
Zengzi said to Zixiang,
*"When reflecting upon oneself and not shrinking back,
even if clad in coarse garments and living in a humble dwelling,
I would not fear."*」

Self-reflection brings contraction—
though facing ten thousand men,
I would still go forward.

Is there one who possesses a single piece of compassion,
yet lacks the courage to bend even a hair?
Necessarily, what one guards must be restrained,
before what one extends can be vast.
Without restraint, how can there be breadth?

Only by carefully attending to actions,
moderating thoughts,
focusing the mind within the inch of heart,
gathering the hundred limbs at the elixir field—
endlessly, unceasingly,
unbroken, unflagging,
unceasingly pure—
without slackness, without waste,
one naturally refines the self
and the world finds peace.

Without frugality, how could there be breadth?

To dare not act before all under Heaven—
this is the radiant virtue of humility and reverence,
the stable and auspicious goodness.
If one can yield and conform to all under Heaven,
none will dare contend with them,
and if one can follow and receive the Dao/Way,
the Dao/Way in silence will aid its fulfillment.
Without the virtue of harmonious surge,
how dare one act before all under Heaven,
and yet achieve such greatness in late maturity?

Thus, compassion, frugality, and yielding—
these are the fundamental beginnings of seeking the Dao/Way.
Courage, breadth, and precedence—
these are the later manifestations of serving the Dao/Way.

Yet today’s scholars are not so.
Abandoning compassion for courage,
they nurture a heart of ruthlessness;
abandoning frugality for breadth,
they harbor greedy desires;
abandoning yielding for precedence,
they court contention—
all are paths to death.
Even if they barely survive,
they are but walking corpses among the living,
mingling recklessly with the crowd—
what difference is there between them and the dead?

In summary, compassion is the vital principle of humanity,
a nature shared by all.
Only by guarding it with restraint,
expressing it with harmony,
does compassion and pity flow naturally from sincerity,
and none under Heaven can fail to be moved and submit.
Compassion suffices to command others—
the virtuous have no rival.
Why should they not be invincible in battle,
unshakable in defense,
and free from shame before all under Heaven?

The *Book of Documents* says:
*"Heaven’s dark and nurturing influence descends upon the people,
harmonizing their dwellings."*
Granting them life and fulfillment,
they eternally enjoy the peace of Heaven—
this is what is meant by Heaven’s intent to save them.

The *Odes* say:
*"The mandate of Heaven is profound and unceasing."*
It reveals the pure and boundless *qi*,
flowing and unfailing, boundless in its nurturing—
truly, it is guarded by compassion alone.

The Dao/Way is called the Great Dao,
its essence is the boundless and the Supreme Ultimate.
Yet without beginning from the boundless,
how could one, amid the indistinct and formless,
discern the origins of the Supreme Ultimate’s roots?

Scholars must first clarify the Dao’s source,
within the unseen and unheard,
seeking the most hidden and subtle essence—
this is the so-called empty yet responsive.

Though its subtlety is extreme—
like a grain of millet or a pearl—
it is as if there were something to perceive.
In truth, it is form without form,
shape without shape—
approach it, and you see no head;
follow it, and you see no tail.
It is the gentle compassion within the human heart.

Even the most hardened and base of men
cannot extinguish their innate compassion.
Confucius said:
*"If I desire virtue,
virtue will immediately come."*

「Refining the elixir—what else is there to it?
It is nothing but nurturing this innate compassion.

What is this compassion?
Like King Qi of the State of Qi who, seeing a cow trembling in fear, could not bear to harm it,
or a commoner who, upon witnessing a weak child fall into a well, feels a surge of pity—
these are all stirrings of the *ren* heart,
where the *Dao*’s gentle breath returns.

When one reflects upon this,
this impulse, this thought, is ever-present—
only fearing that men may not yet perceive it.

Scholars must begin from the natural and true,
expanding and cultivating outward,
thus laying the foundation for elixir refinement.

Yet one must not seek breadth at the expense of neglect—
only guard simplicity and subtlety.

With a single mind devoted to preserving life,
and the five senses in full submission,
the ancients said:
*"The heart must dwell within the chest,
and thoughts must never stray beyond the *Zongzhi* gate."*

From this, the more refined the simplicity,
the broader the expansion;
the more subtle the essence,
the more manifest its brilliance.

The more precise the restraint,
the more vast its reach.
Can scholars not take frugality as their root?

Though *virtue/power* lies in humility,
strict simplicity is indeed a fine method to avoid loss—
yet if one does not temper it with modesty and harmony,
the danger of impulsive violence may arise,
like flames scorching the elixir.
Thus, guarding simplicity must also be utterly harmonious,
ever humble, ever small,
never presuming to lead—
only then does one achieve *emptiness* and serve others.

When compassion is ever-present,
the *Dao*’s breath will surely remain.

If one values courage over compassion,
luxury over frugality,
and seeks to lead rather than follow,
how can one hope to possess the Three Treasures,
unshaken and unlost?

Moreover, compassion brings favor—
use it in battle, and victory will be certain;
employ it in defense, and the fortress will stand firm.
This is like the metaphor of tending the furnace,
burning away the Three Corpses of malice,
and bathing in the *Dao*’s purity—
thus safeguarding the embryo, the fetus, and the original spirit.

It is this gentle and harmonious heart
that is essential for refining the elixir and nurturing the *Dao*.

Consider that Heaven, in creating man,
grants him life and endows him with compassion—
none are without it.
If one can temper thoughts to align with compassion,
the elixir of eternal life is already within reach.

**Chapter 68: The Virtue of Non-Contention**
A true warrior does not rely on force,
a true fighter does not rage,
a true victor does not contend,
and a true leader serves those beneath him.

This is the virtue of non-contention,
this is the power of serving others,
this is aligning with Heaven—
the ancient ideal.

Warriors (*shì*, 士), are judges of men.
Judges of men wield armies,
and their essence is martial valor.
The *Yì* says:
*"Firm yet responsive, venturing risks while yielding to them,
mighty in spirit yet never slaying."*
This is martial valor without violence—
the warrior’s true excellence.

When facing a great enemy,
two armies locked in battle,
one must not fail to deploy troops,
brandish weapons, and charge with the fury of rivers and thunder.
Yet, when examined closely,
soldiers are few—
yet a mighty foe suddenly presses the borders.
It is not difficult to repel the invaders of the Central Plains
with music, or to break the siege of a hostile state
with harmonious melodies.
This is the power of unyielding yet without anger—
the iron mace tempered in gentleness.

When the time comes to return in triumph,
vibrant with victory,
while others boast in the court,
he alone humbles himself—
the so-called "Great Tree General"—
and can stand without shame.

Even if rewards are lavishly bestowed,
he remains ever modest—
this is how the true victor does not contend.

The *Shū* says:
*"You alone do not contend,
and thus none under Heaven can contend with you."*
Is this not what it means?

Such men arise from heeding wise counsel,
gathering broad thoughts for mutual benefit,
not relying on their own talents,
but using the talents of others as their own;
not boasting of their own wisdom,
but employing the wisdom of others as their own.

This is the meaning of humbling oneself to serve others.
If one does not listen to noble words,
consult good paths,
keep an open heart like a valley,
and maintain modesty while shining with virtue,
how can one hope to rule the world
with the skill of a warrior or victor?

This is the virtue of non-contention,
which wins the people.

This is the power of serving others,
which commands the world.

This is the way of following the people below,
aligning with Heaven above—
thus winning the hearts of the people.

This is the power of serving others,
which commands the world.

This is the way of following the people below,
aligning with Heaven above—
to stand with Heaven and Earth,
and achieve the ancient ideal of humanity.

Alas! Without the saint’s utmost sincerity,
exhausting his nature,
how could one, in the midst of war and chaos,
embody the spirit of peace and courtesy,
wielding force without violence,
acting in anger yet without rage,
contending yet without strife?

And when it comes to ruling with benevolence,
planning with humility,
placing the worthy in office,
and employing the capable in their roles—
the men of the world all offer their loyalty,
pour out their sincerity,
and eagerly rush to perform their duties,
sacrificing their lives as commanded—
as the seasons flow naturally without speech,
and all things present their offerings in harmony,
this is how one aligns with Heaven and Earth
and achieves the ancient ideal of humanity.

The *Shī* says:
*"Ponder the virtuous Lord Ji, who aligned with Heaven,
and none could surpass his excellence."*
If not for such men, who else could we follow?

This speaks of the elixir’s growth and the furnace’s fire—
though there are methods of fierce refining and rapid tempering,
they must still adapt to the moment,
follow the natural course,
and act with force without violence—
thus avoiding the calamity of overturned elixir or shattered crucible.

When the *qi*’s movement is unrestrained,
the true *yang* energy flourishes,
mighty and unyielding,
filling the two great voids—
what difference is there from a warrior’s blazing fury,
sweeping all before him?

Even when gathering and refining the fire,
it is only because the boundless *qi* is expanded—
not a love of strength for its own sake.

Thus, after purification,
common bones transmute into jade bones,
and mortal flesh becomes a golden body.
The *Yì* says:
*"Taking the Golden Elixir in the palm of one’s hand
is like seizing the world as one would pick up a mustard seed."*

Only when the spirit is concentrated without distraction,
and breath is regulated without effort,
does one purely follow Heaven,
unmixed with human interference.
Even when Heaven and man clash,
reason and desire vie,
one must not fail to suppress desire with reason,
and exhaust humanity to merge with Heaven.

When study reaches its essence and virtue is perfected,
when compassion is deep and wisdom mature—
free of all selfish ambition, of striving for merit or skill—
this is how the virtuous stand unchallenged beneath Heaven and Earth.

Such is the way of the humble and yielding,
of emptying oneself to serve others,
listening to the rise and fall of *qi* and moving with it.
Thus the Elixir forms unseen yet manifest,
unchanging yet transforming,
effortless yet complete—
what difference is there from a master’s skill in guiding others?

In cultivating the human Dao,
if one remains ever at peace,
ever yielding,
desire suppressed without force,
virtue upheld without effort—
this is the virtue of *wu zheng* [non-contention].

With a heart of non-contention,
following principle to act,
responding to circumstances with ease—
it is as if using others’ strength to achieve one’s own purpose,
thus encompassing Heaven and Earth’s transformations without overreaching.

Confucius said:
*"What does Heaven speak? The four seasons move in it,
and all things take life from it."*

The Sage aligns with the Dao’s truth,
upright as the celestial threads and terrestrial measures,
setting the standard for all ages to come.

**Chapter LXIX: The Victorious Through Compassion**

There is a military saying:
*"I dare not be the aggressor while the enemy is passive,
nor advance an inch without retreating a foot."*

This is called:
moving without moving,
pushing without arms,
holding without weapons,
yet standing unconquered.

No greater calamity than underestimating the enemy—
to do so risks losing what is most precious.
Thus, when armies clash,
the compassionate prevail.

Ancient strategists spoke thus:
*"I dare not be the aggressor but must be the guest."*
The aggressor is like a sovereign—
one who issues commands and rules with authority;
the guest is like a minister—
one who obeys the sovereign’s will, as in *"Let others be the host, I the guest."*

Thus, when I am the aggressor,
it is the post-Heaven human heart that takes command,
while the pre-Heaven Dao-heart steps back in submission.
How dare I make the post-Heaven human heart the aggressor,
only to then make it the guest,
relying on it as if it were my very life?

*"I dare not advance an inch without retreating a foot"* means:
when victorious, even an inch gained must be held firm;
when defeated, not even a single step withdrawn may be conceded.
To advance an inch in gain while retreating a foot in loss—
this is to gain little and lose much,
easy to fail, hard to succeed.
In warfare, this is the mark of an incompetent general;
in Dao cultivation, this is the fate of one who achieves nothing.
How dare I?

Only by yielding to others as the aggressor,
submitting myself as the guest,
can I ensure they have openings to exploit,
while I have no vulnerabilities to attack—
thus controlling others without being controlled,
avoiding humiliation.

Advance when opportunity arises,
retreat when danger looms.
In advancing, one must rouse the spirit of boldness;
in retreating, one must not present a vulnerable posture.
Such caution, such vigilance—
it is nothing but always yielding precedence to others,
while placing oneself last.

Thus, when marching an army,
it seems others can move while I cannot;
yet when I press forward with resolve,
brandishing my arms—
it seems others have arms while I have none.
When facing the enemy in battle,
two armies locked in combat—
it seems others can contend while I cannot.

Though drums thunder and flags billow,
though they wield weapons, I do not lack arms.
Yet I advance not lightly, retreat not lightly—
truly knowing that the fate of the state,
the rise or fall of the nation,
rests on this single battle.
How can the enemy be treated with contempt?

Observe the ancients who treated the enemy with caution—
they often succeeded;
those who underestimated the enemy—
they often failed.
As seen in Guan Zhong’s campaign against the Shanrong,
or Zi Yu’s battle at Chengpu.

Moreover, the prosperity or decline of the court
depends on the success or failure of its generals.
If one does not approach matters with utmost prudence,
but instead flaunts their talent or relies on their wit,
boasting *"None surpass me"*—
like Meng Ming’s reckless advance or Gao Gu’s overconfidence—
there has never been one who did not bring ruin to the state,
destroy their own house,
or extinguish their ancestral rites.

The Sage’s greatest treasure is *xin* [trust].
To underestimate the enemy is to betray the sovereign’s trust.

When two armies oppose each other,
two forces clash—
yet one remains weak, yielding,
utterly compassionate and benevolent.
They fear the *sha fa* [killing energy] disrupting Heaven and Earth’s harmony,
and take no joy in military might expanding territory.
Warfare is resorted to only as a last necessity.
Though no weeping for fallen soldiers,
the heart of compassion and sorrow
already permeates the assembled armies,
and the common people are moved to gratitude.

Thus, the virtuous either do not war,
or if they do, they prevail.
Is this not because the heart of sorrow
extends to others,
entering their very beings?

This illustrates the birth of true *yang* energy,
the *qi* flourishing—
only then may the fire be refined.
If one does not patiently await the *yin* [lead] *qi*’s movement,
but instead forces the spirit’s ascent and descent,
advance and retreat in rigid cycles—
there will be no failure to burn the body with wild fire,
to suffer great humiliation.

At the four critical junctures,
one must await the *kan* [water] *qi*’s natural impulse,
lest *li* [fire] usurp dominance—
hence, *"I dare not be the aggressor but must be the guest."*

In the Dao of cultivation,
progress is constant, regression brings disaster.
Like Heaven’s ceaseless motion,
water’s unceasing flow—
only thus does one ascend daily.
If one proceeds intermittently, then halts,
exposed to cold after brief warmth—
this is to advance an inch while retreating a foot,
gaining little, losing much,
and thus never achieving success in this lifetime.

This arises from ignorance of the way of returning to the Root,
restoring the Mandate—
the path of daily practice,
which cannot be seized by clever schemes,
nor attained through contrivance.

To cultivate the Dao of alchemy in reverse—
to align with Nature’s spontaneous flow—
is to learn as though unlearning,
to labor without labor,
to follow the natural sequence,
to advance with the momentum,
unhindered, unforced—
like Yu taming the floods,
acting where no action is needed.

If one recklessly stokes the fire,
lightly advancing and retreating,
it is like a general underestimating the enemy—
never fails to kindle disaster,
burning the elixir,
shattering the furnace and crucible,
until lead and mercury scatter in ruin.

Ah! To trust Nature purely,
with reverent caution,
is to weave through radiance unbroken.
But if one contrives clever schemes,
the treasure of longevity
will be ravaged by the post-heaven corpse-thief,
destroyed utterly—
how then could one hope for an elixir
both enduring and vast?

Only with boundless compassion,
tenfold sorrow,
does one act with peace,
proceed with gentleness—
then Nature yields without resistance,
and victory is inevitable.

The seeker must not let post-heaven intellect dominate,
but let primordial *qi* retreat and listen.
Only then may one avoid error.

**Chapter Seventy: The Humble Garment, the Precious Jade**
My words are easy to know,
easy to practice.
Yet none under Heaven can know them,
none can practice them.

Words have their root,
affairs have their master.
Only the ignorant fail to recognize me.
Few know me—
thus I am noble.

Therefore, the Sage wears coarse rags,
yet cradles jade within.
He obscures his traces in mountains and caves,
hiding his life in rocks and hollows—
only to follow the nature of life and fate,
harmonize with Heaven and Earth,
cultivate what lies within himself,
while others’ knowledge or ignorance
he does not concern himself with.
Thus the wise grow wiser,
while the foolish grow more foolish.

The Supreme’s words—
every line is Dao,
every character touches the self.
To speak of Dao is to speak of the self,
and to speak of the self is to speak of Dao—
nothing is simpler than this.
Yet what is difficult to know or practice?

It is only that people seek far
when the Dao is near,
demand difficulty
when the matter is easy.
They neglect the true, constant Dao,
and instead pursue dregs and remnants—
like rhetoric, memorization, legalisms, and divination.
The more they learn, the more their minds stray;
the more they do, the more their nature decays.
No wonder the Supreme’s words
were universally understood in his time,
yet only he alone preserved them afterward.
It is because they failed to grasp
that words have a root,
affairs have a master.

This root, this master—
it is the “middle” within the self.
When Yao and Shun passed on the heart-transmission,
it was nothing but *“faithfully holding to the middle.”*
Later, in Confucius’ *“pure unity”*,
Zengzi’s *“solitary vigilance”*,
Wang Yangming’s *“innate knowledge”*—
all point to the self’s *“middle.”*
This *“middle”* encompasses much.

Within the body, it lies in guarding the *xing* (formal) middle—
Zhu Xi said: *“Guard the middle, regulate the outer.”*
To guard the middle is to turn light inward,
focus on the center.
At the point one inch below the navel—
neither clinging nor rejecting.

It also lies in guarding the formless middle—
as the *Zhongyong* says:
*“Before joy, anger, sorrow, or delight arise—this is the middle.”*
Lu Congyan taught Li Yanping:
*“In stillness, observe the state before joy, anger, sorrow, or delight arise—
this unseen, unheard moment,
where caution and awe are natural,
nature settles, the spirit clears,
and the original face appears.
Then human desires grow pure,
Heaven’s principle shines anew.
Since ancient sages, immortals, and Buddhas—
all took this as their first step.
Yet one must first guard the strenuous middle,
and finally attain the effortless middle.”*

Though the Three Teachings name it differently,
they all take *“middle”* as root and master.
Even in my teaching—
to concentrate the spirit, regulate the breath,
then return to observe the original orifice,
letting the heart have no heart,
the *qi* have no *qi*—
only then does the heart grow still,
the breath harmonize.
The key lies in *“heart still.”*
No disturbance in the heart is stillness;
to hold fast to the middle is stillness.
Stillness is within this middle.

The heart here is the *Xuan Guan* orifice of the *Dan Jing*—
when spirit and *qi* become interdependent,
the substance of *Xuan Guan* is already established,
this is the root of the Dao/Way,
the origin of the Golden Elixir.

As for advancing fire and retreating symbols,
shifting and transporting the *He Che*,
all actions and efforts—
they ultimately value humility, harmony,
softness, and compliance.

Be composed and tranquil,
neither aiding nor neglecting.
What is essential returns to the Void,
yet the Void gives rise to the Form.
When Form and Void stand not,
then one aligns with Heaven’s natural Dao/Way.

This is the attainment of unity,
whereby all matters are concluded—
the essence of my teaching’s *“One with a thread that runs through all.”*

If scholars grasp this thus,
the *Taishang*’s subtleties may be understood,
and they shall not be misled by peripheral or heterodox paths.

If one fails to recognize the *宗* of speech,
the *君* of practice,
and does not permit entry into the hall of truth
while wandering in other directions—
the person who knows this and acts accordingly
will naturally find fulfillment within,
no longer coveting the external.
Like the sage who bears coarse robes yet harbors precious virtue,
they radiate effortlessly, ceaselessly.

**Chapter 71: The Superiority of Knowing the Unknown**
*"Knowing the unknown"* is superior—

「Unknown yet knowing, is an ailment.
Only when one recognizes this ailment as an ailment,
does one truly avoid falling ill.

The sage does not fall ill,
because they recognize the ailment of knowing what they do not know,
thus they do not fall ill.

When illuminated by sagacious wisdom,
one’s mind is like a spotless mirror,
still water without ripples—
all things come and are reflected with perfect clarity,
not the slightest trace of evasion.

This is the divine insight, naturally penetrating;
it arises from things as their condition,
emerging like the heart’s own expression.
Not conjectured as clarity,
nor divined as knowledge.

Their knowing stems from the innate luminosity of nature,
not from preordained foresight,
yet they discern what others cannot.

This is the loftiest of sages,
not the reach of common folk.

Common folk cannot fathom principles with their intelligence,
nor illuminate things with their insight—
they often surmise the sincerity or deceit of others,
and guess at the rise or fall of matters—
if by chance they hit upon the truth,
people call their mind clear as a mirror,
and they themselves boast of their piercing discernment like a god.

This kind of divined knowing
is not the divine penetration of insight,
but forcing ignorance into knowledge:
though they claim to know,
their hearts labor and fret,
their hearts labor and fret—
the illness is already severe,
for they deceive themselves as clever,
yet only drain their vital *qi*.

Only when one regards forced knowing as the true illness,
does one then cure the illness they have recognized.

Thus, one exhausts principles to fulfill nature,
refines life to await Heaven’s timing.
Wise yet not using wisdom,
intelligent yet appearing foolish—
naturally, their hearts are empty as water,
their nature bright as ice,
a single luminous spirit,
shining through the *Triśarā* (三千),
why would they suffer from scheming or contrivance?

Therefore, they do not fall ill.

The sage’s understanding of affairs and principles
possesses innate foresight,
utterly devoid of divination or guesswork.
Hence, while common folk fall ill,
the sage does not fall ill—
for they can recognize what they do not know,
what they do not see clearly,
and thus with wholehearted devotion,
they surrender to the mystery of life,
their five senses aligned in truth,
all gathered within the single aperture of the *玄关* (Mysterious Pass).

With time, their luminous spirit blazes forth,
illuminating without remainder—
truly, wherever they are, it is fitting,
and nothing they do is unadvantageous.

Because they recognize the ailment of knowing what they do not know,
they do not fall ill.

This speaks of knowing through insight—
the highest attainment;
if knowing through feigned cleverness,
it is indeed a grave misfortune.

Only when one regards forced knowing as the true misfortune,
does one then have no misfortune.

The sage’s escape from misfortune
lies in always treating this misfortune as misfortune,
thus they have no misfortune.

The grand meaning is now clear;
no need to elaborate further.»

「Now let us further elucidate the subtleties of the **Dao/Way**:

In general, when practicing seated meditation (*dàzuò*), one must first cultivate **定** (meditative stability) within the **離宮** (Palace of Rebirth), achieving a single resonance before self-reflection and self-verification. If indeed it is found to be utterly empty and devoid of substance, then the mind is clarified and **性** (true nature) is seen.

Thus, I have often said: At the outset of still-sitting, this mind hangs suspended in the **太虚** (Great Emptiness). Only when the body and mind are settled, and **意** (intent) and **氣** (qi) are harmonized, does one gradually gather and return the mind to reflect upon its own **本宫** (Root Palace). When one arrives at the state of absolute emptiness, with nothing between the chest, then from this moment of awakening and illumination, the **十方三界** (Ten Directions and Three Realms) are nowhere that does not enter one’s awakened illumination.

Yet, **awakening-nature** neither arises nor perishes—it merely is, **了了自了** (clearly self-illuminating), **如如自如** (suchness self-so). Through this, one seeks the **玄** (Mysterious), and the source water becomes supremely pure, naturally becoming the foundation for my **結丹** (alchemical elixir).

In a flash, the **性光** (nature-light) spontaneously manifests. How does one perceive this? It is within the **恍恍惚惚** (dimly vague) state I previously demonstrated. Suddenly, upon awakening, movement occurs—this is the essential beginning of cultivating the Dao/Way. Yet, to gather **情** (passions) with **性** (nature), if one does not first uncover the true nature of **性**, and abruptly descends into the **水府** (Abode of Water) to seek the Mysterious, how can one possibly gather **情** when there is no **性**?

For indeed, there exists the **虛靈** (void-spirit) and **蓬勃** (vigorous) **機** (mechanism). Know that **離** is not of the mind—when one concentrates the ears on sound, the eyes on light, and restrains the senses from scent, taste, and touch, these are all affairs of the **神火** (spiritual fire), hence it is called **屬離** (attributed to Rebirth). **坎** is not **氣** (qi)—wherever **精氣** (essence-qi) resides, it is **屬坎** (attributed to Abyss). Even if one directs the spirit into the blood, and the fire into the water, the **氣機** (qi-mechanism) may not yet be activated—one must firmly grasp and pull, stirring the waves from the **海底** (ocean’s depths), ascending them into the **丹田** (Elixir Field). After prolonged refining, when the **火功** (fire cultivation) is sufficient, suddenly the **天機** (heavenly mechanism) is activated, causing the entire body to surge with vitality, from the ten fingers to the whole body, ceaselessly trembling. The body stands like a wall, the intent as cold as ashes. When the **丹田**’s **氣** grows warm, this is the **火** (fire) at its **不老不嫩** (neither too strong nor too weak) peak of coalescence. If this effect is not observed, one remains merely **微微** (slightly faint), and fire cannot be practiced. If one sees this state for long yet fails to ignite the fire, only then does one apply fire to the already dissipated **氣**—this is the **藥老無用** (elixir past its prime, useless).

Students must carefully discern this.

However, when the **微陽** (subtle yang) first stirs, it may not immediately produce such vigorous **氣**—one need only settle the mind and ease the intent, harmonizing the breath, and may proceed with the **子午河車** (Child-Midday Elixir Circulation). For indeed, the human body possesses **形質** (form-substance) blood, which remains **污污渾渾** (filthy and turbid), a mass of stagnant blood, unless refined by the **神火** (spiritual fire). Only through the illumination of the spiritual fire does a single point of **真氣** (true qi) emerge from the blood.

This is what the **Buddha** spoke of: *“In this fivefold turbid and evil world, I cultivated and attained the fruit of the Dao/Way.”*

Again, the ancients said: *“To take treasure from the ghost’s nest, to seek lead beneath the black mountain”*—these are all but the **真氣** (true qi) extracted from turbid essence and decayed blood through the refining of spiritual fire. Once the **氣** (qi) moves, **陽** (yang) arises—one must then know that **子** (Child) advances the yang-light, while **午** (Midday) retreats the **陰符** (yin-symbol). The **卯酉沐浴** (Tiger-Dog Bathing) methods, when practiced, allow one to gather this **真陽** (true yang), making it circulate and flow internally to dispel the **陰私** (hidden yin) within the **臟腑** (zang-fu organs), and externally to attract the **天地靈陽** (heaven-earth’s numinous yang). With prolonged practice, the **氣質** (qi-nature) also transforms.

This method of **河車** (Elixir Circulation) holds boundless profundity. The ancients spoke of *“gas brightens, Child-Midday draws and adds”*—drawing refers to extracting **鉛** (lead) from the Abode of Water, and adding refers to supplementing **汞** (mercury) from the Palace of Rebirth. **汞** is the **靈液** (numinous fluid) within the heart, the **後天** (post-heaven) containing the **先天** (pre-heaven). From the **色身** (form-body), **渾精敗血** (turbid essence and decayed blood), the **神火** (spiritual fire) refines and produces this **甘露** (ambrosia)—this is **鉛** (lead), the **氣** (qi) within the blood. **氣** (Qi) is what the ancients called the **金** (gold) in water, a **先天** (pre-heaven) within **後天** (post-heaven); it can only solidify the **凡林** (mundane body), not generate the **法身** (Dharma-body).

This is the medicine produced by the **坎離交** (Abyss-Rebirth interaction), yet still insufficient for **神丹** (spiritual elixir). One must then gather **情** (passions) with **性** (nature), and return **性** to **性** (nature to nature), harmonizing **性情** (nature-passions) to refine together in the **坤爐** (Kun Furnace). Suddenly, the **真陽** (true yang) emerges, forming the **乾坤交** (Heaven-Earth interaction) to congeal the elixir—only then can one refine **神丹** (spiritual elixir) into the **真仙子** (true immortal embryo).

In summary, there is no other method of cultivation—only the **河車** (Elixir Circulation) turning. At the **初關** (First Pass), one must exert effort; at the **中關** (Middle Pass), the **河車** (Elixir Circulation) is activated by both **天人** (heaven and humanity); when one reaches the **上關** (Upper Pass), the **河車** (Elixir Circulation) is purely natural, without fail.

As for the **卯酉沐浴** (Tiger-Dog Bathing) methods, these are merely precautions for beginners lest their minds be troubled and the fire be stirred prematurely, causing their work to falter. When one reaches pure mastery, no methods are needed. It all depends on the student’s ability to **神而明之** (manifest and clarify the spirit).」

**Chapter Seventy-Two: The People Do Not Fear Authority**

The people do not fear authority—then true authority has arrived.

Do not confine their dwelling, do not exhaust their life.
Only because they are not exhausted, they are not exhausted.
Thus, the Sage knows himself but does not display himself,
loves himself but does not exalt himself.
Hence, he discards that and embraces this.

What is called authority is the great framework of norms and teachings,
the most sacred principle of Heaven, which must not be violated.
If one knows to be cautious in solitude, even in bed and shadow,
and fears Heaven’s authority in hidden deeds,
then naturally Heaven bestows pure blessings,
and longevity extends without bound.

The *Classic of Poetry* says:
*"Fear Heaven’s authority,
and in its time, you shall be preserved."*

If Heaven’s authority looms close at hand,
yet one does not guard against it with vigilance and clarity,
causing moral order to collapse,
and propriety and righteousness to vanish—
then conscience perishes,
Heaven’s punishment is unrelenting,
and calamity cannot be avoided,
while life itself is imperiled.

This is why it is said:
*The people do not fear authority—then true authority has arrived.*

Yet this arises because they do not know
to take *ren* (benevolence) as their secure dwelling,
but abandon it without inhabiting it;
to take *qi* (vital breath) as their life,
but discard it and bring ruin upon themselves.

Alas! Those people, unfortunate,
born not in the ancient age,
fear authority as *de* (virtue/power) and *wei* (awe),
revere clarity as *ming* (illumination) and *huai* (embrace)—
thus, in ignorance and transgression,
Heaven’s signs are ignored,
and droughts, floods, plagues, and famines arise in succession.

Only the rulers above should guide them
to dwell in the boundless realm of the world,
so that their minds may roam in the harmonious expanse of *Taihe*,
not confining themselves in narrowness,
nor daily courting ruin;
should lead them to draw upon the vast *he* (noble) *qi*,
so that they may nourish themselves in the pure and empty Heaven,
not exhausting their life,
nor inviting self-destruction.

Only by cherishing the principle of preserving life,
safeguarding Heaven’s innate goodness,
and never growing weary or decaying—
then the *Classic of Poetry* says:
*"Revere Heaven’s wrath,
and dare not trifle with it;
revere Heaven’s decrees,
and dare not rush blindly."*

Heaven observes their virtue,
granting them prosperity and flourishing,
granting them longevity and preservation—
truly, they share in the eternal duration of Heaven and Earth.
Thus, they are not exhausted.

Who but the Sage can achieve this?

The ancient emperors, reverent and non-actional,
exalted in great virtue,
day by day, month by month,
cleansing their hearts and purifying their thoughts,
penetrating the mysteries of creation,
discerning the original Heaven—
only knew themselves.

As for deeds, writings, and outward achievements,
the Sage utterly rejects them as means of self-display.

Moreover, he remains ever-vigilant, morning and evening,
cherishing the Dao and guarding his essence,
never straying from *ren* for a single breath—
none can surpass him in this.

His self-love is such that
even what others hold in highest esteem—
names, positions, fame, and glory—
the Sage does not deem them worthy of pride.

Though the Sage’s own self-awareness and self-love
are what all people commonly know and cherish—
yet common people, knowing them, must display them,
loving them, must exalt them;
while the Sage knows himself but does not display himself,
loves himself but does not exalt himself.

He is cautious in solitude, yet does not flaunt himself before others;
he values preservation, yet does not boast before the world.
How can common people compare?

It is said: abandon desire, fulfill humanity, align with Heaven,
thus transcending humanity to become Sage,
separating from the common to become divine,
and attaining immortality of countless eons—
all this arises from
self-awareness of dwelling in boundless ease,
self-love of eternal joy,
uniting in this and not dividing from it,
so that the people naturally turn to goodness without knowing why.

Apart from this, how could it be so?

This speaks of not confining one’s dwelling—
then one’s dwelling must be vast.
Not exhausting one’s life—
then one’s life must endure.

Yet in the practice, the original spirit and the recognizing spirit
must be understood.

When a person first receives *qi*,
from the moment of parents’ union,
a single grain of millet forms—
at this time, indistinct and indistinct,
only a cluster of *Taihe* qi,
with no trace of knowledge.

Yet it is supremely divine, supremely wondrous,
exhausting all transformations,
giving rise to boundless affairs in the world—
all from this single spark of sentient qi,
from non-knowing to knowing,
from non-action to action,
all beginning here.

At this stage, Heaven and humanity share one principle,
things and self share one source,
substance and function are complete,
manifest and subtle are unbroken—
thus, it is called the *original spirit*.

This is what Heaven bestows.

When the flesh-and-blood form is complete,
ten months in the womb fulfilled,
a cry, and the infant is born—
then the *recognizing spirit* begins to take shape.

The *original spirit* is the primordial *qi* of Heaven and Earth,
shared by all things, hidden within the void.
Once it enters the human body,
it dwells concealed in the empty cavities within.

This is Heaven’s gift.
Those who cultivate the Great Dao
cannot seek it through emptiness or form.

Only by being utterly without knowledge,
utterly without possession,
sweeping away all dust and haze,
does the inner truth reveal itself,
and divine mystery arise.

As for the *recognizing spirit*,
it is the ghostly essence of the human body,
the seed of countless rebirths,
requiring the completion of the five senses
and the hundred bones—
only when descending into birth and taking form
does the soul have a place to depend upon.

The ancients called this the *postnatal recognizing spirit*,
for it arises from having form and body.

This is the great distinction between the *original spirit*.

Yet after birth, the two spirits—original and recognizing—
merge into one.
Sometimes the *original spirit* acts,
while the *recognizing spirit* retreats in obedience—
though the intentions of the postnatal self may stir,
they must emerge through *ren, yi, li, zhi*,
manifesting as joy, anger, sorrow, or delight,
and the *recognizing spirit* transforms into the *original spirit*.

Sometimes the *recognizing spirit* dominates,
while the *original spirit* hides unseen—
though the traces of *ren, yi, li, zhi* appear,
they all become private affection, private love,
private hatred, private resentment,
and the *original spirit* transforms into the *recognizing spirit*.

Thus, all that arises from the body and senses—mouth and ears—is the *recognizing spirit*.
When the *recognizing spirit* dominates,
where is the radiant clarity, the upright and just,
capable of aligning with Heaven and Earth,
questioning the spirits and deities?

Only in the boundless chaos,
does the spirit stir at a single sensation—
here, Heavenly Principle is pure and complete,
untainted by postnatal *recognizing spirit* and perception.
If one can firmly stand in this state,
proceeding with steadfast resolve,
then one acts purely in accordance with Heavenly Principle,
without the slightest trace of human desire’s private influence.

Thus, I instruct people:
when in a state of no knowing, no awareness,
to seek the *play of the mysterious* at the Pass.
For at this moment,
one is unified with Heaven and Earth,
harmonious with the void and space.

If one can grasp this from the outset,
then Heaven and Earth’s eternal generation
will not be difficult to manifest within oneself;
the void’s transformations
will not be difficult to embody as spirit.
At this moment, when one awakens,
truly, it is the root of Heaven, Earth, and humankind.

If practitioners do not begin here,
then from where shall they ascend to the stages of Immortal and Sage?

In essence,
that which emerges without thought or deliberation is the *original spirit*;
that which arises with purpose, perception, and from the colored body is the *recognizing spirit*.
The *original spirit* is formless,
the *recognizing spirit* leaves traces.
One comes from emptiness,
the other issues from the colored body—
these two are vastly dissimilar.

Having understood that the *original spirit* is born from emptiness,
and the *recognizing spirit* from the colored body,
I thus rectify the root and clear the source,
striving to close the inner and outer *Three Treasures*,
not permitting a single thought or perception
to arise from form, image, thought, or deliberation.

Through such cultivation and such nurturing,
over time, even the vital essence of the corpse-like body
will transmute into pure and serene *original spirit*,
and the eighty-four thousand hairs
will become protective spirits of the *Dharma*.

This is called
*transforming the recognizing spirit into the original spirit*,
*converting the yin into yang*—
this is achieved by firmly grounding oneself in emptiness,
not allowing the colored body’s perceptions to entangle the mind.

---

**Chapter 73: Not Summoning Yet It Comes**
*"The bold are slain; the bold in restraint are saved."*
These two paths bring either gain or harm.
What Heaven detests, who can fathom its reason?
Thus, even the Sage finds it difficult.

The Dao of Heaven
is victorious without strife,
responds without speaking,
comes without being summoned,
and yet its schemes are ever-good.
The Heavenly net is vast and sweeping,
coursed yet never failing.

The *Odes* say:
*"The Heavenly mandate—
mysterious and ceaseless."*

He who steals the *qi* of Heaven and Earth to make elixir,
steals from the mysterious and ceaseless Heavenly mandate.

This mandate, in Heaven, is the pure and serene *qi*;
in humankind, it is the *Great Harmony qi*.
Only through nurturing from dawn’s first light,
until the *boundless and overflowing qi* fills the two great voids,
does one return to the root and recover the mandate,
flowing above and below with Heaven and Earth alike.

What is this nurturing?
First, in the *death of false thoughts*—
the *death of false thoughts* values firmness;
firmness does not bend to external things,
thus allowing *upright qi* to persist.
Second, in the *life of true Principle*—
the *life of true Principle* values gentleness;
gentleness pleases all hearts,
thus allowing *boundless qi* to solidify.

These two—
one pressing forward without hesitation, resolute in daring,
is the *death-mind*;
one hesitating and faltering, willingly yielding to weakness,
is the *life-qi*.

The bold in daring are slain;
the bold in restraint are saved—
this is the foundation of advance and retreat,
the root of failure and triumph.
The *Book of Changes* says:
*"At high noon, the sun declines;
when the moon is full, it wanes."*

Heaven and Earth’s fullness and emptiness
follow the rhythms of time,
sometimes bringing gain, sometimes harm,
often contrary to the world’s expectations.

Thus, what humankind rejoices in,
Heaven may detest.

But what does Heaven truly detest?
The *Heavenly Sovereign’s* love for life—
sometimes it does not foster life but brings death;
the *Highest Sovereign’s* reverence for *virtue*—
sometimes it does not reward *virtue* but metes out punishment.

This is life within death,
death within life—
its meaning profound and subtle,
beyond what humankind can measure.

What Heaven detests, who can know its reason?
Thus, the Sage who cultivates the Dao
knows that *blessing* is the foundation of *calamity*,
that *gentleness* is the essence of *firmness*,
and balances *experience* and *authority* in their use,
forgetting gain and dulling ambition,
to uphold what is right,
not contending with the common for profit or harm,
but discerning within oneself what aligns or opposes.

As for disasters and blessings,
bestowing fortune or misfortune,
even the Sage struggles to perceive their subtleties,
let alone those below them.

For the Dao of the Sage
is also the Dao of Heaven.
The Sage follows nature purely,
while advance, retreat, ascent, and descent
turn within the body’s transformations.
Heaven’s Dao is effortless and natural,
while life, growth, contraction, and storage
flow ceaselessly through the *Great Void’s* expanse—
thus it does not contend with all things for strength,
yet its influence is ever-present,
so that even the most skilled cannot resist it—
this is victory without strife.

It does not speak of Principle to the common folk,
yet its disasters and blessings reach everywhere,
no place escaping—
this is response without speaking.

Though its effects may vary in swiftness or weight,
in darkness, there is an unseen balance,
a measure beyond mortal scheming.
Thus it is said:
*"Not summoned yet it comes,
spontaneous yet its schemes are ever-good."*

Even if one’s talent and cunning surpass others,
and one’s insight pierces the depths,
though one may fluster and conceal—
what use is it?
*"The Heavenly net is vast and sweeping,
coursed yet never failing."*
Truly, this is not false.

To suppress desire is to value daring;
without daring, the human heart runs wild,
human desire clings endlessly—
thus the bold in daring are slain—
this is the *death of the recognizing spirit*.
To preserve Principle is to value gentleness;
without gentleness, ordinary *qi* becomes violent,
the *original spirit* unsettled—
thus the bold in restraint are saved—
this is the *life of the original spirit*.

Yet the *death of the false heart* aids the *life of the true*—
the human heart is easily extinguished,
the Dao-heart easily kindled.
But within, there is the Dao of Heaven.

Heaven has its affections and aversions,
punishment and virtue applied in tandem,
life and death employed together.
Though some may grasp this,
they fail to perceive how life thrives within death’s mechanism,
or how vitality lurks within mortality’s grasp—
the world struggles to fathom this.

Who can fathom Heaven’s aversions?
The Sage aligns their heart with Heaven and Earth,
knowing that what Heaven abhors is precisely what it cherishes—
for without aversion, there could be no true affection.
This cyclical marvel, even the Sage finds hard to grasp.
Yet the Sage’s Way is none other than Heaven’s Way.

Heaven does not contend with the common folk over right and wrong,
yet nurtures all things—none escape its warmth and care,
nor transgress and stray.
It does not speak of trust and response,
yet yin and yang alternate in perfect accord—
none fail to harmonize, nor transgress and stray.
Truly, Heaven and humanity share the same Way:
still and unmoving, yet responsive and unified—
how wondrous its transformation!
All things and I share the same source,
boundless and impartial—
when things come, they are met with fitting response—
how could action be otherwise?

The Perfect One, with their unthinking, unburdened truth,
silently works divine power in the realm of life and death,
stealthily seizing Heaven’s intent within the palace of creation.
They enter the Watery Realm, forge the Golden Country,
follow the Venerable One, severing sight and sound.
To them, death is life, life is death—
they know not how victory and response intertwine,
like parent and child, husband and wife—
unsummoned yet arriving, unplanned yet harmonized.
Is this not the swiftest trust and response, the most divine?

As for calamities and blessings, rewards and punishments,
fortune and misfortune, the Heavenly net is vast and sweeping—
none escape its threads.
For emptiness is the Dao, the Dao is Heaven—
one cannot escape emptiness, nor the Heavenly net.
To not defy the Dao is to not defy Heaven—
how could Heaven’s rest be otherwise?

**Chapter Seventy-Four: The People Do Not Fear Death**
If the people do not fear death,
how can you frighten them with death?
If I could make the people always fear death as something extraordinary,
I could seize and execute them—who would dare?
But there are always those charged with execution who carry it out.
To substitute for those who execute is like substituting for the Master Carpenter.
He who substitutes for the Master Carpenter—
how few escape uninjured?

Ancient rulers who governed the world
must follow what easily moves the people’s emotions
and preemptively guard against it.
They do not follow the ruler’s joys and sorrows,
but observe the people’s affections and aversions,
guiding them by their natural inclinations for their benefit—
thus their teachings succeed without rigidity,
their governance thrives without excess.

If the people destroy propriety and family bonds,
transgressing norms and rank,
yet show no fear of death,
then my establishment of the Five Punishments,
displayed on the ceremonial grounds,
declared on auspicious days—
this is but wasted effort,
offering no benefit to the state or its people.
Is this not a waste of effort?

Only by leveraging the people’s love of life and fear of death
can I punish those who dare commit wickedness and deceit—
this is to strike one to warn a hundred,
to deter few while inspiring many to sincerity.
Thus the people themselves—
fathers admonish their sons,
elders encourage their younger brothers—
none dare act as a stepping-stone to chaos,
risking life to court death.

Yet execution rests with those above,
but the reason for killing lies in the nature of the offender.
Only the supremely virtuous may execute the supremely unvirtuous—
then the people themselves execute without resentment,
and die with acceptance.

Mencius said, *“Only Heaven’s officers may be executed.”*
If Heaven’s officers may be killed,
