then there are always those charged with execution.
But if it is not Heaven’s officer and yet the violent are punished with violence,
this is to govern disorder with disorder—
not only does the people’s chaos worsen,
but substituting for those who execute is like
a substitute craftsman wielding the axe to shape the wheel—
how could they rival the Master’s skill and perfection?

Examining artisans past and present,
none can match the Master’s craft—
yet those who substitute for the Master Carpenter,
how many escape unharmed?
Alas, the people are unfortunate—
not born in an age of virtue,
so bandits and criminals act without restraint.
Even more unfortunate, they encounter no executioners—
no guidance or awakening is possible,
and so the thin grow thinner,
while even the virtuous become thin.
Is this not deeply lamentable?

「To liken caution in solitude to the fear of death,
one must know that if a person is diligent in self-cultivation,
then the distinction between Heaven and humanity becomes clear,
and the division between principle and desire is manifest.

When desires are few but fail to be fully restrained,
and one strives to avoid transgressions yet cannot—
here, with constant vigilance and earnest striving,
reflecting on even the subtlest inclinations,
and curbing the false and deceptive,
it is not difficult to achieve purity of principle and clarity of mind,
and instantly reveal the original face of things.

But in places where no one observes or hears,
and where there is no daily practice of moral cultivation or self-restraint,
when desires surge and emotions overwhelm,
thinking to uproot evil and eradicate sin,
and suddenly perceiving the true nature—
the result must be that evil is not uprooted but multiplies,
and the more one strives to purify the heart, the more it becomes disordered.

The *Tàishàng* says: *"The people do not fear death—how can you frighten them with death?"*
The principle and its effect are thus interdependent—
only by being cautious in solitude can one truly know the falsity of desires.
Then, when desires arise in the moment,
one can restrain them with resolve,
and thus return to the rectitude of Heaven’s heart.

When one thought is swept away,
and another remains pure,
evil will not spontaneously regenerate at that juncture.

As for the people who habitually fear death,
those who dare to transgress or disrespect
and corrupt the norms of propriety,
I shall seize and execute them—who will dare?
For the Daoist drives out the non-Daoist,
just as a benevolent ruler soothes the masses,
governs the common folk,
and with utmost compassion restrains the most uncompassionate,
with supreme justice punishes the unjust—
naturally, none who submit will remain secure,
while the near ones are content and the distant ones come,
preventing the rise of rebellions or counterattacks,
nor turning into enemies or causing harm.

A scholar who seeks to discard falsehood and preserve sincerity,
to return to the root and reflect on the origin,
must do so gradually and steadfastly,
until one insight opens and all faculties become responsive,
and the original spirit is at peace, obeying the commands of the mind.

As it is said, *"If a person can remain constantly pure,
then all of Heaven and Earth shall be his domain."*
And again: *"If a person can rectify his spirit,
then all evils will not dare to approach."*

This is akin to the executioner’s punishment—
no resentment when he kills,
no unease when one dies—
the same natural Dao, the same rare effect.

**Chapter 75: The Sage Values Life Less Than Nobility**
The people’s hunger arises from their rulers’ excessive tax consumption,
thus they hunger.
The people’s difficulty in governance stems from their rulers’ interference,
thus they are hard to govern.
The people’s indifference to death comes from their rulers’ extravagant pursuit of life,
thus they hold life lightly.

Only those who do not prioritize life itself
are truly wiser than those who revere it.

From ancient times, the people have been the foundation of the state,
and food is their Heaven.
A state without people—who shall support it?
People without food—how shall they live?

Thus, for those in power,
there must be diligence in opening fields and cultivating land,
deepening the roots before the people have eaten;
establishing rites and moderating changes,
regulating the flow after the people have eaten;
and further, reducing labor to make up for shortages,
and curtailing expenditures to aid those in need—
so the people themselves provide ample sustenance,
and there are no laments of scarcity or hunger.
Even if drought strikes and famine follows,
if the granaries are stocked,
worries of calamity vanish.

But in the world today,
rulers indulge in arrogance and licentiousness,
their wasteful spending ever increasing:
some seek rare delights and curiosities for amusement,
others build palaces of jade and pavilions of precious stones to satisfy their desires.
Often, the granaries are emptied,
yet their expenditures do not diminish.
Even basic provisions are lacking,
and they impose heavier burdens:
first, they add extra taxes and levies,
still fearing the people’s resistance;
then, they seize ruthlessly and demand excessively,
even disregarding Heaven’s will.
Their love of music and beauty is paramount,
luxury and extravagance reach their height.
They drain the vital essence of the people,
while indulging their own debauchery.
When the state’s treasury is exhausted,
yet their demands relentlessly continue—
how can one expect no hunger,
even in years of abundant harvests and frequent blessings?

In the age of *Zhuó Lóng*,
clothing was for all, food for all,
homes for all, fields for all—
each cherished their kin,
each honored their elders.
The virtuous did not need to be taught rites and propriety,
for they naturally resided in benevolence and followed righteousness;
the common folk did not require lessons in loyalty and sincerity,
for they found contentment in their work and peace in their homes.
The ruler’s conduct itself was the governance,
while the people transformed themselves effortlessly.
Customs were pure without intent,
manners ancient without seeking them—
how flourishing and harmonious was that era!

But later,
as laws and regulations multiplied,
customs grew ever more corrupt;
the more decrees were proclaimed,
the more thieves and bandits thrived.
Even in tyrannical rulers, this is expected,
but even wise and enlightened kings,
who strive with vigor and exert themselves in governance,
find that the more complex their policies become,
the more falsehoods arise,
and the stricter their laws,
the more deceitful schemes emerge.
Is this not because the *qi* and its influence are hard to reverse,
nor because Heaven’s will is unchangeable?
Or is it because they fail to grasp the root and foundation,
and instead seek remedies in superficial measures?
Not only does this fail to benefit the people,
it also burdens the Way of the world.

The people’s hearts are inherently without strife,
yet their rulers disturb them with policies;
the people’s emotions are naturally without desire,
yet their rulers disrupt them with rigid codes.
For every additional decree from the court,
the common folk devise a hundred more deceits;
for every new regulation,
the people invent a hundred more clever evasions.
No wonder the world’s ways are so corrupt,
the people’s hearts shift daily,
and governance becomes ever more difficult.

Only when the ruler sits upright and relaxed,
does the people below naturally settle into order and obedience.
Above and below live in harmony under the natural governance of Heaven—
is this not joyful?

Moreover,
the more one seeks to preserve life,
the heavier the burden on the mind grows,
and the greater the depletion of vital *qi*.
As the spirit and *qi* dissipate,
life itself is extinguished—
the more one clings to life, the sooner death comes.
Thus, the more extravagant the pursuit of life,
the more lightly death is held.

Only those who do not regard life as an honor,
nor place excessive weight on preserving it,
who take food and clothing as they come,
and do not compete with others in their expenditures,
will find peace of mind and bodily ease,
their true nature restored and life prolonged,
and enjoy eternal, boundless blessings.»

Nurturing the *Taihe* within,
one naturally invites Heaven’s favor—
how much more excellent than those who prize artificial contrivance
by a millionfold measure?

The spirit is the sovereign; the people, the vital *qi*.
Following the constant Dao,
taking the spirit as master,
while the *qi* moves in accordance.

Thus, when the spirit strays,
the *qi* immediately dissipates.
The depletion of *qi*
arises from the spirit’s unrest—
just as the people’s hunger
comes from excessive taxation above.
Though their forms differ,
their principle is one.

The heart is sovereign over the body;
when the Heavenly Lord is serene,
all limbs obey his command.
But if the Heavenly Lord is disturbed,
then the body’s *qi* is exhausted—
how much more so when the fields are laid waste?

Thus, the Dao of immortals lies in returning to the original,
taking *qi* as master,
while the spirit follows its command—
just as a ruler yields to desires and follows the people’s will,
so that *qi* is full and the spirit complete,
and the people at peace, the state flourishing.

This is the way of the superior serving the inferior,
of the above having surplus to supplement the below’s lack—
treating the world as oneself, not oneself as the world.

Though the Dao of the Elixir speaks of *action*,
it must arise from *non-action* to *action*,
and *action* return to *non-action*—
only then does one grasp the primordial *qi* of Heaven,
able to transcend humanity and attain sainthood.

If one relies solely on contrivance,
the spirit will not be still, nor the *qi* strong.
Necessarily, refining will fail to gather *qi*,
the more one refines, the more scattered the *qi* becomes.

Only by following its natural course and guiding it appropriately,
adapting measures to the time,
whether cultivating oneself or governing the people,
must one hold this view.

If one fears poverty and death,
exhausting the spirit and *qi* in pursuit of a finite, later-born life,
night and day in torment,
sleep and wakefulness in distress—
how much the spirit and *qi* are wasted!

Moreover, life is Heaven’s decree,
not something one can claim.
The ruler fears that those who cling to life
will only hasten their own demise.
Only by not valuing this limited, later-born life,
and instead preserving the boundless, primordial essence,
can one safeguard the true nature and secure life—
then the body itself becomes precious.

---
**Chapter 76: The Soft and Weak Hold High Ground**
The complete person is soft and weak in life,
but rigid and strong in death.
Grasses and trees, in life, are tender and fragile,
but in death, withered and brittle.
Thus, the rigid and strong are companions of death,
while the soft and weak are companions of life.

Therefore, the strong in arms do not conquer,
the rigid in wood cannot bend.
The strong and great must yield to the lowly,
while the soft and weak ascend above.

When a person receives the warm *yang* *qi*, they live;
when they receive the cold *yin* *qi*, they die.
When the warm *yang* *qi* gathers,
the four limbs grow supple,
the body soft and pliant,
and life’s vitality never ceases.
But when cold *yin* *qi* congeals,
the flesh dries and withers,
the skin and fur crack and fall away,
and death’s *qi* approaches.

Observe the pot and steamer—
when steaming, the *yang* *qi* rises thickly,
and things dissolve and transform.
But in freezing weather,
things grow cold and hard.

Observe the earth in spring and summer—
when *yang* *qi* is fierce, all things flourish and grow,
none failing to thrive.
But when autumn and winter meet,
*yin* *qi* prevails, and all things wither and fall.

Human and thing share the same source,
with no distinction between them.
Thus, all things under Heaven,
whether great or small,
are companions of death in their rigidity,
and companions of life in their softness.

Consider warfare—
often the strong are defeated,
while the weak prevail—
as when Ziyu’s overrigidity led to defeat,
and Bibi’s yielding won victory over Chu.
Why is this?
Because the strong decline gradually,
while the weak hold the seeds of rise—
thus, they cannot prevail.

Observe the trees—
wood is most rigid,
yet when *yin* *qi* is strong and *yang* *qi* weak,
it is bound and broken,
with no way to nourish itself.

The strong and great exhaust their life *qi*,
while death’s *qi* approaches—
truly, the lowest of things.
The soft and weak dissolve *yin* and strengthen *yang*,
becoming the highest of things.

How can one not embrace weakness and reject strength,
not yield to the lowly and ascend above?

The Dao of cultivation most emphasizes the *Xuan Guan* orifice,
the origin of Heaven, earth, and all things’ life-giving *qi*.
This *qi* is supremely soft yet firm,
supremely weak yet strong—
yet neither soft nor hard, neither weak nor strong is seen.
In the hazy, distant void,
suddenly, *yin* contains *yang*,
death gives birth to life—
seemingly present, yet absent;
seemingly real, yet empty—
this is the true, scentless, soundless,
Heaven’s initial mechanism.

If one can steal this primordial, void-beginning *qi*,
then the origin of Heaven’s life-giving essence is obtained,
and attaining immortality is no difficulty.

Even if one steals the *Xuan Guan* origin-*qi*
as the Elixir’s treasure,
yet in the four seasons of gathering medicine,
one must focus *qi* to achieve softness—
like an infant, bones soft and body weak yet grasp firm—
only then does one obtain the initial *qi* as the Elixir’s foundation.

In the four seasons of refining fire,
one must know the body’s softness, like cotton,
unmatched in comfort—
this is the primordial, unspun, blooming vitality,
the symbol of harmonious, lively *qi*.
With this *yang* *qi*, one can refine the Elixir.

At the stage of returning to the cauldron and sealing,
still one must be softly persistent,
clearly aware,
neither slack nor reckless—
like drunkenness, like madness,
the spirit lazy in thought,
the mouth lazy in speech—
as the saying goes:
*"Under Heaven, spring clouds are as lazy as I—
who knows I am even lazier than spring?"*

Only such softness and weakness
preserve the primordial *yang* *qi*,
able to endure without decay.

In summary,
ten months in the womb,
three years at the breast,
nine years of seclusion—
all are the primordial soft and weak *qi*,
which completes the Elixir and achieves immortality.

The cultivator must seek this tender *qi*,
lest they burn and refine in vain,
wasting their spirit and *qi*.

---
**Chapter 77: Acting Without Relying**

**Chapter 78: Receiving the Realm’s Filth**

The Way of Heaven—is it not like drawing a bow?
The high it depresses, the low it raises;
The excessive it diminishes, the deficient it supplements.
The Way of Heaven supplements the deficient and diminishes the excessive,
But the Way of Man is otherwise—
It diminishes the deficient to serve the excessive.
Who then can serve the world with their excess?
Only the Sage of the Way.

Thus the Sage acts without relying,
Achieves without dwelling—
Does he not wish to be seen as virtuous?
The Way of Heaven flows and nourishes all things,
Through ceaseless interplay of yin and yang,
Forward and backward, alternating without end—
Great, central, and perfectly just,
Neither partial nor biased.
Hence, when yin reaches its extreme, yang arises;
When yang reaches its extreme, yin arises.
If yin is excessive and yang is weak,
Then yin is suppressed and yang is bolstered;
If yang is excessive and yin is weak,
Then yang is suppressed and yin is bolstered.
Ebb and flow, swelling and waning,
Move in harmony with the seasons,
Thus life and transformation unfold,
Achieving effortless spontaneity,
An eternal, unexhausted Heaven.
What differs from one who draws a bow?
With bow held firm and intent upright,
The body straight, the front and rear arms aligned—
The high is depressed, the low is raised,
The excessive is diminished, the deficient is supplemented.
Then, following the natural motion,
The arrow finds its mark without fail.
How unlike the Way of Heaven’s natural course—
Taking the people’s fat and marrow,
Filling their bellies,
Yet often diminishing the deficient to serve the excessive.
Who then can use the ruler’s excess
To supplement the people’s deficiency?
Only the Sage of the Way,
Who follows the Way of Heaven and aligns with human nature,
Diminishing where diminished, supplementing where supplemented,
So that the common folk have no cause for grievance.
Though acting for oneself,
It is still in accordance with the Way of Heaven—
Never boasting of one’s deeds;
Though achieving, it is only sincerity—
Never claiming credit for one’s work.
Such a person,
Truly aligns with Heaven’s effortless transformation,
Not forcing the natural course,
Not desiring to be seen in action.
The glory of creation shines upon all—
Is it not because the virtuous do not seek to be seen as virtuous?
This is why Heaven has no limits,
And only the Sage unites Heaven and Earth.

At birth, man is pure yin and pure yang,
Perfectly balanced, neither superior nor inferior.
Thus, day by day, month by month,
His bones grow soft, his body weak yet thrives.
After life begins,
Fire dwells above, water below—
Water and fire do not intermingle,
So yin is always excessive, yang always deficient.
When yang-water is scorched by yin-fire,
The human heart grows ever more restless,
Worldly passions burn brighter,
While the true heart fades daily,
True *qi* dwindles away—
The vital principle of life is lost.
If the excessive yin-fire supplements the deficient yang-water,
And after supplementing yang-water,
Still restrains the excessive yin-fire—
Like one drawing a bow,
Diminishing the excessive, supplementing the deficient—
Then yin and yang are restored.
This is all the natural movement of water and fire,
The natural interplay of yin and yang,
And Heaven knows not of their doing.
The Way of Man is burdened by action,
The Way of Heaven is honored by non-action.
What else is cultivation but
Returning post-Heaven yin and yang
To pre-Heaven yin and yang,
Flowing endlessly in effortless spontaneity?

**Chapter 79: Receiving the Realm’s Filth**
The world has nothing softer or weaker than water,
Yet nothing surpasses it in overcoming the hard and strong.
There is no substitute for it.
Thus, the weak overcomes the strong,
The soft overcomes the hard—
All know this, yet none can practice it.
Therefore the Sage says:
*"To bear the realm’s filth is to be its sovereign.
To bear the realm’s misfortunes is to be its king."*
This is contrary speech.

The *Taishang* chapter before spoke of the soft and weak as the source of life,
And the hard and strong as the source of death.
Thus, the soft and weak occupy the high,
The hard and strong occupy the low—
It is clear that the utmost softness is the utmost strength,
The utmost weakness is the utmost power.
One must practice day and night,
Ever choosing the soft and weak as beneficial to all,
Always dwelling in the low and filthy without aversion.
Though but a drop, men may scorn it.
Though but a ladleful, men may despise it.
Yet when it accumulates into a deep pool,
Gathers into an ocean,
It becomes vast and boundless—
It can bear ships and also capsize them;
It can nurture life and also destroy it.
Not only does the world have no equal,
But even those skilled at overcoming the hard and strong
Cannot break what is firm, cannot shatter what is strong,
And none can resist it.
Thus, the world’s utmost softness
Can govern the world’s utmost hardness;
The world’s utmost weakness
Can drive the world’s utmost strength.
Water, water!
How soft and weak it is,
Yet how hard and strong it becomes!

Moreover, there is no task easier than attacking water,
Yet the hard and strong cannot conquer it.
Why then do men not embrace softness and reject hardness,
Not choose weakness and reject strength?
Is it not because, though they know the soft overcomes the hard,
The weak overcomes the strong,
Their innate virtue, once stirred,
Is immediately stained by desire and habit,
So they bristle with arrogance,
Unwilling to rest in softness and weakness.
Thus, cleverness grows while principle fades,
Partiality thrives while true nature is lost.
The result is that the moral heart departs,
The human heart arises;
Foreign *qi* flourishes, true *qi* wanes.
Life’s foundation is gone, its vitality extinguished—
How could one then hope for ceaseless generation?
The Sage says, *"To bear the realm’s filth is to be its sovereign,"*
As did King Tang: *"If I have guilt, it lies not with the myriad lands;
If the myriad lands have guilt, it lies with me."*

「To retreat is to advance—
Thus one receives the Heavenly Mandate from the boundless.

*"To bear the realm’s ill fortune is to rule the world."*
As did King Wu: *"I have conquered you, not because my father, King Wen, was guilty,
But because I, your humble subject, was unvirtuous."*

To later is to be prior—
Thus one bears Heaven’s grace without replacement.

How could it compare with later ages’ *bed of thorns and tasting gall*,
Or those who endure filth and accept slander?
This is the eternal, unchanging principle,
An immutable scripture through the ages—
The true words of the world,
Yet the Sage only turns inward to seek within oneself,
Never demanding this of others.

Water is a metaphor for the initial movement of *yang*—
The true essence begins to form.
Its mechanism is most weak,
Its force most soft.
Yet gradually it gathers and solidifies,
Day by day, month by month,
Until it becomes the vast and boundless *qi*,
Most great and most hard,
Filling the two extremes,
Unifying all things—
Yet nothing is too firm for it not to penetrate,
Nothing is too strong for it not to overcome.

*The Awakening to Truth* says:
*"The White Tiger’s first scripture is a priceless treasure,
The Godly Pool’s true scripture of essence.
The highest excellence is like water, benefiting its source deeply—
Not comparable to ordinary elixirs."*

Observe how *qi*’s softness and weakness resemble water:
Most soft yet containing inherent hardness,
Most weak yet encompassing utmost strength—
Truly possessing the power to prop up the heavens and support the earth,
To cradle the sun and raise the moon,
To summon wind and call rain,
To drive thunder and wield lightning.
This is the world’s ultimate firmness and strength.

Though called *vast qi*,
Its essence is the true elixir.
One must nurture it with utmost softness and weakness,
And act with *wuwei* as the method,
Achieve *wuwei* as the function—
Only then may one attain it.

What is meant by *"To bear the realm’s filth is to be its sovereign,
To bear the realm’s ill fortune is to rule the world"*?
It is the ancient practice of self-reproach,
Of declaring, *"Truly, I am unvirtuous—
What wrong have the people committed?"*

If scholars seek virtue in others,
Why not first cultivate it within oneself?

**Chapter Seventy-Nine: Always Do Good to Others**
*"To reconcile great grievances will surely leave lingering grievances—
How then can it be called virtue?"*
Thus the Sage holds the left contract,
Yet does not blame others.
Therefore, the virtuous govern contracts,
The unvirtuous govern disputes.
Heaven’s Dao has no favorites,
Yet always favors the virtuous.
The way of cultivating oneself,
Is to cherish virtue as its treasure.
The way of doing virtue,
Is to first govern oneself.
For the Dao resides within,
Not without;
Cultivation lies in oneself,
Not in others.
Only by observing inwardly at all times,
And reflecting back upon oneself at every moment—
To correct faults and reinforce virtues—
May one truly clarify virtue within oneself,
And forever be the worthy child of Heaven and Earth,
The complete Sage and Saint,
Without any deficiency.

It is clear that those who do virtue
Only question their own cultivation and reflection,
Not others’ compliance or defiance.
If one blames others yet does not blame oneself,
Observes only outward appearances and not inward truth—
Though one may momentarily disperse anger,
And not reach the point of enmity,
Yet without the method of inward self-reproach,
Or the practice of restraining anger and purifying desire,
Even if one resolves conflicts externally,
One cannot release them from the unseen depths—
How could one then remain pure and dust-free,
Free and joyful in oneself,
And return to the original virtue of Heaven and Earth?

Thus, *"To reconcile great grievances will surely leave lingering grievances—
How then can it be called virtue?"*

Only the Sage governs the self and interacts with others,
In dealing with oneself and awaiting others,
Always prioritizing self-cultivation,
And never concerning oneself with others’ judgments or preferences.
Like sealing a contract,
Dividing left and right to hold it—
Forever relying on it,
And making clear the intention:
*"No fear of you deceiving me,
No fear of I deceiving you."*

The Sage holds *virtue/power* as one holds the left contract,
Only cultivating within oneself and never blaming others—
This is why they align with Heaven and Earth in their greatness.

This is called *"The virtuous govern contracts."*
The unvirtuous, however,
Place weight on the external and slight the internal,
Often regard scrutiny as clarity,
And must account for others’ enmities—
This is *"The unvirtuous govern disputes."*

Those who *"govern disputes"*,
Take fault-finding as their task,
Never reflect inwardly yet the people do not follow—
How can they compare with those who *"govern contracts"*,
Who blame themselves heavily and others lightly,
And thus all are trusting and faithful!

It is known that blaming others
Is to slight oneself—
One’s own virtue cannot be complete;
Blaming oneself
Is to slight others—
One’s own virtue is truly achieved.

If one does good to others,
Heaven’s favor will attend—
For endless ages without fail.

*The Classic of Documents* says:
*"The August Heaven has no favorites,
It aids only virtue"—
Is this not the same as *The Supreme Dao*’s saying:
*"Heaven’s Dao has no favorites,
Yet always favors the virtuous?"*

The Sage’s learning,
Is to wash the heart and retreat into secrecy.
Regarding outward virtues and vices,
Right and wrong, compliance or defiance—
All are not considered.
Thus, one filters thoughts and sinks into reflection,
Concentrates the spirit and silently observes,
Until the heart is clear and nature revealed,
Desires purified and principles refined—
Above, aligning with Heaven in virtue,
Through endless ages without erosion.

Its foundation begins with *guarding the center*,
Its completion lies in *embryonic breathing*—
Do you know of this within?

The ancients spoke of *embryonic breathing*—
Students must not mistake it for external breath or external *qi*.
It is when ordinary breath ceases,
And the true *yin* and true *yang* of the *dantian*,
The original spirit and original *qi*,
Fuse into one cluster,
Mixing into one breath—
*Yīnyīn yǔnyǔn* (vaporous and misty),
*Péngpéng bó bó* (vigorous and flourishing),
Opening and closing like gates,
Having and not having like a phantom.
Visible yet unseen,
Audible yet unheard—
Imagined yet with traces,
Faintly perceived yet with form.
This is perhaps the original breath of life.

The heart attains it and has substance,
The nature attains it and has function—
Without this breath, one cannot live.
To become a Sage of the highest rank,
One cannot be without this breath as its master.

The ancients said:
*"At the beginning of life,
The foundation is *li* (principle), giving rise to breath,
The breath giving rise to form—
This is the natural sequence of Heaven and Earth in generating life."*

Returning to reverse cultivation, one truly begins by slowly stirring the *yang* fire and *yin* talisman from the myriad forms and colors, drawing them back into the Five Bright Palaces. With the primordial *Taiyi* ancestral *qi* and the natural divine fire, one refines them—thus transforming form into a single breath.

Once this breath is refined into one, it can then be transmuted into spirit.
If one steadfastly guards the void and nurtures the numinous *yang*,
even without returning to the primordial *Wuji*,
one may manifest form when exiting and leave no trace when entering.
What difference does the Dao have from ordinary people?

In essence, this prenatal breath is none other than the moment when one returns to the state of *yin-yang* fusion—the original *qi* and blood of one’s parents’ union.
If one cultivates this prenatal breath,
day and night with the true intent of *wuwei* (non-action) as *youwei* (action),
and *wusi* (non-thought) as *yousi* (thought),
preserving and gathering it,
it will coalesce into a spiritual embryo and become the original spirit.

When the ten months are complete and the form is whole,
it sheds its shell and ascends through the crown,
piercing the heavens and soaring to the celestial realm—
riding the *luán* crane and the *hè* heron,
soaring among the clouds,
wandering beyond the skies,
and ascending to the Jade Capital—
all in the blink of an eye.

Yet this prenatal breath,
though refined from the mortal’s colored body,
is not the same as the vulgar *jing* and *qi* or the coarse spirit that forms it.
Though alchemists cannot do without the postnatal, tangible *jing* and *qi* as their foundation,
they do not rely on them entirely.
For postnatal *jing* and *qi* are all substantial and bound by fate—
subject to birth and death, the wheel of rebirth is inevitable.
Moreover, coarse *jing* and coarse *qi* are all dull and sluggish—
how could they possess any numinous essence?
It is only by subjecting these vulgar substances within the mortal form
to countless refinements,
extracting that pristine, dust-free, supremely numinous and divine *jing-qi-shén*,
and returning it to oneself as the True One *qi*,
that one may forge the immortal embryo and spiritual elixir.

What is called *"extracting lead and adding mercury"* is no more than this.
All else that clings to form and color is not the true Dao.

The ancients said:
*"The embryo forms from the latent breath,
and the breath rests within the embryo."*
Thus, to forge the spiritual elixir and achieve an ageless body,
one cannot do without nurturing the prenatal breath.
To have the prenatal breath condense within the void of the *dantian*,
one must first form the embryo—
otherwise, it will not return.
If even a trace of vulgar *qi* mingles in,
or if the spirit wanders externally while the mortal mind strays,
the spirit will scatter and the *qi* will dissipate,
unable to unite like parent and child, husband and wife,
gathering without dispersing.
Do you understand?

---
**Chapter Eighty: The Small State, the Few People**
In a small state with few people,
let there be implements of war numbering ten to a hundred,
but let them not be used.
Let the people hold death in reverence
and not wander far from home.
Though there are boats and carriages,
they shall have no need to ride them.
Though there are armor and weapons,
they shall have no place to display them.
Let the people return to using knotted cords for record-keeping.
Let their food be simple,
their clothing plain,
their dwellings secure,
their customs joyful.
Neighboring states, though nearby,
shall hear only the sounds of roosters and dogs—
the people shall not visit one another
until they die of old age.

A small state with few people,
remote and sparsely populated,
would find it difficult to become a great and flourishing realm,
or to uphold the refined customs of antiquity.
Yet if one reduces wasteful expenditures
and simplifies elaborate rituals,
so that implements of war numbering ten to a hundred
are not used,
then consumption will be less while goods will be more—
the wealth of the state can thus be achieved.
Moreover, if one does not indulge desires lightly,
nor risk death for private gain,
nor wander to distant lands or settle in foreign soils,
but instead cherishes parents and loves siblings,
holding death in reverence and not straying far—
then a world of tranquility, harmony, and kinship can be attained.

Thus, *"charming our sovereign, cherishing this land,"*
though there are boats and carriages,
no one will venture far to foreign lands,
leaving their ancestral homeland.
When the court’s benevolence and grace penetrate to the marrow,
its favor as deep as that of a parent,
its bond as close as siblings—
even rebellious and stubborn men will be transformed into the virtuous.
Though there are armor and weapons,
they shall have no place to display them.

Thus, with the ruler serene and the people content,
the people at ease and the state prosperous,
if one restores the governance of knotted cords
and embraces the harmony of the *Tahe*,
cherishing kin and honoring elders,
settling in their homes and tending their fields,
delighting in their food and clothing,
providing secure dwellings and joyful customs,
and fostering simplicity to shape the wind of virtue—
what fear is there that the state will be small or the people few,
or that it will be difficult to achieve greatness and abundance through benevolence and virtue?

When the people love their ruler as they do their parents,
and the ruler regards his people as his children and siblings,
with unwavering loyalty and deep affection—
even a moment’s separation would be unbearable.
Though neighboring states are close enough to see and hear,
from youth to old age, from birth to death,
the people shall not once visit them.

This is because the people, moved by gratitude and transformed by virtue,
hold their ruler in profound reverence.
Thus, they enjoy the governance of *wuwei*
and bask in the celestial favor of the Dao,
never willing to stray even a step.
How then could the state fear being small,
or the people fear being few?

Necessarily, their influence will extend to the four quarters,
their reputation spread far and wide,
and the world will turn to benevolence,
all nations uniting in harmony.

---
This is an analogy for those aged and weakened in *jing* and *qi* in their cultivation.
When a person grows old,
their *jing* and *qi* scatter,
their lead and mercury diminish—
to practice the Great Dao of the Golden Elixir seems nearly impossible.
Yet one does not realize that the matter of the Golden Elixir
does not depend on postnatal *jing* and *qi*,
but on the primordial lead and mercury.
If one attains the supreme method of the True One,
extracting and ingesting it,
whether old or young,
one may obtain the medicine in an instant or half a breath,
achieving success in a thousand years or three months.

The only misfortune is that one does not hear of the True One *qi* of the primordial,
but instead relies on the postnatal, tangible *jing*—
not only will the elderly fail to achieve anything,
but even the young and vigorous will never succeed.

At the very outset of practice, one must forcefully endure,
keeping the hands from moving recklessly,
the feet from stepping lightly,
the eyes from gazing outward,
the ears from listening to extraneous sounds,
the mouth from speaking,
and the mind from straying in idle thought—
from morning till night,
cleansing the mind of anxieties,
nurturing the inner self,
retiring into seclusion,
so that not even a thread may pull at you,
nor a speck of dust burden you.
With time, this accumulates until sincerity gives rise to clarity,
and naturally,
the gaze turns inward,
the tongue’s vitality is preserved within,
the heart’s essence is stored internally,
the limbs move with ease,
and every action aligns with the Dao.

This illustrates the principle of
*"a hundredfold vessel unused, yet when used, it lacks nothing."*
The term *"min"* (民) here compares to the body.

As one grows old,
who does not fear death with utmost intensity,
or cherish life with profound longing?
Yet to fear death without knowing how to seek life
is merely futile dread.

Only through meticulous solitude and constant inward observation,
moment by moment reflecting back,
never straying from the space of one’s own mind,
does one, over time, attain harmony within—
so that even the heavens and earth may find their proper place,
and all things flourish under cultivation.
How much more so for the self,
which remains unaligned and uncultivated?

This is the practice of nurturing the *Xuanhua* (玄化),
cultivating the *Gu* (谷) spirit—
enduring like a thread unbroken,
using it without strain;
ever-present like a steady glow,
guarding it without flaw;
still yet ever luminous,
luminous yet ever still—
*(that is, ever responsive yet ever tranquil,
without force or artifice).*

This is what is meant by
*"moving yet unrestrained, still yet nurturing harmony."*

It does not involve manipulating the *Hecar* (河车) or shifting the *Doubing* (斗柄);
nor does it require wielding martial might—
though battle may befit the field,
defense suits the city,
and ritual purity is paramount—
taking analogy from the alchemist’s careful tending of the furnace,
and the master’s strategic victory over adversity.

This is the method of pure cultivation,
not the craft of balancing *yin* and *yang* through supplementation.
Not only can the elderly, through such discipline,
obtain the elixir and return to their original state,
but even the young, adhering to this practice,
may persist unceasingly, without division or pause,
harmonizing with the serene and reverent heavens.

Yet those who rush impatiently seek no swift results,
and those who demand hasty completion are no true vessels.
Only through slow, leisurely cultivation—
like water’s gradual seepage,
or fire’s steady permeation—
does virtue refine and benevolence mature,
and the Dao find fulfillment.

Thus,
*"Though there be chariots and carriages, they serve no purpose;
Though there be armor and arms, they find no deployment."*

The advance and retreat, the ascent and descent,
the laws of morning concentration and evening obscurity—
the Supreme Sage has already spoken of this:
*"War is the precursor to years of calamity."*
This clearly shows that the practice of tending the furnace,
gathering elixir, and kindling the fire
is established solely for those whose post-natal *qi* is bound
and whose essence is obscured by materiality—
for if one does not adhere to this method,
then ordinary *qi* cannot be transformed,
and the true gold cannot be refined.

If one instead takes stillness as their practice,
avoiding all methods of boiling and simmering,
and merely abides in emptiness and tranquility,
then unknowingly and without effort,
in a state of non-action and non-thought,
one naturally becomes vast and boundless,
pure and upright in essence,
silently harmonized with Heaven,
without need of speech, thought, or deliberation,
flowing seamlessly with Heaven and Earth.
This is the *"restoring of the people to the use of knotted cords."*
No texts are established, no words are relied upon,
and yet *"the Sage above does not use calculations."*
*"Let them find their food sweet and their garments fine,"*
and thus the essence permeates within,
while the *qi* circulates without.
Sweet within and beautiful without—
there is that which cannot be named.

*"Let them dwell in contentment and take joy in their customs,"*
and then the heart finds its natural virtue,
moving wherever it wills, in perfect accord.
When cultivation reaches this stage,
one’s clarity is ever-present, effortless, and unbound—
encountering the world without attachment,
meeting things without being stained—
though sights and sounds may arise,
they are as though unseen and unheard,
never giving rise to thought through form and sound.
Thus it is said:
*"Neighboring states may be in view, yet there is no coming and going between them."*

This is the supreme and highest path,
the lowest and lowest path—
the most profound of profundities,
the most wondrous of wonders.
Ah!

When cultivation reaches this stage, one is in great harmony with the Dao.

Speaking of Daoist practice, the ancients had two primary methods: those who cultivated through purity, and those who cultivated through yin-yang supplementation. Cultivation through purity means refining the void and unity—no need to refine essence or *qi*. Yet this is not for the ordinary practitioner; only those of superior root and capacity can speak of it. If one’s innate foundation is truly extraordinary, then from this single step onward, all actions align with the natural Dao of Heaven and Earth—unlike the teachings of my master today, which still involve contrivance.

For within the human body lie the mechanisms of yin-yang *kan* and *li*, *qián* and *kūn*, opening and closing, sun and moon, water and fire, ascent and descent, advance and retreat—just as the heavens move in their courses, all naturally, without need of artificial manipulation. One need only rectify the original spirit, letting it act without knowing, without thinking, without deliberation. Then the pure and empty *qi* surges boundlessly, naturally inhaling and exhaling, rising and falling like the opening and closing of *qián* and *kūn*, the movement of sun and moon, the ascent of water and descent of fire, the ebb and flow of yin and yang. Though there are stages of fire and heat, they are merely about calming the mind, reducing desires, maintaining stillness, and inner observation—letting true *qi* circulate without ceasing. Though there are advances and retreats, ascents and descents, they are merely about letting true water ascend and true fire descend.

Even in alchemical bathing, it is only about restraining anger, suppressing desires, purifying thoughts, and cleansing the heart—so that the Great Harmony may dwell within. Though one may obtain elixirs and refine the pill, it is only about taking spirit as father and *qi* as mother, intertwining the two into a unified whole, merging without separation, and generating that primordial point of true essence that gave birth to Heaven and Earth—the true essence of the *li* palace, also called the true mercury of the human body. By refining my spirit-*qi* into this true mercury, a fetus forms, and later gives rise to the yang spirit, bones and flesh, blood and vessels, the five viscera and six bowels, hair and skin, clarity and awareness—none of which differ from the human form. Divided, it can manifest as myriad bodies; united, it returns to one *qi*—all arising from the union of spirit-father and *qi*-mother, their fusion forging this true mercury’s essence as the yang spirit.

Yet this true mercury requires the right moment to manifest. The heart governs the *qi* of the five viscera; when the central *qi* ascends, the *qi* of the five viscera ascends with it; when the central *qi* descends, the *qi* of the five viscera descends in turn. Its movement stems from the activity of true mercury; its stillness, from its repose. In essence, movement and stillness, ascent and descent—all belong to the natural Dao. One need only follow its natural operation.

Yet few ancient immortals began with this method. What is meant by yin-yang supplementation? First, one must recognize the foundation of the *Tàijí*, the emergence of primordial yang, and then invest this point of true yang *qi* into the *dāntián*, as if parents unite, their essence and blood merging into a cluster within the womb. This is the primordial true seed, planted in the *jiāngàn* palace of *qián*, where lead and mercury are refined daily, gradually growing until, one day, it emerges from the shell—only then does the immortal, free of the mortal form, appear. Without this true seed, one’s efforts are in vain. Though some attainment may be made, it will only preserve the physical body and never generate the transcendent form.

Do you understand? With this point of true yang *qi* planted in the womb, one may then direct divine light downward. Over time, as the true yang stirs, one may draw the water of *kān* upward and guide the fire of *lí* downward, refining all the turbid yin dross and corpse-*qi* of the physical form, extracting only pure *qi* to combine with my luminous yang, merging into the pill, nourishing it into spirit—thus achieving flight and transformation. Yet even this is the natural Dao.

Ordinary mortals, trapped in the post-heavenly realm, have depleted spirit and *qi*, and their years grow old. Like travelers far from home, they must return from afar—hence the need for effort. Since spirit and *qi* remain divided, unable to unite, during seated practice, one must focus intent and expend some energy, drawing both spirit and *qi* into the *dāntián*, forbidding even a thread to escape or a breath to stray. One breath in, ten breaths out; ten breaths in, a hundred out—it matters not. Thus, sealing the great gate, preserving spirit at the elixir bolt, one practices in stages. Then, when external breathing pauses, true breathing begins. Again, one nourishes it, and soon the true yang *qi* surges like wind and clouds—then one quickly opens the pass and guides it upward. Its ascent is guided by spirit, not *qi*; one need only focus the mind on the *wěilú* and proceed.

When the true *qi* rushes forth, one nourishes it briefly before descending. In short, when true yang first stirs, some effort is required before ascent or descent becomes possible. For the turbid *qi* of the mortal form is too heavy; only vigorous agitation can dispel its filth, revealing the pure and luminous spirit-*qi* that will become the foundation of the pill. Hence the ancients said: *"At first, one gathers the medicine—without fierce, martial fire and urgent refining, true gold cannot be extracted from the ore. This is why martial fire is called ‘wild battle.’"*

Once ascent and descent are complete, when the *dāntián* is full and the mind at ease, one may then employ the method of refining the void, following the *qi*’s natural movements to perfect it.

「This, though seemingly forced, is indeed the natural way things must be—one should practice accordingly.

**Chapter Eighty-One: Acting Without Striving**
*"Genuine words are not adorned; adorned words are not genuine.*
*The virtuous do not argue; those who argue are not virtuous.
*The wise do not seek vast knowledge; those who seek vast knowledge lack true wisdom.
*The Sage does not hoard.*

*Having given to others, the Sage gains even more;
Having shared with others, the Sage’s virtue increases.*

*The Dao of Heaven is beneficial yet not harmful;
The Dao of the Sage is to act without striving.*

This chapter summarizes the entire text, showing that all words of the *Moral Teachings* are true and unerring. One must not dismiss this scripture merely because its language is not elegant, placing it aside without discussion or contemplation.

*Know that the Dao has no name—it is forcibly named as the Way;
The Way has no words—all words are obstacles.*

Yet for the sake of teaching and guiding beings, one must establish provisional names as stepping-stones for later learners. Once words are spoken, they must come from sincerity, and their expression must be measured and true.

*This is indeed a profound and earnest discourse,*
*aligned with antiquity yet free from error,*
*applied to the present yet universally fitting.*
*Without embellishment or falsehood,*
*it requires no rhetorical flourish—why seek excess?*
*Direct transmission and unadorned guidance reveal the very nature of one’s own mind.*

*Words are genuine when spoken from sincerity—no need for ornamentation.*
*As for eloquent prose and literary grace,*
*that is the speech of scholars and literati,*
*who adorn themselves with superficial brilliance to please the world,*
*yet their words lack true authenticity.*

*If their words are genuine and they act with virtue,*
*they need not resort to sophistry to deceive others—what need for argument then?*
*Those who argue are like the deaf and blind claiming sharp hearing and keen sight,*
*pretending to wisdom beyond their actual grasp,*
*covering ears while stealing bells—*
*such behavior is hardly virtuous.*

*Virtue lies within oneself, wisdom within one’s heart—*
*why must it be vast?*
*Confucius said, “My Way is one, and it runs through all things.”*
*Mencius said, “The Way is but one.”*
*What need for breadth is there?*

*Those who seek breadth are like those who fail to grasp the Way’s true essence,*
*whose words lack proper alignment,*
*who wander through the doctrines of myriad schools—*
*this is mere memorization and rhetorical study,*
*not the Sage’s profound learning in the classics,*
*nor the cultivation of the Great and Just through proper conduct.*

*In essence, the Way is such that nothing exists beyond it.*
*It can be taken without restraint,*
*used without exhaustion.*

*The Sage is both empty and not empty,*
*both possessing and not possessing.*
*Like a bright mirror suspended in the void,*
*reflecting clear waves without end—*
*how could there be any accumulation?*
*If there were accumulation,*
*it would mean the mirror is stained with dust,*
*the water is muddied with sediment—*
*this is not the Sage’s original, empty and luminous essence,*
*nor the boundless and impartial nature of the Sage.*

*Only when the Sage’s essence is thus empty and luminous,*
*does he respond to circumstances and follow natural inclinations.*
*Though the myriad people are in chaos,*
*virtue cannot be universally applied,*
*yet if one man bears guilt and takes responsibility,*
*his virtue is already complete.*

*Even if all nine regions are aligned,*
*blessings cannot reach everywhere,*
*yet if one place remains untouched by kindness,*
*this heart will always bear sorrow.*
*In giving to others,*
*how could there be any excess in humility?*

*Thus it is said:*
*Having given to others, the Sage’s merit grows even greater;
Having shared with others, the Sage’s virtue becomes even more abundant.*

*It is like the mirror’s light—*
*when things approach, they are reflected;*
*when things depart, the reflection ceases,*
*with no premeditated intent in between.*

*The Sage’s heart is also as such.*

*Moreover,*
*The Sage’s heart is the very heart of Heaven,*
*The Sage’s Way is the very Way of Heaven.*

*Heaven operates in silent transformation,*
*though there are phases of waxing and waning,*
*of fullness and emptiness—*
*all belong to the mechanism of nurturing life,*
*offering benefit without harm.*

*The Sage acts without contrivance,*
*though there may be loss or gain,*
*bestowal or deprivation—*
*all belong to the response of benevolence,*
*also acting without striving.*

*If Heaven and Earth were to act with benefit or harm,*
*then Heaven and Earth would be partial, not impartial,*
*and how could they endure for ten thousand years without change?*
*If the Sage were to act with contrivance or striving,*
*then the Sage would hoard, not disperse,*
*and how could his utmost sincerity persist without end?*

*Ah!*
*Heaven and Earth are vast; the Sage is vast.*
*Even if words are genuine,*
*they arise from the heart’s expression,*
*without pretense or artifice.*
*Rooted in self and verified among the common people,*
*it is the inherent virtue of Heaven,*
*a shared faculty of all human hearts.*

*To act for others is to act for oneself,*
*to give to others is to give to oneself.*
*This is the principle of unity between self and other,*
*of shared origin between Heaven and humanity,*
*where the Sage aligns with Heaven’s virtue,*
*and his measure is revealed.*

**Closing Words**
Now that this scripture’s annotations are complete, I call upon all my disciples and say:

*"Today, the Great Dao is as fragile as a pile of eggs.
It depends on you, those who study the Way,
to uphold Heaven and Earth,
to rectify and restore the cosmic balance.

Even if one speaks of the wicked and rebellious,
there may be conflict and violence,
yet Heaven has its arrangements—
it will never allow you to face unforeseen calamity.

Only fear that you may hold virtue too narrowly,
fail to embrace the Way with sincerity,
waver in resolve—
sometimes acting, sometimes abandoning it—
for then you sever your connection with Heaven.

Unable to align with Heaven above,
even if Heaven bestows its tenfold benevolence
and desires to nurture you in a realm of peace and tranquility,
how could you receive and uphold Heaven’s grace if you cannot meet it halfway?

You have now nearly attained the Way and understood its essence,
mastering its practice with firm conviction.
Only when you move or speak,
must you breathe in harmony with Heaven—
then Heaven will love and cherish you,
embrace and protect you without ceasing.

For the Way is Heaven’s presence,
to cherish the Way is to cherish Heaven,
to love the Way is to love Heaven.

When such silent accord is achieved,
you will naturally attain auspicious blessings.

The Way of Heaven is inherently aligned with the Way of humanity—
observe throughout antiquity and the present,
only those who defy the Way fail to gain Heaven’s favor,
never has one who follows the Way failed to receive Heaven’s blessing.
You should now awaken to this truth.

In summary,*
*each should follow their own conviction,*
*each should fulfill their own sincerity.*
*Beyond this, external judgments of right and wrong,*
*of fortune and misfortune,*
*all belong to Heaven’s ultimate decree.*
*You must not fret over taking others’ roles—*
*leave such concerns to Heaven."*」

「The essence of the Great Dao lies solely in the interplay of **shén** [神, spirit/mind] and **qì** [氣, qi].

Yet there is a distinction between **xiān tiān** [先天, prenatal] and **hòu tiān** [後天, postnatal]—this the cultivator must never fail to grasp.

The ancient scriptures say:
The **xiān tiān yuán shén** [先天元神, primordial prenatal spirit] is the **tǐ** [體, substance];
The **hòu tiān shí shén** [後天識神, postnatal awakened spirit] is the **yòng** [用, function].
Without the **xiān tiān yuán shén**, the Great Dao has no **zhǔ** [主, master];
Without the **hòu tiān shí shén**, the Great Dao lacks application.

You must diligently refine and cultivate—
at the moment when all is **hún hún** [混混, obscure] and **duì duì** [沌沌, chaotic],
when there is neither knowing nor awareness,
you must nurture the **xiān tiān yuán shén** to serve as sovereign.

Then, with a sudden start and awakening,
the **hòu tiān shí shén** emerges—
not the fleeting, erratic **sī shí** [私識, private perceptions],
but the **zhèng děng zhèng jué** [正等正覺, true and proper awakened spirit],
which stirs and brings forth cognition—hence it is called **shí shén** [識神, spirit of discernment].

Fear only that once this **shí shén** arises,
it will be **fēn fēn rǎo rǎo** [紛紛擾擾, tumultuous and disturbed],
with delusive, chaotic thoughts flooding endlessly—
and you will fall into **sī liú** [私流, private currents] and **yù** [欲, desire],
rendering you incapable of refining the **dān** [丹, elixir].

Only when the mind is **yī xīn liào zhào** [一心了照, singularly illuminated]
and your resolve is **shì zhì méi tā** [矢志靡它, unwaveringly fixed]
will your scattered thoughts cease,
your **shén** [神, spirit] congeal,
and your breath become regulable—
for breath adjusted, the elixir will form.

Thus it is said:
*"A single mind focused on the silk threads,
no sight of the white reeds across the river."*

Even though called **shí shén** [識神, spirit of discernment],
its nature is that of the **yuán shén** [元神, primordial spirit].

Hence the ancients declared:
*"Heaven’s heart as sovereign, primordial spirit as function.
Skillfully employ the thief’s mechanism,
returning it to the造化 [zào huà, creative transformation] of yin and yang."*
What need then for fear of failing to ascend to **shèng shén** [聖神, sacred spirit]!

Do you also comprehend this truth?
In essence, once Heaven’s heart is activated,
you must constantly **wěn xù** [常常穩蓄, stabilize and contain it],
not allowing a single thought to wander,
a single breath to stray into impurity—
then Heaven’s heart will remain steadfast,
the Dao-heart will congeal,
and though **shí** [識, discernment] exists, it will be as if it does not.

For the scholar refining **zhēn** [真, the True],
at the very outset of practice,
the key lies in subduing the mind
and silencing the **máng shí** [妄識, false perceptions] of the eyes, ears, body, and intentions.
Thus, you gather the spirit at the **dān qiāng** [丹扃, elixir gate],
and regulate the breath at the **dān tián** [丹田, elixir field],
striving to extinguish all **fán xī** [凡息, ordinary breaths],
until the **yuán qì** [元氣, primordial qi] begins to submit.

Once the **yuán qì** [元氣, primordial qi] returns,
**yīn yīn** [氤氤, misty] and **huó pō** [活泼, lively],
**wǎn zhuǎn yōu yáng** [宛轉悠揚, undulating and free],
like a living dragon in motion—
tenfold vigor and vitality.

This robust **yuán qì** [元氣, primordial qi] suffices to operate the **hé chē** [河車, River Vehicle].

If the **qì** [氣, qi] and **jī** [機, mechanism] surge violently,
and you fail to perform the **hé chē** [河車, River Vehicle] method—
transforming **jīng** [精, essence] into **qì** [氣, qi],
and **qì** [氣, qi] into **shén** [神, spirit]—
you must still congeal at the **dān dǐng** [丹鼎, elixir cauldron].
Yet without proper **huǒ huà** [火化, fire transformation],
the **yīn jīng** [陰精, yin essence] cannot solidify,
and will not remain long in the **hòu tiān dǐng** [後天鼎, postnatal cauldron].
The moment **fán huǒ** [凡火, ordinary fire] stirs,
it will incite **yīn gēn** [淫根, lustful roots]
and **shēng yín shì** [生淫事, generate lustful matters],
and all will be lost.

Even if you forcefully suppress it,
preventing any movement,
the **hòu tiān jīng qì** [後天精氣, postnatal essence-qi]
are all of pure **yīn** [陰, yin],
untempered and unrefined—
they will inevitably leak,
whether suppressed or not.

With this method practiced,
you can seize control from Heaven, Earth, and the **guǐ shén** [鬼神, spirits and demons],
and master the **zào huà yīn yáng** [造化陰陽, transformative laws of yin and yang],
thus commanding and reclaiming:
*"My life is mine to command, not Heaven’s."*

This is the Dao of the **cháng shēng bù lǎo** [長生不老, immortal longevity],
the so-called **Yán luó lǎo zǐ** [閻羅老子, Lord of the Underworld]
having no power over you.

Thus, the method must not be revealed to the unworthy,
lest Heaven’s spirit lose its authority over **shàn è bào yìng** [善惡報應, virtue and vice, rewards and retributions].

Do you also grasp this intent?
You must **jǐn cuī màn gǔ** [緊催慢鼓, tightly urge and slowly strike],
activating the **tuó tǔ jī guān** [橐龠機關, bellows mechanism],
until **fán xī** [凡息, ordinary breaths] cease,
**zhēn xī** [真息, true breaths] emerge,
the human heart dies,
and the Dao-heart is reborn.

This **hé chē** [河車, River Vehicle] method is as described.
Yet if applied indiscriminately to **shǒu zhōng** [守中, holding the center],
before the **qì jī** [氣機, qi mechanisms] are unobstructed,
and the **xīn shén** [心神, mind-spirit] is at peace,
merely relying on **chún rèn zìrán** [純任自然, absolute passivity],
how can the spirit and qi ever coalesce?
What medicinal substance could you then extract?

This must be done when the **xuān guān** [玄關, Mysterious Gate] first appears—
as the **shèn qì** [腎氣, kidney qi] ascends
and the **xīn yè** [心液, heart fluid] descends,
employing the **shù xī** [數息, counted breaths] as **wǔ huǒ** [武火, martial fire],
without allowing a single thought to stray,
a single breath to rush unchecked.

Only then will the **fán xī** [凡息, ordinary breaths] still,
the **zhēn xī** [真息, true breaths] manifest,
the human heart perish,
and the Dao-heart arise.

If you speak loosely of **naturalness** [自然],
there can be no **naturalness** at all.

Thus it is said: Even those with **innate wisdom** [生知, *shēngzhī*]—the **sages** [聖人, *shèngrén*]—
must still **descend to labored knowledge** [下困知勉行工夫, *xiàkùn zhī miǎnxíng gōngfū*],
and only then will they attain it.

The ancients spoke:
*"The White Tiger of West Mountain rages unchecked,*
*the Green Dragon of East Sea cannot be withstood.*
*With both hands seize them to make them fight to the death—*
*transformed into a single mass of purple jade frost."*

And again:
*"To subdue the Dragon, your will must be as vast as Heaven,*
*to tame the Tiger, your heart must be bold, your qi like smoke.*
*Even a **foolish and dullard** [痴蠢愚人, *chīchōng yúrén*] can grasp this,*
*and I will teach them to stand upon the earth as **transcendent beings** [神仙, *shénxiān*]."*

This **martial fire** [武火, *wǔhuǒ*],
when applied to the time when **Dragon and Tiger** [龍虎, *lónghǔ*] do not yet meet,
when **water and fire** [水火, *shuǐhuǒ*] do not yet harmonize,
then it may be used.
But if practicing the **River Vehicle** [河車, *héchē*],
when **Dragon roars and Tiger howls** [龍吟虎啸, *lóngyín hǔxiào*],
when **husband and wife respond in unison** [夫唱妇随, *fūchàng fùsuí*],
and you still employ this method,
then you risk **forcing the true qi to scatter and disperse** [迫逐真气散乱, *pòizhú zhēnqì sànluàn*],
as **Mencius** [孟子] said:
*"Like chasing a **fleeing piglet** [放豚, *fàngtún*],*
*once it has entered the pen,*
*you call it back—*
*this is a grave error indeed."*

I will reveal the entire **cultivation method** [功, *gōng*],
so you must **strive with diligence** [努力修持, *nǔlì xiūchí*],
to fulfill my teacher’s hopes.
Do not **recklessly disclose** [妄泄, *wàngxiè*],
lest you **bring harm upon yourself** [自干罪咎, *zìgān zuìjiù*].

**The Annotations of Huang Yuanji on the Daoist Classic of Virtue and the Way (Final Section)**

「黄元吉道德经注 终」
